Gray Matter III

Music

Jewish Perspectives on Music

Cultural activities often constitute a defining characteristic of a society, and they certainly represent a large percentage of many people’s leisure pursuits. In the next few chapters, we will discuss Jewish perspectives on one such activity, namely, the propriety of enjoying various forms of music. Of course, the ideas we will share apply to a great extent to all leisure activities.
<b>General Perspectives on Music</b>
Before we explore the halachic issues concerning music, we should review some basic ideas about it from the <i>Tanach</i> and Gemara, which are replete with illustrations of the major role that song and music can play. After the splitting of the Yam Suf, for example, Moshe Rabbeinu led the Jewish men in song, and Miriam likewise led the women. The <i>levi’im</i> accompanied the service in the <i>Beit Hamikdash</i> with singing and musical instrumentation, an important aspect of the service and one that we mention in our daily prayers. The Gemara (Megillah 32a, see <i>Tosafot</i> ad. loc. s.v. <i>V’hashoneh</i>) strongly encourages us to sing the Torah we study. As these and other sources indicate, music is a central element in many aspects of Torah life.
Two sources in particular demonstrate the great importance that the Torah ascribes to music. The first is the Torah’s description (Bereishit 4:20-22) of some of humanity’s first great accomplishments and advances. Included in this list are the breeding of cattle, the use of iron and copper implements (see Targum Onkelos ad. loc.), and the development of music. This shows that the Torah regards music as a core achievement of mankind.
The second source is a powerful Gemara (Chagigah 15b, see Rashi and Maharshah ad. loc.), which wonders how the great Tanna (scholar of the Mishanic period) Elisha ben Avuyah lost his faith. Why did his great knowledge of Torah fail to prevent him from abandoning the Torah? The Gemara answers that it was because “Greek music never ceased to emerge from his mouth.” The lesson is obvious. Music has a profound effect on both the individual and the community. The negative impact of some popular music on society during the past forty years is a contemporary example of this phenomenon. Music can constitute a crowning achievement, drawing us closer to Hashem and His holy Torah, but it also has the potential, God forbid, to lead us astray. With this idea in mind, let us explore some of the halachic issues concerning music.
<b>Talmudic Sources</b>
In light of the above, it is not surprising to find that <i>Chazal</i> issued a number of restrictions regarding music. The Mishnah (Sotah 9:11) records that when the Sanhedrin (high rabinnic court) ceased to function, <i>Chazal</i> forbade song in the wine houses. The Yerushalmi (Sotah 9:12) explains the reason for this decree: “At first, people feared the authority of the Sanhedrin and would not introduce inappropriate content in song, but now [that the Sanhedrin has ceased to function], people no longer fear its authority and would introduce corrupt lyrics into music.”
The Gemara (Sotah 48a), in keeping with this theme, declares that the song of the ship workers and the farmers is permitted, but the song of the weavers is forbidden. Rashi (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Zimri D’nagdei</i>) explains that the permitted songs were not frivolous – they helped the workers and animals perform their tasks. The weavers’ songs, on the other hand, were forbidden because they served no constructive purpose – they were entirely frivolous.
The Gemara elsewhere (Gittin 7a) presents a seemingly more drastic prohibition. It records that <i>Chazal</i> simply forbade listening to all music subsequent to the <i>churban</i> (destruction) of the <i>Beit Hamikdash</i>.
<i><b>Rishonim</b></i><b> – Rashi, <i>Tosafot</i>, and the Rambam<i></i></b>
The <i>Rishonim</i> debate to what extent the Rabbis prohibited the enjoyment of music in the post-<i>churban</i> era. Rashi (Gittin 7a s.v. <i>Zimra</i>) indicates that the prohibition is limited to singing in a tavern. <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>ibid</i>. s.v. <i>Zimra Mina Lan D’assur</i>) support Rashi’s contention based on the aforementioned Mishnah in Sotah. <i>Tosafot</i> argue that this source leads us to conclude that the prohibition applies only to playing music in a drinking house.
<i>Tosafot</i> also add two important qualifications. First, they state that it is inappropriate to listen to music excessively, regardless of the context. As proof, they cite an anecdote that appears in the Yerushalmi (Megillah 3:2) in which Mar Ukva chastised the <i>Reish Galuta </i>(Exilarch) for listening to music when “going to sleep and waking up” – i.e., excessively.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">On this basis, Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach (<i>Halichot Shlomo </i>1:13:18, <i>Devar Halachah</i> 28) rules that it is forbidden to wake up to an alarm clock that plays music, as this is considered “excessive.”</i>
Second, <i>Tosafot</i> assert that music that is played in the context of a <i>mitzvah</i>, such as at a wedding celebration, is entirely permissible. The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Ta’aniyot</i> 5:14), despite the general stringency of his rulings on this issue (see below), similarly writes that it is permissible to play music of a religious nature. This exception dates back at least to the Geonic era, as Rav Hai Gaon (cited by the Rif, Berachot 21b in the pages of the Rif) espouses this approach. The Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 560:3) codifies this ruling uncontested.
Although Rashi and <i>Tosafot</i> rule fairly leniently on this issue, permitting listening to music on a moderate basis outside of taverns, the Rambam adopts a much stricter approach. He writes (<i>Hilchot Ta’aniyot</i> 5:14) that instrumental music (other than religious music) is entirely forbidden, and even vocal music without instrumental accompaniment is prohibited if it is in the context of wine consumption. The <i>Tur</i> (O.C. 560) cites a responsum of the Rambam in which he adopts an even stricter stand, prohibiting even vocal music unaccompanied by instruments and not sung in the context of wine-drinking.
<i><b>Shulchan Aruch</b></i><b> and Its Commentaries<i></i></b>
The dispute between the Rambam and Rashi/<i>Tosafot</i> has remained a matter of controversy in every subsequent generation, even to this day. The Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 560:3) rules in accordance with the Rambam’s view as presented in <i>Hilchot Ta’aniyot</i>, but the Rama cites the opinion of Rashi and <i>Tosafot</i>. The Magen Avraham (560:9) cites the <i>Bach</i>, who rules even more strictly, positing that the Rambam’s view as set forth in his responsum is normative. Accordingly, they rule that music is always forbidden unless it is of religious content and nature.
In addition, the Sha’ar Hatziyun (560:23) cites the Rama’s (<i>Hagahot Mordechai</i>,<i> Gittin </i>1:1) position that even playing music over wine is prohibited only if wine is the only thing being served. If there is a meal being served with the wine, it is permissible to play music. He writes that “perhaps” one need not protest against those who rely on this position.
<b>Nineteenth-Century Codes</b>
The great nineteenth-century authorities continued to debate this issue. While the <i>Chayei Adam</i> (137:3) and Mishnah Berurah (560:13) cite the ruling of the Magen Avraham and <i>Bach</i> as normative, the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (560:7) seems to adopt a more lenient approach. He does not cite the opinion of the Magen Avraham and <i>Bach</i>, but he does cite the opinion of the Rama. Whereas the former two authorities are critical of women who sing while doing their work,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This ctitique is independent of the prohibition for a man to listen to a woman sing (<i>kol ishah</i>).</i> the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> does not criticize them. He appears to regard the lenient approach of Rashi and <i>Tosafot</i> as acceptable.
<b>Contemporary Authorities</b>
Contemporary authorities, as well, have disagreed about this question. Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> O.C. 1:166) adopts a fairly strict stance concerning this matter. Although he writes that one is not required to follow the most stringent opinion of the Magen Avraham and <i>Bach</i>, he regards the strict opinion of the Shulchan Aruch to be normative. On the other hand, Rav Eliezer Waldenberg (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 15:33) endorses the common practice to follow the ruling of the Rama that music in moderation is permitted outside a tavern. Rav Yehudah Amital told me that he agrees with Rav Waldenberg. In addition, even Rav Moshe (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> O.C. 3:87) writes that one should not object to someone who follows the ruling of the Rama regarding music.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">We should note that the traditional custom in Jerusalem is not to play music even at weddings; the only music is a drummer who also sings. For a discussion of this custom, see <i>Teshuvot Salmat Chaim </i>1:77 and<i> Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer </i>15:33:3.</i>
<b>Possible Exceptions</b>
An interesting argument appears in Rav Yaakov Breisch’s responsum on this issue (<i>Teshuvot Chelkat Yaakov</i> 1:62). He suggests that even according to the Magen Avraham and <i>Bach</i>, this prohibition applies only to live music, but not to recorded music. This ruling has been applied in practice by some individuals to the periods of time during which it is our custom to refrain from listening to music, such as during <i>sefirat ha’omer</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (O.C. 493:2), <i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> (O.C. 3:87), <i>Teshuvot Minchat Yitzchak </i>(1:111), and <i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> (3:30) for an assessment of the sources for this custom.</i> The Three Weeks,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe Bleich summarizes the various opinions on this topic in <i>Tradition</i> (41:1:11-15).</i> and the twelve-month mourning period for a parent. However, Rav Moshe Feinstein (in his aforementioned responsum and Y.D. 2:137:2) clearly indicates that he does not subscribe to this approach, as he prohibits instrumental recorded music during <i>sefirat ha’omer</i>. Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> 6:34) likewise states explicitly that he does not permit listening to recorded music at any time when live music is prohibited.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Dov Brisman (<i>Teshuvot Shalmei Chovah </i>O.C. 78) writes that the accepted practice has become to forbid listening to recorded music during <i>sefirat ha’omer</i> and The Three Weeks. Similarly, Rav Moshe Bleich (<i>Tradition </i>41:1:20) concludes, “The consensus of halakhic opinion is that taped music is forbidden during <i>sefira</i> and the Three Weeks.” We should note that Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik (reported by Rav Aharon Lichtenstein) believes that music was not forbidden at all during these time periods, but most <i>poskim</i> do not share this view.</i>
Rav Shmuel David (<i>Techumin</i> 13:187) suggests another possible exception to this prohibition. He contends that classical music is not necessarily included in the post-<i>churban</i> rabbinic decree against listening to music. He bases this suggestion on the Maharshal (<i>Yam Shel Shlomo</i>, Gittin 1:17), who writes that listening to music “to hear pleasant sounds or to hear something fresh” is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">It is reported in the name of Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik that classical music was not included in the rabbinic decree. The decree, in Rav Soloveitchik’s view, applies only to music of revelry.</i>
<b>Conclusion</b>
Despite music’s central role in Jewish beliefs and practices, Halachah imposes definite restrictions on listening to it. The general practice tends to follow those <i>poskim</i> who permit listening to at least some forms of music, but other forms, such as drinking songs, are universally prohibited. Though the exact parameters and applicability of the restrictions remain unresolved, it is clear that whatever we do listen to must be compatible with a Torah <i>hashkafah</i> (worldview).<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Dr. Shalom Buchbinder and Dr. Jason DiPoce have co-authored an (as yet unpublished) essay outlining the dangers and halachic problems associated with playing music loudly. One should exercise moderation and caution not only regarding the content of the music he chooses to listen to, but also in the realm of how he listens to it.</i>

Torah Passages in Song

<b>A Strict Ruling</b>
An interesting article appears in the 5754 edition of <i>Beit Yitzchak</i>. In this essay, Rav Yosef Levenbrown seeks to demonstrate that it is forbidden to sing or listen to songs whose lyrics are derived from Torah sources unless they are conducted in the context of a <i>mitzvah</i>, such as Shabbat, <i>Yom Tov</i>, or a wedding. In fact, he concludes with the following citation from Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe </i>Y.D. 1:173, written in 1957): “In my humble opinion, it is forbidden <i>l’chatchilah </i>(ideally) to make records of [songs whose lyrics are from] <i>pesukim</i> purely for listening enjoyment.”
This ruling is based on the Gemara (Sanhedrin 101a) that states:
One who reads a <i>pasuk</i> from Shir Hashirim and transforms it into a sort of song or sings a <i>pasuk</i> in a party house (<i>beit hamishta’ot</i>) at an inappropriate time brings evil to the world, since the Torah wears sackcloth and complains before Hashem, “Your children have made me into a musical instrument that is played by scorners (<i>leitzim</i>).”
Although this passage is not quoted by the Rambam, the Shulchan Aruch, <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This omission is quite puzzling in light of the fact that both the Rif (Berachot 21b and Sanhedrin 19b-20a in the Rif’s pages) and the Rosh (Berachot 5:1 and Sanhedrin 11:3) cite this passage as normative.</i> or the Rama, the Magen Avraham (560:10) cites from the Maharil that it is improper to use <i>pesukim</i> as lyrics for songs that are sung at a “<i>simchat merei’ut</i>” (social gathering). The Taz (O.C 560:5) adopts a similar approach, and the Mishnah Berurah (560:14) and the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (O.C. 560:7) cite the words of the Magen Avraham as normative.
Nonetheless, the very widespread practice today among observant Jews is to sing and listen to music whose lyrics are from Torah sources. Indeed, even Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 2:142, written in 1963) acknowledges:
Many are lenient and listen to tapes [of music whose lyrics are from Torah passages], and even in the previous generation, people would play records of music whose lyrics were derived from Torah passages, and the rabbis of that generation did not protest. And we see [today] that most people listen to such music, including even the most pious of individuals.
Rav Moshe thus relaxes his strict stance on this matter and advises only those who are <i>ba’alei nefesh</i> (individuals who are exceptionally scrupulous in their observance of Halachah) to avoid listening to “Torah songs” if they are merely for enjoyment. In this chapter, we shall marshal four defenses for the common practice of the overwhelming majority of the observant community to listen to Torah passages set to music.
<b>Justification One - Shir Hashirim</b>
Rav Moshe (in his more lenient responsum) suggests that those who are lenient might argue that the Gemara refers only to Shir Hashirim, as there is more concern that the verses from this <i>sefer</i> might be misinterpreted as a simple love song between a man and a woman rather than as an allegory of the love between Hashem and <i>Am Yisrael </i>(the Jewish people), as <i>Chazal</i> interpret it. Rav Moshe notes that the difficulty with this approach is that Rashi (Sanhedrin 101a s.v. <i>Hachi Garsinan</i> and s.v. <i>Hakorei</i>) implies that this prohibition applies to all of the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashi, the Gemara chose the example of Shir Hashirim because one might have thought that the <i>pesukim</i> of this <i>sefer</i> should be allowed to be made into a song, as they originally were written as one.</i>
Moreover, the <i>Kitzur Piskei Harosh</i> (Sanhedrin 11:3), <i>Ra’avyah</i> (at the beginning of his commentary to the fifth chapter of Berachot), and Maharshah (s.v. <i>Hakorei</i>) agree with Rashi that this issue applies to all Torah passages. Nonetheless, Rav Reuven Margaliyot (<i>Margaliyot Hayam Sanhedrin</i> 101a) cites the <i>Zohar</i> and <i>Avot D’rabi Natan </i>(at the end of chapter 36) as supporting the view that the prohibition is limited to Shir Hashirim. In addition, Rav Mordechai Willig (in a shiur delivered at the Morasha Kollel) noted that the Rivevan (at the beginning of his commentary to the fifth chapter of Berachot s.v. <i>Pasuk</i>) clearly confines the prohibition to verses from Shir Hashirim.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Mordechai Willig told me that he once sat next to Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik at a wedding ceremony at which the <i>chazzan</i> (cantor) intoned a song from Shir Hashirim. Rav Soloveitchik commented, “<i>Issur gamur mi’dinah d’Gemara</i>” (this is forbidden based on a passage in the Gemara). It is not clear, though, whether Rav Soloveitchik intended to limit the prohibition to Shir Hashirim or merely was commenting on the case at hand. Rav Willig believes that Rav Soloveitchik intended to confine the prohibition to Shir Hashirim, as Rav Soloveitchik never (to his knowledge) objected to any other songs that contained Torah lyrics.</i> On the other hand, the aforementioned Magen Avraham, Taz, and Mishnah Berurah appear to apply this prohibition to all parts of Torah.
<b>Justification Two - A Mockery</b>
A much stronger justification for the common practice might be derived from Rashi (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Hakorei</i>). He explains that the Gemara censures one who “while drinking wine, derives his amusement from words of Torah and reads <i>pesukim</i> aloud to amuse those at the party.” Rashi seems to imply that the prohibition applies only when Torah verses are used in a degrading manner, as the language of the Gemara (ad. loc.) seems to suggest. This gives the impression (unlike Rav Moshe) that as long as the music does not degrade the Torah, there is no prohibition, even if one is not listening to the music in the context of performing a <i>mitzvah</i> (such as rejoicing with a <i>chatan</i> and <i>kallah</i>). This limitation of the prohibition also is implied by the comments of the Rambam (Avot 1:17), wherein he writes, “The Torah forbade turning words of prophecy into songs of baseness and inappropriate things.” This indicates that the prohibition exists only if the <i>pesukim</i> are sung in a degrading manner. If, on the other hand, the songs are not of “baseness and inappropriate things,” it would be permissible.
Indeed, the <i>Yad Ramah</i> (Sanhedrin 101a s.v. <i>Tanu Rabanan) </i>writes that this prohibition exists only if the <i>pesukim</i> are sung “<i>derech sechok</i>” (in a joking or degrading manner), although he cautions that he is unsure about this matter. However, Rav Yaakov Emden<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Emden’s opinion appears in his comments to Sanhedrin (101a) that recently have been republished in the Vagshal printing of <i>Masechet Sanhedrin</i>, Yerushalayim 5754.</i> states unequivocally that this prohibition applies only if one degrades the Torah verses into a base love song. Indeed, Rav Aharon Lichtenstein endorsed this position in a personal conversation. This might explain why the Rambam, Shulchan Aruch, and Rama omit any mention of this passage in Sanhedrin. They seem to believe that there is no specific prohibition to enjoy listening to Torah music for entertainment purposes. Rather, the Gemara simply articulates a specific example of the general prohibition of degrading our holy Torah.
Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3 O.C. 15 and 4 Y.D. 20) also rules that the prohibition applies only if the <i>pesukim</i> are used for love songs or <i>leitzanut</i> (frivolity). He argues that the <i>Yad Ramah</i>’s uncertainty is resolved by the many eminent rabbanim and communities who have adopted the lenient approach to this issue. The <i>Piskei Teshuvot</i> (560:14 notes 68 and 69) cites several authorities that accept this position. Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Shlomo Zalman’s position is reported in <i>Halichot Shlomo</i> (<i>Orach Halachah: Tefillah </i>13 note 72).</i> similarly permits songs that are sung for “<i>hit’orerut</i>” (awakening feelings of closeness to Hashem) even if they contain <i>pesukim</i>.
<b>Justification Three - Connection to Hashem</b>
A third defense is that in today’s world, one has a choice of literally thousands of music stations, both on the radio and the internet, as well as innumerable music CDs of a virtually endless array of styles and artists. Accordingly, one who chooses to listen to “Jewish music” is doing so because he wishes to be inspired and deepen his connection to Hashem and His Torah. Such a person could be considered as doing so for <i>mitzvah</i> purposes, not simply for pleasure, and thus all authorities would permit listening to or singing such songs, since even the Magen Avraham, Taz, and Mishnah Berurah permit singing songs with <i>pesukim</i> if they are sung for a <i>mitzvah</i> purpose.
Indeed, Rav Yehuda Henkin’s concern (<i>Teshuvot Bnei Banim</i> 3:125) that this prohibition is violated when setting Torah lyrics to rock and country songs if the primary intention is for the music and not the Torah lyrics seems to be irrelevant in most cases. If the primary intention was for the music, one simply would procure the original rock and country music.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Only rock and country music that does not contain offensive lyrics is permissible to listen to. </i>
<b>Justification Four - Spiritual Survival</b>
Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik (see the sources cited in <i>Nefesh Harav</i> p. 88) believes that history sometimes can resolve certain halachic and <i>hashkafic</i> disputes, as it indicates the will of Hashem. In this context, Jewish music has proven itself to be a potent component of the critical struggle for Orthodox Jewish cultural survival. In an environment that is saturated with music, much of which is antithetical to Torah values, the existence of a vibrant Jewish music scene is essential as an alternative to the other deleterious options.
Moreover, establishing a sort of Orthodox subculture is absolutely essential for cultural survival. Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In an essay entitled <i>Confrontation</i>.</i> argues that we must follow the example of Avraham Avinu, who presented himself to his non-Jewish neighbors as a “stranger and a resident” (Bereishit 23:4). We believe that while on one hand we must be “residents” and integrate into the economic, scientific, and certain aspects of the cultural life in our country of residence, we also must be “strangers” and form our own “subculture” in order to survive in a country that is hospitable and inviting. Along with the creation of Orthodox shuls, schools, camps, and youth groups, the world of Jewish music has made an enormous contribution to the creation of this Orthodox subculture.
Indeed, the powerful impact of music is evident from the Gemara’s assertion (Chagigah 15b; see the comments of Rashi and the Maharshah ad. loc.) that the famous sage Elisha ben Avuyah, lost his faith because “Greek music never stopped emanating from his mouth.” Additionally, the Gemara (Megillah 32a; see <i>Tosafot</i> ad. loc. s.v. <i>V’hashoneh</i>) stresses the importance of singing the Torah that one studies. <sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See our earlier chapter on Jewish Perspectives on Music.</i> Accordingly, the experience of the past four decades (since Rav Moshe’s second <i>teshuvah</i> was written) teaches that the approach of Rav Moshe’s later <i>teshuvah</i> must be adopted, since Torah songs are an absolutely essential part of Torah life for the overwhelming majority of the members of our community.
Interestingly, Rav Moshe permits (even in his first, stricter responsum) making records composed of Torah songs if it will help a child learn Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This assertion is based on the next passage in the Gemara (Sanhedrin 101a) and Rashi (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Hakorei</i>).</i> Based on this, we may argue that history teaches that Jewish music helps Orthodox Jews to remain Orthodox Jews and is therefore unquestionably permissible.
<b>Conclusion</b>
Rav Eliezer Waldenberg (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 15:33) cites a very compelling comment made by the <i>Teshuvot Halachot Ketanot</i> (1:9). In the course of defending the common practice to recite “<i>shehakol</i>” on coffee rather than “<i>ha’eitz</i>,” he asserts:
The following is a fundamental principle has been passed to us as a tradition from earlier generations: If a certain Halachah is unclear to you, go and see what the common practice is. This is true because Hashem, in His love for His nation, would not have allowed the broader community to go follow a minority opinion had that authority not articulated a viable approach.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See the commentary of the Ramban to Devarim 17:11 for a possible basis for this argument.</i>
The same can be said for the issue of creating and listening to Torah songs. Those who wish to adopt a strict approach to the issue may do so, but they should not impose this stringency on others, for it is too difficult to bear and even culturally dangerous.
<b>Postscript</b>
When I presented the basic ideas of this chapter to Rav Hershel Schachter, the approach “found favor in his eyes,” though he felt that there was room to be <i>machmir</i> (stringent) for those who want to do so. Avi Levinson also presented the key elements of this chapter to Rav Mordechai Willig, who agreed that they were sound. Both Rav Schachter and Rav Willig note that common practice is to adopt the lenient approach to this issue.

Medical Issues

The Prohibition to Smoke

Anecdotal evidence strongly indicates that it has become accepted within some segments of the Orthodox community not to smoke. Indeed, most <i>poskim</i> have unequivocally stated that it is prohibited to smoke. These authorities include Rav David Cohen, Rav Aharon Lichtenstein, Rav Hershel Schachter, Rav Gedalia Schwartz, Rav Ahron Soloveitchik, Rav Mordechai Willig, and the Halacha Committee of the Rabbinical Council of America. Moreover, one of Rav Moshe Feinstein’s leading students, Rav Efraim Greenblatt, rules (<i>Teshuvot Rivevot Efraim </i>8:586) that smoking is prohibited, as do three major Israeli halachic authorities: Rav Chaim David Halevi (<i>Teshuvot Aseih Lecha Rav </i>2:1, 3:18, and 9:28-29), Rav Avigdor Nebenzahl (<i>Assia</i> 5:261), and Rav Eliezer Waldenberg (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer </i>15:39). Similarly, Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach (<i>Teshuvot Minchat Shlomo</i> 2:58:6) writes, “I have never joined those who believe that it remains permissible to smoke [on any day] in our times.” Even Rav Ovadia Yosef, who states in his earlier writings (such as <i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> 5:39, published in 1983) merely that it is preferable to refrain from smoking, has concluded that it is completely prohibited to smoke (<i>Halichot Olam</i> 1 pp. 265-266, published in 1998). Finally, Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> C.M. 2:76) writes that it is forbidden to begin the habit of smoking. Although Rav Moshe’s <i>teshuvah </i>explicitly forbids one to smoke only if he did not begin to do so before the ruling was issued, we shall endeavor to prove that, given current medical data, smoking is prohibited even according to Rav Moshe’s standards.
In this chapter, we shall examine in depth the rulings of these eminent <i>poskim</i> and seek to explain why smoking is unquestionably forbidden.
<b>Smoking on <i>Yom Tov</i></b>
<i>Poskim</i> began to discuss the halachic propriety of smoking in the eighteenth century. At first, they did not discuss health concerns, as smoking was not, generally speaking, known at the time to pose any,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Dr. Fred Rosner (<i>The Jounral of Halacha and Contemporary Society</i>, Fall 1982 p. 33) points out that “Tobacco was first implicated as a cause of cancer in 1761,” but it is possible that this information was not widely known or accepted.</i> but they did debate the permissibility of smoking on <i>Yom Tov</i>.
The Torah (Shemot 12:16) permits <i>hav’arah</i> (kindling a fire) on <i>Yom Tov</i>, but <i>Chazal</i> (see Bei’ur Halachah 502:1 s.v. <i>Ein</i>) forbade creating a new flame. Hence, when we light any fire on <i>Yom Tov</i>, we light it from a preexisting flame. Even on a Torah level, however, it is forbidden to burn incense on <i>Yom Tov</i> (Beitzah 22b and Shulchan Aruch O.C. 511:4), because Halachah does not permit any <i>melachah </i>(creative activity) that is not <i>shaveh l’chol nefesh</i> (customarily enjoyed by all). Burning incense is regarded as an exotic luxury and therefore is not a permissible form of <i>hav’arah</i> on <i>Yom Tov</i>.
<i>Poskim</i> in the eighteenth century began to debate whether smoking is considered <i>shaveh l’chol nefesh</i>. The <i>Korban Netanel</i> (Beitzah 2:10) forcefully argues that smoking is not <i>shaveh l’chol nefesh</i>, pointing out that if one who is not accustomed to smoke were to begin smoking on <i>Yom Tov</i>, he would become ill and disoriented. This demonstrates that smoking is not universally enjoyed. The <i>Chayei Adam </i>(95:13) also prohibits smoking on <i>Yom Tov</i>. The Bei’ur Halachah (511:4 s.v. <i>Ein Osin</i>), on the other hand, presents many <i>Acharonim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">He makes reference to the <i>Sha’arei Teshuvah</i> (511:5), who quotes, among others, the <i>Chacham Tzvi</i>. This position also is espoused by the <i>Pnei Yehoshua</i> (Shabbat 39b s.v. <i>Omnam</i>), who, along with several other <i>Acharonim</i>, is cited approvingly by the <i>Sha’arei Teshuvah</i> (210:9). </i> who permit it. These <i>poskim</i>, the Bei’ur Halachah explains, believe that “Now that many people are accustomed to this, it has become <i>shaveh l’chol nefesh</i>.”
Interestingly, the <i>Sha’arei Teshuvah</i> (511:5, cited in the Bei’ur Halachah) observes that some (outside of Israel) refrain from smoking on the first day of <i>Yom Tov</i> but smoke on the second day. This practice emerges from the Talmudic rule that one should be strict regarding a Torah matter and but may be lenient regarding a rabbinic matter. Since the observance of the second day of <i>Yom Tov</i> outside of Israel is only a rabbinic obligation, one may be lenient regarding an activity disputed by the <i>poskim</i>. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (O.C. 511:11) criticizes this practice, arguing that it diminishes the dignity of the second day of <i>Yom Tov</i>. Indeed, <i>Chazal</i> strove to ensure that we not take the observance of the second day of <i>Yom Tov</i> lightly (see, for example, Shabbat 23a).
<b>Smoking on <i>Yom Tov</i> Nowadays</b>
Many in prior generations, as is well-known, followed the lenient opinion in practice. However, the dramatically lower percentage of people who smoke today, resulting from the great health risks that smoking entails, has prompted <i>poskim </i>to reexamine this matter. Rav Simcha Bunim Cohen (<i>The Laws of Yom Tov</i> p. 106 footnote 1) observes, “In the United States, it should certainly be forbidden to smoke according to all opinions, as the overwhelming majority refrains from smoking.” He cites (p. 108 footnote 3) the precedents of Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach (<i>Teshuvot Minchat Shlomo </i>2:58:6) and Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv (quoted in <i>Sefer Hazikaron Mevakshei Torah</i> 1:264), who both rule that today it is forbidden to smoke on <i>Yom Tov</i>. Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg (in a responsum printed in <i>Pe’eir Tachat Eifer </i>p. 52) also agrees with this position.
Additionally, Rav Moshe Shternbuch (<i>Teshuvot V’hanhagot</i> 1:316) argues that since we rely on doctors’ opinions in many serious areas of Halachah, such as the need for a sick individual to eat on Yom Kippur,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See our later chapter for more on this topic.</i> we certainly should follow their advice to refrain from smoking, which is merely a recreational activity. Rav Shternbuch believes that the <i>Acharonim</i> who permitted smoking on <i>Yom Tov</i> would not have issued tolerant rulings in the current climate, in which it is accepted that smoking poses a grave health hazard.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Shternbuch, in this context writes, “I testify about an eminent Torah scholar who smoked heavily and was struck, Heaven forfend, with lung cancer. A short time before his death, he gathered a <i>minyan</i> of people and proclaimed, ‘I recognize that I caused this sickness and that I am going to die because of my smoking, and I fear that I will be judged by Heaven with the punishment meted out to one who commits suicide, Heaven forfend. Therefore, I seek for my soul some rectification; I ask you to zealously avoid smoking, as it constitutes a very serious prohibition, and with this I hope that since I have thereby brought merit to the community, the Holy One, blessed be He, will ease my punishment’”</i>
<b>Avoiding Danger Year-Round – <i>V’nishmartem Me’od L’nafshoteichem</i></b>
The question of whether smoking is prohibited not only on <i>Yom Tov</i> but at all times hinges upon the general halachic requirement that we refrain from dangerous and unhealthy activities. The source for this requirement is Devarim 4:15, where we are instructed, “<i>V’nishmartem me’od l’nafshoteichem</i>,” “You shall guard your souls exceedingly carefully.”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For a full discussion of this matter, see the essay written by Dr. Shalom Buchbinder and Dr. James DiPoce in <i>The Journal of Halacha and Contemporary Society</i> 42:70-99.</i> The Rambam seems to divide this requirement into two different categories. He lists (<i>Hilchot Dei’ot</i> 4:1) certain foods and activities that, because they weaken the body, one needs (“<i>tzarich</i>”) to avoid, but also mentions (<i>Hilchot Rotzeiach Ushmirat Nefesh</i> 11:5) numerous dangerous activities that <i>Chazal</i> outright prohibited. The activities listed in <i>Hilchot Rotzeiach Ushmirat Nefesh</i> appear to be strictly forbidden, while those in <i>Hilchot Dei’ot</i> seem to be discouraged, but not technically forbidden. Rav Waldenberg (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 15:39) understands that this is because the former are far more dangerous than the latter. Thus, while it is technically prohibited to drink from water in which a snake may have placed its venom, it is not outright prohibited to overindulge in fattening foods.
<b>“Hashem Preserves the Fools”</b>
Into which of these two categories should we place smoking? One way to distinguish between the two categories is the litmus test suggested by the Gemara in a number of places (Shabbat 129b, Yevamot 12b, and Niddah 31b). The Gemara permits certain activities that involve some risk, positing that “Since the multitudes have trodden upon this matter, [the <i>pasuk</i>] ‘Hashem protects the foolish’ (<i>Tehillim</i> 116:6) [applies].” This Gemara teaches that Halachah has allowed reasonable members of society to define the parameters of the prohibition to engage in risky activities; Halachah permits activities that such people judge to involve a tolerable risk. Based on this standard, <i>Teshuvot Chelkat Yaakov</i> (3:11) writes that it is permissible to travel in an airplane or car even though there is some risk involved.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">For further explanation of the concept of Hashem protecting the foolish, see Rav Shlomo Cohen-Duras’s discussion of hazardous sporting activities (<i>Techumin</i> 22:120-126) and our chapters about hazardous medical procedures and cosmetic surgery.</i>
Accordingly, if smoking is included within the “Hashem protects the fools” principle, it should be avoided, but cannot technically be categorized as prohibited. If it is not included within this principle, on the other hand, then it is unequivocally forbidden.
<b>When Does “Hashem Preserves the Fools” Apply?</b>
Two great later <i>Acharonim</i> offer definitions of the parameters of the “Hashem preserves the fools” principle. Rav Chaim Ozer Grodzinsky (<i>Teshuvot Achiezer</i> 1:23) seems to believe that it applies only when the danger is minimal and results in disaster in only a small minority of cases. According to this standard, airplane travel is acceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Ahron Soloveitchik stated as much in a shiur he delivered at Yeshiva University in 1986.</i> whereas cigarette smoking is forbidden, since it involves much more than minimal danger.
It seems, however, that Halachah tolerates greater risk-taking in case of great need. For example, the Gemara (Bava Metzia 112b) notes without criticism that people risk their lives working high up in trees to earn a living. Apparently, <i>Chazal</i> permit a person to risk his life in order to earn a living, even though such risks would not be tolerated if taken merely for recreation. Accordingly, Rav Yechezkel Landau (<i>Teshuvot Noda Biy’hudah</i> 2 Y.D. 10) permits hunting animals to earn a living but forbids recreational hunting.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This Gemara seems to permit entering professions that involve risk to life, such as coal mining and serving as a physician. Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> C.M. 1:104) and Rav Eliezer Waldenberg (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 9:17:5:9) do not limit the <i>Noda Biy’huda</i>’s permissive ruling to a situation in which one does not have any other way to earn a livelihood.</i> Similarly, since smoking is a recreational activity, Halachah is less tolerant of the risks involved.
Rav Yaakov Ettlinger (<i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion</i> 1:137, written in the nineteenth century), in discussing the permissibility of embarking on a sea voyage or a trip across the desert, offers a different definition of “tolerable risk.” He distinguishes between an immediate danger and a long-term danger. Whereas an immediate danger is prohibited in all situations, a future danger may be risked if, in the majority of cases, it can reasonably be expected that no harm will result.
<b>Rav Moshe’s Application of the Principle to Smoking</b>
Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> C.M. 2:76, published in 1981) writes that smoking should be discouraged, like all other unhealthy habits, as the Rambam states in the fourth chapter of <i>Hilchot Dei’ot </i>(as we discussed above). However, smoking cannot technically be declared forbidden, since only a minority of smokers is afflicted with health problems as a result of the habit. In such circumstances, the “Hashem preserves the fools” principle applies. This argument appears to be in harmony with the <i>Binyan Tzion</i>’s criterion for forbidden dangerous activities.
Rav Moshe’s lenient ruling seems no longer to apply, as current research indicates that a majority of smokers will suffer ill effects from this unhealthy habit. Rav J. David Bleich writes (<i>Tradition</i> 37:3:97) that according to “presently available evidence, it appears that the cumulative risks of lung cancer, cardiovascular disease and respiratory illnesses will, in the aggregate, foreshorten the lives of a majority of smokers.” Indeed, Dr. Jeffrey Berman<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Dr. Berman is an expert on recovery from addiction (including smoking) at the Robert Wood Johnson Medical Center in New Jersey.</i> reports that a staggering eighty-five percent of smokers will suffer health problems as a result of their habit.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In its ruling, the Halacha Committee of the Rabbinical Council of America cites the <i>British Medical Journal</i> (June 22, 2004, available at www.bmj.com), which reports, based on a fifty-year study, that smokers die, on average, ten years younger than non-smokers, with between one half and two thirds of those who begin smoking during their youth dying from this habit.</i>
Moreover, Rav Bleich notes (ad. loc. p. 96) that the Gemara applies the “Hashem preserves the fools” principle only when the behavior is “trodden upon by the multitudes.” Although smoking was a path well-trodden by the multitudes when Rav Moshe wrote his original lenient responsum (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 2:49) in 1964, Rav Bleich contends “It is more than likely that, at present, that condition no longer obtains.” In its ruling, the Halacha Committee of the Rabbinical Council of America reports that only about twenty-five percent of Americans currently smoke.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The Committee also reports that studies show that in the early-mid 1960s, over fifty percent of adult males were smokers.</i>
This contrasts sharply with what Rav Bleich wrote in an earlier issue of <i>Tradition</i> (16:4:122-123): “There is little doubt that although the road is fraught with danger it is – at least for the present – indeed a path well-trodden by the multitude.…Since even in light of presently available evidence it appears that the majority of smokers do not compromise their health and do not face premature death as a result of cigarette smoking there is, according to <i>Binyan Zion</i>’s thesis, no halakhic reason to ban this activity.” The contrast between Rav Bleich’s writings in 1977 and 2003 demonstrates that the reality and available information have changed dramatically between these years, and thus Rav Moshe’s responsum from 1981 probably does not reflect the scientific evidence presently available.
Furthermore, Rav Moshe’s student, Rav Efraim Greenblatt (<i>Teshuvot Rivevot Efraim </i>8:586, printed in 1998), observes that society no longer regards smoking as a tolerable risk. Smoking is outlawed even in bars, despite the fact that imbibing considerable quantities of alcohol is tolerated. Clearly, Rav Moshe’s somewhat lenient ruling about smoking is no longer in effect, as medical information and societal standards have changed.
<b>Rav Moshe Feinstein’s Prohibition to Begin Smoking After 1981</b>
Although Rav Moshe did not prohibit smoking entirely, even he agrees that it is forbidden to smoke if one did not begin the habit before 1981.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe presumably would require one who began smoking after the time of his ruling to quit. </i> He explains that it is forbidden to habituate oneself to and develop a desire for frivolous worldly pleasures. Rav Moshe also discusses this idea in another responsum (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 3:35), in which he rules that it is forbidden to smoke marijuana or use any other illegal drug. His source is the concept of the <i>ben soreir u’moreh </i>(see Devarim 21:18-21), a rebellious son who is punished for developing frivolous worldly desires (see Sanhedrin 68b).
<b>Other Arguments Against Smoking</b>
Many <i>poskim</i> advance even stronger rationales to proscribe smoking. “Who would lie down in the middle of the street,” Rav Greenblatt argues, “and claim ‘Hashem protects the fools?!’ Certainly, smoking is just such a suicidal act and is absolutely prohibited. Smoking is definitely forbidden and there is no halachic justification for it….I have spoken to <i>gedolim</i> and <i>poskim</i> who agree with my conclusion.” Rav Chaim David Halevi (<i>Teshuvot Aseih Lecha Rav</i> 3:18) similarly labels smoking as “slow suicide.” Additionally, Rav Avigdor Nebenzahl argues (<i>Assia</i> 5:261) that we cannot apply the “Hashem protects the fools” principle when we clearly witness that it is not Hashem’s will to protect those who indulge in the activity in question.
Even the <i>Chafetz Chaim</i>, argues Rav Eliezer Waldenberg (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer </i>15:39), would agree, despite having permitted smoking on <i>Yom Tov</i>, <sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chafetz Chaim</i> is also the author of the Mishnah Berurah, whom we cited above as permitting smoking on <i>Yom Tov</i>.</i> that under today’s circumstances smoking is prohibited. Rav Waldenberg cites the <i>Chafetz Chaim</i>’s assertion (<i>Likutei Amarim </i>chapter 13 and <i>Zechor L’miriam </i>chapter 23) that it is forbidden for “weak individuals” to smoke given that doctors have concluded that smoking further weakens and endangers those who are already weak. The logical conclusion from the <i>Chafetz Chaim</i>’s assertion is that he would require everyone to adhere to contemporary doctors’ warnings to refrain from smoking, since doctors currently believe that smoking endangers even those who have a strong constitution. It is important to note that Rav Shmuel Wosner (in a letter written in 2000) also writes that it is completely forbidden to begin to smoke and that cigar smoking is included in this prohibition. It seems that he includes pipe smoking as well, as he writes about smoking “cigars, cigarettes, etc.” Rav Wosner also writes that one who already has begun to smoke should make every effort to wean himself from this bad habit.
<b>The Importance of Avoiding Forbidden Risks</b>
Halachah regards taking forbidden risks very seriously. The Shulchan Aruch (C.M. 427:10) writes that whoever endangers himself and claims, “Why should others care about my endangering myself if I do not care about it?” should receive <i>makkat mardut</i> (disciplinary lashes) – but whoever refrains from dangerous activities will receive “a blessing of good.” The Torah does not believe that one can do as he pleases with his body; rather, as we state in <i>Selichot</i>, “<i>Haneshamah lach v’haguf pa’olach</i>,” “The soul is Your’s (Hashem’s) and the body is Your handiwork.” In several places in the liturgy, we quote chapter 24 of <i>Tehillim</i>, which begins with the declaration that the entire world belongs to Hashem because He created it. In this vein, the <i>Chafetz Chaim</i> writes that if doctors tell someone that he must stop smoking, he must obey the order, because “How may a slave choose to do as he pleases if he belongs to his Master?”
The <i>Be’ier HaGolah</i>, commenting on the aforementioned citation from the Shulchan Aruch, offers an explanation for why Halachah forbids us to engage in dangerous activities. Hashem, in His kindness, created the world to benefit His creations – for them to recognize His greatness, worship Him by observing Torah and <i>mitzvot</i>, and earn reward for their positive efforts. One who endangers himself spurns the will of his Creator by implying that he does not want to serve Him and to be rewarded by Him. There can be no greater denigration of and disregard for our Maker than this.
<b>The <i>Chillul Hashem</i> Argument</b>
Rav Chaim David Halevi (<i>Teshuvot Aseih Lecha Rav</i> 3:18) advances another argument to forbid smoking. He writes:
In enlightened countries, smoking is banned in public places, commercial advertisements of smoking are banned, and manufacturers of cigarettes are compelled to print health warnings on every pack of cigarettes. Should we, whose holy Torah is a “<i>Torat Chaim</i>” (a life-giving Torah), lag behind?
In a number of places, the Torah presents us with the mission of serving as a role model for other nations.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See Shemot 19:6, <i>Seforno</i>’s comments ad. loc., and Devarim 4:6.</i> Indeed, part of every Jew’s role is to emulate the <i>kiddush Hashem </i>created by Avraham Avinu, who is referred to by his Hittite neighbors as “a prince of God amongst us” (Bereishit 23:6). <i>Chazal</i> regard a <i>chillul Hashem </i>as such a major infraction (see, for example, Yoma 86a) because setting a positive example for others is at the core of the mission of the Jewish people.
Accordingly, the sight of an observant Jew smoking in our time constitutes a <i>chillul Hashem</i>, and it certainly does not create the impression of “a knowledgable and wise nation” (Devarim 4:6).
<b>Why Do Some Pious Jews Smoke?</b>
When smokers are informed that so many great rabbis rule that smoking is forbidden, they inevitably ask why some pious Jews smoke. A response is that this reality is changing, as, in addition to those cited earlier, many <i>poskim</i> (for example, the Debrecziner Rav, <i>Teshuvot Be’eir Moshe</i> 6:160:9) have either declared smoking to be prohibited or have stated (in 2000) in a joint letter that one is obligated to make all efforts to stop smoking. The <i>gedolim </i>who have signed this letter include Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Note the dramatic change from what Rav Eliashiv wrote in 1981 in a <i>teshuvah</i> that is printed in <i>Kovetz Teshuvot </i>1:219.</i> Rav Aharon Yehudah Leib Shteinman, Rav Nissim Karelitz, and Rav Shmuel Auerbach. Rav Shlomo Wolbe (in a letter dated 1987), in an impassioned plea to cease smoking, stresses the point that each cigarette that one smokes reduces one’s life expectancy by five minutes. <i>The Jewish Observer</i> (November 2007) even published an article strongly discouraging smoking, citing at some length the ruling of the Halacha Committee of the Rabbincal Council of America.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Gidon Weitzman reports that Rav Dr. Mordechai Halperin stated (at a conference held by the Puah Institute of Jerusalem) that Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach ruled that one who smokes violates the Torah obligation to guard his health (<i>“V’nishmartem me’od l’nafshoteichem</i>”) and is <i>pasul l’eidut</i> (disqualified from giving testimony). Disqualifying a smoker from giving testimony appears difficult in light of Shulchan Aruch C.M. 34:3 and <i>Teshuvot Rav Akiva Eiger</i> 96.</i>
<b>Is <i>Oness</i> an Excuse?<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I acknowledge Rav Asher Bush’s assistance in developing this approach.</i></b>
Many smokers seek to excuse their behavior by stating that they are <i>anusim</i>, in effect coerced to smoke, since it is so difficult to free oneself from this addictive habit. In general, Halachah excuses one from sins committed under duress (see Devarim 22:26 and Ketubot 3a). This defense does not apply here, however, as smokers were not forced to begin smoking. The <i>Chafetz Chaim</i> (<i>Likutei Amarim </i>chapter 13 and<i> Zechor L’miriam </i>chapter 23) chides smokers who seek to excuse their behavior on the grounds that it is difficult to stop smoking, arguing that they had no right to start. As proof to his assertion, the <i>Chafetz Chaim</i> cites the Gemara (<i>Bava Kama </i>92) that states that one is not permitted to harm himself.
In addition, Halachah forbids one to voluntarily put himself into a situation that likely will require him to violate Halachah later. For example, the <i>Ba’al Hama’or</i> (Shabbat 7a in the pages of the Rif) writes that one is forbidden to deliberately put himself into a situation that will necessitate desecrating Shabbat to save a life. Based on the <i>Ba’al Hama’or</i>’s assertion, Rav Moshe Feinstein forbids choosing to undergo elective surgery three days before Shabbat.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Gray Matter 2</i> p. 22.</i> The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Yesodei Hatorah</i> 5:4) strongly condemns those who choose to remain in positions in which they will be coerced to violate the Torah. In fact, the confession we make on the <i>Yom Kippur </i>about sins committed <i>b’oness</i> (under duress) refers, according to <i>Siddur Hagra</i>, to cases where we willingly put ourselves into situations in which we were then coerced to sin.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">For further discussion of the prohibition to willingly enter a situation in which one will be coerced to sin, see Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik’s responsum regarding the propriety of rabbis volunteering to serve as chaplains in the United States Armed Forces (<i>Community, Covenant, and Commitment </i>pp. 23-67).</i>
In the context of the Halachot of <i>gittin </i>(Shulchan Aruch E.H. 134:4; see Pitchei Teshuvah E.H. 50:8), the Rama rules that a husband who gives his wife a <i>get</i> based on an earlier commitment is not considered coerced. Even though the husband currently does not consent to give the <i>get</i> and is giving it only because of his earlier voluntary agreement, the Rama regards the transfer of the <i>get</i> as voluntary. The Taz (134:6) explains, “There is no coercion, since he voluntarily entered into this agreement.” Accordingly, we see that one cannot claim that he is coerced to smoke, since he initially chose to begin smoking. This is especially true in light of the many medicines and therapies that have helped numerous smokers quit their deadly habit.
<b>A Father’s Request to Purchase Cigarettes</b>
Rav Chaim David Halevi (<i>Teshuvot Aseih Lecha Rav</i> 6:58 and 7:65) was asked whether one must honor his father’s request to purchase cigarettes for him. Normally, Halachah requires one to fulfill a parent’s request for service (Kiddushin 31b). On the other hand, one is not required to follow a parent’s order to violate Halachah (Bava Metzia 32a).
The <i>Beit Lechem Yehudah</i> (commenting on Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 240:15) and <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 240:41) address a similar situation. Doctors ordered someone not to drink water or eat a certain food. This individual subsequently asked his son to bring him water and the specified food, and he threatened that he would not forgive his son either in this world or in the next if he failed to obey. The <i>Beit Lechem Yehudah</i> and <i>Aruch Hashulchan </i>rule (based on Bava Metzia 32a) that the son is not obligated to obey his father’s command. Rav Halevi explains that bringing very unhealthy food to someone to eat would violate the prohibition of assisting another to sin (“<i>Lifnei iveir lo titein michshol</i>” [Vayikra 19:14]). Rav Halevi argues that it follows from this ruling that one should not give his father cigarettes if he requests them. Rather, he should politely and gently explain to his father (in accordance with Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 240:11) that smoking is very dangerous and that the Torah obligates us to preserve our bodies.
<b>Criticizing the Practices of Earlier Generations</b>
In general, Halachah frowns upon calling into question the halachic practices of earlier generations (<i>motzi la’az</i>, see Gittin 5b). In this vein, Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 2:49) writes that we cannot declare smoking to be technically forbidden, since great Torah scholars of previous generations smoked.
There are a number of potential responses to this argument. First, the Pitchei Teshuvah (E.H. 125:12) cites opinions that limit the cases in which one should not be <i>motzi la’az</i> on the practices of earlier generations. One suggestion is that this rule applies only to attempting to introduce a mere stringency (<i>chumrah b’alma</i>), not to establishing a behavior as technically forbidden. Another opinion believes that this principle applies only to particularly sensitive matters of <i>ishut</i> (family law), such as calling into question the validity of <i>gittin</i> executed by prior generations. According to these two approaches, considerations of <i>hotza’at la’az </i>on the practices of the past do not impede contemporary <i>Poskim</i> from issuing a stringent ruling regarding smoking.
Furthermore, previous generations did not have the access to medical data that we have today. Thus, they did not violate the prohibition of endangering themselves, since they did not perceive smoking as dangerous. Similarly, we are not being <i>motzi la’az</i> on Rav Moshe’s rulings, since he was basing himself on data that were current when he wrote his responsa but are no longer up to date.
<b>Conclusion</b>
The comments of the Rama (Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 116:5) should dispel any doubts that smoking is prohibited. He writes, “One must avoid dangerous activities because we treat danger even more seriously than <i>issurim </i>(forbidden behaviors; see Chullin 9b). We must be more concerned about even possible danger than about possible violations of <i>issurim</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In fact, avoiding possible danger allows for violation of definite prohibitions. See <i>Gray Matter 2</i> pp. 3-18 for a discussion of this rule as it pertains to the laws of Shabbat.</i> The fact that many prominent <i>poskim</i> have issued rulings forbidding smoking raises the activity at the very least to the level of being possibly forbidden. Thus, smoking is forbidden even if one is uncertain whether it should be technically forbidden or not.
We can discern three stages in the development of the attitude of contemporary <i>poskim</i> towards smoking. In 1976, Rav Chaim David Halevi became the first major <i>poseik</i> to state publicly that smoking is forbidden, and Rav Hershel Schachter<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Schachter’s position is reported by Rav Asher Bush.</i> did likewise at around the same time. Rav Eliezer Waldenberg followed suit in the early 1980s. The third stage came in the late 1990s and early 2000s, when Rav Ovadia Yosef, who previously had believed that it is not technically forbidden to smoke, concluded that it is forbidden to smoke based on changing societal behavior and medical data. Accordingly, at this point we may safely affirm that the age in which smoking was possibly permissible has passed, and that it is now forbidden. Indeed, the Halacha Committee of the Rabbinical Council of America ruled (June 30, 2006): “…that smoking is clearly and unquestionably forbidden by Halachah and that this should be made known to all who care about the Torah and their health.”
Parents and educators must impart to their children and students the unequivocal message that smoking is forbidden according to Halachah. There are sufficient major rabbinic figures who have issued stringent rulings to resolve any uncertainty about this issue.
Hashem has privileged us to live in an age where it is common for <i>chatanim </i>and <i>kallot </i>to enjoy the presence of grandparents and even great-grandparents at their weddings. What a shame it would be for a person to smoke and very likely miss the opportunity to bestow the great joy of his or her presence at the weddings of his or her children, grandchildren, and great-grandchildren.

High Risk Medical Procedures



Pregnant Women Fasting on Yom Kippur

It is well-established that one who is dangerously ill on <i>Yom Kippur</i> need not fast (see Yoma 82a). It is also well-known that such a dangerously sick individual should try to limit his eating and drinking to very small quantities on <i>Yom Kippur</i>. In this chapter, we will seek to elucidate some of the issues involved, review the dispute as to whether this rule applies to <i>Tish’ah B’av</i> as well, and offer some suggestions for pregnant women who face this question.
<b>The <i>Shiur</i> for Eating</b>
The Torah (Vayikra 23:29) teaches that one who eats on <i>Yom Kippur</i> is punished with <i>kareit</i> (spiritual excision).<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (Vayikra 17:9 s.v. <i>V’nichrat</i> and 20:20 s.v. <i>Aririm</i>) mentions that <i>kareit</i> can entail dying childless, burying one’s children, or dying prematurely. The Gemara (<i>Mo’eid Katan</i> 28a) defines “prematurely” as before age fifty.</i> This punishment takes effect, according to the Mishnah (Yoma 8:2), only if he eats or drinks the <i>shiur</i> (requisite amount), which, for <i>Yom Kippur</i>, is a volume of food equivalent to the size of a large date (<i>kakotevet hagasah</i>) or a quantity of liquid that could fill one’s cheek (<i>melo lugmav</i>).
It should be noted that, to incur the punishment of <i>kareit</i>, the <i>shiur</i> must be consumed in an interval shorter than the time it takes to eat half a loaf of bread (<i>kedei achillat pras</i>). <i>Acharonim</i> debate exactly how many minutes this refers to. The Mishnah Berurah (618:21) cites the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>’s ruling (<i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer</i> 6:16) that <i>kedei achillat pras</i> is the equivalent of nine minutes, while the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (O.C. 618:14) writes that it is six to seven minutes.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For a review of the opinions on this matter, which range from two minutes to nine minutes, see the sources cited in the <i>Shemirat Shabbat K’hilchatah</i> (39:18 footnote 71).</i>
In a celebrated dispute (Yoma 74a), Reish Lakish and Rabi Yochanan disagree regarding one who ate or drank a <i>chatzi shiur</i> (partial measure) of these amounts. Reish Lakish argues that although it is rabbinically forbidden to eat or drink even a tiny amount of food on <i>Yom Kippur</i>, one who does so has not violated a Torah prohibition, since the Torah does not consider consuming such a small amount to be “eating.” Rabi Yochanan, on the other hand, believes that one who eats or drinks a tiny amount violates a biblical prohibition. He reasons that since the tiny scrap is “<i>chazi l’itzterufei</i>” (has the potential to be combined with more food and contribute to a full <i>shiur</i>), it still qualifies as a Torah prohibition, even though eating this smaller amount it is not punishable by <i>kareit</i>. The position of Rabi Yochanan is codified as normative Halachah (Rambam <i>Hilchot Shevitat Assor</i> 2:3 and Shulchan Aruch O.C. 612:5).
<b>Eating Less than the <i>Shiur</i></b>
In case of a potential threat to life, however, one may eat less than the <i>shiur</i>. The Gemara (<i>Keritot</i> 13a) states, “The Rabbis permitted a pregnant woman to eat less than a <i>shiur</i> due to the danger.” The Gemara clarifies that she certainly may eat more than a full <i>shiur</i> if emergency health considerations require eating and less than a <i>shiur</i> will not suffice to preserve her life. The Ramban (<i>Torat Ha’adam </i>2:28 in Rav Chavel’s <i>Kol Kitvei Haramban</i>), the Rosh (Yoma 8:13), and the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> (commenting on <i>Hilchot Shvitat Assor</i> 2:8 s.v. <i>V’im</i>) apply the Gemara’s ruling to all illnesses. In their view, any dangerously ill individual should try to limit his eating on <i>Yom Kippur</i> to less than a <i>shiur</i>, as we would prefer that he avoid violating a transgression that is punishable by <i>kareit</i>. Obviously, though, as the Rosh explains, we will require a patient to eat less than a <i>shiur </i>only if the doctor states that eating this amount will suffice; if the doctor believes that more is required, the individual should eat more than a <i>shiur</i>. The Shulchan Aruch (618:7) rules in accordance with this view of the Ramban, Rosh, and <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>. The Netziv (<i>Ha’ameik She’eilah Parashat V’zot Habrachah</i>167:17), however, demonstrates that the Rif and Rambam disagree with this view. These <i>Rishonim</i>, the Netziv argues, believe that a sick individual whose life is endangered and who must eat on <i>Yom Kippur</i> to preserve his life may do so without limitations.
In practice, Rav Mordechai Willig (<i>Kol Zvi</i> 9:31) believes that one fluid ounce is the <i>shiur</i> for both food and drink. Rav Dovid Heber,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Heber’s essay on this topic is available at www.star-k.org.</i> though, presents Rav Moshe Heinemann’s ruling that a <i>kotevet hagasah</i> is 1.5 fluid ounces. He recommends, in order to maximize the amount of food consumed (if necessary), to compact bread, crackers, or cereal into one-and-a-half “schnapps glasses” that are marked as 1oz.-29 ml.
Rav Heber also explains how to calculate one’s personal <i>melo lugmav</i>, as this <i>shiur</i> is determined by each individual’s own cheek size (<i>Keilim</i> 17:11). He writes that one should fill his mouth completely with water, expel the water into a measuring cup, and divide the resulting measurement in half.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This differs from the <i>Shemirat Shabbat K’hilchatah</i>’s (39:18 and 20) method for measuring <i>melo lugmav</i>.</i> He states that <i>melo lugmav</i> of an average adult is 1.5 fluid ounces and that a teenager may have a smaller <i>shiur</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rav J. David Bleich (personal communication) and Rav Hershel Schachter (personal communication) do not restrict drinking (for those permitted to drink) to water; juice may also be consumed. Rav Dr. Zalman Levine recommends drinking isotonic fluids such as sports drinks or Pedialyte. He explains that neither water nor juice will hydrate a person anywhere near as effectively as an equivalent volume of an isotonic fluid, which stays in the bloodstream better than any other drink because it contains the requisite electrolytes.</i>
<b>Rav Chaim’s Understanding of this Rule</b>
Rav Chaim Soloveitchik<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Chaim’s view is cited by his son, Rav Yitzchak Zev Soloveitchik (commenting on the Rambam <i>Hilchot Shevitat Assor</i> 2:8), and by his grandson, Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik (<i>Halachic Man</i> p. 39). Rav Yosef Adler reports that Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik fully concurred and ruled in accordance with Rav Chaim’s approach. See <i>Halachic Man</i> (<i>ibid</i>.) for a philosophical perspective on Rav Chaim’s ruling.</i> greatly limits the applicability of this restriction on eating even according to the Shulchan Aruch. He feels that the Shulchan Aruch’s ruling applies only to someone who is not dangerously ill, but who must eat on <i>Yom Kippur</i> to avoid becoming dangerously ill. However, a <i>choleh sheyeish bo sakanah</i> (dangerously ill person) may eat on <i>Yom Kippur</i> without limiting himself to less than a <i>shiur</i>.
This approach is based on the ruling of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> (commenting on <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 2:14) and Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 328:4) that one may violate Shabbat in order to take care of even the non-critical needs of a <i>choleh sheyeish bo sakanah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Gray Matter 2</i> pp. 17-18.</i> Similarly, explains Rav Yitzchak Zev Soloveitchik, once someone is considered dangerously ill to the extent that he must eat on <i>Yom Kippur</i>, all of his needs must be met in an unlimited manner. Thus, he need not limit his eating to less than a <i>shiur</i> even according to the Shulchan Aruch.
<b>Criticism of Rav Chaim’s Approach</b>
Although Dr. Abraham S. Abraham (<i>Nishmat Avraham</i> 1:310) records that Rav Yechezkel Abramsky ruled in accordance with Rav Chaim’s view,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Although Rav Abramsky was a student of Rav Chaim, his opinion counts as a separate and supporting one (see <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> C.M. 18:5).</i> the general practice among rabbis, as Rav Shlomo Yosef Zevin (<i>Moadim Bahalachah</i> p. 28) notes, is not to rely on this leniency. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (O.C. 618:15) does not seem to subscribe to Rav Chaim’s leniency. Similarly, Rav Yehoshua Neuwirth (<i>Shemirat Shabbat K’hilchatah </i>39:6) and Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at </i>6:39) rule against Rav Chaim. Rav Moshe Snow informed me that Rav Moshe Feinstein, as well, did not adopt Rav Chaim’s approach. Those who disagree with Rav Chaim point to the Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 618:7), who simply states that a sick person should try to eat less than a <i>shiur</i>. By not specifying that this rule applies only to one who is not dangerously ill, the Shulchan Aruch apparently extends it even to a <i>choleh sheyeish bo sakanah</i>.
Furthermore, the Mishnah Berurah (328:14 and Bei’ur Halachah ad. loc. s.v. <i>Kol</i>) demonstrates that many <i>Rishonim</i> disagree with the aforementioned <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> (which serves as the basis for the opinion of Rav Chaim). The Mishnah Berurah concludes that when Torah prohibitions are involved, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>’s ruling should not be followed. Hence, since eating even less than a <i>shiur</i> on <i>Yom Kippur</i> constitutes a Torah prohibition, it seems that the Mishnah Berurah should be added to the list of those who dispute the ruling of Rav Chaim. One should ask his Rav for guidance when this issue arises in practice.
<b>Application to <i>Tish’ah B’av</i></b>
<i>Acharonim</i> debate whether the rule of eating less than a <i>shiur</i> applies to <i>Tish’ah B’av</i>. While the Mishnah Berurah (Bei’ur Halachah 554:6 s.v. <i>D’bemakom Choli</i>) states that it does apply,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A careful reading of the Mishnah Berurah suggests that he speaks only of a situation where the individual is not dangerously ill but finds it necessary to eat in order to avoid becoming dangerously ill.</i> the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (554:7) rules that it does not. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i>’s ruling seems, at a glance, to be more persuasive. He argues that only on <i>Yom Kippur</i> is there reason to distinguish between eating less than a <i>shiur</i> and a full <i>shiur</i>, as it is possible to violate a biblical prohibition without incurring the penalty. Therefore, it is halachically meaningful to eat less than a <i>shiur</i>. On <i>Tish’ah B’av</i>, on the other hand, it seems that one accomplishes nothing by eating less than a <i>shiur</i>. It is a rabbinic prohibition to eat on <i>Tish’ah B’av</i>, and the Rabbis made no distinction between eating more or less than a <i>shiur</i>. Nonetheless, the dispute is unresolved, so one should consult his Rav for a ruling regarding this matter.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">For further analysis of this dispute, see <i>Ohr Hamizrach</i> (44:3-4:178). The <i>Marcheshet</i> (1:14), a proponent of the strict view, writes, “I recall when I was [once] sick on <i>Tish’ah B’av</i> and it was difficult for me to fast that I ate less than a <i>shiur</i> so as not to lose the fast whose source is in the Prophets and is as strict as <i>Yom Kippur</i>.” Rav Reuvein Feinstein told me that Rav Moshe Feinstein did not require a sick individual to eat less than a <i>shiur</i> on<i> Tish’ah B’av.</i></i>
<b>Practical Suggestions for Pregnant Women</b>
Very often, a Rav will rule that a pregnant woman may eat on <i>Yom Kippur</i>, but only less than the <i>shiur</i>. I have heard of problems resulting from such rulings, as it is claimed that fasting triggers the labor process, which can prove disastrous for many women. For example, I was informed of a case of a woman who was twenty-two weeks pregnant and was ordered by her doctor to eat and drink on <i>Yom Kippur</i>. An eminent Rav told her to eat and drink less than the <i>shiur</i>, which proved to be insufficient for this woman. She went into labor and was rushed to the hospital, where she narrowly missed losing the baby.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Dovid Heber commented to me that he would find it very surprising for this to happen to a woman who drank the appropriate amount of liquid at the appropriate time. As noted, Rav Heber has found that 1.5 fluid ounces is the average <i>melo lugmav</i>, and he follows Rav Moshe Heinemann’s ruling that a person need wait only seven minutes between each drink. If a woman follows this protocol, she will drink around twelve fluid ounces per hour. Rav Heber reports that physicians have told him that this is more than sufficient for a pregnant woman. </i>
Accordingly, a woman who needs to eat should ask her Rav and physician if she may follow the ruling of Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Yalkut Yosef</i>,<i> Kitzur Shulchan Aruch </i>618:5-6) that although one should wait nine minutes between the small portions when eating (in accordance with the aforementioned view of the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>), one need wait only five minutes between portions when drinking. There is more room to be lenient in regard to drinking because some <i>Rishonim </i>and <i>Acharonim </i>do not require waiting <i>kedei achillat pras</i> for it at all (see Shulchan Aruch O.C. 612:10 and Mishnah Berurah 612:31). Thus, in the context of drinking amounts less than a <i>shiur</i>, one may perhaps follow the opinions that endorse a shorter span for <i>kedei achillat pras</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">We should clarify that although one must wait between each drink, there is no need to wait between eating and drinking, because eating and drinking do not combine toward a <i>shiur</i> (Yoma 73b). Rav Hershel Schachter told me that one may follow this ruling of Rav Ovadia Yosef, especially in light of the opinion of the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> (313:2) that <i>kedei achilat pras</i> varies from food to food. A <i>melo lugmav</i> of room temperature liquid certainly can be consumed within five minutes, so the amount of waiting need not be greater than that. Rav Mordechai Willig told me that a woman who feels that she must drink more often may rely on Rav Ovadia’s opinion; otherwise, she should wait nine minutes between drinking less than a <i>shiur</i>. </i>
Following Rav Ovadia Yosef’s ruling might help pregnant women receive the hydration they need to avoid triggering premature labor. In addition, it would seem prudent for pregnant women to remain at home on <i>Yom Kippur</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Fasting on <i>Yom Kippur</i>, a biblical obligation that carries the punishment of <i>kareit</i> for its violators, is much more strict than and enjoys priority over the rabbinic obligation (from which women are exempt) to attend services on <i>Yom Kippur</i>.</i> in an air-conditioned environment and to minimize their activity in order to help reduce the risk of dehydration (see <i>Shemirat Shabbat K’hilchatah </i>39:28 and footnote 94). A woman might also consider drinking large volumes of water<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Dr. Zalman Levine recommends that the liquid consumed for this purpose also should be isotonic fluids.</i> throughout the two days prior to <i>Yom Kippur</i> in order to help prevent dehydration. Furthermore, if on <i>Yom Kippur</i> itself a woman intuits that the minimal amounts of water are insufficient, she should be sure to satisfy her body’s need<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Dr. Jacob Markovitz informs me that if one already has become dehydrated to the extent that he expresses symptoms of dehydration, drinking less than a <i>shiur </i>is insufficient for rehydration. He advises that one who has experienced such symptoms (which include lightheadedness, lethargy, dizziness, dry mouth, or decreased urine) should drink beyond the <i>shiurim</i> so as to satisfy the body’s need for water. </i> if she feels that she would otherwise be endangering herself or her fetus (see Shulchan Aruch O.C. 618:1). Finally, if any woman on <i>Yom Kippur</i> who is fasting, or eating and drinking limited amounts goes into labor, she should inform her doctors of the restrictions she has been following, as this information might be vitally important for medical personnel, especially for the anesthesiologist.
<b>Conclusion</b>
We have highlighted only a few of the many issues involved in eating less than the <i>shiur</i> on <i>Yom Kippur</i>. Thus, it is imperative for one who finds himself or herself in such a situation to consult his or her Rav and doctor, who can offer competent guidance on how to observe safely the holy day of <i>Yom Kippur</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">It is vital to note that if one’s Rav and doctor require eating, it is <b>forbidden</b> for the patient to ignore their ruling and fast. Indeed, Rav Ovadia Yosef writes (<i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> 1:61), “If the sick individual adopts a strict approach and fasts [when ordered to eat], not only is he not acting piously, but, just the opposite, he will surely be punished by Heaven for having done so.” On the other hand, we should note that although Rav Nachum Rabinovitch (<i>Techumin</i> 17:343-346) rules that every woman more than five months pregnant should drink less than a <i>shiur</i> at a time, almost all <i>poskim</i> reject his view. Thus, each pregnant woman must consult her Rav for a ruling specific to her situation.<br>We should note that if a Rav has permitted someone to eat and/or drink on <i>Yom Kippur</i>, it is not preferable (and might be forbidden) to be fed intravenously in order to avoid the necessity of eating on <i>Yom Kippur</i> (see <i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> O.C. 3:90, <i>Moadim Uzmanim</i> 1:60,<i> Shemirat Shabbat Kehilchata </i>(39:30), and my essay on this topic available at www.koltorah.org).</i>

Cosmetic Surgery, A Review of Four Classic Teshuvot

In this section, we will discuss the range of opinions regarding the halachic propriety of cosmetic surgery, reviewing four classic responsa on this topic from four great, late twentieth-century <i>poskim</i> – Rav Moshe Feinstein, Rav Yaakov Breisch, Rav Eliezer Waldenberg, and Rav Yitzchak Yaakov Weisz.
<b>Rav Moshe Feinstein</b>
Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe </i>C.M. 2:66) was asked whether it is permissible for a young woman to undergo cosmetic surgery to improve her chances of finding a suitable marriage partner. Rav Moshe approved the surgery based on the Rambam’s (<i>Hilchot Chovel Umazik</i> 5:1) definition of the prohibition of <i>chavalah</i> (wounding). In general, the Torah prohibits both wounding another person (see Devarim 25:3) and, as the Gemara (<i>Bava Kama</i> 91b) adds, even wounding oneself. The Rambam specifies that wounding is forbidden when it is performed “in a degrading manner,” (<i>derech bizayon</i>) or, according to an alternative text, “in a belligerent manner” (<i>derech nitzayon</i>). Thus, although the Rambam rules in accordance with the Tannaitic view (<i>ibid</i>.) that an individual is forbidden to wound himself, Rav Moshe infers from the Rambam’s proviso that if the wounding (whether of others or of oneself) is done in a beneficial manner, the prohibition of <i>chavalah </i>does not apply.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For further discussion and a summary of the authorities who either agree or disagree with Rav Moshe interpretation of the Rambam, see Rav Daniel Feldman, <i>The Right and the Good</i> pp. 159-161.</i>
Rav Moshe cites four Talmudic sources for the Rambam’s ruling. First, the Gemara (<i>ibid</i>.) records that when Rav Chisda walked among thorns, he would roll up his pants so that his skin would be scratched instead of his clothes.
