He explained that, unlike the clothes, the skin would heal itself. It appears that Rav Chisda was permitted to wound himself because it was not done in a degrading or belligerent manner.
Another proof is that the <i>Tanach</i> (<i>Melachim I</i> 20:35-36) and Gemara (Sanhedrin 89a-b) condemn the individual who refused to follow the prophet Michayhu’s divinely-received order that the individual wound him. It was necessary for the prophet to appear wounded in order to emphasize what King Achav should have done to the enemy king, ben Haddad of Aram. Once again, we see that wounding for a positive purpose (in this case, fulfillment of the divine command) is permissible when not done in a degrading or belligerent manner. One could question this proof, however, since an explicit divine command would supersede the prohibition in any case.
Third, the Gemara (Sanhedrin 84b) permits one to perform a bloodletting on his father,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">We explain later that generally speaking, one may not perform a medical procedure on a parent when another health care professional is available (Rama Y.D. 241:3). For a summary of the sources on this topic, see <i>Nishmat Avraham</i> (2:148-151). </i> citing as a source the celebrated <i>pasuk</i>, “<i>V’ahavta l’rei’acha kamocha</i>,” “Love thy neighbor as thyself.” Rashi (s.v. <i>V’ahavta</i>) elaborates, “We are forbidden only to do to others that which we would not want done to ourselves.” Rav Moshe explains that beneficial wounding, which bloodletting was considered, is something that all (prudent) people want done to them if necessary. Hence, this Gemara would permit wounding for any purpose that most prudent people would want, which Rav Moshe believes the case in question to be.
Finally, the Mishnah (Bechorot 7:6) discusses a <i>bechor</i> (firstborn) who had an extra finger and removed it, but the Mishnah does not comment on his right to do so. In contrast, earlier Mishnayot (Bechorot 1:1 and 2:1), discussing one who sells his cow to a <i>nochri </i>(gentile), do indeed criticize the sale, adding, “even though one does not enjoy the right to do so.” Since the Mishnah does not condemn removing an extra finger, we may infer that it permits doing so.
In light of this considerable evidence, Rav Moshe rules that the girl is permitted to undergo cosmetic surgery, since it is done for her benefit and with her consent. <sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">For a discussion of the applicability of the prohibition to injure another person to a situation in which the latter gave consent, see Rav Shlomo Yosef Zevin, <i>L’or Hahalachah</i> pp. 318-328.</i> As it is not done in a degrading or belligerent manner, Rav Moshe asserts that cosmetic surgery does not violate the prohibition of <i>chavalah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Hershel Schachter told me that Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik also permitted cosmetic surgery because it is not done in a belligerent or degrading manner. </i>
<b>Interpreting and Applying Rav Moshe’s <i>Teshuvah</i></b>
It is difficult to determine how far Rav Moshe would extend his permissive ruling. Does this <i>teshuvah</i> constitute a sweeping endorsement of the propriety of cosmetic surgery provided that it benefits the patient and has his or her consent? Or does Rav Moshe’s lenient ruling apply only in a situation in which the surgery is of great need, such as in the specific case that he adjudicated? Would Rav Moshe permit one to undergo eye surgery, for instance, to avoid the inconvenience of wearing eyeglasses or contact lenses? Rav Hershel Schachter told me that he believes that Rav Moshe’s permission applies only in a case of need, such as in the case that Rav Moshe adjudicated, where the girl wanted the surgery for purposes of finding an appropriate marriage partner.
Rav Mordechai Willig noted (in a personal conversation) that Rav Moshe does not at all address the question of safety. Rav Willig infers that Rav Moshe’s permission applies only when there is no safety concern involved in the surgery. The greater the danger, the greater the need must be in order to permit the surgery.
<b>Rav Yaakov Breisch</b>
Rav Yaakov Breisch (<i>Teshuvot Chelkat Yaakov</i> 3:11) was also asked whether a young woman, seeking to facilitate her finding a suitable marriage partner, could undergo cosmetic surgery to straighten her nose and reduce its size. Rav Breisch cites the ruling of the Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 241:3) that forbids removing a thorn, performing a bloodletting, or amputating a limb for one’s father even though he intends to heal him. The Rama (ad. loc.) adds that this is forbidden only if there are others available to perform this task. However, if no one else is available and the father is in pain, the son may perform the bloodletting or amputate the limb so long as the father consents. Rav Breisch, assuming that the Rama addresses even a patient whose life is not in danger, infers that he would permit a doctor to cut a limb merely to alleviate pain.
Rav Breisch then expands the definition of pain for which a doctor may injure. His source is the Gemara’s (Shabbat 50b) permission for a man to remove scabs from his body to eliminate pain, but not merely to beautify himself. Rashi (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Mishum L’yafot</i>) explains that removing scabs for beautification purposes is forbidden for a male because it is regarded as feminine behavior.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah forbids both men and women from imitating the behaviors of the opposite gender; see Devarim 22:5 and Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 182).</i> <i>Tosafot</i>, however, (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Bishvil</i>) qualify the Gemara’s statement: “If the only pain that he suffers is that he is embarrassed to walk among people then it is permissible, because there is no greater pain than this.” Thus, <i>Tosafot</i> extend the definition of pain to include psychological distress. Accordingly, since the inability to find an appropriate spouse is certainly most distressing and the prohibition to wound does not apply when attempting to alleviate pain, Rav Breisch permits the young woman to undergo cosmetic surgery.
In addition, Rav Breisch’s <i>teshuvah</i> addresses an issue that Rav Moshe leaves untouched – the prohibition to place oneself in danger (see Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 116 and C.M. 427). The questioner cited a responsum of the <i>Avnei Neizer</i> (<i>Teshuvot Avnei Neizer</i> Y.D. 321) forbidding a child to undergo surgery to straighten his crooked leg because of the danger involved. Rav Breisch, in turn, notes that the Gemara in numerous places (such as Yevamot 72a) permits certain activities that involve some danger if people commonly engage in such behavior.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This principle is discussed at length in our chapter about smoking.</i> The Gemara teaches that if society deems an activity to constitute a tolerable risk, the activity is entirely permissible. Accordingly, Rav Breisch writes, we are permitted to travel in an automobile or an airplane despite the risks. Similarly, the risks associated with surgery have decreased dramatically since the times of the <i>Avnei Neizer</i>, and hence society today regards surgery as a tolerable risk, rendering it permissible.
Rav Breisch’s explicit permission to undergo cosmetic surgery obviously applies only to a situation of great need, as the precedents he cites sanction <i>chavalah</i> only when the individual is suffering physically or psychologically. Indeed, Rav J. David Bleich (<i>Judaism and Healing</i> pp. 126-129) adopts this position as normative. However, Rav Breisch also does not explicitly forbid cosmetic surgery performed for reasons of convenience; he simply does not address the issue.
<b>Rav Eliezer Waldenberg</b>
Rav Eliezer Waldenberg (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 11:41) presents a radically different approach from Rav Moshe’s and Rav Breisch’s. He categorically prohibits all cosmetic surgeries, forbidding both a doctor to perform cosmetic surgery and patients to undergo it.
Some background information is necessary in order to understand Rav Waldenberg’s concerns. The Gemara (<i>Bava Kama</i> 85a) infers from the obligation that the Torah (Shemot 21:1) imposes upon an injurer to pay his victim’s medical bills that “The Torah permits a physician to heal.” Absent such permission, explain <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Bava Kamma </i>85a s.v. <i>Shenitnah</i>), we would have thought that we are forbidden to heal because we “appear to be contradicting the King’s decree.” By authorizing medical attention, however, the Torah teaches that we are not contradicting Hashem’s will, because the King who issued the decree for the illness or injury also permitted physicians to heal.
Rav Waldenberg forcefully argues that the divine license to heal applies only to curing an illness, not to altering one’s appearance. It is certainly forbidden, he adds, to risk one’s life to undergo cosmetic surgery, even if the risk is not great.
Rav Waldenberg even calls cosmetic surgery an insult to our Creator, because it implies that His work is inadequate. To prove this point, he cites a story in the Gemara (<i>Ta’anit</i> 20b) in which Rabi Elazar ben Shimon met an exceptionally homely individual and asked him whether all of the people in his town were as ugly as he. The man responded that Rabi Elazar had insulted Hashem by implying, “What an ugly vessel You have made!” Rabi Elazar sought his forgiveness, and though the man refused to extend it at first, the townspeople eventually convinced him to relent. <i>Tosafot</i>, citing <i>Masechet Derech Eretz</i>, comment that the ugly man was none other than Eliyahu Hanavi in disguise.
In another responsum (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 12:43), Rav Waldenberg addresses the permissibility of undergoing elective surgery on a Thursday or a Friday. While the questioner was primarily concerned about the surgery interfering with Shabbat observance and enjoyment,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Gray Matter 2</i> pp. 19-23.</i> Rav Waldenberg simply responds that Halachah never condones elective surgery. If a surgery is not necessary, it may not be undertaken.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See the observations of Rav Immanuel Jacobowitz (<i>Noam</i> 6:273) and Dr. Abraham S. Abraham (<i>Nishmat Avraham</i> 2:49) for more regarding Rav Waldenberg’s opinion.</i>
<b>Rav Yitzchak Yaakov Weisz</b>
In a very brief responsum (<i>Teshuvot Minchat Yitzchak</i> 6:105:2) concerning cosmetic surgery, Dayan Weisz focuses on two issues - <i>chavalah </i>and <i>sakanah</i> (the prohibition to enter into a dangerous situation). He adopts an identical stance to Rav Moshe’s regarding <i>chavalah</i>, namely, that it is not forbidden unless it is done in a belligerent or degrading manner. Thus, the prohibition of <i>chavalah</i> constitutes no impediment to undergoing cosmetic surgery. Unlike Rav Breisch, however, Dayan Weisz does believe that the danger involved in any surgery, small though it may be, is of major concern.
Dayan Weisz refers to an earlier responsum (<i>Teshuvot Minchat Yitzchak</i> 1:28:2) in which he forbids undergoing any surgery unless it is necessary to save the patient’s life. In this vein, he interprets the Rama’s aforementioned permission for a son to cut his father’s limb as referring only to a case of life-threatening danger.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This reading appears difficult, as had the Rama had intended this, he should have said so explicitly. According to Dayan Weisz, the Rama omits a vital condition from his ruling.</i>
Accordingly, although Dayan Weisz concedes that some would-be cosmetic surgery patients are defined as <i>cholim</i> (as Rav Breisch argues), he hesitates to permit the surgery, since these people are not <i>cholim sheyeish bahem sakanah</i> (sick individuals whose lives are endangered). Dayan Weisz concludes that he is unsure of this matter, and remarks that with God’s help he might look into the matter further in the future. Though he does acknowledge that Rav Breisch’s argument is a “<i>sevara gedolah</i>” (cogent argument), he stops short of endorsing it.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Shmuel Wosner (<i>Teshuvot Sheivet Halevi </i>(6:198) similarly forbids cosmetic surgery out of concern for the danger involved, small though it may be.</i>
I find it illuminating that neither Rav Moshe nor Rav Breisch shares Rav Waldenberg’s fundamental theological concerns about cosmetic surgery. Arguably, cosmetic surgery does not insult the work of the “Craftsman” because He also revealed to mankind the knowledge and ability to perform cosmetic surgery. Perhaps Rav Moshe and Rav Breisch view such surgery as part of our role as “junior partners” with Hashem in the ongoing creation of the world (see Shabbat 10a and Ramban to Bereishit 1:28).<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Gidon Weitzman suggested to me that Rav Waldenberg might respond that we are considered partners with Hashem only when improving the world, such as when creating a child (see Niddah 31a) or when turning wheat into cake (see <i>Tanchuma</i>, <i>Parashat Tazria</i>, <i>parashah </i>5). Rav Waldenberg could argue that because cosmetic surgery does not constitute a real “improvement” in the world, we are not considered partners with Hashem when performing such a procedure. As such, we are declaring Hashem’s work to be inferior when we attempt to alter it without any real improvement.</i>
<b>Conclusion</b>
The four classic <i>teshuvot</i> on the topic of cosmetic surgery present significantly different approaches to the issue. Rav Moshe and Rav Breisch both permit such surgery, at least when it alleviates distress, while Rav Waldenberg and Dayan Weisz both prohibit it. Rav J. David Bleich (<i>Judaism and Healing</i> pp. 126-128) concludes that it is permissible in case of great need. However, there is no published ruling from a major halachic authority that explicitly permits cosmetic surgery for no reason other than convenience. One who is contemplating cosmetic surgery should consult his or her Rav for a ruling on its permissibility.

Sonograms and Kohanim's Wives

<b>A Halachic Stringency Imposed by Technology?</b>
Usually, technology improves our lives in both mundane and halachic matters. For example, the problem of <i>agunot</i> (women unable to remarry because it is not known if their husbands are dead or alive) has been dramatically ameliorated in our generation due to vastly improved communication technologies and DNA evidence.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Gray Matter 2</i> pp.114-138 and our section about the admissibility of DNA evidence in <i>beit din</i>.</i> However, the emergence of ultrasound testing of pregnant women as a standard procedure might impose some restrictions upon the wives of <i>kohanim</i> if it reveals to them that they are carrying a male child.
A few points need to be clarified before we begin our discussion. The Torah (Vayikra 21:1) forbids <i>kohanim</i> to come in contact with the dead. This restriction, however, applies only to male <i>kohanim</i> (Sotah 23b, cited by Rashi to Vayikra 21:1). Contact with the dead includes being in the same building as a dead body (Bemidbar 19:14). <sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For a summary of whether this rule prohibits <i>kohanim</i> from entering hospitals, see<i> Nishmat Avraham</i> (2:209-210). For further discussion of this issue, see <i>Techumin</i> (19:323-334).</i> Although children are not personally obligated to observe this restriction, adults cannot deliberately cause even the youngest of <i>kohanim</i> (even an infant) to come in contact with a dead body (Mishnah Berurah 343:3 and <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> Y.D. 373:1).
Thus, a pregnant wife of a <i>kohen</i> who discovers that her child is male might be prohibited from even entering a funeral home or any other building containing a dead body, because she thereby causes her unborn male child to come in contact with the dead. In this chapter, we shall outline both the lenient and strict approaches to this issue, about which the <i>poskim</i> remain divided. Our discussion is based on essays by Rav J. David Bleich, (<i>Tradition </i>39:2:90-96) and Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg (<i>Ateret Shlomo </i>pp. 33-39) and on a conversation with Rav Hershel Schachter. The discussion of this issue among the <i>poskim</i> is extraordinarily rich, touching on many fundamental principles and disputes regarding various aspects of the halachic process.
<b>The <i>S’feik S’feika</i> Argument – the <i>Rokei’ach</i></b>
Traditionally, pregnant wives of <i>kohanim</i> did not have to be concerned about the possibility that they might be carrying a boy. The <i>Rokei’ach</i> (366) articulates a leniency based on the rule of <i>s’feik s’feika</i> (double doubt). Although normally one must rule strictly if there is a doubt regarding a Torah-level prohibition, one may be lenient if two aspects of the situation are uncertain. We have such a double uncertainty, argues the <i>Rokei’ach</i>, in the case of the <i>kohen</i>’s wife: first, the child might be a female, and second, even if the child is a male, it might be a <i>neifel</i> (a non-viable child), to whom the restrictions on contact with the dead do not apply. The Shach (Y.D. 371:1) cites the <i>Rokei’ach</i> as normative Halachah.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Shulchan Aruch, Rama, and <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> do not raise the question of a fetus coming in contact with the dead. We shall deal with this omission later in our discussion.</i>
Accordingly, many question whether the <i>Rokei’ach</i>’s leniency still applies today, when women are able to discover the gender of their unborn children at their routine sonograms. Resolving the gender of the fetus might break the first prong of the <i>s’feik s’feika</i>. The applicability of the <i>Rokei’ach</i>’s explanation is the focus of much of the debate concerning this issue.
<b>The Strict Approach – Rav J. David Bleich</b>
Rav Bleich focuses on a debate amongst the <i>poskim</i> as to whether a <i>s’feik s’feika</i> remains in effect if the doubt can be resolved. The Rama (Y.D. 110:9) rules leniently, but the Shach (Y.D. 110 <i>Klalei S’feik S’feika</i> 35:66) notes that some are strict.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This question seems to hinge upon how one understands the role of <i>s’feik s’feika</i>. See Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik’s analysis cited by Rav Schachter in the <i>Journal of Halacha and Contemporary Society</i> 9:158. If one believes that we treat a case of <i>s’feik s’feika</i> as if the leniency is conclusively correct with no <i>safeik</i> at all, there is no need to investigate further about either <i>safeik</i>. If, however, a <i>s’feik s’feika</i> is treated as a doubt that is resolved leniently, there might be reason to investigate one or both of the doubts, if possible.</i> The Shach concludes that one should be strict in a case in which it is easy for one to check and there is no expense involved in resolving the doubt. Thus, since it is easy (and involves no extra cost) for a pregnant wife of a <i>kohen</i> to inquire as to the gender of the fetus when she undergoes her routine sonogram, it would seem that she is obligated to make this inquiry and then to avoid contact with the dead if she finds that the fetus is male.
Nonetheless, the <i>Teshuvot Noda Biy’hudah </i>(Y.D. 43, cited in Pitchei Teshuvah Y.D. 110:35) writes that even the strict opinion would be lenient in a case where only one prong of the double doubt can be clarified. The Pitchei Teshuvah records that Rav Akiva Eiger (<i>Teshuvot Rav Akiva Eiger</i> 77) and the <i>Maharshal </i>(cited in <i>Teshuvot Beit Yaakov</i> 84) agree with this approach, and he cites no dissenting opinions.
Accordingly, since only one of the prongs of the <i>Rokei’ach</i>’s <i>s’feik s’feika</i> – the fetus’ gender – can be resolved by a sonogram, the <i>s’feik s’feika</i> remains in effect. Hence, the wives of <i>kohanim</i> should not inquire as to the gender of their unborn children, thus preserving the <i>s’feik s’feika</i> and obviating the need to avoid contact with the dead.
Rav Bleich notes, however, that some <i>Acharonim</i>, including the Pri Megadim (O.C. <i>Eishel Avraham</i> 343:2) and Gilyon Maharsha (Y.D. 371:1), challenge the validity of the <i>s’feik s’feika</i> presented by the <i>Rokei’ach</i>. They note that <i>Tosafot</i> (Ketubot 9a s.v. <i>Ve’iba’it Eima</i>)<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i>’s view is cited as normative by the Shach (Y.D. 110 <i>Klalei S’feik s’feika</i> 33) and the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 110:115).</i> rule that a doubt can be utilized to create a legitimate <i>s’feik s’feika</i> only if each side is a <i>safeik hashakul</i> (at least a fifty percent chance of occurrence). Accordingly, while the <i>safeik</i> as to the gender of the fetus is a <i>safeik hashakul</i>, since at least fifty percent of babies are female, the <i>safeik</i> as to whether the baby is a <i>neifel</i> is not, since <i>nefalim</i> comprise only a very small percentage of babies.
This leads the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (<i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer </i>Y.D. 354, referenced in Pitchei Teshuvah Y.D. 371:1) to reinterpret the reasoning of the <i>Rokei’ach</i>. The principle that permits pregnant wives of <i>kohanim</i> to come in contact with the dead, according to the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>’s reading, is not <i>s’feik s’feika</i>. Rather, it is <i>rov</i> (majority): since about fifty percent of fetuses will be girls and several more will be <i>nefalim</i>, there is no concern for contact with the dead regarding the majority of unborn children.
Rav Bleich notes that basing this lenient ruling on <i>rov</i> rather than on <i>s’feik s’feika</i> has major repercussions. Unlike a <i>s’feik s’feika</i>, which remains intact even if one of the prongs can be readily resolved, one cannot rely on <i>rov</i> to resolve a doubt if the doubt is readily resolved upon inspection.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Investigation is not necessary in cases of <i>s’feik s’feika</i>, even where it would be for <i>rov</i>, because <i>s’feik s’feika</i> is a more potent tool for resolving doubt (though there is much discussion of this point, summarized by the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> Y.D. 110:96-98).</i> In a situation in which there is a very significant minority (<i>mi’ut hamatzui</i>) of cases that differ from the <i>rov</i>, rabbinic law requires one to investigate the situation if it is possible to do so (see Rashi to Chullin 12a s.v. <i>Pesach </i>and Ramban, <i>Milchamot Hashem</i> to Chullin 4a in the pages in the Rif). Though there is some dispute as to what constitutes a <i>mi’ut hamatzui</i>, it is generally accepted that more than ten percent is considered a <i>mi’ut hamatzui</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Teshuvot Mishkenot Yaakov</i> 1 Y.D. 17, <i>Teshuvot Minchat Shlomo </i>2:61:1, and Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik (cited in <i>Nefesh Harav</i> p. 228). Rav J.David Bleich (<i>Tradition</i> 41:1:56) notes that ten percent is the most widely accepted standard for this matter, though he cites alternative views. Rav Mordechai Willig also mentioned ten percent in a <i>shiur</i> delivered to the Rabbinical Council of America.</i>
For example, Rav Hershel Schachter told me that if most suit jackets do not contain <i>sha’atnez</i> (a forbidden wool and linen mixture), but more than ten percent do,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Schachter noted that this percentage varies based on the price of the suit and the country in which it is manufactured. </i> one would be required, on a rabbinic level, to inspect a suit jacket he has purchased to see if it contains <i>sha’atnez</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Also see the last paragraph of <i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 1:72.</i> In our case, Rav Bleich rules that since viable males constitute a very significant minority of unborn children, a <i>kohen</i>’s wife would be required by rabbinic law to inquire at her sonogram whether the fetus is male. If it is, she would be required to avoid contact with the dead.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Included in this requirement is avoiding awnings of a funeral home that hang over a sidewalk, trees that overhang from a cemetery onto a sidewalk or street, and museums that house dead bodies.</i>
Rav Bleich does, however, permit the wife to give birth in a hospital (despite the possible presence of dead bodies) because of concern for <i>pikuach nefesh</i> (life-threatening danger). Rav Bleich notes that Halachah always regards childbirth as a situation that constitutes such a danger (see Shabbat 128b-129a), and he marshals many recent medical studies to prove that the danger is lessened when the birth takes place in a hospital. Although there are some studies that indicate that home births with a certified professional midwife might be as safe as hospital deliveries, every individual has the halachic right to choose which health care provider and service is the better option for him or her (<i>Shemirat Shabbat K’hilchatah</i> 32:38). Accordingly, even if the sonogram indicates that the fetus is a boy, the <i>kohen</i>’s wife may choose to give birth in a hospital if she believes that it is safer to do so there.
<b>The Lenient View – Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg</b>
There are, however, many reasons to adopt a lenient approach to our issue. First, it is possible to defend the integrity of the <i>s’feik s’feika</i> from the question of the Pri Megadim and Gilyon Maharsha. Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg notes that the Gemara (Yevamot 119a) constructs a similar <i>s’feik s’feika</i>, although it does not specifically mention the term, in the context of the <i>halachot </i>of <i>yibum </i>and <i>chalitzah</i>.
Some background is necessary to understand the Gemara’s case. If a man dies childless, the Torah (Devarim 25:4-11) obligates the brother of the deceased either to marry the widow (<i>yibum</i>) or participate in a ceremony in which he officially declines to marry her (<i>chalitzah</i>). If the deceased left offspring, however, there is no obligation for his brother to perform either <i>yibum</i> or <i>chalitzah</i>. The Mishnah (Yevamot 16:1) discusses a case where a woman’s husband died childless and the deceased husband did not have a brother, thus avoiding the requirement for <i>yibum</i> or <i>chalitzah</i>. However, the deceased husband’s mother remains alive in a distant land where the wife could not communicate with her. The Mishnah teaches that the wife need not be concerned that the mother gave birth to a male child who would be required to perform <i>yibum</i> or <i>chalitzah</i> because of what may be construed as a <i>s’feik s’feika</i>: “Perhaps she miscarried and perhaps she gave birth to a female.” It thus appears that such a <i>s’feik s’feika</i> is valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Alternatively, the Gemara’s case could be understood as employing the principle of <i>rov</i>, as we noted earlier from the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>. Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg, however, understands <i>s’feik s’feika </i>to be merely a more potent form of<i> rov</i>, and thus one cannot distinguish between the two, since they are identical concepts. For further discussion of this issue, see<i> Aruch Hashulchan </i>Y.D. 110:96-98.</i>
Rav Zalman Nechemia also points out that the <i>Rivash</i> (372, cited by the <i>Shev Shemateta</i> 1:18) cites this passage as a contradiction to the aforementioned <i>Tosafot</i> in Ketubot. The <i>Rivash</i> distinguishes between the two cases, explaining that a <i>safeik</i> pertaining to a naturally occurring event can be marshaled to construct a <i>s’feik s’feika</i> even if it is not a <i>safeik hashakul</i>. <i>Tosafot</i>, on the other hand, address a case in which the <i>s’feik s’feika</i> is based on doubts regarding how a particular person behaved. Such a doubt, according to the <i>Rivash</i>, can be used to create a <i>s’feik s’feika</i> only if it is a <i>safeik hashakul</i>.
Accordingly, concludes Rav Zalman Nechemia, the <i>s’feik s’feika</i> of the <i>Rokei’ach</i> is legitimate, since both doubts relate to which naturally occurring event happened, even though one prong is not a <i>safeik hashakul</i>. Since the <i>Rokei’ach</i>’s <i>s’feik s’feika</i> stands, a <i>kohen</i>’s wife may rely on the opinions that one need not resolve a <i>s’feik s’feika</i>, and thus she need not ask about her child’s gender at her sonogram.
<b>Support for the Lenient View – <i>Tum’ah Belu’ah</i></b>
The Magen Avraham (343:2) wonders why the <i>Rokei’ach</i> finds it necessary to construct a <i>s’feik s’feika</i> to permit pregnant wives of <i>kohanim</i> to come in contact with the dead. He notes that the Gemara (Chullin 71a) teaches that something that is “swallowed” (<i>belu’ah</i>) in another item does not contract <i>tum’ah</i> (ritual impurity) from the item that surrounds it. For instance, if someone swallows a <i>tahor</i> (ritually pure) ring and subsequently becomes <i>tamei</i>, the ring remains <i>tahor</i>, since the person shields it from the <i>tum’ah</i>. Accordingly, the fetus should not become <i>tamei</i> even if the mother does, since the mother shields the fetus that is <i>balu’a</i> within her from <i>tum’ah</i>. The Magen Avraham concludes that he is unable to resolve his question.
The <i>Radbaz</i> (<i>Chadashot</i> 200, cited in Pitchei Teshuvah Y.D. 371:1), writing 150 years before the Magen Avraham, also asks this question. He concludes that the <i>Rokei’ach</i> must be speaking about a particular situation that mandates relying on such a leniency – when the woman is very close to term, and may go into labor suddenly. Since the <i>tum’ah belu’ah</i> leniency would cease to apply the moment the baby is born, which could happen at any time, the <i>Rokei’ach</i>’s <i>s’feik s’feika</i> is needed to justify a non-emergency visit to a place with dead bodies.
The <i>Netiv Chaim</i> (printed in the standard editions of the Shulchan Aruch O.C. 343) concurs with the <i>Radbaz</i>’s approach, and both the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (<i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer</i> Y.D. 354) and the Mishnah Berurah (343:3) rule in accordance with this lenient interpretation of the <i>Radbaz</i>. Rav Shmuel Wosner (<i>Teshuvot Sheivet Halevi</i> 2:205) endorses this reading of the <i>Rokei’ach</i>, though he notes that Rav Yaakov Emden (<i>Teshuvot Ya’avetz</i> 2:177) disagrees. Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg, based on the Mishnah Berurah’s approach, likewise rules leniently.
According to this understanding, a <i>kohen</i>’s wife need not be concerned about coming in contact with the dead until it appears that she is about to give birth. Even after this point, the mother may give birth in a hospital in order to minimize the danger to life during childbirth, as we discussed above.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">We should note that this argument also allows a <i>kohen </i>to remain at home with his wife if she has miscarried (heaven forfend), even if the expired fetus will remain in her body for a short while until it is medically appropriate to remove. This is because <i>tum’ah belu’ah </i>also prevents the “swallowed” <i>tum’ah </i>from spreading outside its “container.” See also Rav Hershel Schachter’s <i>Be’ikvei Hatzon</i> pp. 234-235 footnote 7.</i>
<b>Objections to the <i>Tum’ah Belu’ah </i>Justification</b>
Many <i>Acharonim</i> challenge the validity of the <i>tum’ah belu’ah </i>justification. Both Rav Elchanan Wasserman (<i>Kovetz Shiurim</i> 2:41) and Rav Chaim Ozer Grodzinsky (<i>Teshuvot Achiezer</i> 3:65:5-6) argue that the fetus’ safety from <i>tum’ah</i> does not solve the entire problem, as they claim that a <i>kohen</i> is prohibited to have contact with the dead even if he does not become <i>tamei meit</i> (ritually impure through contact with the dead) thereby. Conversely, according to this logic, a <i>kohen</i> may become <i>tamei meit</i> even though he is not considered to have come in contact with the dead.
Rav Hershel Schachter (<i>B’ikvei Hatzon</i> pp. 232-238) vigorously supports this view and marshals many sources to prove its correctness.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">For example, Rav Schachter notes that some authorities (cited and accepted by the Rama Y.D. 369:1) allow a <i>kohen</i> to touch metal that has come in contact with the dead, even though Halachah gives such metal the status of the dead itself (<i>cherev harei hu k’chalal</i>). See <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Nazir </i>54b s.v. <i>Ta Shma</i>). </i> Accordingly, it is not surprising that he is inclined to reject the Mishnah Berurah’s ruling, which assumes that <i>tum’ah belu’ah</i> is sufficient cause for leniency. Moreover, he told me that the <i>Radbaz</i>’s entire limitation of the <i>Rokei’ach</i>’s explanation does not seem to fit the straightforward reading of the words of the <i>Rokei’ach</i> (Rav Wosner believes otherwise), which removes the issue of <i>tum’ah belu’ah </i>entirely.
On the other hand, many <i>Acharonim</i> dispute this understanding of the prohibition for <i>kohanim</i> to come in contact with the dead. They believe that the prohibition is indeed focused on contracting <i>tum’at meit</i>. Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg, for example, concludes this based on <i>Tosafot </i>(Ketubot 28b s.v. <i>Beit Hapras</i>), who apply the principle of <i>sefeik tum’ah birshut harabim tahor </i>(an item whose purity is in doubt in a public place is assumed to be <i>tahor</i>) to the <i>kohanim</i>’s prohibition. According to Rav Elchanan and Rav Chaim Ozer, it should be forbidden for a <i>kohen</i> to enter an area of doubtful <i>tum’ah</i> in a public place, since he might in fact be coming in contact with the dead despite remaining <i>tahor</i>. The fact that <i>Tosafot</i> believe that a <i>kohen</i> is permitted (in some circumstances) to enter such an area simply because he is not rendered <i>tamei</i>, even though he might come in contact with the dead, seems to demonstrate that <i>Tosafot</i> do not subscribe to Rav Elchanan and Rav Chaim Ozer’s approach.
The <i>Avnei Milu’im</i> (82:1) objects to the approach of the <i>Radbaz</i> and Magen Avraham for a different reason. Only a foreign object in a body is considered <i>belu’ah</i> and is protected from contracting <i>tum’ah</i> from its host. The <i>Avnei Milu’im</i> argues that a fetus is not “foreign,” and therefore is not shielded by its mother from contracting <i>tum’ah</i>. As proof for his assertion, he cites the Gemara’s (Yevamot 78a-b) ruling that when a pregnant non-Jewish woman immerses in a <i>mikveh</i> to convert to Judaism, the fetus becomes Jewish along with its mother. The Gemara explains that the mother does not constitute a <i>chatzitzah</i> (barrier) between the baby and the <i>mikveh</i> water because “<i>haynu reviteih</i>,” this is the normal manner in which the fetus develops. The <i>Avnei Milu’im</i> infers that just as the mother does not separate between her fetus and the <i>mikveh</i> water, she also does not serve as a barrier between her fetus and <i>tum’ah</i>.
Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg, though, cites the Mishnah’s (<i>Parah</i> 3:2) description of the extreme measures that were taken to ensure the <i>tahorah</i> of the individuals who drew water for the <i>parah adumah</i> (red heifer) ceremony.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The only way for a person to remove himself from the status of <i>tum’at meit</i> is to go through a process of purification (described in Bemidbar 19:1-22) involving the ashes of the <i>parah adumah</i>.</i> The Mishnah describes how women would come to specially designed homes in Yerushalayim where they would give birth and raise children who were guaranteed not to have become <i>tamei</i>. This Mishnah, contends Rav Zalman Nechemia, clearly indicates that the concern for <i>tum’ah</i> begins only at birth, as the women would come to this type of home only to give birth, not immediately after conception. Apparently, the mother does shield her fetus from <i>tum’ah</i>, presumably because the fetus is <i>tum’ah belu’ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This inference from the Gemara also is made by the <i>Me’iri</i> (Sukkah 21a).</i> To defend the <i>Avnei Milu’im</i>, Rav Zalman Nechemia suggests that a fetus can become <i>tamei</i> in utero, but loses all <i>tum’ah</i> when born because it emerges as a new entity (see, however, <i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion Hachadashot</i> 96). He notes, however, that this answer is inadequate for the <i>Avnei Milu’im</i> himself, who writes (Ketzot Hachoshen 209:1)<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The author of the <i>Avnei Milu’im</i>, Rav Aryeh Leib ben Yosef Hakohen, also wrote the Ketzot Hachoshen.</i> that a fetus does not emerge as a new entity at birth.
Moreover, Rav Shmuel Wosner (<i>Teshuvot Sheivet Halevi</i> 2:205 and 6:175) adds that the Mishnah (Niddah 5:3) describes how a baby on the day it is born has the potential to become <i>tamei</i>. This clearly indicates that before the baby is born it does not have this potential. Rav Wosner seems to share Rav Zalman Nechemia’s view that the prohibition for a <i>kohen</i> to come in contact with the dead applies only in a situation where the <i>kohen</i> becomes <i>tamei</i>. Indeed, he writes that a wife of a <i>kohen</i> should not hesitate to give birth in a hospital despite the presence of <i>tum’at meit</i>. It seems that Rav Wosner would not require a <i>kohen</i>’s wife to inquire as to the gender of her child when she undergoes a sonogram and that he would permit a <i>kohen</i>’s wife to visit a hospital or funeral home even when she is pregnant.
Surprisingly, the Shulchan Aruch and Rama do not mention the <i>Rokei’ach</i>’s comment at all. This omission might indicate that they disagree with his basic assumption that a <i>kohen</i> has the potential to become <i>tamei</i> in utero. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> also does not cite the <i>Rokei’ach</i> at all. These authorities might believe that the entire issue is moot, since a fetus cannot become <i>tamei</i>.
<b>Is a Fetus a <i>Kohen</i>?</b>
Another possible explanation for the silence of the Shulchan Aruch and Rama on this matter is that they do not share the <i>Rokei’ach</i>’s assumption that a <i>kohen</i>’s son is considered a <i>kohen</i> before he is born. Indeed, the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (<i>Teshuvot</i> Y.D. 354) proves this from an explicit comment of the Gemara (Yevamot 67a): “A fetus in the womb of a non-<i>kohen</i> is not a <i>kohen</i> (even if the father is a <i>kohen</i>).” The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> explains that since we rule that “<i>Ubar yerech imo</i>,” “A fetus is considered to be a limb of the mother,” the fetus has the same status as its mother. Thus, just as the unborn child’s mother (even if she is the daughter of a <i>kohen</i>) is not forbidden to come in contact with the dead, the fetus is also not forbidden to be in contact with the dead. The <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> (263:3) also suggests that the fetus does not have the status of a <i>kohen</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion Hachadashot </i>(96) argues that even if the fetus is permitted to become <i>tamei</i>, it will maintain that status after it is born, whereupon, if it is a male, it will violate the prohibition. The cogency of the argument may depend on the aforementioned dispute regarding whether a <i>kohen</i> may become <i>tamei meit</i> if he does not actually come in contact with the dead.</i> However, Rav Moshe Shternbuch (<i>Teshuvot V’hanhagot</i> 1:679) cites Rav Yitzchak Zev Soloveitchik as asserting that a son of a <i>kohen</i> is classified as a <i>kohen</i> even in utero. According to Rav Soloveitchik, the Gemara cited by the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> should not be understood as an all-embracing statement, but rather as a rule that applies uniquely to the ability of a <i>kohen</i>’s wife to eat <i>terumah</i> (the <i>Kohen</i>’s tithe, which a non-<i>Kohen</i> is strictly forbidden to consume), the subject the Gemara there discusses. The <i>Rokei’ach</i> apparently shares this view, since he does not advance this argument to allow a <i>kohen</i>’s pregnant wife to come in contact with the dead.
<b>Conclusion</b>
Rav Bleich concludes that a <i>kohen</i>’s wife must inquire at a sonogram if her unborn child is a male, and if she discovers that it is, she must avoid contact with the dead unless this poses a danger to life (such as in childbirth). However, Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg rules in accordance with the Mishnah Berurah that a <i>kohen</i>’s wife need not be concerned about <i>tum’at meit </i>until she is ready to give birth, a view that Rav Wosner appears to share. Rav Shternbuch even allows a wife who already knows that her child is male to come in contact with the dead until within a few days of birth, at which point we must be concerned that she suddenly will go into labor. Even after this point, Rav Shternbuch is lenient in case of need.
The lenient approach is remarkably well-founded on at least three considerations: the <i>Rokei’ach</i>’s <i>s’feik s’feika</i>, the idea that the mother shields the fetus from becoming <i>tamei meit</i>, and the assertion that the fetus does not have the status of a <i>kohen</i>. Nevertheless, Rav Schachter informs me that he is not convinced of any of these lenient approaches. Thus, <i>kohanim</i> and their wives must consult their Rav for a ruling regarding this matter.
<b>Postscript</b>
We should note that there is a well-known custom in many communities, independent of the <i>kohanim </i>issue, for pregnant women not to enter cemeteries.

Cosmetics and Halacha

Permanent and Semi Permanent Makeup, Cosmetic Tattooing

Recently, procedures have been developed to tattoo permanent or semi-permanent makeup on women. In the process of applying permanent makeup, also known as micropigmentation, dermapigmentation, or cosmetic tattooing, a needle deposits colored pigments made from iron oxide into the skin’s dermal layer (the layer between the permanent base layer and the constantly changing top layer). This procedure is performed on the lips or around the eyes under antiseptic conditions, with the patient under anesthesia. The tattooing can be either permanent or semi-permanent, with the latter form lasting up to three years and eventually disintegrating. These procedures are very tempting for observant women (especially those blessed with the task of caring for young children), as they save time and avoid the problem of applying makeup on Shabbat.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">We discuss the issue of applying makeup on Shabbat in a later chapter.</i> However, there are serious halachic problems associated with such makeup. The consensus among contemporary <i>poskim </i>is that it violates the prohibition of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> (tattooing; Vayikra 19:28). We will explore the prohibition of applying a tattoo and see how contemporary <i>poskim</i> relate it to the issue of permanent and semi-permanent makeup. As with countless other contemporary halachic issues, <i>poskim</i> are compelled to define the parameters of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> very rigorously in order to arrive at a conclusion.
We will base our discussions on two essays on this topic that have been published in <i>Techumin</i>, one by Rav Ezra Basri (10:282-287) and the other by Rav Baruch Shraga (18:110-114). In addition, two prominent <i>poskim</i>, Rav Shraga Shneebalg of London (<i>Teshuvot Shraga Hamei’ir</i> 8:44 and 45) and Rav Natan Gestetner of Bnei Brak (<i>Teshuvot L’horot Natan</i> 10:64), have published <i>teshuvot </i>in recent years regarding cosmetic tattooing. Rav Shmuel Wosner (<i>Teshuvot Sheivet Halevi</i> 10:137) wrote a brief responsum on this issue as well.
<b>How Long Must <i>Kitovet Ka’aka</i> Last?</b>
The <i>Rishonim </i>disagree about how long a tattoo must last in order to constitute a violation of the prohibition of<i> kitovet ka’aka</i>. While Rashi (commentary to Vayikra 19:28 and Gittin 20b s.v. <i>Kitovet</i>) and the Ritva (Makkot 21a s.v. <i>Hakotev</i>) describe <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> as something permanent, the <i>Nimukei Yosef</i> (Makkot 4b in the pages of the Rif s.v. <i>Ad Sheyichtov</i>) defines <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> as something that lasts “for a long time.”
Rav Gestetner points out that the Rambam (<i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 12:11) and the Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 180:1-4) do not limit the prohibition to permanent tattooing, thus implying that one violates the <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> prohibition even if the markings are not meant to last permanently, as the <i>Nimukei Yosef</i> believes. Moreover, Rav Gestetner suggests that when Rashi writes that <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> lasts “<i>l’olam</i>,” he does not mean “forever” literally. Elsewhere, (Shabbat 111b s.v. <i>V’eilu Kesharim</i>) Rashi uses this term in a context from which it is fairly obvious (in light of his comment on Shabbat 112a s.v. <i>B’d’chumrata</i>) that he means for a long period of time, not necessarily forever.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See the Mishnah Berurah’s introduction to O.C. 317, wherein he defines “<i>l’olam</i>,” in the context of tying a knot on Shabbat, as when one does not set a time to undo the knot and the knot is capable of lasting permanently.<br>Ariel Herzog notes that the Torah (Shemot 21:6) states that if a Jewish slave elects to stay with his master at the end of his six years of servitude, he remains in bondage “<i>l’olam</i>,” which the Gemara (Kiddushin 15a) interprets to mean until the <i>yovel</i> (the fiftieth year of the agricultural cycle). This is another instance in which the term “<i>l’olam</i>” does not mean literally forever. Indeed, the Ibn Ezra (<i>Peirush Ha’aroch</i> to Shemot 21:6) proves from elsewhere in <i>Tanach</i> that the term <i>l’olam</i> means for a lengthy time and not forever. </i> Rav Gestetner rules that three years is considered “a long time,” and thus even semi-permanent cosmetic tattoos that last for three years are biblically prohibited even according to Rashi.
Rav Shneebalg disagrees, but only about the severity of the prohibition – he understands Rashi to mean literally forever, and he therefore is inclined to consider semi-permanent cosmetic tattooing a rabbinic violation.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This understanding of Rashi seems to accord with the Mishnah Berurah’s comment cited in the previous footnote.</i> Indeed, all <i>Rishonim </i>agree that one violates at least a rabbinic prohibition even if the tattoo is not a permanent one. The proof to this is the debate in the Gemara (Makkot 21a) about whether one is permitted to put stove ashes on an open wound, which creates a mark that resembles a tattoo. This mark does not last very long and is undoubtedly classified as temporary. That the Gemara even raises the possibility of regarding such a mark as <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> proves that one violates at least a rabbinic prohibition even if the mark does not last forever. The Rivan<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The primary Talmudic discussion of tattooing appears in <i>Masechet Makkot</i> (21a). Rashi did not finish his commentary to the last few pages of <i>Masechet Makkot</i>, so his son-in-law, the Rivan, completed his father-in-law’s commentary. Hence, we will be referring to the Rivan frequently in this chapter.</i> (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Uchtovet</i>) might also indicate this, as he writes that “It is forbidden to write any writing” on the flesh.
<b>How Deep Must the Tattoo Be?</b>
How deeply must the ink or dye be inserted to qualify as <i>kitovet ka’aka</i>? The Ritva (ad. loc.) writes that “The dye enters between the skin and the flesh.” Rav Shneebalg derives from this comment that one violates the prohibition even if the dye is inserted immediately below the skin level. It seems that the Shulchan Aruch (ad. loc.) agrees with this assessment, as he describes <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> as “scratching the flesh.” Even by penetrating one layer of skin, then, one violates the prohibition. As mentioned above, the process of cosmetic tattooing involves the insertion of the pigments into the skin’s dermal layer, which would appear to be included in the prohibition.
We should clarify that the Mishnah (ad. loc.) imposes the punishment of <i>Malkot</i> (lashes) only on one who both writes and cuts the skin. The <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> (253:1) cites a dispute among the <i>Acharonim</i> as to whether one violates a rabbinic prohibition by merely writing on the skin without cutting it. The <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> observes that since the common practice among Jews is to avoid making indelible markings even if the skin is not cut, the normative Halachah follows the opinions that believe that it is a rabbinic prohibition to do so.
The <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> clarifies, though, that this rabbinic prohibition obviously applies only if the mark cannot be erased. Simple writing on the hand with ink, however, is not prohibited at all. Although writing with ordinary ink on one’s skin is, generally speaking, undignified, it appears that it is not technically prohibited - <i>Chazal</i> forbade only activities that resemble <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> (see <i>Tosafot Gittin</i> 20b s.v. <i>B’chtovet</i>). Accordingly, it seems that it is permitted to have one’s hand stamped at an amusement park to prove that the admission fee has been paid – amusement park stamps and ordinary writing on the body do not resemble a tattoo at all. The fake tattoos that small children apply also seem to be permitted, even on a rabbinic level.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Mordechai Willig told me that he believes this to be permissible.</i>
<b>The Order of the Process</b>
As mentioned, the Mishnah (Makkot 3:6) states that one receives <i>malkot</i> only if he both inserts the dye and scratches the skin. The Rivan (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Kitovet</i>) describes the process of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> as specifically following that order – first writing and then making a tear in the skin. The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 12:11), on the other hand, describes <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> as first tearing the skin and subsequently inserting the dye. Might the status of cosmetic tattooing, in which a cut is made before dye is inserted, hinge on this dispute between the Rambam and the Rivan?
The <i>Bach</i> (Y.D. 180 s.v. <i>V’hu Shekoteiv</i>) argues that there is in fact no dispute between the Rambam and the Rivan. The Rivan is merely following the order as it is found in the term “<i>kitovet ka’aka</i>” – writing then cutting – whereas the Rambam describes the process as it is normally conducted – cutting before writing. Although the Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 180:1) describes <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> as first cutting the skin and subsequently inserting the dye, the Shach (Y.D. 180:1) indicates that one violates the biblical prohibition even if the order is reversed.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> (253:1), who interprets the Shach in this manner, but cites authorities who contend that the biblical prohibition applies only to the sequence described by the Rambam and the Shulchan Aruch.</i> Rav Gestetner adds that one also violates the biblical prohibition even if the writing and cutting occur simultaneously, which is what he understands cosmetic tattooing to entail.
<b>What Must Be Written?</b>
The <i>Teshuvot Me’il Tzedakah </i>(31, cited in the Pitchei Teshuvah Y.D. 180:1) asks whether one must write letters in order to violate the prohibition of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> or whether any marking constitutes a violation. Though he suggests that creating a marking without writing is only a rabbinic prohibition, other <i>Acharonim</i> (such as the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i>) note that the <i>Rishonim</i> already disputed this matter.
The <i>Semak</i> (72), Rabbeinu Peretz (cited in the <i>Semak</i>), the <i>Orchot Chaim</i> (22:4), and the <i>Sefer Hachinuch</i> (253) seem to believe that one violates this prohibition only if he tattoos letters into his skin. This approach might be based on the reason offered by the <i>Rishonim</i> (Rambam <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 12:11 and <i>Tur</i> Y.D. 180) for the prohibition of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> – that idolaters would often tattoo the names of their gods into their skin, wishing to communicate that they were committed slaves to those particular gods. Accordingly, a number of <i>Rishonim</i> and <i>Acharonim</i> – the majority of them, according to Rav Basri – believe that one violates <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> only by writing letters, since this was the manner in which the idolaters expressed their commitment to idolatry.
On the other hand, the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> (253:3) judges that most <i>Rishonim</i> believe that one violates <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> even if he does not write letters. Among the <i>Rishonim</i> who explicitly state that writing is not necessary are the Ra’avad (<i>Torat Kohanim</i>, <i>Kedoshim</i> 76) and the Rash MiShantz (<i>ibid</i>.). Rav Gestetner argues that the Rambam (ad. loc.) and Shulchan Aruch (ad. loc.) also indicate that one violates <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> even without inscribing letters, as they make no mention of any such requirement.
Rav Shneebalg asserts that all <i>Rishonim</i> would agree that one transgresses at least a rabbinic prohibition even if he does not write letters. He proves this from the Gemara’s suggestion that placing stove ashes on a wound might be prohibited because the resultant scab resembles a tattoo, as we discussed above. The scab obviously does not appear in the form of a letter. Nonetheless, the <i>Teshuvot Me’il Tzedakah</i> (31) suggests that the scab actually does look like a letter, in which case there is not necessarily even a rabbinic prohibition if no letters are written.
Rav Avigdor Nebenzahl (cited in <i>Techumin</i> 18:113) believes that even the strict opinion among the <i>Rishonim</i> prohibits only imprinting a picture or figure, such as an anchor or a heart. Imprinting amorphous color, he asserts, does not qualify as writing and thus does not constitute <i>kitovet ka’aka</i>. Indeed, in the context of Shabbat, writing letters and drawing pictures are included under the single <i>av melachah</i> (one of the thirty-nine categories of prohibited Shabbat activity; see Rambam <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 12:9-17) of <i>koteiv </i>(writing), while coloring a surface is defined as <i>Tzovei’a </i>(coloring), an entirely separate <i>av melachah</i>. The Torah prohibits only <i>kitovet</i> (writing), not coloring. Accordingly, Rav Nebenzahl is inclined to permit semi-permanent cosmetics based on a combination of two lenient factors: the absence of any writing and the impermanence of the markings.
In contrast, Rav Basri, Rav Gestetner, and Rav Shneebalg argue that according to the strict opinion, any form that is imprinted on the body is included in the prohibition. The Rambam and Shulchan Aruch also apparently endorse this position, as they declare that one transgresses this prohibition when he injects dye beneath the skin. Not only do they make no mention of a requirement for writing letters to violate the prohibition, but they also make no mention of a requirement for a picture or figure to be drawn. Furthermore, we may question Rav Nebenzahl’s proof based on the <i>Sefer Hachinuch</i>’s ruling (ad. loc.) that <i>beit din</i> punishes someone for tattooing just one letter, even though <i>beit din</i> does not punish for writing fewer than two letters on Shabbat. Since the rules governing writing itself are not the same for Shabbat and <i>kitovet ka’aka</i>, the distinction between writing and coloring that we find in the former might be irrelevant to the latter.
Intriguingly, Rav Wosner forbids cosmetic tattooing not because of a technical violation of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i>, but because of “<i>serach issur kitovet ka’aka</i>,” which translates loosely to “because it will habituate one to <i>kitovet ka’aka</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See a similar usage of “<i>serach</i>” in <i>Masechet Eruvin </i>(40b).</i> Even if cosmetic tattooing is not exactly like tattooing proper (perhaps because of Rav Nebenzahl’s reasoning), it nevertheless resembles <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> too closely for comfort. As <i>Tosafot</i> (Gittin 20b s.v. <i>B’chtovet</i>) teach, <i>Chazal</i> forbade even acts that resemble <i>kitovet ka’aka</i>, such as making permanent markings on the skin without cutting it. Thus, even if Rav Nebentzahl’s argument is correct, it still would not suffice to permit cosmetic tattooing. Nevertheless, it might bolster the argument that the tattooing is only a violation of a rabbinic law.
<b>Purpose of the Tattooing</b>
The Mishnah (Makkot 3:6) records the opinion of Rabi Shimon that one is flogged for violating the prohibition of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> only if he tattoos the name of an idol onto his skin. The <i>Chachamim</i>, however, require flogging for any tattoo. The <i>Rishonim</i> disagree about which opinion is regarded as normative. While we usually accept the majority opinion, the Gemara (<i>ibid</i>.) records a discussion of the opinion of Rabi Shimon, leading some <i>Rishonim</i> to conclude that his opinion is the accepted one. <sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For a summary of the opinions, see the <i>Otzar Meforshei Hatalmud</i>, Makkot p. 847 note 20.</i> The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (Y.D. 180 s.v. <i>Shechayav</i>) quotes Rabbeinu Yerucham, who cites conflicting opinions and concludes that the Halachah follows the view of the <i>Chachamim</i>. The <i>Beit Yosef</i> agrees, noting that this also appears to be the opinion of the Rambam.
The Rivan (Makkot 21a s.v. <i>Uchtovet</i>) writes that even Rabi Shimon prohibits tattooing any writing, even if it is not the name of an idol - Rabi Shimon and the <i>Chachamim</i> disagree only about whether one is flogged for such tattooing. It is possible, however, that one violates the prohibition of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> on a biblical level only if his intention is for idolatry, even according to the stringent opinion of the <i>Chachamim</i>. Recall that the Rambam and the <i>Tur</i> maintain that the reason for the <i>kitovet ka’aka </i>prohibition is to avoid idolatry. Indeed, the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (commentary to Gittin 20b s.v. <i>B’chtovet</i>) writes that one does not violate a biblical prohibition if he tattoos for non-idolatrous reasons. The Shach (Y.D. 180:6) seems to support this view, as he explains that it is permitted (on a biblical level) to put ashes on a wound because his wound proves that he is not coloring with idolatrous motives. The <i>Teshuvot Sho’eil Umeishiv</i> (2:1:49) agrees with the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>.
The <i>Aruch Laner</i> (commentary to Makkot 21a s.v. <i>Gam Im</i>), on the other hand, states that one violates a biblical prohibition even if his intention is not for idolatry. The <i>Aruch Laner</i> and Rav Gestetner note that the Rambam and Shulchan Aruch seem to agree with this view, as they do not confine this prohibition only to one whose intention is for idolatry. Moreover, Rav Gestetner observes that <i>Tosafot</i> (Gittin 20b s.v. <i>B’chtovet</i>) clearly indicate that a biblical prohibition is violated even if one’s intention is not for idolatry.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Maharam Schick (commentary to <i>Sefer Hamitzvot</i>, <i>mitzvah</i> 254) contends that the prohibition of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> is related to the prohibition to imitate gentile practices (Vayikra 18:3) and as such is violated as long as the tattoo is made for no (substantive) reason.</i>
<b>Branding a Slave</b>
The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (ad. loc.) acknowledges that normative Halachah undoubtedly forbids tattooing for non-idolatrous purposes, since the Rama (Y.D. 180:4) forbids branding one’s slave to avoid his escape. It seems from the Shulchan Aruch and Rama, however, that this is prohibited only on a rabbinic level. Commenting on the Shulchan Aruch’s statement that one who performs such a branding is “exempt,” the Rama adds that one should nonetheless refrain from this activity “<i>l’chatchilah</i> (initially).” Generally speaking, the Shulchan Aruch and Rama do not address issues of when one deserves to be flogged, because these authorities do not address issues that apply only in messianic times.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">When one deserves flogging is currently relevant, though, regarding the laws of giving testimony; see Shulchan Aruch C.M. 34:2.</i> Consequently, it seems that the Shulchan Aruch is “exempting” the brander from the biblical prohibition, and the Rama is adding that one should still avoid such branding <i>l’chatchilah</i> because it entails a rabbinic violation. The reason why no biblical prohibition is violated, asserts the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>, is that there is no intention for idolatry. If so, cosmetic tattooing, which likewise does not involve idolatrous intent, constitutes only a rabbinic prohibition.
The <i>Get Pashut</i> (124:30, cited by the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> 253:2), offers a slightly different explanation for the exemption in the case of the slave. He suggests that the criterion for whether one violates the biblical prohibition of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> is not whether his intent is for idolatry, but whether the purpose of the tattooing is for the writing to appear on one’s body. The <i>Get Pashut</i> asserts that the idolaters’ purpose in tattooing was for the actual writing to appear on the body. Therefore, one who does not do so for the purpose of the writing itself is not imitating the idolatrous practice and is exempt from the biblical prohibition. The objective of one who tattoos his slave is not the actual writing - he simply wants to deter the slave’s escape. As such, he does not violate a biblical prohibition. This is comparable to performing a prohibited labor on Shabbat when one’s purpose is not for the primary result of the labor (<i>melachah she’einah tzerichah l’gufah</i>). Normative Halachah (see Mishnah Berurah 316:34) accepts the opinion of Rabi Shimon that this type of action does not violate a biblical prohibition.
It is not entirely clear how the <i>Get Pashut</i> would treat cosmetic tattooing. Rav Basri argues that he would not consider it a biblical prohibition, since its purpose is beauty, not writing <i>per se</i>. Rav Gestetner disagrees with this reasoning, contending that the purpose of cosmetic tattooing is indeed the writing, which makes it a full-fledged biblical prohibition.
Moreover, both the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> and Rav Gestetner question the <i>Get Pashut</i>’s explanation of the Shulchan Aruch. Rav Gestetner cites <i>Acharonim</i> (based on <i>Tosafot Shabbat</i> 75a s.v. <i>Tfei</i>) who posit that the exemption of <i>melachah she’einah tzerichah l’gufah</i> is unique to Shabbat. In fact, the <i>Pnei Yehoshua</i> (commenting on <i>Tosafot Shabbat </i>46b s.v. <i>D’chol</i>) argues that it does not even apply to <i>Yom Tov</i>! Thus, the concept is entirely irrelevant to the prohibition of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i>.
<b>Who Violates the Prohibition?</b>
Precisely how does one violate the prohibition of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> – by actually inscribing the tattoo, or even by having it inscribed on his body? Which does the Torah intend when it commands (Vayikra 19:28), “<i>Uchtovet ka’aka lo titenu bachem</i>,” “You shall not put a tattoo on yourselves?” The Tosefta (Makkot 3:9) states that both aspects are included – both one who inscribes a tattoo and one who allows a tattoo to be made on his body violate the biblical prohibition.
The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 12:11) seems to qualify this, stating that the one who permits the tattoo to be inscribed on his body is punished with <i>Malkot</i> only if he actively assists the inscription of the tattoo. This is an application of the rule that one is flogged only if he violates a sin that involves a physical action, such as eating non-kosher food or wearing <i>sha’atnez</i>. Nonetheless, the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> (253:4) writes, one who allows a tattoo to be inscribed on his body violates a biblical prohibition despite his exemption from <i>Malkot</i>.
It is not clear whether the Shulchan Aruch agrees with this last point. The Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 180:2) writes that one who permits a tattoo to be inscribed in his body is “<i>patur</i>” if he did not assist in the inscription. Rav Shneebalg asserts that although one who has a cosmetic tattoo inscribed on his face does not assist in the process (an anesthetic is administered), he nevertheless transgresses a biblical prohibition. It is possible, though, that the terminology of “<i>patur</i>” in the Shulchan Aruch (in contradistinction to the Rambam’s phrasing, “He is not flogged”) might imply that only a rabbinic prohibition is violated. Thus, it is possible that one who undergoes cosmetic tattooing might violate only a rabbinic prohibition.
It is important to add that Rav Shneebalg cites the <i>Yad Ketanah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> (253:4) adopts this approach as well.</i> who asserts, based on the Tosefta and Rambam, that one violates the <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> prohibition on a Torah level even if the individual who inscribes the tattoo is not Jewish. Thus, the problem of cosmetic tattooing is not mitigated by using a non-Jewish technician, since one is forbidden to have a tattoo inscribed in his body, regardless of who is performing the inscription.
<b>Must One Remove a Cosmetic Tattoo?</b>
As we have explained, the Torah prohibits both applying a tattoo and allowing a tattoo to be applied to one’s body. Nowadays, though, tattoo removal is readily possible and is a common procedure. Does the Torah also forbid maintaining a tattoo on one’s body when he can so easily remove it? It seems from the Rambam and Shulchan Aruch that no such prohibition exists. However, it is possible that these authorities did not address this issue because in their time it was impossible to remove a tattoo.
Dayan Weisz (<i>Teshuvot Minchat Yitzchak</i> 3:11) discusses a case in which someone had a disgraceful tattoo on his body (applied before he became observant) on the place where he now affixed his <i>tefillin</i>. Dayan Weisz advises him to remove the tattoo, but makes no mention of an obligation to remove the tattoo under the <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> prohibition. Furthermore, Rav Ephraim Oshry (<i>Teshuvot Mima’amakim</i> 4:22) strongly urges a Holocaust survivor not to remove the tattoos that the evil Nazis (<i>yemach shemam</i>) branded on her. He writes, “God forbid that this woman should remove this number, because by doing so, she aids those who wish to erase the evil actions perpetrated on the Jewish people….Just the opposite, it is incumbent upon us to bear this mark with pride.” Accordingly, there appears to be no obligation to remove a tattoo. Still, Rav Eliyahu Bakshi-Doron (<i>Techumin</i> 22:387) recommends removing the tattoo to avoid the constant reminder of an earlier sin. In certain circumstances, he even permits the removal of Hashem’s name that was (sinfully) tattooed on one’s body, even though it is ordinarily forbidden to erase Hashem’s name.
<i><b>Kevod Habriyot</b></i>
Part of the reason for our preoccupation with whether cosmetic tattooing constitutes a biblical or rabbinic prohibition is that under certain exceptional circumstances, Halachah tolerates the violation of a rabbinic prohibition. The Gemara (Berachot 19b) states, for example, that one may violate a rabbinic prohibition for the sake of kevod habriyot (preserving human dignity).<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Daniel Feldman discusses this issue in depth in his <i>The Right and the Good </i>pp. 189-206.</i>
Both Rav Basri and Rav Shneebalg are inclined to permit cosmetic tattooing in a case of <i>kevod habriyot</i>. For example, Rav Basri permits surgeons to tattoo eyebrows onto the forehead of a woman who has no eyebrows. Rav Shneebalg is inclined to permit cosmetic tattooing even in a more common situation – scar removal. In this procedure, a pigment that matches the color of human skin is injected beneath the scar, allowing that area to appear like the rest of the person’s skin. Although it seems to qualify as <i>kitovet ka’aka</i>, Rav Shneebalg permits it due to <i>kevod habriyot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Shneebalg adds another compelling reason to be lenient. The procedure is conducted so that the scar should appear no different from the rest of the skin. “This,” suggests Rav Shneebalg, “does not at all resemble <i>kitovet ka’aka</i>.”</i>
<b>Conclusion</b>
The consensus of contemporary <i>poskim</i> forbids permanent and semi-permanent makeup. Rav Wosner rules that cosmetic tattooing is forbidden, though it is not clear if he believes it to constitute a biblical or a rabbinic prohibition. In addition, it is not clear whether Rav Wosner is addressing only permanent makeup or even semi-permanent makeup. Much less ambiguous are the <i>teshuvot</i> of Rav Gestener and Rav Shneebalg, who unequivocally rule that even semi-permanent makeup is forbidden. Rav Shneebalg is inclined to define it as a rabbinic prohibition, whereas Rav Gestetner is inclined to consider it a full-fledged biblical prohibition.
Rav Baruch Shraga writes that he posed this question to many leading Israeli <i>poskim</i>, all of whom responded that even semi-permanent makeup is prohibited. These authorities included Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv, Rav Yisrael Yaakov Fischer, Rav Chaim Kanievsky, and Rav Ovadia Yosef. Rav Dovid Heber writes (p. 49 of the Star-K’s 2004 guide to Passover and Cosmetics) that he consulted many <i>poskim</i> about this matter, all of whom ruled strictly.
Even so, some <i>poskim</i> are inclined to permit cosmetic tattooing in cases of extraordinary need. Other than such circumstances, it is difficult, as the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> notes, to develop lenient approaches to this matter when the <i>Rishonim</i> and Shulchan Aruch all seem to allow no exceptions to this prohibition (as Rav Gestener underscores in his responsum).
<b>Two Final Thoughts</b>
Yet another problem with cosmetic tattooing, Rav Wosner writes, is that it violates the spirit of Halachah. Elsewhere (<i>Teshuvot Sheivet Halevi</i> 6:33), Rav Wosner frowns upon women putting on an excessive amount of makeup. Immoderate use of cosmetics is one of the reasons that the Gemara (Shabbat 62b) offers for the destruction of the <i>Beit Hamikdash</i>. The same applies, argues Rav Wosner, to cosmetic tattooing. As the Rambam teaches (<i>Hilchot Dei’ot</i> chapters 1-3), the Torah emphasizes moderation as a central value. While makeup for women can be appropriate if used in moderation – if a woman feels makeup is necessary for her in order to have a dignified appearance – inscribing makeup on one’s body is entirely out of proportion and immodest.
Additionally, the Torah concludes its prohibition of <i>kitovet ka’aka</i> with the addendum, “I am Hashem.” A reason for this might be that Hashem wishes for us to internalize the essential idea that our bodies do not belong to us; rather, they are on loan to us from Him as tools with which to perform His <i>mitzvot</i>. Thus, we are not permitted to do just anything with our bodies. Whereas moderate use of cosmetics is certainly acceptable, almost all <i>poskim</i> deem cosmetic tattooing to be inconsistent with technical Halachah as well as with fundamental Torah values.

Applying Cosmetics on Shabbat and Yom Tov



Chapter 1

<b>In the next two chapters, we shall discuss a most sensitive topic, that of the use of cosmetics on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>. Many women consider it a necessity to wear makeup on these days, since it is specifically then that they appear in public in formal dress. Unfortunately, there are myriad halachic challenges associated with applying makeup on these occasions. In this chapter, we shall outline some of the major issues involved in the use of makeup on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> as well as potential solutions that are approved by some <i>poskim</i>.</b>
Gemara, <i>Rishonim</i>, and Classic <i>Acharonim</i>
The Mishnah (Shabbat 10:6) records a dispute as to what level prohibition exists against applying blue color to the area around one’s eyes on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>. The <i>Chachamim</i> believe that it entails a rabbinic violation, while Rabi Eliezer believes that it is a biblical prohibition. The Gemara (Shabbat 95a) explains that Rabi Eliezer defines this act as <i>Tzovei’a</i> (coloring), one of the thirty-nine categories of creative activities that are forbidden on Shabbat.
The <i>Rishonim</i> appear to disagree about which opinion is accepted as normative. The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (O.C. 303 s.v. <i>V’lo L’kchol</i>) writes that the Rambam rules in accordance with the <i>Chachamim</i>. The <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> (32:15) explains that this may be inferred from <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 22:23, where the Rambam writes, “It is forbidden for a woman to put <i>serek</i> (paint) on her face [on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>] because it is like painting.” The Rambam’s use of the word “like” implies that he believes that this is only a rabbinic prohibition – otherwise, he would have written, “It is painting.” Moreover, all of the other acts that the Rambam lists in chapter twenty-two are violations of rabbinic law, not biblical law. Thus, the context of this Halachah in the Rambam also indicates that the Rambam categorizes applying <i>serek</i> on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> as a rabbinic prohibition.
The <i>Beit Yosef</i> remarks, though, that the <i>Semag</i> (<i>Lo Ta’aseh</i> 65) seems to rule in accordance with the opinion of Rabi Eliezer. The <i>Nishmat Adam</i> (<i>Hilchot Shabbat </i>24:1) notes that another <i>Rishon</i>, the <i>Sefer Yerei’im </i>(102) also agrees with the opinion of the <i>Semag</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Nishmat Adam</i> believes that even the <i>Semag</i> and the <i>Sefer Yerei’im</i> accept the view of the <i>Chachamim</i>. He reinterprets the Gemara to allow for this conclusion.</i> Nevertheless, the <i>Beit Yosef</i> rules in accordance with the <i>Chachamim</i>. This is hardly surprising, as the majority opinion, which the “<i>Chachamim</i>” in the Mishnah generally represent, is usually accepted as normative.
Among the major commentaries to the Shulchan Aruch, the Magen Avraham (303:19) rules in accordance with the Rambam, and the Vilna Gaon (Bei’ur Hagra 303:25) cites both the Rambam and the <i>Semag</i> (though he critiques the <i>Semag</i>). Later codifiers remain divided. The <i>Chayei Adam</i> (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 24:2) cites both views without deciding which is accepted as normative. On the other hand, the Mishnah Berurah (303:79) and the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (O.C. 303:30) rule unequivocally that it constitutes a rabbinic prohibition, in accordance with the position of the <i>Chachamim</i>, Rambam, <i>Beit Yosef</i>, and Magen Avraham.
<b>The Category of <i>Davar She’eino Mitkayeim</i></b>
Those who rule that applying <i>serek</i> constitutes only a rabbinic prohibition do not explain why this should be. The <i>Chayei Adam</i> elucidates that the Rambam (who follows the <i>Chachamim</i>) considers <i>serek</i> to be an action whose effects are <i>eino mitkayeim</i> (temporary), rendering it only a rabbinic prohibition. The <i>Minchat Chinuch </i>criticizes this explanation, claiming that <i>serek </i>should in fact be considered <i>mitkayeim</i> (permanent). In order to appreciate this dispute, we must briefly explore the concept of <i>eino mitkayeim</i>.
In the context of the rules concerning <i>boneh</i> (building) on Shabbat, the Mishnah (Shabbat 12:1) states a broad rule: “This is the rule – one who performs a creative act (<i>melachah</i>) that has a permanent effect (<i>mitkayeim</i>) has violated a biblical prohibition.” A <i>melachah</i> that has only a temporary effect, in contrast, entails only a rabbinic prohibition, and a <i>melachah</i> that is <i>eino mitkayeim klal</i> (fleeting) is actually permissible in certain limited situations.
Although the rule is clear, its application is not, as it is difficult to define precisely the concept and parameters of <i>davar hamitkayeim</i>. The <i>Tiferet Yisrael</i> (<i>Kalkelet Hashabbat</i>, <i>Haboneh</i>) quotes from the Pri Megadim (<i>Eishel Avraham</i> 315:1) that a building that lasts eight or nine days is defined as <i>kavu’a</i> (lasting)<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kavu’a</i> and <i>mitkayeim</i> seem to be synonymous.</i> regarding the laws of building on Shabbat. The Sha’ar Hatziyun (303:68) infers from the Rambam (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 9:13) that a <i>melachah</i> is defined as <i>mitkayeim</i> if it lasts through the end of Shabbat, but he adds that Rashi (Shabbat 102b s.v. <i>B’shabbat</i>) believes that <i>mitkayeim</i> means lasting forever.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah Berurah (introduction to O.C. 317), in the context of tying a knot on Shabbat, explains this to mean that one does not set a time to undo the knot and the knot is capable of lasting permanently. </i> In the context of the <i>melachah</i> of <i>kosheir</i> (tying a knot), the Rama (O.C. 317:1) cites two opinions regarding when a knot is considered “<i>shel kayama</i>” (lasting). One believes that it must last just one day to be defined as “permanent,” while the other holds that it must last seven days.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">We should clarify that this debate is about whether a rabbinic prohibition is violated. A biblical prohibition is violated only if the knot is meant to last indefinitely (as defined in the preceding footnote).</i>
To complicate matters further, it is clear that certain acts are considered <i>mitkayeim</i> even though their effects are fleeting. The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 12:2) writes that it is biblically prohibited to heat metal on Shabbat until it glows, even though the metal will cool down relatively quickly after it is heated. It is regarded as <i>mitkayeim</i> because one has accomplished his goal thereby.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">It therefore constitutes <i>melechet machashevet</i> (creative, calculated labor; see <i>The 39 Melochos</i> p. 177-178 for more explanation), which is a prerequisite for violating the biblical prohibition of <i>melachah</i>. See the Rashba, Shabbat 115b s.v. <i>Ha D’amrinan</i>, who asserts that this is the reason why a <i>davar she’eino mitkayeim</i> is not biblically forbidden.</i> Rav Dovid Ribiat (<i>The 39 Melochos</i> 1:134 in the Hebrew section) explains that the concept of <i>mitkayeim</i> varies from <i>melachah</i> to <i>melachah</i> as well as from act to act.
