Since metal is usually heated to a glow only for a brief period, it is regarded as <i>mitkayeim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">For further discussion of this question, see Rav Mordechai Eliyahu’s essay in <i>Techumin</i> (11:107-112) concerning doctors writing on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> with ink that lasts only until the end of the day.</i>
Accordingly, we can appreciate the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i>’s criticism of the <i>Chayei Adam</i>’s assertion that putting <i>serek</i> on one’s face is considered <i>eino mitkayeim</i>. The <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> argues that since the applier has accomplished her goal, her act should be considered <i>mitkayeim</i>. Moreover, the <i>Ketzot Hashulchan </i>(146, <i>Badei Hashulchan</i> 20) notes that the Rambam (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 22:23) does not classify the <i>serek</i> on a woman’s face as <i>eino mitkayeim</i>, whereas he does describe (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 9:13) <i>serek</i> placed on iron as such. We must therefore look to other<i> Acharonim</i> for further explanation of this Rambam.
<b>Alternative Explanations for the Rambam</b>
The Mishnah Berurah (303:79) explains that applying <i>serek</i> is only a rabbinic prohibition because the biblical <i>melachah</i> of <i>tzovei’a</i> does not apply to coloring human skin. This is a somewhat novel assertion, as it is unclear when <i>melachot</i> do not apply to acts performed on the human body - there is no general rule that they do not. For example, the <i>melachot</i> of <i>boneh</i> (see Shabbat 95a and Ketubot 6b) and <i>koteiv</i> (writing; see Rambam <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 11:16) undoubtedly do apply to the human body. Rav Ribiat (<i>The 39 Melochos</i> p. 820) records a dispute among twentieth-century <i>poskim</i> as to whether the <i>melachah</i> of <i>tofeir</i> (sewing) applies to human skin.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This has serious implications regarding the question of physicians sewing stitches on Shabbat.</i> Thus, it is not self-evident that the <i>melachah</i> of <i>tzovei’a</i> does not apply to the human body, nor does the Mishnah Berurah cite a source for this assertion, leaving his underlying reasoning indeterminate.
The <i>Ketzot Hashulchan</i> (146, <i>Badei Hashulchan </i>20) suggests that since women normally apply makeup with the intention to remove it within a few hours (such as before they go to sleep), applying makeup is considered <i>tzovei’a al menat limchok</i> (coloring with the intention to erase). Thus, a woman’s face is not a surface that is normally painted in a manner regarded as <i>mitkayeim</i>, which is why the biblical prohibition of <i>tzovei’a </i>does not apply to human skin and is considered <i>eino mitkayeim</i>.
Accordingly, although the consensus opinion is that applying <i>serek</i> constitutes only a rabbinic prohibition, the basis for this approach remains unclear. In addition, significant <i>Rishonim</i> and <i>Acharonim</i> consider or rule in accordance with the view that applying <i>serek</i> constitutes a biblical prohibition.
<b>Twentieth-Century <i>Poskim</i> – the Strict View</b>
A straightforward application of the sources outlined seems to yield no room for leniency regarding the application of cosmetics on Shabbat – the only debate is whether applying makeup on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> constitutes a biblical or rabbinic prohibition. Accordingly, it is hardly surprising that many contemporary <i>poskim</i> oppose applying any colored makeup on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>. These authorities include Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach (<i>Shemirat Shabbat K’hilchatah</i> 14:59 footnote 158 and <i>Tikkunim Umili’um ibid</i>.), Rav Shmuel Wosner (<i>Teshuvot Sheivet Halevi</i> 6:33), Rav Gedalia Felder (<i>Yesodei Yeshurun</i> 4:72-73), and Rav Shimon Schwab (reported by Rav Dovid Heber). It is important to clarify that these authorities forbid even the use of what is called “<i>Shabbos</i> makeup,” special cosmetics that are produced for Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> use and are approved by some <i>poskim</i>.
Equally unsurprising, the authors of halachic works geared to popular audiences advocate (with slight variations) the strict approach to this issue. These works include Rav Yehoshua Neuwirth’s <i>Shemirat Shabbat K’hilchatah</i> (14:58-59), Dayan Yechezkel Posen’s <i>Kitzur Hilchot Shabbat</i> (21:4), Rav Dovid Ribiat’s <i>The 39 Melochos </i>(3:743), and Rav Doniel Neustadt’s <i>The Monthly Halachah Discussion</i> (p. 276). Rav J. David Bleich (<i>Contemporary Halakhic Problems IV</i>:113-119) also clearly indicates his preference for the strict approach to this issue. It seems, then, that the consensus among most <i>poskim</i> is to forbid virtually all forms of colored makeup on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>.
<b>The Motivations for the Lenient Approach</b>
Nevertheless, many major <i>poskim</i> present a lenient approach to permit women (in certain circumstances) to apply some cosmetics on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>. These authorities include Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe </i>O.C. 1:114 and 5:27), Rav Avraham Chaim Na’eh (<i>Ketzot Hashulchan </i>ad. loc.), and Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer </i>6 O.C. 38 and <i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at </i>4:28). Rav Yosef Adler reports that Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik also agreed with the lenient approach to this issue.
Rav Ovadia Yosef (in <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i>) explicitly states a motivation for adopting a lenient stance on this issue. He registers concern that domestic tranquility might be disturbed if women are forbidden to wear makeup on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>. The source for this idea is the Gemara (Shabbat 64b) that records that Rabi Akiva permitted wives to wear makeup even when they were <i>niddot</i> in order to promote <i>shelom bayit </i>(domestic tranquility) between husband and wife.
We may add that among the ten <i>takanot</i> (decrees) enacted by the biblical Ezra, as recounted in the Gemara (<i>Bava Kama </i>82a), was that towns could not restrict travelling salesmen from supplying perfume and fragrances to the women of each community. This demonstrates the sensitivity the Torah and <i>Chazal</i> display toward the needs of women and families regarding these matters.
In <i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i>, Rav Ovadia refers to the oft-quoted statement of the Gemara (Pesachim 66a) about an area of uncertainty with respect to <i>korban pesach</i>: “Leave it to the Jewish people; if they are not prophets then they are the children of prophets.” Rashi (ad. loc. s.v. <i>B’nei</i>) elaborates, “And [therefore] observe what they (the Jewish people) do,” which will resolve the uncertainty.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This principle applies only to an area of uncertainty in Halachah and to the practices of those who carefully observe Halachah.</i> Rav Ovadia applies this principle to our situation, noting that many women who are meticulously observant rely on the lenient approaches of Rav Moshe and Rav Na’eh.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Before we begin our presentation of the basis for the lenient view, we should note that there are some cosmetics that are specially formulated to last throughout Shabbat. Some women who follow the strict view apply these cosmetics before Shabbat. Wearing this type of makeup for all of Shabbat does not violate the prohibition of mar’it ayin (appearing to have sinned), since, as Rav Yonatan Eybeshitz (Kereiti Upeleiti), the Mishnah Berurah (467:33), and the Aruch Hashulchan (Y.D. 298:4) record, mar’it ayin does not apply when permissible ways to perform the action in question are known. For example, it is not forbidden to drink red wine, even though it appears like drinking blood, since it is known that people drink red wine. Similarly, a woman need not be concerned that people will think she applied her makeup on Shabbat, since people know that makeup that lasts throughout Shabbat even if it is applied beforehand is available.</i>
<b>The Lenient Approaches of Rav Moshe, Rav Na’eh and Rav Ovadia</b>
The point of departure for the lenient view is that the prohibition to apply <i>serek </i>is only rabbinic in nature. Once it is proven that the prohibition to apply <i>serek</i> is only rabbinic in nature, there is more room to be lenient about applying cosmetics than there would be if it were classified as a biblical prohibition.
To this end, the <i>Ketzot Hashulchan</i> distinguishes between the <i>serek</i> cosmetic that the Gemara, <i>Rishonim</i>, and classic <i>Acharonim</i> discuss and the blush used today that does not adhere to the skin for a significant period of time. He argues that <i>Chazal</i> prohibit only <i>serek</i>, which adheres to the skin. However, blush that is applied directly to the skin without a cosmetic base (“foundation”) does not adhere to the skin, and thus is not included in the rabbinic prohibition to apply <i>serek</i> to one’s face. Rav Ovadia clarifies, though, that this leniency applies only to powders that are not oil-based and contain no creams.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Dovid Heber informs me that the fact that the word “oil” does not appear on the label of a cosmetic is not sufficient evidence that it does not in fact contain oil, as one of the other ingredients may be oil-based.</i>
Rav Moshe takes a similar tack to the <i>Ketzot Hashulchan</i>’s, writing in his first (and exceptionally brief and cryptic) responsum on this topic (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> O.C. 1:114), “White<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In the later <i>teshuvah</i>, he clarifies that this applies to colored powder as well. </i> powder that does not last at all (“<i>eino mitkayeim klal</i>”) is not included in the prohibition of <i>tzovei’a</i>.”
We must elucidate the concept of <i>eino mitkayeim klal </i>in order to understand Rav Moshe’s ruling. We mentioned earlier that in very limited circumstances, a <i>melachah</i> that lasts for an exceptionally brief period of time is permitted. The fact that one is permitted (see Shulchan Aruch O.C. 317:1) to tie his shoes on Shabbat (if he ties and unties them daily) is a classic application of this principle. Another example is the lenient ruling of many <i>poskim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shemirat Shabbat K’hilchatah</i> 15 footnote 250; <i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at </i>6:24; and Rav Moshe Feinstein, cited in <i>The 39 Melochos </i>1:137 in the Hebrew section.</i> that fastening the adhesive tabs on a child’s diaper on Shabbat is permitted, even though fastening two objects to each other is forbidden under the rubric of <i>tofeir</i> (sewing; see Shulchan Aruch O.C. 340:14).
We can explain Rav Moshe’s leniency regarding makeup in a similar manner. Whereas applying <i>serek</i> is a rabbinic prohibition because <i>Chazal</i> classify it as <i>eino mitkayeim</i>, cosmetic powder that does not last long is not forbidden because it is <i>eino mitkayeim klal</i>.
Even so, applying those cosmetics that are specially formulated to be long-lasting might constitute a biblical prohibition on Shabbat. These cosmetics, which are often designed to be applied before Shabbat and to last throughout Shabbat, are sometimes referred to as “<i>Shabbos </i>makeup.” Rav Dovid Heber points out that this is a serious misnomer – they should more properly be labeled “<i><b>erev </b>Shabbos </i>makeup.” A woman should be especially careful not to apply such makeup on Shabbat, as it seems to qualify as <i>mitkayeim</i> according to the Rambam’s definition (presented above), in which case it constitutes a biblical prohibition.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Two types of “Shabbos makeup” are marketed today. One type is intended to be applied on Shabbat itself in accordance with the lenient approach of Rav Moshe, Rav Na’eh, and Rav Ovadia. The other type is designed to last for a longer period of time and is applied before Shabbat.</i>
Rav Shlomo Zalman, however, rejects the lenient approach, stating that there is no source to permit <i>tzovei’a</i> even if it is <i>eino mitkayeim klal</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Shlomo Zalman was an extraordinary expert regarding <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i>, so his assertion that no source exists is exceptionally authoritative. </i> Moreover, he writes that one should be especially cautious about this issue since, according to a number of <i>Rishonim</i> (as we discussed above), the application of makeup is always biblically prohibited. In fact, Rav Gedalia Felder (<i>Yesodei Yeshurun </i>4:72) points out that the Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 303:25) forbids a woman to spread dough on her face to give it a red appearance, which seems to indicate that Halachah forbids coloring the face even in a manner that is fleeting.
<b>Practical Applications</b>
Rav Moshe (in his later responsum <i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe </i>O.C. 5:27; see also the publication of Rav Moshe’s Mesivta Tifereth Jerusalem, <i>L’Torah V’hora’ah</i>, 7:28) clarifies that after extensive testing, he discovered that many of the commercially available cosmetic powders are analogous to <i>serek</i>, since they last for a significant amount of time. These powders are therefore subject to the prohibition of <i>tzovei’a</i>. However, he notes that some powders do not last very long and would be permissible, concluding, “Without experience regarding this matter, it is difficult to issue a decision about it.”
Rav Dovid Heber reports that there are still <i>poskim </i>who participated in Rav Moshe’s thorough investigation and testing of cosmetic powders. He therefore instructs<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Heber’s essay on this topic is available at www.star-k.org.</i> that to rely on Rav Moshe’s leniency, one must use only powders that have been tested by a <i>poseik</i> who has specific and proper training and who has determined that the powders in question are “temporary” enough to conform to Rav Moshe’s standards.
Undoubtedly, Rav Moshe’s standards are difficult to quantify objectively. Indeed, Rav Bleich and Rav Neustadt argue that it is nearly impossible to implement Rav Moshe’s lenient ruling in practice because of this lack of objective standards. On the other hand, there are other areas of Halachah that are difficult to quantify, yet we rely on the judgment of <i>poskim </i>who are properly trained and experienced in these matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For example, judging colors in the context of <i>hilchot niddah</i>; see Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 188 and <i>Badei Hashulchan</i> 188:6. <i>Poskim</i> do not use specific color charts as standards for determining colors in this context. The ability to make such determinations is passed from teacher to student.</i>
<b>Conclusion</b>
Many cosmetics are forbidden on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> according to all opinions. No consensus has been reached, though, regarding the permissibility of the use of temporary makeup on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>. Undoubtedly, it is best to avoid applying any makeup on these days. However, some <i>poskim</i> believe that if a woman feels that it is essential for her to apply makeup on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>, she has the right to follow the lenient opinion if she strictly adheres to Rav Moshe’s guidelines. This is especially true in light of the fact that the Rambam, <i>Beit Yosef</i>, Magen Avraham, Mishnah Berurah, and <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> all agree that the prohibition of applying makeup is only rabbinic in nature. Even those who wish to follow the lenient <i>poskim</i>, however, must keep in mind that they do not permit applying makeup indiscriminately, as we shall see in our next chapter.

Chapter 2

In the last chapter, we reviewed the strict and lenient approaches articulated by the twentieth-century <i>poskim</i> regarding the application of makeup on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>. We shall now conclude with a discussion of some specific issues and challenges regarding the application of makeup on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> even according to the lenient opinion.
<b>Lipstick and Lip Gloss</b>
Rav Moshe writes (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe </i>O.C. 1:114) that lipstick can not be applied on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> because doing so violates <i>tzovei’a</i> and <i>memacheik </i>(smoothing), the latter of which occurs when the surface of the lipstick bar is rubbed.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This is similar to the practice not to use bar soap on Shabbat; see Mishnah Berurah 326:30. See, though, <i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> (2:50).</i> However, Rav Dovid Heber reports that some commercially available lipstick powders<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The use of powder rather than a bar circumvents the issue of <i>memacheik</i>.</i> made for Shabbat use are not long-lasting and meet Rav Moshe’s criteria of <i>eino mitkayeim klal</i>, making them permissible for use on Shabbat. Some <i>poskim</i> object to the use of these lip powders anyway, claiming that the powder mixes with saliva and becomes long-lasting. Rav Heber relates, though, that he consulted with three cosmetic chemists, all of whom agreed that the saliva does not make the lip powder long-lasting.
Rav Moshe forbids even liquid lipstick on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> because of <i>tzovei’a</i>, which Rav Bleich explains to refer to lip gloss. This appears difficult, as untinted, clear lip gloss merely produces a shine but does not color the lips. The answer lies in the Mishnah Berurah (327:12, as explained by Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach, <i>Shemirat Shabbat K’hilchatah</i> 14: footnote 152), who writes that he believes there is concern for <i>tzovei’a </i>even if one creates only a shine. Thus, both Rav Moshe and Rav Shlomo Zalman rule that clear lip gloss is forbidden to be used on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov </i>(unless it is <i>eino mitkayeim klal</i>, in which case Rav Moshe would permit its use).
Interestingly, Rav Heber relates that he heard that when Rav Moshe was shown clear lip gloss, he responded that it is forbidden because it creates a shine, but remarked that some authorities are lenient regarding this issue. Indeed, the Mishnah Berurah does not cite a source for his belief that creating a shine constitutes <i>tzovei’a</i>, stating only that there is a concern for <i>tzovei’a</i>, which may indicate that he was not thoroughly convinced of his assertion. Nonetheless, I have not discovered any prominent <i>poskim </i>who disagree with the strict rulings of Rav Moshe and Rav Shlomo Zalman.
<b>Loose Powder</b>
Rav Moshe writes (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe </i>O.C. 5:27) that any make-up powder must be prepared before Shabbat. Though he does not present a reason for this requirement, Rav Heber reports that Rav Moshe explained to his students that there is a problem of <i>tochein</i> (grinding)<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tochein</i> is the <i>av melachah</i> forbidding the breaking of certain items into very small pieces.</i> if one removes the powder from the cake on Shabbat.
Rav Moshe’s ruling is somewhat debatable, given that the powder was ground before it was formed into a cake during its manufacture. Thus, it would seem to fall under the rule of <i>ein tochein achar tochein</i>, which dictates that one may perform <i>tochein</i> a second time on an item that was previously ground. For instance, the Rama (O.C. 321:12) specifically permits crumbling bread into crumbs to feed one’s animals on Shabbat, since the bread was ground during its production. The same rule should apply, at first glance, to makeup powder, which would allow the consumer to “grind” the previously ground makeup powder.
Nevertheless, the principle of <i>ein tochein achar tochein</i> does not appear in the Gemara, nor is it self-evident. The Ran (32a in the pages of the Rif s.v. <i>Amar Rav Papa</i>) and the <i>Sefer Yerei’im </i>(274) present this idea, which is supported by the Tosefta (Shabbat 13:12). However, the <i>Chayei Adam</i> (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 17:4) notes that a minority view does not subscribe to this principle,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Rishonim</i> cited in the <i>Encyclopedia Talmudit</i> (19:179 footnote 334), who seem not to subscribe to this notion.</i> and the <i>Chayei Adam </i>himself urges one to avoid relying on this leniency.
Thus, since the principle of <i>ein tochein achar tochein</i> is somewhat novel and is subject to debate, several <i>Acharonim</i> seek to limit its application. In fact, the <i>Ketzot Hashulchan</i> (129:16) cites <i>Acharonim</i> who argue that the principle of <i>ein tochein achar tochein</i> applies only to food items. Since the Rama’s ruling is presented in the context of food – crumbling bread – perhaps we should not extend it beyond the Rama’s ruling (<i>ein lecha bo ela chiddusho</i>). Indeed, Rav Heber reports, Rav Moshe explained to his students that he ruled strictly regarding makeup because he believes that <i>ein tochein achar tochein</i> applies only to food items.
<b>Moisturizers and <i>Memarei’ach</i></b>
Rav Moshe (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> O.C. 5:27) cautions women to avoid violating the <i>melachah</i> of <i>memacheik </i>when applying makeup. A prime example of this is the application of moisturizer on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> – as Rav Heber observes, almost all varieties of moisturizers are in a cream form, making their application fall under the “<i>memarei’ach</i>” subcategory (<i>toladah</i>) of <i>memacheik</i>.
<i>Memarei’ach</i> refers to “smoothing soft, pliable substances that may be pressed or molded to a shape” (Rav Dovid Ribiat, <i>The 39 Melochos</i>, 3:913). A prime example of this is the Mishnah (Shabbat 22:3) that forbids spreading wax to seal a hole in a barrel. The Gemara (Shabbat 146b) records a dispute between Rav and Shmuel whether this prohibition applies also to spreading oil to seal such a hole. Rav asserts that it is a rabbinic prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Memarei’ach</i> on a biblical level applies only to pliable solids. Oil does not fall into this category and as such is prohibited only on a rabbinic level.</i> to spread oil lest one come to spread wax, whereas Shmuel permits this activity, as he does not see the necessity for creating such a <i>gezeirah </i>(rabbinic enactment). The Halachah follows Rav (Rambam <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 23:11 and Shulchan Aruch O.C. 314:11), as is the usual protocol in the context of ritual matters (<i>issurei</i>). Rav Ribiat (<i>The 39 Melochos </i>3:919) writes that rouge creams, eye-shadow creams, petroleum jelly, and hand creams such as Nivea and Desitin are included in this rabbinic prohibition.
However, even Rav appears to concede that this rabbinic prohibition does not apply to all substances. Rashi (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Mishcha</i>) specifies that it applies only to thick oil, and the Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 314:11, as emphasized by the Mishnah Berurah 314:46) rules in accordance with Rashi. The Mishnah Berurah explains that since thick oil may be spread somewhat, it is similar enough to wax to necessitate the <i>gezeirah</i>, while thin oil does not resemble wax at all and therefore is permitted.
<b>Defining Memarei’ach</b>
The question, then, is how to determine precisely which items are considered “thick oils” to be included in this rabbinic prohibition. A classic illustration of this problem is the question of using liquid soap on Shabbat. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (O.C. 326:11) and the <i>Ketzot Hashulchan</i> (146:32) permit the use of liquid soap on Shabbat, contending that liquid soap is not comparable to thick oil and thus is not subject to the prohibition of <i>memarei’ach</i>. Dayan Posen (<i>Kitzur Hilchot Shabbat</i> p. 74) notes that the common practice is to follow this lenient ruling. The <i>Shemirat Shabbat K’hilchatah </i>(14:16) also rules in accordance with this opinion.
Based on this ruling, Dayan Posen (ad. loc.; p. 147, 32:19) sets a standard for what items are included in the rabbinic prohibition of <i>memarei’ach</i>: Anything that is thick to the extent that it cannot pour, does not flow by itself, and needs to be smoothed out is similar to <i>memarei’ach </i>and is forbidden. Liquid soap is permissible since it pours, flows by itself, and does not need to be smoothed out.
Dayan Posen adds that since this is only a rabbinic prohibition for which the classic <i>poskim</i> present no objective standard, one has the right to adopt a limited view of its scope. Rav Ribiat (<i>The 39 Melochos </i>3:920) essentially adopts this approach as normative.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">He discusses this issue at length in a Hebrew footnote (3:682-685). </i> Based on this standard, Rav Ribiat permits the use of baby oils, lubricating jellies, and olive oil on Shabbat.
On the other hand, Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> O.C. 1:113) expresses serious reservations regarding the lenient practice of many to use liquid soap on Shabbat. He is concerned that even liquid soap can be spread and thus should be included in the rabbinic prohibition of <i>memarei’ach</i>. Rav Ribiat notes (ad. loc. 3:680 in a Hebrew footnote) that there are those who accommodate Rav Moshe’s strict view by watering down their liquid soap before Shabbat.
Rav Heber reports that Rav Moshe Heinemann adopts a compromise position between Rav Moshe and Dayan Posen. Rav Heber reports that Rav Heinemann tested the viscosity<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Viscosity refers to the property of resistance to flow in a fluid, measured in centipoises (cP).</i> of various liquid soaps. He concluded that only oils with a viscosity of 600 cP or less are not included in the rabbinic prohibition. Thus, he forbids the use of Softsoap Liquid Hand Soap on Shabbat, since its viscosity is higher than 600 cP. On the other hand, he permits Ultra Dawn Concentrated Dish Liquid/Anti-Bacterial Hand Soap, because its viscosity is 600 cP. He notes, though, that this measurement applies only when the room temperature is 70 degrees Fahrenheit; the lower the temperature, the higher the viscosity.
In summary, three standards exist regarding the definition of <i>memarei’ach</i> on Shabbat: Rav Moshe’s, Dayan Posen’s, and Rav Heinemann’s. One should consult his Rav for a ruling regarding which opinion to follow. This dispute impacts the permissibility of using moisturizers, as a specially prepared, watered-down moisturizer might be permissible to use on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>. The unresolved question is how much it must be watered down to become permissible for Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> use.
Finally, we should note that one must be careful when removing makeup to ensure that no <i>melachah</i> is performed in the process. Examples of possible violations include <i>memarei’ach</i> if a thick makeup remover is used and <i>sechitah</i> (squeezing) if a wet cotton ball or Q-tip is used.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>The Daily Halachah Discussion </i>(pp. 241-2) for a discussion whether <i>sechitah </i>is violated when using cotton balls and Q-tips.</i>
<b>Conclusion</b>
Although many cosmetics are forbidden to be used on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> according to all <i>poskim</i>, no consensus has emerged regarding the permissibility of temporary makeup. Even according to the lenient opinion, only products that a competent and trained <i>poseik </i>has permitted for use on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> may be used. It is also strongly recommended that a woman who wants to follow the lenient approach consult Rav Heber’s essay (available at www.star-k.org) for a lengthy description of how to avoid the numerous pitfalls involved in applying and removing makeup on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>.

Cosmetics and Toiletries for Pesach

There is much debate as to whether cosmetics and toiletries must be <i>chametz</i>-free in order to be used on <i>Pesach</i>. In this chapter, we hope to clarify the points of contention and outline the halachic basis for both the lenient and strict approaches to this issue.
<b>Talmudic Background – <i>Eino Ra’ui L’achilat Kelev</i> and <i>Achsheveih</i></b>
The Gemara (Pesachim 21b) states that if <i>chametz</i> is burned before <i>erev Pesach</i>, one is permitted to benefit from it on <i>Pesach</i>. <i>Tosafot</i> (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Chorcho</i>) clarify that this rule applies only if the bread was so thoroughly burned that it is no longer fit even for a dog to eat.
A similar rule applies to all forbidden foods – as the Gemara (Avodah Zarah 67b-68a) states, the Torah forbids only food that is fit for human consumption. Regarding <i>Pesach</i>, however, we are stricter, requiring that the <i>chametz</i> be unworthy even for canine consumption.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This is consistent with Halachah’s unusually strict overall approach to <i>chametz</i> on <i>Pesach</i>, which manifests itself in such laws as the prohibition to own <i>chametz</i> and the impossibility of nullifying it in a mixture. </i> The Ran (13b in the pages of the Rif s.v. <i>Tanu Rabbanan</i>) and the Magen Avraham (442:14) explain that <i>chametz</i> that is fit for canine consumption has the potential to ferment bread. Hence, it is similar to sourdough, which the Torah (Shemot 12:19) specifically forbids for <i>Pesach</i> consumption.
Although the Gemara makes clear that one may benefit from <i>chametz</i> that is no longer <i>ra’ui l’achilat kelev</i>, the <i>Rishonim</i> debate the permissibility of actually eating it. The Rosh (Pesachim 2:1) cites opinions that one may even eat such <i>chametz</i>. He himself rejects this view, however, and rules that one may only benefit from such <i>chametz</i> (as explicitly stated in the Gemara); by eating it, one would “elevate” the <i>chametz</i> (<i>achsheveih</i>) from a non-food item to a food item. The basis for this is the Gemara’s (Shevuot 24b) ruling that if one swears not to eat an inedible item and subsequently eats it, he has then violated his oath because he has “elevated” that item to the status of food by consuming it. The Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 442:9; see Mishnah Berurah 442:43) rules in accordance with the Rosh.
Although normative Halachah does accept that <i>achsheveih</i> creates a food status, the principle has some limitations. The Taz (O.C. 442:8) clarifies that <i>achsheveih</i> creates only a rabbinic prohibition, with which the Mishnah Berurah (442:43) and <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (O.C. 442:30) agree.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Bach</i> (O.C. 442 s.v. <i>V’yeish Omrim Shemutar</i>), though, seems to disagree with the Taz.</i> Furthermore, the Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 442:10) permits writing on <i>chol hamo’eid Pesach</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is assuming that the situation is one in which writing itself is permissible on <i>chol hamo’eid</i> (see Shulchan Aruch O.C. 545).</i> with ink that was cooked in barley beer in the course of its preparation. The Mishnah Berurah (442:45, citing the Magen Avraham 442:15 in the name of the <i>Terumat Hadeshen </i>129) adds that this is permitted even though a scribe might absentmindedly put his quill in his mouth (as was done in the pre-modern age), because <i>achsheveih</i> applies only if one intentionally eats the item.
<b>Is Denatured Alcohol Considered <i>Eino Ra’ui L’achilat Kelev</i>?</b>
It is often difficult to determine what is considered <i>nifsal mei’achilat kelev</i>. In fact, Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach (<i>Teshuvot Minchat Shlomo </i>1:17) suggests that just because we modern eaters are finicky (<i>mefunakim</i>) and regard certain items as disgusting does not necessarily give us the right to classify these items as unsuitable for human consumption.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Shlomo Zalman is discussing the classification of inedible foods for non-<i>Pesach</i> use, for which the determining factor is whether the food is fit for human consumption. The same question, though, should apply to <i>eino ra’ui l’achilat kelev</i> foods for <i>Pesach</i> use. Just because we think, based on common sense, that a dog would not eat a certain food does not mean that it truly falls into the category of <i>eino ra’ui l’achilat kelev</i>.</i> For example, the Rambam (<i>Hilchot Tum’at Ochlin</i> 10:2) writes that human ear and nose excretions, as well as human urine, are considered suitable for human consumption. Rav Shlomo Zalman is unsure whether such definitions of <i>ra’ui l’achilah</i> are subject to change depending on the standards of each generation.
One example of the challenge of defining <i>eino ra’ui l’achilat kelev</i> is the dispute between modern <i>poskim</i> about denatured alcohol. The alcohol in cosmetics and toiletries is not pure alcohol – it is significantly modified in ways that render it unsuitable for drinking as a beverage. This is done so that the companies that use such alcohol in their products do not have to pay a liquor tax.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Dovid Heber reports that the United States Bureau of Alcohol, Tobacco, Firearms and Explosives is extraordinarily strict (even stricter than the Internal Revenue Service) in monitoring the sale of alcohol. Thus, in order to avoid paying liquor taxes, companies strictly ensure that the alcohol they use for such products is denatured to make it unsuitable for drinking as a beverage.</i> However, certain chemicals can be added to reconstitute the alcohol and render it drinkable. Does Halachah assess the denatured alcohol in cosmetics and toiletries according to its present state or according to its potential state of restored potability?
Rav Tzvi Pesach Frank (<i>Mikra’ei Kodesh</i> 54) argues that it is forbidden to derive benefit from denatured alcohol on <i>Pesach</i>. He points out that the <i>Chavat Da’at</i> (Y.D. <i>Bei’urim</i> 103:1) distinguishes between a case in which a prohibited item itself is <i>nifsal</i>, which is permissible to benefit from, and a case in which the item is <i>nifsal</i> only due to being mixed with other ingredients, which, if it can be restored, is prohibited even while it remains unrestored. Since denatured alcohol falls into the latter category, it should be prohibited. Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> O.C. 3:62) also prohibits benefiting from denatured alcohol, arguing that it is considered suitable for consumption. Rav Moshe explains that “There are those [indigent alcoholics] who drink this with only slight additions and modifications.” The <i>Teshuvot Minchat Elazar </i>(5:37), on the other hand, rules that pure denatured alcohol technically is not considered suitable for consumption, though he notes that common practice is to be strict about this matter. The <i>Minchat Elazar</i> believes that the aberrant behavior of marginal members of society cannot determine the standards of <i>ra’ui l’achilat kelev</i> for the entire community. In general (see, for example, Shulchan Aruch O.C. 168:6 and Y.D. 198:1), Halachah would say of such people, “<i>Batlah da’atan eitzel kol haberiyot</i>” (their opinion is disregarded in the face of everyone else’s). Rav Yosef Adler told me that Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik agreed with this approach.
Rav Moshe takes it as a given that the potential for the “slight modifications” that make denatured alcohol potable are halachically significant. As Rav Frank notes, many of the great early-twentieth-century <i>poskim </i>doubted whether this potential renders denatured alcohol potable for mainstream society. While Rav Eliyahu Klatzkin (<i>Devar Eliyahu</i> 5) rules stringently, the <i>Teshuvot Atzei Halevanon </i>(17) is lenient and disregards the possibility of chemical restoration. The <i>Teshuvot Minchat Elazar</i> buttresses this lenient view with the observation of <i>Tosafot</i> (Pesachim 46b second s.v. <i>Ho’il</i>) that the Mishnah (Pesachim 2:2) allows benefiting from <i>chametz</i> that a non-Jew owned during <i>Pesach</i>, even though a Jew could easily have purchased the <i>chametz</i> from the non-Jew. Apparently, the feasibility of changing an item’s status is halachically irrelevant. The <i>Minchat Elazar </i>therefore permits one who fueled his car with gasoline that had alcohol mixed in to drive the car on <i>chol hamo’eid Pesach</i>, even though the alcohol can potentially be restored to a drinkable form by a chemical process.
<b>Proofs to Each Approach</b>
One proof to the strict approach, offered by Rav Gedalia Felder (<i>Yesodei Yeshurun </i>6 p. 227), is the Gemara (Eruvin 28b) that deems bitter almonds edible because they can be rendered edible by roasting. Apparently, any item that can be rendered edible by a comparably simple process is considered edible even before this process occurs. Raw spaghetti, which can be rendered edible simply by being boiled, is an apt modern analogue to the Gemara’s bitter almonds. Since, Rav Moshe claims, denatured alcohol can be restored to an unquestionably potable state with little effort, it should have the same status as the almonds and spaghetti.
On the other hand, Rav Chaim Ozer Grodzinsky (<i>Teshuvot Achiezer</i> 3:31)<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Chaim Ozer is addressing the permissibility of a medicine that contains an inedible non-kosher ingredient. </i> contends (based on the <i>Chavat Da’at </i>Y.D. <i>Bei’urim</i> 103:1) that we consider the potential of an inedible item to be reconstituted only if this is what normally occurs to such items (<i>omeid l’kach)</i>. Raw spaghetti is thus considered edible, since it is normally cooked. Similarly, the Torah forbids using sourdough on <i>Pesach</i> even though it is inedible because it normally facilitates baking bread. Denatured alcohol, on the other hand, is not generally modified for consumption purposes, so it is not analogous to bitter almonds and is not considered edible.
Rav Felder also suggests a proof to the lenient view. Although we are forbidden even to benefit from <i>chametz</i>, the Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 442:9, and see Mishnah Berurah 442:42) permits using <i>chametz</i> as a seat if the <i>chametz</i> has been smeared with clay. The Mishnah Berurah explains that the clay removes the <i>chametz</i> from the category of food. The Taz (O.C. 442:8) seems to imply that one even may benefit from this <i>chametz</i>, a position that the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (O.C. 442:30) endorses explicitly. The Sha’ar Hatziyun (442:72), however, writes that the smearing of clay merely excuses one from the requirement to remove this <i>chametz</i> from his home. This Halachah appears to prove the lenient approach, as the Shulchan Aruch permits one to own such <i>chametz</i> even though its status as food can easily be restored by removing the clay. This proof does seem compelling according to the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i>’s view that it is actually permissible to benefit from this <i>chametz</i> so long as it remains covered with clay. According to the Sha’ar Hatziyun, though, this Halachah does not prove that we disregard future restoration; it merely proves that one may own such <i>chametz</i> while it remains in its current state, not that it may be benefited from.
Rav Frank cites two reasons to consider alcohol <i>chametz </i>even if it is unfit for canine consumption. First, Rav Yosef Shaul Nathanson (<i>Teshuvot Sho’eil Umeishiv </i>1:1:141) rules that the exemption of <i>eino ra’ui l’achilat kelev</i> applies only to items that are normally consumed. Since these items are consumed only when they are edible, the prohibition to consume them does not apply if they are unfit even for canine consumption. However, if an item is not meant for consumption, the prohibition to own and benefit from it (if it contains <i>chametz</i>) applies even if it is inedible.
Rav Frank himself judges this line of reasoning to be unpersuasive. In fact, he adds, even Rav Nathanson seems to contradict himself in another responsum (<i>Teshuvot Sho’eil Umeishiv</i> 3:2:148) in which he permits soap for <i>Pesach</i> use because the <i>chametz</i> in the soap is inedible. We may add that Rav Nathanson’s reasoning also appears to conflict with the Shulchan Aruch’s aforementioned permission to use ink that was cooked in barley beer, wherein the Shulchan Aruch applies <i>eino ra’ui l’achilat kelev</i> even though ink is not normally consumed. Accordingly, most <i>poskim</i> reject Rav Nathanson’s stringent view, although some take it into account as a reason to be strict when possible about such issues on <i>Pesach</i> (see, for example, <i>Yesodei Yeshurun</i> 6:227).
The other reason for stringency that Rav Frank quotes is the opinion of the <i>Teshuvot Levushei Mordechai </i>(O.C. 86), who argues that the category of <i>nifsal mei’achilat kelev </i>is irrelevant to alcohol, since dogs would not consume even the conventional alcohol that humans regularly drink. The <i>Teshuvot Minchat Elazar</i> (ad. loc.) challenges this argument, asserting that even dogs can potentially acquire a taste for alcohol (see Rosh Hashanah 4a)<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Yehuda Ness notes that this response is questionable based on the aforementioned argument that Halachah is not formulated based on exceptions (<i>batlah da’ato eitzel kol haberiyot</i>). Most dogs certainly do not indulge in alcohol. On the other hand, the Gemara refers to a dog being trained to drink alcohol. It is perfectly normal for dogs to do as they are trained, so perhaps the rule of <i>batlah da’ato eitzel kol haberiyot</i> is inapplicable.</i> just as humans can.
Ultimately, Rav Frank concludes that one should not use denatured alcohol as cooking fuel (apparently a common practice in the early twentieth century) on <i>Pesach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For further discussion of this debate, see <i>She’arim Mitzuyanim Bahalachah </i>(3:78-79).</i>
Rav Shimon Eider (<i>Halachos of Pesach</i> p. 25 footnote 90) writes that he heard that Rav Isser Zalman Meltzer, Rav Aharon Kotler, and Rav Yaakov Kaminetzky agree with Rav Moshe’s position on denatured alcohol. Accordingly, Rav Doniel Neustadt (<i>The Monthly Halachah Discussion</i> p. 187) concludes that most <i>poskim</i> rule strictly about this matter. However, as I heard Rav Yosef Rottenberg of Baltimore state, the problem with oral reports such as these (when not supported by a written responsum) is that it is difficult to determine whether these great authorities meant their rulings as <i>ikar hadin</i> (bottom-line Halachah) or merely as <i>chumrah </i>(stringency). In fact, Rav Kaminetzky is cited (<i>Emet L’yaakov Al Shulchan Aruch</i> p. 200 note 432) as being lenient regarding this matter in case of very great need (especially since the alcohol might be synthetic, as we shall explain later).
We should note that even grain alcohol might not be regarded as <i>chametz</i> on a biblical level. Some <i>poskim</i> (cited in <i>Sha’arei Teshuvah</i> 442:3) argue that alcohol is <i>zei’ah b’alma </i>(“mere sweat,” i.e. of no substance; see Berachot 37a) and is forbidden as <i>chametz</i> only on a rabbinic level. Although the Mishnah Berurah (442:4) rules that the consensus opinion classifies grain alcohol as biblically prohibited, Rav Yehudah Amital told me that the lenient opinions might be utilized as a <i>snif l’hakeil </i>(a lenient consideration) regarding the question of owning products that contain <i>chametz</i>. This is also the opinion of Rav Chaim David Halevi (<i>Techumin</i> 3:69).<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Bei’ur Halacha </i>489:10 s.v. <i>Af</i>, who considers the lenient opinions in the context of the laws of <i>chadash</i>. </i>
We should clarify that the Shulchan Aruch’s permitting the use of ink cooked in beer, even though the beer in the ink can be chemically reconstituted, is not an ironclad proof to the lenient opinion, as the technique of chemical reconstitution was not available during the Shulchan Aruch’s time. Thus, the stringent opinion presumably would argue that this lenient ruling of the Shulchan Aruch no longer applies, because reconstitution is now a practicable possibility.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Asher Bush adds that because ink is not poisonous, it is not impossible to imagine someone consuming it, yet the Shulchan Aruch permits benefiting from it. Most cosmetics and toiletries, on the hand, are poisonous when consumed, so it is less likely that a person would consume them. As such, there is even more reason to be lenient about cosmetics and toiletries than about ink, which would suggest that the former should be permissible.</i>
<b>Cosmetics and Toiletries that Contain Denatured Alcohol</b>
Later twentieth-century authorities continue to debate the status not only of denatured alcohol, but even of products that contain denatured alcohol. Rav Gedalia Felder, though he acknowledges the possibility of allowing ownership of such products on <i>Pesach</i>, concludes that it is forbidden. He bases his strict ruling on the potential of chemically rendering these products as edible and on the stringent rulings of the <i>Teshuvot Sho’eil Umeishiv</i> and <i>Teshuvot Levushei Mordechai </i>cited above. On the other hand, many observant Jews, following the lead of great <i>poskim</i> such as Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Yalkut Yosef</i>, <i>Kitzur Shulchan Aruch</i> p. 585 in the 5760 edition) and Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik (cited by Rav Yosef Adler and many others), are lenient in this matter. In fact, the <i>Teshuvot Chazon Nachum</i> (46) writes that although he perceives that the common practice is to be strict in these matters, a Rav who rules leniently should not be criticized.
Rav Shimon Eider (<i>Halachos of Pesach</i> pp. 25-26) articulates a compromise view that is practiced by many people.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The Chicago Rabbinical Council, following the rulings of Rav Gedalia Schwartz, Rav Shmuel Fuerst, and other prominent <i>poskim </i>from Chicago, has adopted this approach as its official policy. </i> He writes, “Many Poskim hold that this problem of alcohol concerns only medications, cosmetics, toiletries and the like which are in liquid form.” Such products include cologne, pre-shave and aftershave lotion, mouthwash, and spray and roll-on deodorants. Rav Eider explains that when the alcohol is not in liquid form, Rav Moshe’s concern for those who drink the alcohol with only slight modifications is not relevant. Moreover, Rav Moshe’s concern does not apply to the alcohol in some liquids, such as nail polish, hand lotion, shoe polish, and paint, and hence Rav Eider permits their use.
Additionally, the alcohol in the products in question often is synthetic, not grain alcohol. Rav Doniel Neustadt (<i>The Monthly Halachah Discussion </i>pp. 187-188) reports that even manufacturers are often unaware of which type of alcohol their products contain. Since they use whatever ingredients are available for the lowest price at the time of manufacture, they often do not have information about the origin of the alcohol in a product manufactured six months before purchase. Obviously, synthetic alcohol would pose no problem for <i>Pesach </i>use.
Moreover, if a majority of the alcohol that is used in such products is synthetic, then one might be permitted to rely on the <i>rov </i>(majority; see Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 110:2). Indeed, Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Yalkut Yosef</i> ad. loc.) rules leniently about using Israeli products specifically because most of the denatured alcohol contained in Israeli products is synthetic.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This obviously is subject to change over time. If it becomes more common in Israel to use grain-based denatured alcohol than synthetic denatured alcohol, Rav Ovadia might forbid using products containing denatured alcohol.</i>
<i><b>Sichah K’shtiyah</b></i>
In addition to the issues already discussed, the principle of sichah k’shtiyah (anointing is equivalent to drinking; see Yoma 76b) poses a potential problem for applying cosmetics that contain inedible chametz. We noted above that although one may own and benefit from inedible chametz, he still may not eat it. Accordingly, if the principle of sichah k’shtiyah applies to chametz on Pesach, it would be forbidden to apply makeup that contains even inedible chametz, as this would be the equivalent of “drinking” the chametz. Thus, we must explore the parameters of sichah k’shtiyah to assess its relevance to the prohibitions of chametz on Pesach.
The Gemara (<i>ibid</i>.) presents the principle of <i>sichah k’shtiyah</i> as a reason why it is forbidden to anoint oneself on <i>Yom Kippur</i> (see Yoma 73b). The Gemara also uses this principle to forbid a non-<i>Kohen</i> to anoint himself with olive oil of <i>terumah</i> (the <i>kohen</i>’s tithe, which a non-<i>kohen</i> is strictly forbidden to consume). Thus, it appears that the principle of <i>sichah k’shtiyah</i> extends to all areas of Halachah. However, Rabbeinu Tam (cited in <i>Tosafot Yoma</i> 77a s.v. <i>D’tnan </i>and Niddah 32a s.v. <i>Uchshemen</i>) argues that <i>sichah k’shtiyah</i> applies only to <i>Yom Kippur</i>, <i>terumah</i>, and <i>issurei hana’ah</i> (items that we are forbidden to benefit from, such as <i>chametz</i> on <i>Pesach</i>). Thus, Rabbeinu Tam rules that we are permitted to use soap made from pig fat, since Halachah permits benefiting from pigs.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Dovid Heber reports that soap continues to be made from pig fat even today.</i>
Many <i>Rishonim</i>, including the <i>Mordechai</i> (Shabbat 338-339), the <i>Sefer Haterumah</i> (238), and <i>Tosafot Rabbeinu Peretz </i>(Pesachim 24b s.v. <i>Hiniach</i>) challenge Rabbeinu Tam’s ruling and state that the principle of <i>sichah k’shtiyah</i> applies to all prohibitions. They rule, therefore, that it is forbidden to use soap derived from animals that would be forbidden to eat (such as non-kosher or improperly slaughtered animals).
The commentaries to the Shulchan Aruch continue to debate this issue. The Vilna Gaon (Bei’ur Hagra O.C. 326:10 s.v. <i>Oh Bish’ar Cheilev</i>) notes that the Rama (<i>ibid</i>.) rules that on Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>, it is forbidden to use soaps made from animal fat (such as those commonly used in Europe at that time). This implies that it is permitted to use such soap on other days (even if made from animals which would be forbidden to eat), as Rabbeinu Tam ruled. The Vilna Gaon himself, though, rules in accordance with the <i>Rishonim</i> who reject Rabbeinu Tam’s ruling and forbids soaps made from prohibited animals except in a situation of distress (<i>mekom tza’ar</i>).
The Shach (<i>Nekudat Hakesef</i>, Y.D. 117:4) observes that the common custom is to be lenient in this matter, but that some follow the strict opinion, which he believes is the “proper” practice. The Bei’ur Halachah (326:10 s.v. <i>Bish’ar Cheilev</i>) also writes that the <i>minhag ha’olam</i> (common custom) is to be lenient, though he, too, notes that “A number of people who are scrupulous about Halachah are strict about this matter.” He concludes that if soap made from a kosher source is readily available, it is “certainly proper” (though not required) to accommodate the stringent opinion.
<i><b>Sichah K’shtiyah</b></i><b> for Inedible Products<i></i></b>
Both the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 117:29) and the <i>Kaf Hachaim</i> (O.C. 326:45) specify that this dispute applies only to soap that is edible; all would agree, however, that it is permitted to use inedible soap. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> records that this is the commonly accepted and unchallenged practice among Jews throughout the world. Nevertheless, Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at </i>4:43), despite agreeing with this lenient position, observes that the Bei’ur Halachah does not distinguish between edible and inedible soap, which implies that it is preferable to avoid using even inedible soap that is derived from a non-kosher source.
The debate regarding <i>sichah k’shtiyah</i> and soap is relevant to cosmetics as well, since they, too, are applied to the skin. Rav Dovid Heber<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Heber’s essay on this topic is available at www.star-k.org.</i> relates that the <i>minhag ha’olam</i> today is to apply cosmetics that contain non-kosher ingredients to one’s face throughout the year. He adds, however, that although cosmetics that are unfit for canine consumption are, strictly speaking, permitted on <i>Pesach</i>, “Nonetheless, many individuals are strict and avoid using creams, lotions, and liquids that contain <i>chometz</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Examples of <i>chametz</i> ingredients in cosmetics include wheat protein, wheat germ, avena, beta glucan, oat extracts, and triticum vulgare. </i> In any case, Rav Shimon Eider (<i>Halachos of Pesach</i> p. 27) comments, that it seems that all would agree that it is proper to use a fresh stick of lipstick for <i>Pesach</i>, as this is common practice regarding items that one puts in his mouth and that come in contact with <i>chametz</i>, such as toothbrushes.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Although one could simply clean the lipstick or toothbrush, there is concern that one cannot thoroughly clean these items, similar to those described by the Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 442:11) and Rama (<i>ibid</i>.).</i>
<b>Toothpaste on <i>Pesach</i></b>
The toothpaste that we use today is inedible (despite the fact that many have a pleasant taste), but it commonly contains glycerin (which is often manufactured from forbidden animals) and might have <i>chametz</i> ingredients. Thus, there are two potential problems with toothpaste on <i>Pesach</i>: it may have non-kosher ingredients and it may contain <i>chametz</i>. The common practice in the observant community is to be lenient and to use regular toothpaste year-round despite the potential non-kosher ingredients, though some are strict and use only toothpaste with entirely <i>kosher</i> ingredients. What, then, is the halachic status of toothpaste on <i>Pesach</i>?
Rav Shimon Eider (ad. loc.) cites that Rav Moshe Feinstein ruled that toothpaste is <i>eino ra’ui l’achilat kelev</i> and hence is permissible for use on <i>Pesach</i>. Rav Yosef Adler reports that Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik endorsed this position. Rav Moshe adds that we are not concerned about swallowing some of the toothpaste, since <i>achsheveih </i>does not apply to unintended swallowing (as we cited previously from the Mishnah Berurah 442:45). However, he states that since toothpaste that does not contain <i>chametz</i> is readily available, one should not use toothpaste that might contain <i>chametz</i>. Rav Eider reports, based on verbal accounts, that Rav Aharon Kotler agreed with Rav Moshe’s position.
Rav Moshe’s recommendation appears to be based on a ruling of the Rama (Y.D. 155:3) that we do not permit an ill person to consume a forbidden item, even if it is necessary to preserve his life, if a permitted item that is equally effective in fighting the illness is available.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The context of the Rama’s ruling might indicate that the Rama speaks even about a forbidden item that has been rendered inedible. If so, Rav Moshe’s recommendation becomes even more powerful, since the Rama requires using a permissible item even if, as in the modern-day case of toothpaste, the item is inedible. Rav Moshe appears to adopt this approach in his ruling regarding dish soap that is cited later in this chapter. Rav Hershel Schachter told me that Rav Moshe’s preference to avoid using non-kosher items even if they are inedible is due to concern for “<i>timtum halev</i>” (spiritual contamination) resulting from the consumption of non-kosher food, even if it is consumed in a permissible manner. The Rama (Y.D. 81:7) expresses similar sentiments in regard to attempting as much as possible to have a child nurse from someone who consumes only kosher food.</i> The Rama adds that one must wait for the permitted item even if there will be a slight delay in obtaining it, assuming that the delay will not endanger the patient.
What is not clear from Rav Eider’s reports of oral rulings from these eminent authorities is whether they considered this ruling to constitute the <i>ikar hadin </i>or a <i>chumra</i>. As we quoted earlier from Rav Yosef Rottenberg, rulings that are not supported by written responsa are difficult to fully scrutinize and understand.
Rav Hershel Schachter told me that even if toothpaste has a pleasant taste, it is nonetheless considered <i>eino ra’ui l’achilat kelev</i> and is permissible to use, since it is still inedible. Rav Schachter understands the Rama’s (Y.D. 108:5) prohibition against tasting a forbidden item to apply only if the item is suitable to be eaten. <sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Pitchei Teshuvah</i> (Y.D. 98:1), the <i>Mateh Yehonatan</i> (Y.D. 108:5), and the <i>Teshuvot Har Zvi </i>(Y.D. 95) support Rav Schachter’s ruling. 
On the other hand, Rav Eider (ad. loc.) infers from this Rama that “Flavored lipsticks may not be used on <i>Pesach</i>.” Apparently, Rav Eider believes that one may not taste even an inedible item.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Asher Bush argues that this reading of the Rama appears to contradict the aforementioned ruling of the Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 442:10, as interpreted by the <i>Mishnah Beruruah</i> 442:45, citing the Magen Avraham 442:15 in the name of the <i>Terumat Hadeshen </i>129) that it is permitted, from the vantage point of <i>chametz</i>, to write with ink that was cooked in barely beer even though the scribe might absentmindedly put the quill into his mouth and swallow some of the ink.</i>
<b>Mouthwash</b>
This debate impacts the question of using mouthwash that contains inedible <i>chametz</i> (many contain denatured alcohol) for <i>Pesach</i>. Whereas Rav Schachter permits using any kind of mouthwash, others forbid using one that has a “minty” or other pleasant taste (as opposed to a medicine-like taste). Indeed, Rav Meir Bransdorfer (<i>Teshuvot Knei Bosem</i> 1:25) writes that mouthwash containing inedible <i>chametz</i> is permitted on <i>Pesach</i> only if one does not intend to eat or enjoy the taste of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Asher Bush suggests that this may vary from person to person.</i> This seems to imply that he would forbid using mouthwash unless it has a “medicine-like taste.” Rav Eider (ad. loc.), on the other hand, forbids any mouthwash that contains <i>chametz</i> ingredients.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Heber writes that breath sprays and breath strip fresheners that are intentionally swallowed require kashrut certification for <i>Pesach </i>and year-round use. Rav Hershel Schachter, on the other hand, told me that these items do not require kashrut certification for <i>Pesach</i> or year round use, since they are inedible and one does not intend to consume them for the purpose of eating.</i>
<b>Dish Soap</b>
Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 2:30) discusses whether one is permitted to use dishwashing liquid that comes from a source that is not kosher. Rav Moshe rules that it is considered <i>nifsal mei’achilat kelev</i>, citing as a precedent the Shulchan Aruch’s (O.C. 442:10) permission to write on <i>chol hamo’eid Pesach</i> with ink that was cooked in barley beer. Interestingly, Rav Moshe embraces both a leniency and a stringency in this <i>teshuvah</i>. On the one hand, he rules, contrary to the position of some earlier <i>Acharonim </i>(see <i>Yad Efraim</i> to Y.D. 99:5), that one is permitted to manufacture such a liquid and that doing so does not violate the prohibition of <i>ein mevatlin issur l’chatchilah</i> (intentionally nullifying forbidden food) even though it transforms a forbidden item into a permissible one. On the other hand, Rav Moshe does not state that such dishwashing liquid is permitted; rather, he writes, “We cannot forbid it,” implying that it is best not to use such a detergent if an alternative is available. The reason for this might be the aforementioned ruling of the Rama (Y.D. 155:3) that it is forbidden to consume non-kosher items even to fight an illness when kosher ones are available.
Rav Shimon Eider (ad. loc.), in contrast, writes that one should use only dishwashing liquid that is approved for <i>Pesach</i> use. Rav Eider writes that this is consistent with the common practice to use only dishwashing liquid that has no non-kosher ingredients even though it is <i>nifsal mei’achilat kelev</i>. Rav Eider suggests that we are concerned that such liquid may not be entirely <i>nifsal mei’achilat kelev</i>, in which case our dishes are absorbing non-kosher ingredients from the dishwashing liquid when washed with it in hot water.
<b>Conclusion</b>
It is our hope that this chapter has shed some light on this controversial topic. We have seen that there is a basis for both the strict and lenient approaches, and thus it is not appropriate to criticize one who adopts either approach to this issue.

Family Matters

Frivolous Wedding Ceremonies

Occasionally, one hears about a story of a young man “marrying” a young woman in a frivolous wedding ceremony. Though these ceremonies may seem trivial, they can have horrific consequences, as Halachah might not regard them as invalid. Some cases might even necessitate a <i>get</i>, albeit sometimes merely as a precautionary measure.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The literature from <i>poskim</i> who have dealt with such regrettable situations in the past is summarized in the Pitchei Teshuvah (E.H. 42:5-6) and <i>Otzar Haposkim</i> (volume 13 pp. 11-26).</i> Tragically, this probably would preclude the woman from marrying a <i>kohen</i> (see Rama E.H. 6:1).<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Rama writes that a divorced woman is forbidden to a <i>kohen</i> even if she received a <i>get</i> merely as a precautionary measure. For further discussion of this Rama and its application, see <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> (6 E.H. 1), Rav Melech Schachter’s essay in <i>Beit Yitzchak</i> (22:157-161), <i>Teshuvot Atzei Besamim</i> (11), and Rav Yirmiyahu Benyowitz’s <i>Kovetz Al Yad</i> (1:148).</i>
In this chapter, we shall a present a case that was adjudicated by Rav Mendel Senderovic that appears in his collection of responsa (<i>Teshuvot Atzei Besamim</i> 22). A full presentation of this case and Rav Senderovic’s decision will elucidate many fundamental issues concerning both <i>kiddushin</i> in general and frivolous <i>kiddushin</i> in particular.
Needless to say, it is vital that youngsters never engage in such reckless behavior, the consequences of which can last a lifetime. If any situation does arise, the case must be presented to a Rav of eminent stature for a ruling.
<b>The Case</b>
Rav Senderovic’s case involved a group of three boys, all nearly fourteen years old, and two girls nearly thirteen years old. Two of the boys left the group, leaving the others standing together. One of the two girls said to the remaining boy that if he loved the second girl, he should marry her, and she handed him a bottle cap to use for the “ceremony.” The boy, following this suggestion, took the bottle cap and handed it to the second girl, who kept it without casting it away. Meanwhile, one of the other boys returned and saw the bottle cap in the second girl’s hand, but he was later unsure (at the time of testimony) whether he had heard the first girl suggest the “marriage” and whether he had witnessed the delivery of the bottle cap.
<b>Lack of Intent</b>
It is tempting to invalidate this ceremony due to the lack of <i>da’at </i>(intent) to marry. <i>Da’at</i> is a basic element of creating valid <i>kiddushin</i>, as is evident in many passages in <i>Masechet Kiddushin</i> (see, for example, Kiddushin 2b, 7b, and 10a).<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Also see <i>Chidushei Rabbeinu Chaim Halevi Soloveitchik</i> to the Rambam (<i>Hilchot Yibum V’chalitzah</i> 4:16) for a full conceptual analysis of the requirement of <i>da’at</i> for <i>kiddushin</i>.</i> Since this couple presumably lacked <i>da’at</i> to marry, the ceremony should be invalid and no <i>get</i> should be required.
However, Halachah does not make this assumption easily, as demonstrated by a ruling of the Maharam of Rothenberg (<i>Teshuvot Maharam Mi’ruttenberg</i> 993). In the case brought before him, a woman at first instructed a man to marry her, but when the man threw an item into her lap, she immediately tossed it away. Even though the woman explained that her words were intended as a mere joke, an intention that her actions seemed to confirm, the Maharam ruled that she was married and would require a <i>get</i> to marry another man. The Rama (E.H.HH 42:1) rules in accordance with the Maharam, and none of the commentaries to the Shulchan Aruch challenge this ruling.
<i><b>Devarim Sheb’leiv </b></i><b>and<i> Umdana D’muchach</i></b>
A bit of discussion is necessary to explain this ruling. The Gemara (Kiddushin 49b) presents a fundamental rule that applies to many areas of Halachah: “<i>Devarim sheb’leiv einam devarim</i>” (thoughts that are not articulated are immaterial). <i>Tosafot </i>(ad. loc. s.v. Devarim) add, though, that if his intent is so obvious that it is assumed even when not stated explicitly (<i>umdana d’muchach</i>), the stipulation need not be articulated to be meaningful.
As an example, <i>Tosafot</i> cite a case (from Bava Batra 132a) in which someone presumed that he had no children and therefore gave his entire estate to someone outside of his family but subsequently discovered that his child was alive. Even though the donor did not explicitly stipulate that his gift was to be valid only on condition that his child was actually dead, the gift is nullified when he discovers that his child is living, because it is glaringly obvious to us – there is an <i>umdana d’muchach</i> – that had he known that the child was living, he would never have gifted his entire estate to a non-family member.
In the case of the Maharam, the woman in question is considered to be married due to the rule of <i>devarim sheb’leiv einam devarim</i>. The woman did not, before she received the ring, issue a disclaimer (<i>mesirat moda’ah</i>) clarifying that she was merely joking and did not intend to be married. Based on two passages in the Gemara (Yevamot 118b and <i>Bava Kama</i> 111a), the Maharam explains that since some women are satisfied with marginal husbands,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For further explanation and discussion of this assertion, see my <i>Gray Matter 1</i> p. 43.</i> there is no <i>umdana d’muchach</i> that she was joking.
<i><b>Umdana D’muchah </b></i><b>for Marriage<i></i></b>
In what appears to be a sweeping statement, the Rama adds to his confirmation of the Maharam that we do not consider <i>umdenot</i> (presumptions) and other evidence that would indicate that the couple did not intend to be married. The scope of this exclusion is somewhat unclear. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (E.H. 42:7-8) construes the Rama very broadly, interpreting that he completely negates the rules of <i>umdana d’muchach</i> in the context of marriage and divorce. He notes that the Rama does not say that a couple with an <i>umdana</i> that they were not serious is considered only possibly married (<i>safeik kiddushin</i>); they are considered married in all respects. He concludes that the Maharam and Rama are presenting an all-embracing rule: with regard to <i>gittin</i> and <i>kiddushin</i>, we always apply the principle of <i>devarim sheb’leiv einam devarim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">We should note, however, that while the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> rules elsewhere based on this understanding (E.H. 145:30), he also seems to contradict it in a different place (E.H. 141:63-64).</i>
The <i>Chazon Ish</i> (E.H. 52:3) adopts a very different approach. He argues that the Gemara never outright excludes <i>gittin</i> and <i>kiddushin</i> from the rules of <i>umdana d’muchach</i>, and we have no evidence that the Maharam and his <i>beit din</i> issued a new edict eliminating the <i>umdana d’muchach</i> exception for <i>kiddushin.</i> Rather, in the specific case addressed by the Maharam and the Rama, there simply was no <i>umdana d’muchach</i> that the couple did not intend to be married. Had there been an authentic <i>umdana</i>, however, the marriage would not have been valid.
An example of a case with such an <i>umdana</i> is addressed in a <i>teshuvah</i> by Rav Shmuel David (printed in <i>Techumin </i>18:92-99). In Rav David’s situation, a non-observant Israeli couple was scheduled to be married, with a Rav from the Israeli rabbinate officiating at the wedding. The husband found himself unable to attend the wedding, so he asked a friend to substitute for him at the ceremony – not to act as an agent, but to present himself fraudulently to the Rav as the actual husband. The “real” husband subsequently lived with his “wife” and had children with her. One of the children later became observant and wanted to know if she was considered a <i>mamzeret</i> (illegitimate child), since her mother seemed to be married to the substitute husband, not her father.
Rav David cites Rav Eliyahu Bakshi-Doron, Rav Mordechai Eliyahu, and Rav Aharon Lichtenstein as ruling that the daughter was not a <i>mamzeret</i> since the “marriage” between her mother and the substitute husband was invalid. One of the considerations behind this assessment of the marriage was the <i>umdana d’muchach</i> that her mother and the substitute husband did not intend to be married to each other.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">We should note, however, that there is more room for leniency in the case of <i>mamzeirut</i>, whose violation is a regular negative prohibition, than in the case of declaring that a <i>get</i> is unnecessary, which could result in violation of the capital crime of adultery and the death penalty associated with it. Moreover, the Torah-level prohibition of <i>mamzeirut</i> does not apply to one who is only a possible <i>mamzeir</i> (Kiddushin 73a).</i>
In our case, however, Rav Senderovic did not feel that this was sufficient reason to rule leniently. Firstly, the dispute between the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> and the <i>Chazon Ish</i> seems to be unresolved, as the <i>Otzar Haposkim</i> (volume 13 pp. 11-26 in the 5740 edition) presents <i>poskim</i> who agree with each. Second, in the case that Rav Senderovic adjudicated, as in the case of the Maharam, there was no <i>umdana d’muchach</i> that the couple had no <i>da’at </i>to marry.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Senderovic does not accept the argument of Rav Shmuel David (<i>Techumin</i> 23:149-157) that there is an <i>umdana d’muchach</i> that early teens in contemporary times have no intention to marry, since no one in this milieu gets married at this age. Rav Hershel Schachter (personal communication) and Rav Mordechai Willig (personal communication) also do not accept Rav David’s argument. </i> As opposed to Rav David’s case, wherein the woman was ready to marry another man and obviously did not intend to marry the substitute, those in Rav Senderovic’s case may very well have intended to marry.
<b>Rav Moshe Feinstein’s Compromise</b>
We should note that while Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 1:84) seems, for the most part, to adopt the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i>’s broader reading, he nevertheless presents two limitations on the strict ruling of the Maharam and Rama. First, it appears (based on the Bei’ur Hagra, E.H. 42:4) that the Rama’s disqualification of <i>umdenot</i> regarding <i>kiddushin</i> is a stringency that is imposed only under rabbinic law (but not on a biblical level). Thus, he reasons that there is no reason to be strict in case where there was only one witness, in which the concern that the <i>kiddushin</i> is valid is itself a mere stringency.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This point will be discussed at greater length below.</i> According to Rav Moshe, no <i>get</i> is required in such a case.
In addition, Rav Moshe (ad. loc. 1:82) advocates a suggestion of the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (E.H. 42:12) that if the witnesses are fully aware that there is an <i>umdana d’muchach</i> regarding the couple’s lack of intent, the <i>kiddushin</i> is not valid. This is not due to lack of intent to be married (about which the Maharam and Rama rule that one cannot be lenient), but rather due to the lack of proper witnesses to the ceremony. Rav Moshe explains that one cannot be considered a witness to a <i>kiddushin</i> if he harbors serious doubts as to the efficacy of the ceremony. In other words, the Maharam and Rama are strict only if the witnesses do not realize that the ceremony was a joke. The <i>Otzar Haposkim</i> (ad. loc. pages 19-21) cites several prominent <i>poskim</i> (such as <i>Teshuvot Atzei Halevanon</i> 90 and <i>Teshuvot Zekan Aharon</i> 84) who agree with this assertion, but records that others (such as Rav Kook, <i>Teshuvot Ezrat Kohen</i> 48) disagree.
However, these two leniencies are not relevant to Rav Senderovic’s case, in which, as mentioned above, there was no <i>umdana d’muchach</i> that the couple did not intend to be married.
<b>The Bottle Cap’s Lack of Value</b>
At first glance, it seems that we should be able to invalidate the ceremony for a different reason: the bottle cap’s lack of monetary worth. A fundamental law of kiddushin is that the item used must be worth a <i>perutah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yaakov Luban (in an essay available at www.ou.org) writes, based on the <i>Chazon Ish</i> (C.M. 16-30), that the value of a <i>perutah</i> is 1/1244 of a troy ounce (1/40 of a gram) of pure silver. Rav Hershel Schachter (personal communication; see <i>B’ikvei Hatzon</i> pp. 268-269) and Rav Mordechai Willig (personal communication) agree with this calculation of the value of a <i>perutah</i>. In 1993, a troy ounce of silver was worth approximately $3.75, making a <i>perutah</i> worth 3/10 of a cent. Accordingly, when the value of silver is depressed, it is possible that a bottle cap indeed is worth a <i>perutah</i> (if not in the United States, then perhaps in an undeveloped country, which, as we will see, may be significant). Rav Moshe Heinemann (in an essay available at www.star-k.org), however, cites Rav Moshe Feinstein, who rules that in order to qualify as a <i>shaveh perutah</i>, a coin must have some purchasing power. Rav Heinemann writes that according to this approach, a <i>shaveh perutah</i> is at least a nickel. </i> (Kiddushin 2a). This is so essential that <i>mesadrei kiddushin</i> (rabbinic wedding officiants) commonly clarify under the <i>chuppah</i> that the ring has a value of at least a <i>perutah</i> (Rama E.H. 31:2).
Nevertheless, the Gemara (Kiddushin 12a) presents the opinion of Shmuel that if one uses an item that was worth less than a <i>perutah</i> in his own locale, we must still be concerned that the item might be worth a <i>perutah</i> somewhere else. Although Rav Chisda disagrees with Shmuel’s opinion, the Shulchan Aruch (E.H. 31:2) rules in accordance with Shmuel, as do almost all <i>Rishonim</i>. Thus, a couple that used an item that was worth less than a <i>perutah</i> is considered only possibly married and would require another <i>kiddushin</i> if they wish to be married to each other (<i>Shulchan Aruch ibid</i>. 4) or a <i>get </i>if they wish to marry others. The Beit Shmuel (31:11) and <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (E.H. 31:18) argue that the mainstream opinion among the <i>Rishonim</i> is that the requirement of the <i>get </i>is only rabbinic, as there is no biblical-level concern for the item being worth a <i>perutah</i> elsewhere.
In our case, although a bottle cap is not worth a <i>perutah</i> in the United States, it might be worth a <i>perutah</i> in an undeveloped country. Accordingly, we cannot assume that the <i>kiddushin</i> is invalid based on the fact that the cap was worth less than a <i>perutah</i>. However, Rav Senderovic cites the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i>’s (E.H. 31:18) suggestion that if the item would seemingly be worthless anywhere in the world, the marriage should be invalid. Rav Senderovic argues that a bottle cap appears to fit this qualification, making the ceremony in our case void. Although the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> himself expresses hesitancy about this distinction, Rav Senderovic uses the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i>’s suggestion as a consideration for a lenient ruling.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Poskim</i> who adjudicate these regrettable cases generally strive to amass many considerations for a lenient ruling (see, for example, <i>Teshuvot Noda Biy’hudah </i>2 E.H. 75, cited in Pitchei Teshuvah E.H. 42:6 and <i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 1:82).</i>
<b>Omission of “<i>Harei At Mekudeshet Li</i>”</b>
In our case, the boy was silent. The only one to speak was the girl who said, “If you love her, marry her.” The boy thus did not fulfill the usual practice of the <i>chatan </i>to say “<i>Harei at mekudeshet li b’taba’at zo k’dat Moshe v’yisrael</i>” (behold you are betrothed to me with this ring in accordance with the laws of Moses and Israel) before presenting the ring to the <i>kallah </i>(Shulchan Aruch E.H. 27). However, the Rama (E.H. 27:1) notes that the <i>kiddushin</i> is valid even if the <i>chatan</i> does not address the <i>kallah</i> as long as marriage is discussed in her presence (<i>asukim b’oto inyan</i>; see Kiddushin 6a).
Even this limited requirement, though, may demand more than was present in our case. Indeed, the Taz (E.H. 27:2) writes that the one to speak must be the <i>chatan</i>, whereas in our case, the boy said nothing. The <i>Otzar Haposkim</i> (27:15:2), however, cites authorities who rule that the marriage is valid even if the <i>chatan</i> is not the speaker. We also cannot invalidate the marriage on the grounds that the sole witness was unsure whether he heard the girl’s words, as the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (E.H. 27:17) raises the possibility that the witnesses might not be required to hear the statement made in the presence of the <i>kallah</i>. It is possible, he asserts, that it is sufficient for the witness simply to see the delivery of the item from the <i>chatan</i> to the <i>kallah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">These disputes hinge to a great extent on the role of the marriage statement. If the statement (<i>amirah</i>) is an essential component of the wedding ceremony, as Rashi (Kiddushin 5b s.v. <i>Hachi Ka’amar</i>) seems to indicate, the <i>chatan</i> is required to make the statement himself and the witnesses must hear it.
