If, however, the statement merely serves to clarify that the delivery of the item from boy to girl is for the purpose of marriage, as the Ran (1b in the pages of the Rif s.v. <i>Tannu Rabbanan</i>) seems to indicate, it might not be necessary for the <i>chatan</i> to make the statement or for the witnesses to hear it.</i> Still, even though the boy’s omission of the statement is insufficient basis to invalidate the ceremony, it does provide another consideration to support a lenient ruling.
<b>Ownership of the Ring</b>
Another potential reason for leniency is the basic requirement of weddings that the ring must belong to the <i>chatan</i> (Shulchan Aruch E.H. 28). In fact, <i>mesadrei kiddushin</i> verify with the <i>chatan</i> that he owns the ring and did not simply borrow it. If the <i>chatan</i> forgot to bring the wedding ring, the <i>mesader kiddushin</i> will ensure that the “lender” of a replacement ring transfers title of the ring to the <i>chatan</i> with proper <i>kinyanim</i> (halachic mechanisms of conveyance). Accordingly, the ceremony in our case would appear to be invalid, since the bottle cap did not belong to the boy.
On the other hand, the Rosh (Kiddushin 1:20) asserts that if a “lender” knows that the “borrower” of a ring intends to use it for <i>kiddushin</i>, it is certain (<i>anan sahadei</i>) that he presents the ring as a gift and not a loan, since he knows that <i>kiddushin</i> can be accomplished only with a ring that belongs to the <i>chatan</i>. This ruling is codified by the Shulchan Aruch (E.H. 28:19) and is applied by the Mishnah Berurah (649:15) in the context of borrowing a <i>lulav</i> on the first day of <i>Sukkot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">On the first day, the <i>lulav</i> must belong to the person who is attempting to fulfill the <i>mitzvah</i> with it. For a discussion of whether this issue applies on the second day outside of Israel, as well, see Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 649:5) and Bei’ur Halachah (<i>ibid</i>. s.v. <i>Pesulei</i>).</i> Accordingly, the bottle cap may have belonged to the boy, since we assume that the girl transferred title of the bottle cap to him for the purpose of <i>kiddushin</i>.
On the other hand, Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 1:90, addressing a case of a frivolous marriage ceremony) argues that the Rosh’s <i>anan sahadei</i> did not apply in his case, as most people in his locale were not aware of the requirement for the <i>chatan</i> to own the ring. <sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe wrote this in the United States in 1949.</i> Moreover, in the context of a joke <i>kiddushin</i>, there is no <i>anan sahadei</i> that the “lender” wished to transfer title to the ring in order to ensure the validity of the <i>kiddushin</i>. In such a situation, it is reasonable to assume that the lender specifically wished to ensure that the <i>kiddushin</i> would be invalid. This is true even if we are unsure whether the lender understood that the couple was not serious, asserts Rav Moshe, as the concept of <i>anan sahadei</i> can apply only where there is relative certainty.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">He makes a similar assertion in <i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 1:76-77. For further discussion and a list of <i>poskim</i> who agree and disagree with this assertion, see <i>Gray Matter 1</i> p. 72.</i> In this case, there is a strong doubt as to whether the “lender” wished to transfer title of the ring to the <i>chatan</i>. Moreover, perhaps the fact that another male was not summoned to serve as the second witness to the betrothal demonstrates that the couple and the other girl were not seeking to create a valid <i>kiddushin</i>. Thus, it seems that one may assume that the girl did not transfer title of the bottle cap to the boy.
Rav Senderovic, however, cogently argues that although Rav Moshe’s responsum addresses the Rosh’s <i>anan sahadei</i>, it does not solve a different problem that distinguishes our case. Rav Moshe speaks of transferring ownership of a ring that one wishes to have returned to him. In such a case, it is reasonable to assume that one who is not learned would not be transferring title, since he knows of no such requirement. In our case, however, the girl gave the boy an item (the bottle cap) worth so little that she had no interest in having it returned to her. Thus, there is no reason not to assume that the girl transferred title of the bottle cap to the boy, even if she was unaware of the requirement for the ring to belong to the <i>chatan</i>.
Rav Senderovic cites <i>Tosafot</i> (Gittin 20b s.v. <i>Ishah</i>), in their comments to the Gemara’s discussion of the requirement that the husband own the <i>get</i> he gives his wife, as a precedent for such a distinction. The Gemara explores whether one may assume that the wife transferred title of her writing tablet (on which the <i>get</i> was written) to the husband. The Gemara suggests that one might assume that the woman was not aware of the need to transfer title of the tablet to the husband, since she presumably was unaware of the requirement that the <i>get</i> belong to the husband, and therefore did not transfer the tablet. <i>Tosafot</i>, in turn, explain that this concern applies only to items that one normally lends; a woman certainly intends to transfer title of a gift that she does not expect to be returned to her. By the same logic, the girl in our case certainly meant to transfer the bottle cap to the boy.
<b>One Witness at a Wedding</b>
One might wonder why this incident constituted any problem whatsoever given that there was only one witness at the ceremony. Any wedding must be observed by two witnesses, in accordance with the principle <i>ein davar sheb’ervah pachot mi’shnayim</i> (at least two witnesses are necessary to alter one’s personal status), which appears many times in the Gemara (such as Gittin 2b). Accordingly, it is surprising to discover that the Gemara (Kiddushin 65a-b) discusses the possibility of a marriage being valid even if there is only one witness present.
The Gemara first cites Shmuel, who insists (as we would expect) that if there is only one witness at the <i>kiddushin</i>, the marriage is invalid, but the Gemara proceeds to cite Rav Yehudah, who is unsure about this point. After the Gemara discusses the matter further, suggesting and rejecting three proofs that we must be concerned for one witness, it queries, “<i>Mai havei alah</i>,” “What is the final resolution of this matter?” The response is that the matter is not resolved, since Rav Kahana rules in accordance with Shmuel, while Rav Papa rules in accordance with Rav Yehudah.
The simplest explanation for the concern for validity in such a case is that this is a stringency imposed by rabbinic law; biblical law clearly requires a minimum of two witnesses for the <i>kiddushin</i> to be valid. Indeed, the <i>Otzar Haposkim</i> (42:21:2) presents a long list of <i>Acharonim </i>who rule that even Rav Papa believes that this is only a rabbinic stringency. Some <i>poskim</i>, though, believe this to be a biblical-level concern.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">For further explanation for Rav Papa’s opinion, see <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (E.H. 42:20) and <i>Otzar Haposkim</i> (42:21:1).</i>
The <i>Rishonim</i> are divided as to how to resolve the Gemara’s dispute. The <i>Tur</i> (E.H. 42) states, as the primary opinion, that the betrothal ceremony is invalid, but he cites the <i>Semag</i>’s (<i>Aseih</i> 48) ruling that since the Gemara did not resolve the issue, we must rule strictly. The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Hamekadeish</i>) cites the Rambam (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:6), the Rif (Kiddushin 28a), and the Rosh (Kiddushin 3:13), who also rule that the <i>kiddushin</i> is invalid, and the <i>Otzar Haposkim</i> (42:2:18) likewise cites a very long list of <i>Rishonim</i> who agree with this view. Indeed, the <i>Noda Biy’huda</i> (2 E.H. 75, cited in the Pitchei Teshuvah E.H. 42:6) observes that the overwhelming consensus is that such a ceremony is invalid. However, the <i>Beit Yosef</i> also cites the aforementioned <i>Semag</i>, who cites the <i>Sefer Yerei’im</i> (167) as a precedent for his ruling.
This matter is not fully resolved in the Shulchan Aruch either. While the Shulchan Aruch (E.H. 42:2) and the Vilna Gaon (Bei’ur Hagra E.H. 42:9) rule that the <i>kiddushin</i> is invalid, the Rama (E.H. 42:2) notes that some are strict about this matter. The Rama concludes that it is proper to be strict (i.e. require a <i>get</i>) except in extraordinary circumstances, which is the ruling accepted by Ashkenazic Jews as authoritative (<i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> E.H. 42:20). Thus, if the husband is cooperative,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband does not cooperate, there is more room for leniency. For instance, in a case I dealt with involving a recalcitrant husband who refused to give a <i>get</i> but whose wedding attendees included only one observant Jew (the marriage was performed by a Conservative rabbi), Rav Gedalia Schwartz ruled that the wife could remarry without a <i>get</i>.</i> as in our case, a <i>get</i> is required in order to satisfy the strict opinions despite the absence of a second witness.
However, Rav Akiva Eiger (<i>Teshuvot Rav Akiva Eiger</i> 2:55) rules that a <i>get</i> would not be required if there is an additional substantive doubt as to the validity of the <i>kiddushin</i>. The <i>Otzar Haposkim</i> (42:21:4) cites an extensive list of <i>Acharonim</i> who agree. This leniency is based in part on a <i>s’feik s’feika</i> (double doubt): perhaps a betrothal ceremony conducted in the presence of only one witness is invalid, and even if it is valid, the additional case-specific doubt indicates that this <i>kiddushin</i> might be invalid anyway. The additional doubt also allows for the lenient argument that since, according to most <i>Acharonim</i>, even those who rule strictly regarding one witness do so only on a rabbinic level, a <i>safeik</i> brings the case into the general category of <i>safeik d’rabbanan l’kula</i> (one may rule leniently about a doubt regarding a matter of rabbinic law).
On this basis, Rav Senderovic rules that the boy and girl in our case do not require a <i>get</i>. He notes that there are no fewer than three doubts even beyond the issue of a lone witness’s validity: whether the bottle cap was worth a <i>perutah</i>, whether the omission of a statement from the boy invalidates the <i>kiddushin</i>, and whether the witness ever saw the delivery of the bottle cap. Accordingly, Rav Senderovic rules that the boy and girl do not require a <i>get</i>.
<b>Minor Status of the Boy, Girl, and Witness</b>
Rav Senderovic proposes another lenient consideration to bolster his ruling. He notes the basic Halachah that the <i>chatan</i>, <i>kallah</i>, and witnesses must be adults in order for the <i>kiddushin</i> to be valid (Shulchan Aruch E.H. 43:1). Although this may seem irrelevant to our case, since the boy and witness were both nearly fourteen and the girl was nearly thirteen, one is actually not considered an adult according to Halachah merely at age twelve or thirteen. Physical maturity, in the form of the appearance of two pubic hairs, is the determining factor. We do generally say that one becomes an adult at <i>bar</i>/<i>bat mitzvah</i>, since Rava (Niddah 48b) articulates a principle (known as <i>chazakah d’rava</i>) that one may assume that the two hairs have appeared by the age of <i>bar</i>/<i>bat mitzvah</i>. However, <i>poskim</i> (Magen Avraham 39:1, Mishnah Berurah 271:3, and <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> O.C.199:4) caution that we may rely on <i>chazakah d’rava</i> to validate teenage youngsters only for rabbinic requirements, not for biblical ones. Thus, we permit <i>bar mitzvah</i> boys to serve as <i>shlichei tzibbur</i> (leaders of communal prayer), because the <i>shliach tzibbur</i> executes responsibilities (e.g. the repetition of the <i>Amidah</i>) that are of rabbinic nature. However, these boys cannot serve as witnesses to a wedding or a <i>get</i>, recite <i>kiddush</i> on behalf of adults, or adjust a knot of a person’s <i>tefillin </i>until it is evident that they have shown signs of physical maturity (see, for example, Shulchan Aruch E.H. 141:25 and Shulchan Aruch C.M. 35:1). This is because we harbor doubts as to whether the individual is among the minority that does not physically mature by the age of <i>bar</i>/<i>bat mitzvah</i>.
Accordingly, if any one of those present – the boy, the witness, or the girl – was not physically mature, the ceremony would be invalid. Although this is certainly insufficient grounds to justify a lenient ruling alone, it might serve as an additional consideration to support one. Rav Senderovic suggests, though, that this may not constitute a valid consideration, since it might be only a rabbinic law that for biblical matters we do not rely on <i>chazakah d’rava</i>. In other words, it is possible that on a biblical level we do rely on the assumption that most of those who have reached the age of <i>bar</i>/<i>bat mitzvah</i> have attained physical maturity.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">On a biblical level, we are permitted to rely on <i>rov</i> (majority), but on a rabbinic level, we must be concerned with significant minorities as well (see Rashi to Chullin 12a s.v. <i>Pesach</i>).</i> Thus, it is questionable whether this consideration can be marshaled as legitimate ancillary support for a lenient ruling.
<b>Conclusion</b>
Rav Senderovic was able to reach a favorable outcome due to a fortunate confluence of factors: the presence of only one witness, the possibility that the witness did not see the transfer of the bottle cap, the possibility that the bottle cap may not have been worth a <i>perutah</i>, and the boy’s failure to speak before his delivery of the bottle cap.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">For other possible considerations for a lenient ruling in such cases, see <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> (E.H. 9:20-21). For a ruling regarding a case where a man presented a situation thirty years after he (as a young man) gave a ring to a young woman who has since married someone else and has had children, see <i>Teshuvot V’hanhagot</i> (4:291). </i> Many similar situations, however, do not transpire so conveniently and do require <i>gittin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See Teshuvot Igrot Moshe (E.H. 4:79:1) and <i>Teshuvot Shaarei Ezra</i> (1:104) for examples. </i> Even though it seems far-fetched, at first glance, to attach any validity to such frivolous ceremonies, Halachah is actually quite stringent when it comes to matters of personal status. <i>Poskim</i> are averse to risking any slight violation of these <i>halachot</i>, which are of paramount importance.
It is crucial that we take marriage very seriously and never countenance such outrageous behavior. Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 1:84) even urges the caretakers of the youngsters involved in such incidents to punish them for their behavior, despite the fact that in the specific case he adjudicated, he determined that no <i>get</i> was necessary. If such a situation unfortunately does occur, an eminent Halachic authority must be consulted to adjudicate.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Mordechai Willig told me that unfortunately, he has been presented quite a number of such cases for adjudication, and he submits the cases to Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg for a ruling. </i>

Limitations on Honoring Parents and Minhagim

In today’s socially integrated <i>Am Yisrael</i>, one of the most complex yet common dilemmas is that of conflicting <i>minhagim</i> (customs). Of course, children must generally attempt to follow their parents’ <i>minhagim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Refer to the beginning of our next chapter for a discussion of why children should keep their parents’ <i>minhagim</i>.</i> but exactly what this entails can be difficult to determine, given different family circumstances. One such question was brought before Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv (<i>Kovetz Teshuvot</i> 1:12), whose <i>teshuvah</i> (responsum) on the parameters of following family traditions we shall discuss in this chapter.
<b>The Case in Question</b>
A man whose non-observant Sephardic parents raised him in an Ashkenazic environment approached Rav Eliashiv with the following dilemma. Despite his Sephardic background, his parents had sent him to Ashkenazic religious schools and synagogues, leading him to follow the Ashkenazic tradition in all matters. Then, when the son was approximately thirty-five years old, his father had returned to his roots and become a fully observant Jew in accordance with Sephardic tradition. The father had demanded that the son return to his Sephardic roots as well, but the son had found this very difficult after following Ashkenazic practice for so long. Now, as the son was planning a wedding for his eldest child, the father insisted that the wedding be conducted according to Sephardic practice, and he even threatened to boycott the wedding if it was not.
The son presented Rav Eliashiv with two questions. First, was he permitted to continue observing the Torah in accordance with Ashkenazic tradition? Second, would Halachah require him to obey his father’s demands under the <i>mitzvot </i>of <i>kibbud</i> and <i>mora av</i> (honoring and revering one’s father)?
<b>Minhagim</b>
Rav Eliashiv begins by emphasizing the importance of abiding by one’s family <i>minhagim</i>. The Gemara (Pesachim 50b) insists that one abide by his family customs even when it is difficult to do so. For example, Rav Eliashiv writes that an Ashkenazic Jew may not change his method of pronunciation to Sephardic or modern Israeli pronunciation; rather, he must recite his prayers using the pronunciation of his ancestors.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Not all authorities agree with Rav Eliashiv on this specific point. Rav Yehuda Amital reports that his wife’s grandfather, Rav Isser Zalman Meltzer, permitted changing to modern Israeli pronunciation. Common practice among Ashkenazic students of Yeshivot Hesder is to follow Rav Isser Zalman’s ruling. Rav Avraham Yitzchak Hakohen Kook (<i>Teshuvot Orach Mishpat</i> 17), though, disagrees with Rav Isser Zalman. For further discussion of this issue, see <i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Uzziel</i> (O.C. 1), <i>Teshuvot Seridei Eish</i> (2:5), <i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> (O.C. 3:5), and Rav Kook’s letter of approbation for <i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Uzziel</i>.</i>
Rav Eliashiv continues, though, that the prohibition against changing <i>minhagim </i>is not without exception, as demonstrated by a ruling of the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (<i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer</i> C.M. 188). The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> was approached by members of a town where two <i>kehillot </i>(communities), one Sephardic and one Ashkenazic, formerly had functioned. However, a pogrom had caused most of the Jews to leave, and since the remaining populace could not sustain two separate <i>minyanim</i>, the two groups now had to combine into one functioning synagogue. The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> ruled that the remaining members of the community should choose which of the two synagogues would continue to function, whose <i>minhagim</i> they then would follow. Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer </i>6 O.C. 10) cites numerous authorities who concur with the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>’s ruling.
The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> reasons that one may change from practicing <b>all</b> Ashkenazic traditions to practicing <b>all </b>Sephardic traditions and vice versa. Just as a non-Jew who converts to Judaism fully integrates into the Jewish community, so too may an Ashkenazic Jew fully integrate into a Sephardic community and vice versa. Rav Yosef Eliyahu Henkin (<i>Eidut L’yisrael </i>p. 162) similarly rules that if an Ashkenazic Jew decides to join a Sephardic community permanently, he may change his <i>nusach hatefillah</i> (liturgy) to the Sephardic one. He notes that historically, the entire Chassidic community changed from <i>nusach Ashkenaz</i> to <i>nusach Sefard</i> with the noble intention of praying in accordance with the mystical teachings of the Arizal.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> (6 O.C. 10) for a summary of the rich <i>teshuvah</i> literature regarding the legitimacy of the Chassidim’s change of <i>nusach</i>. Some “<i>mitnagdic</i>” <i>poskim</i>, such as <i>Teshuvot Sho’eil Umeishiv </i>(3:1:247) and <i>Teshuvot Maharam Schick </i>(O.C. 43), wrote that the change was illegitimate and violates the obligation not to “abandon the teachings of [one’s] mother” (see Pesachim 50b). Other <i>poskim</i> (especially Chassidic <i>poskim</i>), such as <i>Teshuvot Divrei Chaim</i> (2 O.C. 8), defended the change, and as Rav Henkin notes, this opinion has emerged as the accepted view. Rav Henkin cautions, though, that it is forbidden to make such a change arbitrarily. </i> Of course, one should consult with a Rav before deviating from any family practice, as great caution must be exercised before deviating from practices observed by one’s ancestors for generations.
This also explains the rulings of twentieth-century authorities (for example, <i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe </i>O.C. 1:158 and <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 5 O.C. 37) that Ashkenazic women who marry Sephardic men or vice versa, a relatively new phenomenon, should follow the husband’s traditional family practices.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This issue is discussed at greater length in our next chapter.</i> Although doing so entails deviating from the wife’s family tradition, these <i>poskim</i> apparently believe, as the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> asserts, that a Jew may fully integrate into the practices of a different Jewish community.
Based on this, Rav Eliashiv rules regarding the case addressed to him that from the vantage point of <i>minhag</i>, the son may continue to practice Torah in accordance with Ashkenazic tradition despite his Sephardic ancestry. Rav Hershel Schachter similarly rules that if someone was raised in a non-Chassidic community, he need not practice Chassidic <i>minhagim</i> even if his paternal grandfather was Chassidic (see <i>Beit Yitzchak</i> 39:520). Indeed, many of us pronounce Hebrew differently from our fathers or paternal grandfathers.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Many of the communities in which we reside do not pronounce Hebrew in the Chassidic style. This is an example of community practice prevailing over family custom. For a discussion of how to resolve conflicts between community and family customs, see Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg’s <i>Darchei Hapsak</i> (p. 24 note 44).</i>
We must emphasize that exceptional situations notwithstanding, it is imperative to follow the practices of one’s family and community (see, for example Shulchan Aruch O.C. 468:4 and <i>Mishnah Berurah ibid</i>. 14). In fact, Rav Ovadia Yosef would likely disagree with Rav Eliashiv’s ruling for this very reason, especially if the son lived in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Rav Ovadia (<i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer </i>6 O.C. 10:4 and <i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at </i>5:33; see also <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 5 O.C. 37) laments the choice of Ashkenazic Jews in Israel to maintain their Ashkenazic practices instead of acknowledging that the Rambam and Rav Yosef Karo are the halachic authorities of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Though he reluctantly yields to the Israeli Ashkenazim’s adherence to their traditional customs, he would probably instruct anyone of Sephardic origin who lives in Israel to follow Sephardic practice.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">For a defense of the Ashkenazic Jews in Israel retaining their <i>minhagim</i>, see <i>Chazon Ish</i> (<i>Shevi’it</i> 23:5), Rav Yechiel Michel Tukachinsky’s <i>Ir Hakodesh V’hamikdash</i> (3:24), <i>Pe’at Hashulchan</i> (3:11), and Rav Elyakim (Getsel) Ellinson’s <i>Ish V’ishto</i> (pp. 24-25 note 31).</i>
<i><b>Kibbud</b></i><b> and <i>Mora Av</i></b>
Rav Eliashiv proceeds to discuss whether the son must honor his father’s demand that he follow Sephardic practice. This question hinges on a classic debate concerning the character and scope of the <i>mitzvah </i>of <i>kibbud av va’eim</i>. The Ramban (Yevamot 6a s.v. <i>Mah L’hanach</i>), Rashba (<i>ibid</i>. s.v. <i>Mah L’hanach</i>), and Ritva (<i>ibid</i>. s.v. <i>Yachol</i>) define this <i>mitzvah</i> as providing service to one’s parents but do not necessarily include obeying the will of a parent under it. Thus, one is not obligated to obey a parent’s demand if the requested activity does not benefit the parent. The Vilna Gaon (Bei’ur Hagra Y.D. 240:36) notes that <i>Tosafot</i> (Yevamot 6a s.v. <i>Shekein</i> and Kiddushin 32a s.v. <i>Rav Yehudah</i>) agree with this definition of <i>kibbud av va’eim</i>. These <i>Rishonim</i> base their assertion on the description of the <i>mitzvah</i> that appears in the Gemara (Kiddushin 31b), which gives the examples of “providing food and drink, clothing them, and helping them enter and leave a building.” Accordingly, the <i>mitzvah</i> entails only providing service to the parent.
The Vilna Gaon (ad. loc.) believes that the Shulchan Aruch and Rama accept the aforementioned <i>Rishonim</i>’s definition of <i>kibbud av va’eim</i>. The Shulchan Aruch codifies a ruling of the <i>Terumat Hadeshen</i> (40) that if a son wishes to study in a particular Yeshiva, he does not have to honor a parent’s request that he not study at that Yeshiva because it is located in a dangerous area. The Rama, in turn, quotes a ruling of the Maharik (167) that a son is not required to honor a parent’s demand that he refrain from marrying a particular woman. The Vilna Gaon explains that these rulings are based on the definition of the <i>mitzvah</i> of honoring parents as servicing them but not necessarily obeying them.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See, however, <i>Teshuvot Meishiv Davar</i> (2:50), wherein the Netziv rules that this does not apply if it will cause disparagement to the parents.</i> Rav Eliashiv notes that according to this approach, the son would not be obligated to accede to his father’s demand that he practice the Sephardic tradition.
The <i>Sefer Hamakneh </i>(Kiddushin 31b s.v. <i>Tanu Rabbanan Eizehu</i>), on the other hand, rules that the <i>mitzvah</i> of <i>mora av va’eim </i>(revering parents) requires one to obey a parent’s request even if it will not benefit the parent. Indeed, the Gemara (Kiddushin 31b) states that this <i>mitzvah</i> forbids a child to contradict his parents’ words, which, the <i>Sefer Hamakneh </i>believes, includes disobeying the parent’s orders. The <i>Sefer Hamakneh </i>also believes, contrary to the Vilna Gaon’s assertion, that the Rama actually supports this approach. The Rama presents a specific situation – when a parent demands the child not marry a specific woman – in which he rules that the child is not required to abide by the parent’s demand. Had the Rama agreed with the Ramban, Rashba, Ritva, and <i>Tosafot</i>, he should have presented a general rule that one need not obey a parent’s request if it is not intended to benefit the parent. Since he does not, his ruling must be specific to situations of marriage for the reasons that the Maharik outlines.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For example, the <i>Maharik</i> argues that this demand causes the child to neglect his obligation to marry and have children. </i> According to the <i>Sefer Hamakneh</i>’s approach, it would seem that the son must obey his father’s demand that he abide by Sephardic tradition.
Rav Eliashiv notes, however, that even the <i>Sefer Hamakneh </i>does not require obedience when it would cause a loss to the child. Whereas ignoring a parent’s request without good reason constitutes a lack of reverence for the parent, ignoring the request out of concern for loss does not. Since, in the case Rav Eliashiv addresses, it would be very disruptive for the son’s family to change its halachic lifestyle so significantly, Rav Eliashiv rules that the son is not required to honor his father’s request that he do so.
<b>Conclusion</b>
The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Mamrim </i>6:8), Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 240:19), <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 240:42), and <i>Teshuvot Seridei Eish </i>(3:95) urge the parents of grown children to refrain from imposing unnecessary and burdensome demands on their children. Parents should help their children by not making it excessively difficult for them to fulfill the <i>mitzvot </i>of <i>kibbud </i>and <i>mora av va’eim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">For examples of when a child is permitted to disregard a parent’s order, see Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 240:25) and <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 240:36 and 45).</i> On the other hand, children must do their utmost to properly fulfill these <i>mitzvot</i>, which the Rambam (ad. loc. 6:1) greatly extols and the Gemara (Kiddushin 30b) compares to honoring and revering Hashem. Given that the Torah, in a rare occurrence, states the reward for this <i>mitzvah</i> of honoring parents (Shemot 20:12), it is very advisable to consider what is at stake when dealing with these matters.

Divergent Family Customs between Husband and Wife

The obligation to abide by the halachic practices of our families is best illustrated by the following Talmudic passage (Pesachim 50b):
“<i>Bnei Byshan</i>” had a practice not to travel from Tyre to Sidon on Friday (to avoid detracting from their preparations for Shabbat - Rashi). Their children posed the following question to Rabi Yochanan: “Our fathers were able to abide by this stringent practice because they were wealthy. We, however, find it economically cumbersome to abide by this stringency. Are we obligated to maintain their practice?” Rabi Yochanan answered, “Your ancestors accepted it upon themselves [and you must follow your fathers’ custom], as the <i>pasuk</i> states, “Listen, my son, to the teachings of your father, and do not abandon the Torah of your mother” (Mishlei 1:8).
A question arises, though, when a husband and wife have divergent family <i>minhagim</i>: whose <i>minhagim</i> should be followed? In this chapter, we will review both published and unpublished responsa on this topic.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For a thorough review of this topic and the responsa written about it before 1981, see Rav Menachem Slei’s essay in <i>Noam</i> (23:145-180).</i>
<b>Whose Customs?</b>
A major dispute exists regarding the obligation to follow established customs. The <i>Teshuvot Chavot Ya’ir</i> (126), after establishing that this obligation is rabbinic in nature, asserts that it applies only to community <i>minhagim</i> (practices). However, children are not bound to follow the personal stringencies adopted by their parents. This is evident from the Gemara (Chullin 105a) that records that Mar Ukva did not observe his father’s stringency to avoid dairy for twenty-four hours after consuming meat. If children were bound by their parents’ stringencies, Mar Ukva would have been required to continue his father’s practice. Apparently, then, the Gemara that requires children not to abandon their parents’ practices applies only to communal <i>minhagim</i>. This understanding of the story of <i>Bnei Byshan</i> is endorsed by the Gilyon Maharsha (Y.D. 214:2).
On the other hand, the Pitchei Teshuvah (Y.D. 214:5) cites the <i>Teshuvot Zichron Yosef</i> (Y.D. 14), who disputes the contention of the <i>Chavot Ya’ir</i>. He maintains that the story of <i>Bnei Byshan</i> refers to family practices as well. According to this approach, the reason why Mar Ukva was not obligated to observe his father’s stringency was not because there is no such thing as a binding family practice, but rather because he himself never practiced it.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The critical time for evaluating <i>minhagim</i> is when the child becomes independent (<i>Teshuvot Maharam Schick</i> O.C. 259). Rav Shmuel Wosner (<i>Teshuvot Sheivet Halevi </i>6:59) writes that such discretion regarding whether one should follow his father’s practices does not apply to practices of one’s “tribe,” meaning the accepted practices of Sephardic and Ashkenazic Jews. Thus, a Sephardic Jew cannot decide not to recite <i>selichot</i> during the month of <i>Elul</i>, and an Ashkenazic Jew cannot absolve himself of the custom to avoid <i>kitniyot</i> (legumes) during <i>Pesach</i> (see <i>Gray Matter 1</i> pp. 239-254). </i> If, however, Mar Ukva had ever practiced his father’s stringency, he would have been bound to it for his entire life. <sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">There is some dispute regarding how many times a child must observe a parent’s practice in order for it to become binding. The <i>Pri Chadash </i>(<i>Kuntress Haminhagim</i> 7) seems to obligate the child to maintain his family’s practice even if he kept it just one time, whereas the <i>Matteh Efraim</i> (589) appears to require the child to uphold the family custom three times in order for it to become binding.</i>
The issue of communal <i>minhagim</i> has been complicated by contemporary circumstances. As Rav Moshe Feinstein writes (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> O.C. 1:159), “Here in New York and Brooklyn, where people have gathered from many different communities where there were divergent <i>minhagim</i>, everyone should follow the <i>minhagim</i> of the place he came from.” People are resistant to establishing one custom in the United States, as evidenced by a visit to most synagogues on <i>chol hamo’eid</i>, where one will find some men wearing <i>tefillin</i> and others who are not.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Teshuvot Sho’el B’shlomo</i> 30 for a discussion and partial defense of this practice.</i> In many communities, the situation of “two courts in one city” (Yevamot 14a) prevails, as people retain many of the customs of the communities that their parents and grandparents came from. Thus, even according to the <i>Chavot Ya’ir</i>, today, when communal <i>minhagim</i> have not fully emerged in many communities, family practices have to a certain extent replaced them.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Hershel Schachter (<i>Beit Yitzchak</i> 39:516) notes that some <i>minhagim</i> are communal and some are familial; however, it sometimes is difficult to determine the category in which a minhag belongs. For example, the practice of Jews from Germany is to wait only three hours between meat and milk. Rav Schachter believes that this is a communal practice and that one who does not reside in a German-Jewish community should follow the practice of most Jews to wait six hours between milk and meat. Rav Yeshaya Siff and Rav Mordechai Willig, however, told me that they believe this to be a familial custom and that Jews from Germany may continue to wait only three hours between meat and milk even though they do not live in a German-Jewish community. </i> Accordingly, when husbands and wives come from families that came from different communities, whose <i>minhagim</i> should be followed - the husband’s or the wife’s?
<b>The <i>Tashbeitz</i>’s Responsum</b>
In earlier times, it was unusual for people to marry someone who lived far away. Hence, there was little chance of divergent family customs between husband and wife. The development of modern means of transportation and the mass migration movements of the past century facilitated marriages between Jews of different backgrounds. Therefore, many twentieth-century authorities addressed this issue without the benefit of explicit sources in the writings of earlier <i>poskim</i>. Moreover, in pre-war communities, the principle of community custom (<i>minhag hamakom</i>) prevailed, and the question of whose family customs should be followed was almost moot, as the couple would follow the practices of whichever community they elected to reside in.
In one of the few responsa on this topic published before the twentieth century, the <i>Tashbeitz</i> (3:179) presents two reasons why the wife should adopt her husband’s customs. First, it would be highly disruptive if both the husband and the wife were to maintain their respective, conflicting family practices. For example, if the husband is Sephardic and the wife is Ashkenazic, the husband would eat <i>kitniyot</i> (legumes) on <i>Pesac</i>h and the wife would not. It would be extremely difficult for the husband and the wife to abide by two different standards of kashrut. Second, the <i>Tashbeitz</i> invokes the Talmudic principle “<i>ishto k’gufo</i>” (lit. his wife is like his own body; see Sanhedrin 28b and <i>Encyclopedia Talmudit</i> 2:300-301). Because we consider a husband and wife as one person, the Gemara states that one is disqualified from testifying about his wife’s relatives just as one is disqualified from testifying about his own relatives. The <i>Tashbeitz</i> understands that based on this principle, the wife should adopt her husband’s family traditions.
The <i>Tashbeitz</i> also writes that even after the husband dies, the wife should continue practicing her husband’s family customs if the couple has children and she has not remarried. He bases this assertion on the Torah’s laws regarding a woman whose father is not a <i>kohen</i> eating <i>terumah</i> (<i>kohen</i>’s tithe; see Vayikra 22:11-13). If her husband is a <i>kohen</i>, she may eat <i>terumah</i> even after his death if the couple has children and she has not remarried.
<b>The Responsa of Rav Feinstein, Rav Felder, Dayan Weisz, and Rav Yosef</b>
Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> O.C. 1:158), Rav Gedalia Felder (<i>Yesodei Yeshurun</i> 6:239-240), Rav Yitzchak Yaakov Weisz (<i>Teshuvot Minchat Yitzchak</i> 4:83), and Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 5 O.C. 37) rule that the wife must adopt the halachic customs of her husband. As a precedent, they cite the Mishnah Berurah’s (468:19, and see Bei’ur Halachah 468:4 s.v. <i>Haholeich</i>) ruling that if one permanently relocates to a community whose halachic practices differ from those of the community which he left, he should adopt the new community’s customs. This rule applies whether the new community’s customs are stricter or more lenient than the old community’s.
These authorities explain that Halachah views a woman who marries as moving to a new community — her husband’s home. Rav Moshe cites a number of biblical verses that demonstrate that the Torah views marriage as a woman moving into her husband’s home. For example, the Torah (Devarim 24:1), in describing the procedure for a divorce, states that the husband “shall send her from his home.” We see that the Torah considers a wife to be in her husband’s domain during the marriage. Accordingly, the wife must accept the customs of her husband’s family. Rav Ovadia and Rav Felder also cite the <i>Tashbeitz</i> as a precedent for their ruling.
Reflecting the rulings of Rav Moshe, Rav Felder, Dayan Weisz, and Rav Ovadia, wives commonly accept their husbands’ family traditions.
<b>Rav Ovadia Yosef’s Major Limitation of this Rule</b>
Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Ohr Torah</i>, <i>Iyar</i> 5761 and <i>Yalkut Yosef</i> O.C. 318:12), though, imposes a major limitation on the rule that the wife must adopt her husband’s halachic practices. He writes that this rule applies only to practices that the husband’s family has practiced for generations, such as refraining from <i>kitniyot</i>. However, the wife is not obligated to adopt the stringent practices (<i>chumrot)</i> that her husband accepted upon himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This recalls the ruling of the <i>Chavot Ya’ir</i> that the obligation not to abandon established practices does not apply to personal stringencies adopted by parents. Rav Ovadia says essentially the same thing regarding personal stringencies of a husband.</i> For instance, if the husband accepted the stringent level of <i>shemittah</i> observance by refusing to rely on the <i>hetter mechirah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the practice of selling farmland in Israel to non-Jews so as to circumvent the restrictions of <i>shemittah</i>. For a more in-depth discussion of the validity of this practice, see the essays on the topic available at www.koltorah.org.</i> the wife is not required to abide by this stringency.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">We should note that Rav Ovadia is following his approach (<i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 3 Y.D. 19:7) that the <i>hetter mechirah</i> is essentially acceptable. Many <i>poskim</i>, however, regard it as possibly invalid (e.g. Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach, <i>Teshuvot Minchat Shlomo</i> 1:44) or completely invalid (e.g. Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv, cited by Rav Yosef Efrat, <i>Mesorah</i> 1:72).</i> Rav Ovadia writes that if the husband is unable to maintain his strict practice due to his wife’s lack of cooperation, the husband should seek <i>hatarat nedarim</i> (annulment of vows) for his stringency.
Another common application of this ruling is that if the husband avoids relying on communal <i>eruvin</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Gray Matter 1</i> pp. 165-199 for a discussion of the laws of communal <i>eruvin</i> and why some people do not rely on them.</i> the wife is not bound to follow this stringency. Similarly, if the husband accepts the stringency to observe Shabbat according to Rabbeinu Tam’s standards of assessing when nighttime begins,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">For a review of the debate between Rabbeinu Tam and the <i>Geonim</i> as to when the halachic night begins, see the essay about counting the <i>omer</i> during <i>bein hashmashot</i> (twilight) available at www.koltorah.org.</i> she does not have to abide by this stringency.
It appears that common practice reflects Rav Ovadia’s ruling on this matter. For example, Rav Moshe Snow told me that although Rav Moshe Feinstein himself adopted the strict approach to <i>chalav yisrael</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See our later chapter about <i>chalav yisrael</i> for a more in-depth discussion of the issues involved.</i> Rebbetzin Feinstein did not. Indeed, Rav Moshe writes (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 2:12) that a husband “cannot impose his stringencies on her.”
<b>Rav Yehuda Henkin’s Responsum</b>
Rav Yehuda Henkin writes (<i>Teshuvot Bnei Banim</i> 3:29) that although the common practice is for wives to accept their husband’s family customs, in accordance with the aforementioned rulings, there is some room for flexibility. Rav Henkin challenges the fundamental assumption of Rav Moshe, Rav Felder, Dayan Weisz, and Rav Ovadia’s ruling. They assume that the Torah believes that, metaphysically speaking, a wife moves into the home of her husband. Rav Henkin, though, notes the dispute between Rabbeinu Tam and the Maharam of Rothenberg regarding whether a wife must move to the husband’s town or vice versa (see <i>Tur</i> E.H. 75). The Rama (E.H. 75:1) and the Beit Shmuel (<i>ibid</i>. 7) rule that Halacha takes cognizance of Rabbeinu Tam’s ruling that the husband must move to the wife’s town.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Elchanan Grunwald argues that the Rama and Beit Shmuel do not accept Rabbeinu Tam’s ruling. Rather, they merely assert that the wife cannot be forced to move into the husband’s town, as she has a strong opinion (i.e. Rabbeinu Tam) to rely upon. Perhaps even the Rama and Beit Shmuel agree that ideally the wife should move to the husband’s town. Indeed, even Rav Henkin notes that most <i>Rishonim</i> disagree with Rabbeinu Tam concerning this matter. </i> If the husband moves to the wife’s town, observes Rav Henkin, then he will be required to observe the local halachic practices — those of his wife’s family.
Rav Henkin also asserts that the <i>pesukim </i>cited by Rav Moshe do not necessarily constitute a halachic statement that the marital home belongs to the husband. It could be that the Torah merely reflects the sociological reality of the time, in which the husband controlled the marital home and, upon divorce, the wife left the home. Rav Henkin believes that the Torah does not preclude joint-ownership in either an economic<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See our later chapters about marital financial arrangements for a more thorough analysis of this issue.</i>or a metaphysical sense.
Moreover, Rav Henkin argues that the responsum of the <i>Tashbeitz</i> does not constitute a relevant precedent because it runs counter to the ruling of the Rama. Rav Henkin observes that the <i>Tashbeitz</i> (1:97 and 3:87 s.v. <i>V’hasomeich</i>) himself rejects Rabbeinu Tam’s ruling that the man must move to the wife’s town. It follows that the Rama, who does consider Rabbeinu Tam’s ruling, would disagree with the <i>Tashbeitz</i>.
Rav Henkin concludes that we should not abandon the accepted practice for wives to follow their husbands’ family traditions. However, he rules that a wife may continue to follow her family’s traditions regarding a matter that does not impinge on her relationship with her husband and does not impose a hardship on her.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">These two areas are considered within the husband’s domain, as it is in these areas that he has the right to cancel her vows (<i>hafarat nedarim</i>; see Bemidbar 30:7-17 and Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 234:55).</i> Rav Henkin requires that she stipulate with her fiancée before the marriage that she wants to continue to practice her own family’s traditions. We should note, however, that Rav Hershel Schachter (<i>Beit Yitzchak</i> 39:519) confirms the rulings of Rav Feinstein, Rav Felder, Dayan Weisz, and Rav Ovadia that the wife enters the domain of the husband “since this is the essence of marriage – the wife entering the domain of the husband.”
<b>Other Situations</b>
Rav Henkin also writes (in an unpublished responsum) that if the husband is a convert or a <i>ba’al teshuvah</i> and the wife’s family has a longer chain of halachic observance, the husband may adopt his wife’s family traditions. He writes, though, that it might be more appropriate for the husband to follow the prevalent traditions of the community to which he belongs.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This is appropriate only in a locale that has communal <i>minhagim</i>.</i> Rav Henkin counsels that the husband seek the guidance of his Rav in choosing the most appropriate approach to this issue. Rav Hershel Schachter (ad. loc.) writes that if the wife’s family is observant and the husband’s family is not, it makes sense to say that the husband is joining her family as far as <i>minhagim</i> are concerned. As such, the wife’s family’s <i>minhagim</i> should be observed.
Rav Chaim David Halevi (<i>Techumin</i> 6:84) rules that in a marriage where the wife is observant and the husband is not, the family should practice the wife’s family’s traditions. Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg told me that he agrees with this ruling.
Rav Zalman Nechemia also notes (<i>Darchei Hapsak</i> pp. 27-30) that if a couple lives in the same community as the wife’s parents and the husband attends the same synagogue as his father-in-law, he should follow the <i>minhagim</i> of his wife. For example, if an Ashkenazic man marries a Syrian-Jewish woman and moves to the Syrian section of Brooklyn, New York or Deal, New Jersey and joins a Syrian community, he should practice Syrian-Jewish <i>minhagim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Zalman Nechemia cites the Gemara (Eruvin 41a), <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>ibid</i>. s.v. <i>Mibnei</i>), and <i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer</i> (C.M. 188) as the basis for this ruling. Rav Zalman Nechemia stresses that it is not a question of whether to follow the practices of the husband or the wife, but rather depends on which community the couple resides in. The community’s <i>minhagim</i> determine the practice of the couple. Nonetheless, Rav Zalman Nechemia’s approach applies only to communities where there are established communal practices. </i>
In addition, Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach (cited in <i>Ma’adanei Shlomo</i> p. 17 and <i>Yom Tov Sheini K’hilchato</i> p. 188) rules that a wife must follow her husband’s <i>minhagim</i> as part of her obligations to him. Accordingly, the husband enjoys the right to waive this obligation, which accounts for the common practice for wives to continue to pray with the <i>nusach</i> (liturgy) they are accustomed to.
Rav Mordechai Willig rules that wives should practice certain aspects of the laws of <i>niddah</i> in accordance with their mothers’ family traditions.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to matters that do not directly affect the husband. An example is whether the wife should immerse once, twice, or three times in the <i>mikvah</i>.</i> Rav Willig also maintains this position regarding how many Shabbat candles a woman lights, arguing that both of these matters are passed down from mother to daughter.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Doniel Neustadt (<i>The Weekly Halachah Discussion</i> 1:6-8) writes that many women follow the practices of their mothers in regard to Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i> candle lighting, and he defends this practice, citing the <i>Teshuvot Ya’avetz</i> (107). </i>
<b>Conclusion</b>
Generally speaking, a wife should follow her husband’s family traditions, such as those regarding <i>kitniyot</i> on <i>Pesach</i>. Nevertheless, there are certain limited circumstances in which a wife may continue to follow her family’s traditions.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Converts and <i>ba’alei teshuvah</i> should consult their Rav regarding which practices they should follow. The absence of community <i>minhagim</i> in most Jewish communities today complicates this question, as most people simply observe many of the practices of the community from which their families came. Rav Moshe Shternbuch (<i>Teshuvot V’hanhagot</i> 1:369) writes that a <i>ba’al teshuvah</i> should follow the <i>minhagim</i> of his teacher, not of his family before it ceased to be observant. </i>

Beit Din

Marital Finances in Light of Contemporary Arrangements

<b>An <i>Erev Pesach</i> Debate</b>
I experienced an eye-opening interaction on <i>erev Pesach</i> 5767. I joined a gathering of rabbis who assembled in the office of a leading Rav in New Jersey for the sale of <i>chametz</i>. At that time, close to the fifth halachic hour of the day (when it becomes forbidden to benefit from <i>chametz</i>), I phoned my wife to remind her to nullify her <i>chametz </i>(<i>bittul chametz</i>). The other rabbis present expressed their astonishment that I asked my wife to perform <i>bittul chametz</i>. Their reaction stemmed from the Shulchan Aruch’s (O.C. 434:4) ruling indicating that a wife recites <i>bittul chametz</i> only in a situation in which her husband did not perform the <i>bittul</i>.
The basis for the Shulchan Aruch’s uncontested approach is that traditionally, the husband was considered the owner of the marital property. This idea is expressed in part by the Talmudic principle (Gittin 77b), “<i>Mah she’kantah ishah kanah ba’alah</i>” (what a woman acquires automatically comes into the ownership of her husband). In fact, the Mishnah Berurah (434:19) writes that in the unusual case in which a wife does proclaim the <i>bittul</i>, she should state that she nullifies “all of the <i>chametz </i>in my husband’s domain”, not “all of the <i>chametz</i> in my domain,” since Halachah regards marital property as belonging solely to the husband.
I responded that this Halachah might be different today, since contemporary society regards marital property as jointly owned.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Of course, this varies from couple to couple and locale to locale. For example, some couples keep their assets in separate accounts, while others keep everything in joint accounts. Some places (such as Israel, many European countries, and some states in the United States) have community property laws in which civil law views assets owned by either spouse as joint marital property, while other places do not have such laws. Still other locales employ equitable distribution laws, which, simply put, are a modified version of community property laws. In addition, these laws do not apply if the couple signed a prenuptial agreement specifying that either party retains ownership of property owned before the marriage. The local civil law regarding the ownership of marital property during a marriage (not only in case of divorce) also must be considered.</i> In general, Halachah takes contemporary practices into consideration in regard to monetary matters (see, for example, Bava Metzia 74a and 83a). With regard to money, there is much more flexibility than in other areas of Halachah, as the Gemara (Bava Metzia 94a) presents the accepted opinion of Rabi Yehudah that “<i>B’davar sheb’mamon tena’o kayam</i>” (a condition in the context of monetary law is valid).” Generally speaking, one may dispose of his money as he sees fit, and one may arrange his monetary affairs even in contradiction to Halachah. A classic example is a lender and borrower who agree that the borrower’s responsibility regarding the borrowed item applies only to the extent of an unpaid watchman (<i>shomer chinam</i>; see Bava Metzia ad. loc. and <i>Gray Matter 2</i> pp. 170-171).
Indeed, Rav Shlomo Dichovsky of Israel’s Supreme Rabbinic Court insists that Halachah recognizes and incorporates Israeli civil community property laws. I reasoned that according to this approach, my wife and I jointly own the <i>chametz</i>, and therefore both of us should preferably recite <i>bittul chametz</i>. Even though <i>bittul chametz</i> can be accomplished by one’s agent (Shulchan Aruch O.C. 434:3), the Gemara (Kiddushin 41a) articulates a principle, “A <i>mitzvah</i> is greater when done by oneself than when done by his agent.” Hence, I remind my wife every year to recite <i>bittul chametz</i>.
The other rabbis assembled (with the notable exception of one veteran Rav who commented that he thinks that I might be correct), on the other hand, replied that they believed that <i>mah she’kantah ishah kanah ba’alah</i> still applies despite the change in contemporary society.
This debate reflects a much larger debate that currently rages among <i>poskim</i> and <i>dayanim</i> as to whether this Talmudic principle applies in the current social circumstances and milieu. In this section, we shall present this debate and its manifold halachic applications, of which <i>bittul chametz</i> is but one of dozens. We shall begin by outlining the basic rules regarding the classic financial relationship of a husband and wife as presented by <i>Chazal</i>.
<b>A Husband’s Obligations and Entitlements</b>
The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 12:1-4) presents ten obligations Halachah demands from a husband, three of which are of biblical origin and seven of which are rabbinic enactments. The three Torah obligations are to provide food, clothes, and intimate relations for his wife (see Shemot 21:10 and Rashi ad. loc.). The seven rabbinic obligations are paying her <i>ketubah </i>money in case of death or divorce, paying her medical bills if she is sick, redeeming her if she is taken captive (a phenomenon not unheard of in the time of the Gemara and in certain countries even today), burying her if she dies, supporting her from his estate and allowing her to live in his house if he predeceases her,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">She does not, though, inherit him. For ways of allowing a wife to inherit, see our later section about setting up halachically valid wills.</i> supporting their daughters from his estate until they marry, and allowing the male children from their marriage to inherit her <i>ketubah</i> (<i>ketubat banin dichrin</i>). Halachah entitles a man to four items from his wife: her earnings (<i>ma’aseih yadayim</i>), whatever lost items she finds (<i>metzi’ah</i>), the income generated by the property that she brings into the marriage during her lifetime (<i>nichsei melog</i>), and the priority to inherit her if she predeceases him.
<i>Chazal</i> also instituted, the Rambam continues, that three of each partner’s respective obligations be formulated as quid pro quo arrangements. The husband is entitled to his wife’s <i>ma’aseih yadayim</i> in exchange for supporting her, to benefit from her <i>nichsei melog</i> in exchange for redeeming her, and to inherit her in exchange for burying her.
<b>The Wife’s Property</b>
Halachah divides a wife’s property into three sections (see Shulchan Aruch E.H. 85 and <i>Pitchei Choshen </i>volume 8). The first part is the <i>nedunyah</i> (dowry), which is categorized as <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> (“iron sheep property”).<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">It is referred to as “iron sheep property” because its value does not vary.</i> The <i>nedunyah</i> is, generally speaking, listed in the <i>ketubah</i> and assigned a value. The husband assumes the financial responsibility for this property, and upon his death or divorce, the <i>nedunyah</i> is returned to the wife, either in its original form or as its assigned value in the <i>ketubah</i>, regardless of whether that value has appreciated or depreciated.
<i>Nichsei melog</i> (“property that is plucked”)<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">It is so called because the husband may “pluck” this property and keep the revenue thereby generated, such as dividends from stocks or rent for an apartment.</i> is all other property that a wife either brings into the marriage (though not listed in the <i>ketubah</i>) or acquires during the marriage. The wife retains title to the <i>nichsei melog</i>, but the husband is entitled to benefit from it during the wife’s lifetime (provided that he does not mismanage it) and potentially inherit it (if she predeceases him).
Additionally, a wife can own her own property in a marriage. This occurs either when she is gifted the money on condition that the husband enjoys no rights to this property or if the husband waives his rights to the property.
<b>The <i>Ketubah</i></b>
<i>Chazal</i> (Ketubot 11a) instituted the <i>ketubah</i>-obligation to be paid in case of divorce “so that it should not be light in his eyes to divorce her.” This concern was particularly relevant in the era prior to the enactment of <i>cheirem d’Rabbeinu Gershon</i>, which forbade a husband to divorce his wife against her will. The <i>ketubah</i> money was intended to be a very significant sum that in practice constituted a significant disincentive to divorce a woman against her will (as is clear from Gittin 58a, Rama E.H. 119:6, and Pitchei Teshuvah E.H. 154:27). The <i>ketubah</i> also is paid if the husband predeceases the wife so that she will have money to live off before she remarries.
As is apparent from a study of <i>Masechet Ketubot</i>, the <i>ketubah</i> also serves as a potent tool for <i>beit din</i> to prod a spouse to behave appropriately. If a husband misbehaves, <i>beit din</i> can warn him that if he does not mend his ways, he will be pressured to divorce his wife and pay her the <i>ketubah</i>. If a wife is not acting properly, she may be warned that if she persists, her husband can divorce her without the <i>ketubah</i> payment.
In case of his death or a divorce in which the wife is not at fault, she receives the <i>nichsei melog</i> she brought into the marriage, the <i>nedunyah</i> itself or its original value as set forth in the <i>ketubah</i>, and the value of <i>ketubah</i> itself (which traditionally was substantial). She also retains, of course, any property designated as her private property. The husband, on the other hand, takes all the money generated in the course of the marriage.
The monetary arrangements between a husband and wife allow for an even distribution of obligations and rights. A man works hard for his family, and thus the family’s financial assets are in his control. A woman needs to focus her attention on the family, and thus she is relieved from the burden of generating income and managing investments to support the family. In case of death or divorce, she is provided for with the provisions of the <i>ketubah</i>.
Even during the course of a marriage, there are ramifications of the fact that Halachah regards the marital property to be in the sole possession of the husband. For instance, a wife is not permitted to donate significant mounts of money to <i>tzedakah</i> without her husband’s consent (Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 248:4), while a husband has no such limitation. In regard to ritual matters, the home and marital property belong to the husband, and for this reason it is the husband who must nullify the <i>chametz</i>.
<b>Contemporary Application</b>
Applying these rules to the contemporary situation is far from simple. The value of the <i>ketubah</i> as set forth in the Mishnah (Ketubot 1:2) is exceedingly low by contemporary standards and hardly serves as ample means of supporting a woman in case of death or divorce. According to Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 4:91-92), the Ashkenazic <i>ketubah</i> is valued at one hundred pounds of silver, which in January 2008 amounted to $21,870.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For alternative views regarding the value of the <i>ketubah</i>, see <i>Techumin</i> (25:180-194).</i> Indeed, Rav Hershel Schachter (at a conference of the Council of Young Israel Rabbis) articulated<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Schachter told me that Rav Yitzchak Herzog already made this point more than fifty years ago. </i> the need to update the value of the <i>ketubah</i>, but there has been no movement to implement his suggestion.
In addition, the Ashkenazic<i> ketubah</i> is a standard document, and a list and an evaluation of the <i>nedunyah</i> is not made. Furthermore, many couples list their real estate and financial assets, such as stocks and bonds, as jointly owned between the husband and wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Some professionals even list all of their assets in their wives’ names to make them “judgment-proof” (i.e. they cannot be sued for malpractice, since technically they do not own any assets). </i> Moreover, most women, at least at some point in the marriage, earn a substantial salary from a job that entails considerable responsibilities. Today, a wife is expected to assume some of the responsibility for the household finances. These considerations may call for a reevaluation of the responsibilities and rights of spouses towards each other in the contemporary setting.
This issue is the subject of an intense debate between two of the great <i>dayanim</i> of our time, Rav Shlomo Dichovsky and Rav Avraham Sherman, members of the State of Israel Supreme Rabbinic Court.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">They present their opinions in the eighteenth volume of <i>Techumin</i> and respond to each other’s arguments in the subsequent volume of that Torah journal.</i> We shall focus on the three primary issues involved in this debate: whether the <i>tena’im</i> signed at the wedding constitute a financial partnership agreement between husband and wife, whether Halachah recognizes and incorporates Israeli civil community property laws in accordance with the celebrated rule of <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i> (the halachic obligation to respect the law of the land in which we reside), and whether Halachah recognizes the custom among many married couples in the contemporary era to regard their property as a financial partnership.
The original debate concerns Israeli secular law, which since 1973 views marital property as “community property,” meaning a financial partnership requiring an equal division in case of divorce. From 1973 until 1992, Israeli secular law stated that the community property laws do not apply to cases adjudicated by State of Israel Rabbinic Courts. However, in 1992, Chief Justice Aharon Barak of Israel’s secular Supreme Court ruled that State of Israel Rabbinical Courts must also adjudicate disputes in accordance with community property laws. This sparked the huge debate between Rav Dichovsky and Rav Sherman as to whether <i>dayanim </i>can tolerate Justice Barak’s order or must resist it.
<i><b>Tena’im</b></i>
Ashkenazic Jews since the time of the <i>Rishonim</i> have conducted <i>tena’im</i> (lit. conditions) for first marriages, in which the in-laws pledge the financial support they will provide the young couple. Included in this document is a clause stating, “They (the newlyweds) shall not conceal assets from each other, and they shall equally control their property.” Rav Dichovsky points to this clause as a precedent for the halachic recognition of civil community property. He notes that one of the great nineteenth-century halachic authorities, the Maharsham (<i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> 1:45), applies this clause in practice, claiming that it entitles the wife to give a substantial donation to <i>tzedakah</i> even without her husband’s consent.
As a precedent, the Maharsham cites the Maharik (57), who excommunicated a husband for concealing marital assets from his wife. The Maharik cited the aforementioned clause in the <i>tena’im</i> as the basis for his action. The Maharsham explains that such action was not taken in the times of the Gemara since the <i>tena’im</i> in that time apparently did not contain this clause. Indeed, Rav Yehudah Leib Graubart (<i>Teshuvot Chavalim Ban’imim</i> 5 E.H. 34), rules on the basis of this clause in our <i>tenai’im</i> (and <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i> of early-twentieth-century Canada) that a wife in contemporary circumstances shares the same right as her husband to donate large sums to <i>tzedakah</i>.
Rav Sherman responds, however, that two major nineteenth-century halachic authorities do not subscribe to the approach of the Maharsham. Rav Shlomo Kluger (<i>Teshuvot Tuv Ta’am Vada’at </i>3:181) objects to the approach of the Maharik since “so many pages of the Gemara and Shulchan Aruch would be nullified.” He asserts that the clause in the <i>tena’im</i> is merely a blessing bestowed on the couple by the fathers of the bride and groom and the witnesses. He also raises the possibility that the assets mentioned in the <i>tena’im</i> refer only to the <i>nichsei melog</i>. Rav Yitzchak Shmelkes (<i>Teshuvot Beit Yitzchak</i> E.H. 1:110) also expresses doubt as to whether this clause in the <i>tena’im</i> is a binding financial agreement or simply poetics (“<i>shufra d’shtara</i>”).
Moreover, Rav Sherman notes that the <i>tena’im</i> refer only to the time that the couple is married. It gives the wife the right to fully access the family’s assets, he argues, but does not assign her title or partnership rights in said assets. He points to the concluding sentence of the <i>tena’im</i> - “They should live together with love and affection” - as evidence that the clause applies only to financial arrangements during the marriage, not to the ownership of the assets. Finally, even if the <i>tena’im </i>were to be regarded as binding under all circumstances, their potential impact is limited, since Sephardic Jews and even some Ashkenazic Jews do not sign this document.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Hershel Schachter (<i>Mip’ninei Harav</i> p. 213) records that Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik advised against signing <i>tena’im</i>, even immediately before a <i>chuppah</i> (as is the practice in some circles), due to concern for the severe <i>cheirem</i> against canceling <i>tena’im</i>.</i>
Rav Dichovsky responds by acknowledging that <i>tena’im</i> do not assign partnership rights to the wife in the marital assets. However, he explains, it teaches that the system presented in the Gemara and Shulchan Aruch for the financial arrangement in marriage is not immutable and is subject to change in favor of more financial rights for the wife. Rav Dichovsky simply seeks to prove that the Israeli civil law concept of community property is not a “foreign implant within the vineyard of Israel.”
<i><b>Dina D’malchuta Dina</b></i>
The Gemara (Gittin 10b and elsewhere) presents the principle of <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i>. Thus, Halachah obligates us to pay taxes in accordance with the local laws (see the many sources cited by Rav Ovadia Yosef in <i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> 5:64). However, the scope of this principle remains a matter of unresolved debate. One unsolved matter is the question of whether a <i>beit din</i> should follow civil laws in adjudicating financial disputes between Jews when such laws contradict Halachah. The Rama (C.M. 369:11) rules that if such laws are made “for the betterment of society,” the <i>beit din</i> must honor such rules. The Shach (C.M. 73:69), on the other hand, rules that civil laws that do not conform to Jewish financial law should not be applied by <i>beit din</i> in resolving disputes between two Jews.
Rav Dichovsky cites prominent nineteenth- and twentieth-century authorities who endorse the view of the Rama. The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (<i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer</i> C.M. 44) rules that government rules prohibiting the number of wine distributors in a certain region should be honored in <i>beit din</i> even if Halachah imposes no such restrictions.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Interestingly, the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> emphasizes that if the government had not imposed this restriction, the local <i>beit din</i> would have, since it is such a logical and beneficial rule. It is thus not entirely clear that the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> would require following <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i> if a monetary law is made that contradicts Halachah, even if it is for the betterment of society, if it would not have been imposed by the local <i>beit din</i>.</i> Rav Meir Arik (<i>Teshuvot Imrei Yosher</i> 2:152:2) rules that <i>beit din</i> must honor civil laws prohibiting landlords from expelling tenants from an apartment at the termination of the lease.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Arik argues that perhaps even the Shach would agree that disputes relating to land should be governed by <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i>, since the government has ultimate control over the land.</i> Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> C.M. 2:62) rules that Halachah recognizes the validity of certain aspects of civil bankruptcy laws, which undoubtedly contradict halachic norms. Rav Dichovsky argues that <i>beit din </i>similarly should recognize civil common property laws, since the civil authorities make these laws for the benefit of society.
Rav Sherman responds that civil community property laws are not enacted “for the betterment of society.” He cites from the writings of Israel’s Chief Justice Aharon Barak (who issued the decision requiring <i>batei din</i> to honor such laws) that these laws are enacted to inculcate within society the secular belief that males and females should be treated in exactly the same manner. Rav Sherman notes that these laws often are unfair to husbands, as on one hand they must support their wives and pay their medical and clothing expenses, but on the other hand they must cede half of their earnings even though their wives are not required to earn money to support the family. Rav Itamar Warhaftig (<i>Alon Shevut</i> 92:39) writes that an unscrupulous wife could be motivated to divorce by the community property rules, especially if the marital assets are large. Thus, far from being a law that serves to better society, it could actually undermine society. In fact, most states in the United States do not have community property laws precisely because in many cases they can lead to dramatically unfair results. Instead, most states use equitable distribution laws, which we shall discuss below.
Rav Sherman applies a ruling of the Rashba (cited as normative by the aforementioned Rama), who writes that <i>beit din</i> must not adopt the civil laws that state that a father recovers the dowry of his daughter if she dies within the first year of her marriage. The Rashba denounces the adoption of such laws, stating that any <i>dayan</i> who would accept the civil law on this matter “collapses the walls of Torah and eliminates the roots and branches [of Torah], and the Torah will demand justice from his hand.” The Rashba believes that such internal matters of dispute between Jews must be resolved in accordance with Halachah, not by adopting a foreign legal system whose values differ from ours. Rav Sherman believes that the Rashba’s strong reproof applies equally to a <i>beit din</i> that adopts civil community property laws.
Even without utilizing community property laws, <i>dayanim</i> can ensure that a divorced mother is assured of financial support by ordering the husband to provide support for her and her children even after the divorce. A <i>beit din</i> could award such money, even though it is not mandated by Halachah, based on the idea of “divorce compensation,” which a <i>beit din</i> can issue “using its authority of discretion (as provided for in many <i>beit din</i> arbitration agreements) as appropriate to all the circumstances involved and in accordance with the size of the marital assets and the economic situation of the parties” (<i>Piskei Din Rabbaniyim</i> 1:137).<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">We should note that Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv does not accept the approach of divorce compensation (<i>Piskei Din Rabbaniyim</i> 7:111, 8:36, and 9:65). </i>
<i><b>Minhag Hamedinah</b></i>
Halachah grants much flexibility regarding monetary law if both parties consent. Moreover, if a community has adopted a particular practice regarding a monetary matter, common custom, generally speaking, overrides Halachah (<i>minhag mevateil halachah</i>; see <i>Yerushalmi Bava Metzia</i> 7:1). A classic example is presented in the Mishnah (Bava Metzia 6:1), which states that an employer cannot insist that his employees work from dawn to dusk if the local custom among hired workers is not to work such hours.
This rule has particular relevance to the manner of paying the <i>ketubah</i> in case of death or divorce. The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 23:12) and Shulchan Aruch (E.H. 66:11)<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">These comments appear in the context of how much money should be assigned in a <i>ketubah</i>.</i> specifically state that “In all of these matters and those like it, common local custom is a central pillar, and we adjudicate disputes on this basis” unless expressly stipulated to the contrary. One may assume that those who marry or enter in any other relationship without stipulations intend the relationship to function in accordance with the local common custom.
Indeed, the <i>Chazon Ish</i> (<i>Likuttim </i>C.M. 16:1) states that even in cases in which the rule of <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i> does not apply, nonetheless “the law of the land determines the intentions” of the parties to an agreement. Thus, in dealing with a corporation, one may assume that the parties intend to follow the local laws regarding corporations despite the fact that these laws do not conform to Halachah.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Techumin</i> 26:357-361 for a 2005 ruling of the Tiberias <i>beit din</i> on this subject.</i>
Rav Dichovsky, in turn, argues that the <i>minhag hamedinah</i> regards the marital relationship as a full financial partnership. He writes:
The practice, even in <i>Chareidi</i> families – including the families of eminent Torah authorities – is to view the wife as an equal to her husband in the family’s assets. This is expressed in the listing of both spouses as joint owners of the marital residence, by the fact that marital assets are bought and sold only upon mutual consent, and with the bequeathing of the marital estate to the wife. I have probated thousands of wills [as a State of Israel rabbinic judge], including the wills of the most <i>Chareidi</i> families, and I have not found even one of them in which the wife has been requested to forgo her share in the estate in favor of the halachic heirs, with the exception of a second marriage and cases in which the husband died shortly after the wedding….I do not understand why we must object to enforcing community property laws during the husband’s lifetime (i.e. in case of divorce) but consent to it after his death….I believe that the concept of community property has been accepted by Torah-observant families, including the families of practicing <i>dayanim</i>. Every one of us [<i>dayanim</i>], and I say this with certainty, views his wife as a full partner in the family’s assets. Civil courts have not conceived the idea of community property based on an ideology that is antithetical to Torah values; rather, [their rulings] reflect the reality of contemporary families, including <i>Chareidi</i> families.
<b>Rav Sherman’s Response</b>
Rav Sherman, on the other hand, notes that the aforementioned Rambam and Shulchan Aruch confine following <i>minhag hamedinah</i> to a situation in which “that custom has been accepted throughout the community.” One may assume that the parties implicitly consent to the common practice only if it is indeed common practice. Rav Sherman argues that it is not widespread practice among all families to manage their assets as an equal partnership. Moreover, involving one’s wife in decisions regarding the family’s finances does not imply that ownership of the assets has been transferred to her.
Rav Dichovsky responds that what applied during the marriage cannot be reversed in case of divorce. He bases this on the opinion of the <i>Geonim</i> (cited in the Pitchei Teshuvah E.H. 99:7) that if one gifts an item to one’s wife and the marriage later sours, the husband is not entitled to recover the gift. The <i>Geonim</i> compare this to one who presents a gift to a friend. If the friendship later dissolves, he cannot reclaim the gift with the argument that had he known they would become enemies, he never would have gifted the item.
The story that introduced this essay, in which almost all of those assembled to sell <i>chametz</i> were astonished that I asked my wife to nullify her <i>chametz</i>, seems to indicate the inaccuracy of Rav Dichovsky’s assertion that all communities accept the notion that a husband and wife today are financial partners on an equal footing in the couple’s financial assets.
<b>Explicit Agreement to Split Assets Based on Community Property Laws</b>
Rav Sherman goes so far as to rule that Halachah does not recognize community property laws even if both the husband and wife commit in writing to divide the marital assets based on community property laws. Rav Sherman argues that such an agreement violates the prohibition to adjudicate disputes in civil courts. Rav Dichovsky strongly disagrees with this point.
