In fact, based on a ruling of Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg (<i>Lev Mishpat</i> 1:286), the prenuptial agreement endorsed by the Rabbinical Council of America offers the option for a couple about to marry to agree to divide marital assets based on community property laws (or equitable distribution laws) at the time of the signing of the document.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Gray Matter 2 </i>pp. 170-172 for a fuller discussion and analysis of this ruling.</i>
<b>The Response of Other <i>Dayanim</i></b>
Rav Dichovsky (<i>Techumin </i>26:157) concedes that many <i>dayanim</i> have not accepted his approach to communal property. Indeed, Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg (cited in <i>Hadarom</i> 70-71:146), considered one of the leading authorities regarding monetary matters, does not seem to accept Rav Dichovsky’s ruling unless the couple explicitly accepted the community property laws in effect on the day of the signing of the agreement. Rav Yaakov Ariel does not accept Rav Dichovsky’s approach due to the inequity of community property laws, although he acknowledges the halachic ramifications of the change in the manner in which spouses manage their collective finances (as we shall discuss below).
<b>Four Fair Alternatives to Community Property Laws</b>
There remain at least four alternative means of dividing marital assets without resorting to secular community property laws. A variation of community property laws, known as equitable distribution, is employed by many states in the United States. Equitable distribution is not equal distribution - it means that marital assets are divided fairly. It could be divided fifty-fifty, but it also could be split in a more disparate fashion. Among the factors considered (the factors vary from state to state) are the duration of the marriage, spousal abuse or marital infidelity, economic fault of one spouse in wasting and dissipating marital property, amount of work each did to acquire the property, the respective responsibilities for providing for children of the marriage, earning power of each party, the contribution of a party as a homemaker, and the extent to which a party deferred achieving career goals in order to build the marriage and marital assets. Rav Ronald Warburg reports that some <i>dayanim</i> will employ civil equitable distribution laws as criteria in dividing marital assets. As mentioned, some <i>dayanim</i> adopt the idea of divorce compensation, which works along somewhat similar lines.
In addition, Rav Warburg writes (<i>Hadarom</i> 70-71:131) that the prevalent view among <i>dayanim</i> in the State of Israel rabbinic courts is to regard property listed in the names of both spouses as jointly-owned property, even if the husband’s assets alone financed the purchase. They view a case in which the husband financed the purchase of a house and listed his wife as co-owner as a gift from the husband to his wife, which is not returned upon divorce (see the aforementioned Pitchei Teshuvah E.H. 99:7). The same applies to joint bank accounts, stock certificates, bonds, mutual funds, etc. Rav Warburg told me that many <i>dayanim</i> who serve on <i>batei din</i> in the United States adopt this approach as well.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Avi Levinson reports that Rav Mordechai Willig agrees with this approach.</i> Accordingly, in many marriages, the couple creates a partnership by listing themselves as joint owners of their property.
Finally, Rav Hershel Schachter<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Schachter made this assertion in an address to a convention of the Council of Young Israel Rabbis.</i> argues that a woman is entitled to the money she earns from a full time job. Although Halachah states that a wife’s earnings belong to her husband (Shulchan Aruch E.H. 69:3-4), the <i>Dagul Meir’vavah</i> (E.H. 80:1) raises the possibility that a woman is entitled to earnings acquired through extraordinary efforts (<i>ha’adafah al yedei hadechak</i>).
Rav Schachter asserts that the wife’s earnings that accrue to her husband include only small scale work, such as making small amounts of jewelry for sale. However, if she works as a full-time worker, she may claim <i>kim li</i> (“I follow;” see the <i>Bach</i> cited in the Beit Shmuel 80:2)<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This approach assumes that the wife technically owns her earnings before they are transferred to her husband’s domain. Accordingly, if she follows the opinions that <i>ha’adfah al yedei hadechak</i> belongs to the wife, the transfer of ownership never takes place, and she retains title to her earnings.</i> the opinions that <i>ha’adafah al yedei hadechak</i> belongs to the wife and that she therefore enjoys the exclusive right to that salary. Rav Schachter applied this approach to an actual case. A husband donated every spare cent in the couple’s joint bank account to <i>tzedakah</i>, leaving no money to spend on even a very modest vacation. Rav Schachter advised the wife to open a separate account in her name to be funded with the money she would earn from her job, which she could then use to pay for a reasonable vacation for the couple.
We should note that Rav Moshe Feinstein<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe’s position is cited by his son, Rav Reuvein Feinstein, in an article that appears in the Memorial Volume to Rav Tzvi Tennebaum, <i>Sefer Eitz Erez </i>p. 801.</i> also maintains that a wife’s salary belongs to her. He goes so far as to say that if a wife travels to a discount outlet and buys an item for a much lower price than she would have had she purchased the item at a local store, the price differential belongs to her.
<b>Rav Yaakov Ariel’s Compromise</b>
Even halachic authorities who do not accept the approach of Rav Dichovsky may still recognize the many halachic implications of the changing financial relationship between husband and wife. Rav Yaakov Ariel (<i>Techumin</i> 22:129-147) adopts a compromise position. He asserts that even today, the husband assumes the primary responsibility of supporting his wife. He notes that he presented this point to hundreds of couples from all sectors of Israeli society who work in a wide variety of professions, and not one woman stated her willingness to waive the benefit of this right. He contends that a husband also remains responsible to pay for his wife’s medical needs, no matter how steep the cost, based either on his obligation to provide for her needs or on his requirement to redeem her from captivity. He argues, accordingly, that it is profoundly unjust for a husband to shoulder very significant financial responsibilities and yet not retain ownership of the marital assets (notwithstanding the exceptions discussed previously). Rav Ariel does grant, though, that since most women earn a significant salary or have the potential to do so,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">A couple should consider that Rav Hershel Schachter and Rav Mordechai Willig urge mothers to avoid working outside the home during child-rearing years, if possible. </i> women enter marriages today with less financial dependence and more of a sense of mutuality. He also acknowledges that this attitude is legitimate and recognized by Halachah in a host of areas.
<b>Obligations of Wife to Husband</b>
The Shulchan Aruch (E.H. 80) states that as a complement to the husband’s obligations to his wife, a wife has certain obligations to her husband. These include washing his hands and feet, serving him wine, and sewing clothes for the family. Rav Ariel states unequivocally that these specific obligations no longer apply. Halachah mandates that every couple, unless otherwise stipulated, enters a marriage with the intention to act in accordance with commonly accepted community practices. Hence, since the <i>minhag hamedinah</i> is that wives no longer perform these services on behalf of their husbands, husbands have no right to expect their wives to do so.
Interestingly, Rav Ariel argues that if a woman works outside the home, she is not obligated to cook for the family (despite the fact that the Shulchan Aruch E.H. 80:6 lists this as an obligation), since commercially prepared food can be purchased and subsequently warmed in a microwave oven. This last point appears to be highly debatable, as some families cannot afford (see <i>Shulchan Aruch ibid</i>.) to purchase prepared food on a routine basis. In addition, prepared food is often far less healthy than food prepared at home.
Moreover, it seems that even in Orthodox families in which the wife works a demanding job, she very often is the one who prepares the food. Thus, the <i>minhag hamedinah</i> still appears to be that working wives cook for their families. It seems that the same applies to issues concerning child care and household chores.
<b>A Wife’s Control of Family Assets</b>
It does seem clear, however, that Rav Ariel is correct in his assertion that the <i>minhag hamedinah</i> is that husbands and wives in a healthy relationship make joint decisions about significant monetary issues. Rav Ariel argues that this holds true even if title to marital assets is vested with the husband, since the <i>minhag hamedinah</i> is that couples share equal access to the family’s financial assets.
Therefore, unlike the times of the Gemara when only husbands made decisions regarding <i>tzedakah </i>allocations (Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 248:4), Rav Ariel rules that today, neither spouse is permitted to donate significant sums without the other, in accordance with the current <i>minhag hamedinah</i>. This would appear to apply to purchases as well.
Another ramification is in the area of tort liability. In the time of the Gemara, one had difficulty recovering damages from a married woman, since she had no readily available assets from which to collect (<i>Bava Kama </i>87a). However, Rav Ariel argues that today things are different:
…because it is unreasonable to say that a wife can draw from her account to pay for a variety of needs without any objection from her husband, but when she is obligated to compensate for financial loss that she caused to others, her husband can object. Therefore, it appears that even though the Talmudic principle that ‘What a wife earns belongs to the husband’ still applies in our times, this is insufficient reason to excuse her from indemnifying one to whom she caused loss. As far as this matter is concerned, we relate to her as if she has assets of her own.
Other halachic authorities do not seem to concur with this view. For example, Rav Yaakov Blau (<i>Pitchei Choshen</i> 8:8:75, published in 1996) presents the classic Halachah regarding this matter without noting any difference in our times. Similarly, Rav Blau (ad. loc. 8:73) presents as normative the classic rules forbidding a wife to donate money to <i>tzedakah</i> without her husband’s consent.
Perhaps one can reconcile the apparent dispute between Rav Blau and Rav Ariel by distinguishing between different segments of the Orthodox community. Rav Blau’s approach might fit certain communities wherein couples might be presumed to operate based on the classic Talmudic model of the husband-wife financial relationship. Rav Ariel’s approach, on the other hand, seems more appropriate for other communities in which couples seem to function based on a model different from the classic one. Different communities might have differing <i>minhagei hamedinah</i>. In addition, Rav Blau might rule otherwise in a case in which the wife is listed as a joint owner in the family’s bank account and/or securities accounts.
The change in financial relationships between spouses might impact other areas of Halachah. One issue that may depend on this point is the propriety of the wife appointing the family Rav to sell the <i>chametz</i> on behalf of the family without the husband’s consent or vice versa. Another ramification could be whether the wife can write a <i>prozbul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This document allows for the collection of a loan even after the conclusion of the s<i>hemittah</i> year.</i> on her husband’s behalf.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Avi Levinson notes that these issues also may depend on the dispute between the Ketzot Hachoshen (243:8) and the <i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer</i> (E.H. 1:11) regarding whether a person may remove something from another’s control if it is beneficial for the latter not to own the item.</i>
<b>Ritual Matters</b>
The Shulchan Aruch (O. C. 529:2) frames the Torah’s obligation to rejoice on <i>Yom Tov</i> as follows:
One must rejoice and be in a good mood during <i>Yom Tov</i>. He, his wife, his children and all those who are part of his family [must be in a joyous mood]. How does he facilitate their rejoicing? Children are given parched grain and nuts, and for women, one should purchase clothes and jewelry according to his means.
<i>Rishonim</i> debate the nature of the obligation of women to rejoice on <i>Yom Tov</i>. The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Chagigah</i> 1:1) rules that the wife has an independent obligation to rejoice, and the husband merely facilitates her fulfillment of the <i>mitzvah</i>. Since the husband controls the family finances, he allocates the money for the clothes and jewelry. The Ra’avad (commenting on the Rambam ad. loc.) believes that the wife has no independent <i>mitzvah</i> to rejoice on <i>Yom Tov</i>; rather, the Torah obligates the husband to make her happy on these days. The <i>Sha’agat Aryeh</i> (66) and Mishnah Berurah (529:15) rule in accordance with the Rambam.
Rav Ariel raises the question of whether the mechanics of this <i>mitzvah</i> have changed in our times now that spouses share control of the family’s funds. He begins by focusing on the Rambam’s opinion. The Rambam believes that essentially the wife is obligated in this <i>mitzvah</i> but that only the husband can facilitate its fulfillment since he controls the finances. Since, however, contemporary wives control finances equally with their husbands, a wife should be obligated to fulfill the <i>mitzvah</i> of rejoicing on <i>Yom Tov</i> independently. Rav Ariel recommends that the best approach to maintaining marital harmony<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">If it is not assumed that the husband has sole control over the family’s finances, he should not make a unilateral financial decision that will affect the wife (by changing the amount of money available for other needs). </i> is for a couple to plan its <i>Yom Tov</i> budget in such a way that they agree on the amount of money that will be allocated for the purchase of clothes and jewelry for the wife.
<b>Conclusion – <i>Bittul Chametz</i></b>
We opened this chapter with the question of whether wives today should perform <i>bittul chametz</i> along with their husbands. Traditionally, there was no need for her to do so, since all the marital assets including the home and its <i>chametz</i> belonged to the husband.
In our times, however, many women should be concerned with the prohibition to own <i>chametz</i>. Contemporary wives own their <i>chametz </i>either because of Rav Dichovsky’s approach (or a variant thereof) or due to the fact that wives in most cases are listed as joint owners of the marital home. I have heard that in some homes today, husbands and wives recite <i>bittul chametz</i> together, while in others only the husband performs the <i>bittul</i>, as was practiced in preceding generations. In such a case, the husband acts as the wife’s agent in nullifying the <i>chametz</i>.
We will close with a vitally important assertion made by Rav Ariel. Despite the change of financial control and decision-making in our times, a basic distinction between the roles of husband and wife remain in full effect. The primary responsibility to earn a livelihood for the family still rests upon the husband, and the primary responsibility for child-rearing and homemaking still rests upon the wife. The complementary yet not identical obligations of husband and wife typify the special character of the Jewish home.

Wartime Gittin

Since the time that the Torah was given, <i>poskim</i> have exerted great effort to avoid Jewish women becoming <i>agunot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Literally “anchored women,” this term refers to women who wish to remarry but may not do so according to Halachah. In <i>Gray Matter 1</i>, the first several chapters (pp. 3-59) address the problem of <i>agunot</i> who can not remarry because their husbands cruelly withhold <i>gittin</i>. The present chapter deals with a different kind of <i>agunot</i>: women who may not remarry because their husbands have disappeared and might still be alive. In <i>Gray Matter 2</i>, there is an extensive discussion (pp. 114-138) of how rabbinic courts handled the <i>agunot</i> from the World Trade Center tragedy in 2001. </i> A classic example is the practice (Shabbat 56b and Ketubot 9b) of anyone who went to war with King David’s army to write a <i>get</i> to his wife to avoid her becoming an <i>agunah</i> in case he disappeared in battle. Rashi (Ketubot 9b s.v. <i>Get Keritut</i>) believes that such a <i>get</i> was given conditionally, whereas Rabbeinu Tam (cited in <i>Tosafot Ketubot</i> 9b s.v. <i>Kol Hayotzei</i>) argues that such <i>gittin</i> were given unconditionally. The Rambam (<i>Teshuvot Harambam</i> 439 in the Blau edition) clarifies that the expression “King David’s army” refers to any army that is led by a righteous, God-fearing Jew, and not specifically to the army led by King David.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">We see from the Rambam that responsible Jewish leadership in any age should take steps to ensure that soldiers’ wives do not become <i>agunot</i>.</i>
In this chapter, we shall discuss five variations on how such <i>gittin</i> were produced during the wars of the twentieth century, beginning with the Russo-Japanese War (1904-1905), continuing with World War I (1914-1918) and World War II (1939-1945), and concluding with the Israeli War of Independence (1948) and the Six Day War (1967). We will note the advantages and disadvantages of each of the five approaches, discuss the possible applications of the outlined methodologies to contemporary non-war situations, and call for the renewal of the practice of soldiers authorizing such <i>gittin</i> before entering combat.
<b>A Conventional <i>Get</i> – Rabbeinu Tam and Rabbeinu Yechiel of Paris</b>
Rav Yosef Eliyahu Henkin (<i>Teshuvot Ivra</i> 80) and Rav Shlomo Yosef Zevin (<i>L’or Hahalachah</i> p. 67) note that the preferred method to avoid wartime <i>agunot</i> is for a married soldier to divorce his wife with a conventional <i>get</i> before he leaves for war. The advantage of solving the problem in this manner is that it is a straightforward halachic procedure.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Halachically valid conditions are complex and must meet specific criteria in order to be valid. See Shulchan Aruch (E.H. 143-148) for the many detailed regulations that apply to conditional <i>gittin</i>. </i> Indeed, Rabbeinu Yechiel of Paris (cited in the <i>Mordechai</i>,<i> Gittin</i> 423) instituted the practice that if a <i>get</i> is administered when a childless husband is deathly ill in order for the wife to avoid <i>chalitzah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chalitzah</i> is the ceremony (outlined in Devarim 25:7-10) in which the brother of a man who died childless officially declines to marry the deceased’s widow, thereby permitting her to remarry.</i> the <i>get</i> should not be given on condition that the husband dies (so that the couple will remain married if he recovers). Instead, the couple should conduct a standard <i>get</i> and solemnly promise<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This involves both parties accepting, on pain of excommunication, the obligation to remarry if the husband recovers. In addition, a significant financial penalty would be imposed upon a recalcitrant party.</i> to remarry should the husband recover.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I recall a case in the 1990s in which a husband was undergoing very risky surgery and wished to give his wife a <i>get</i> so that she would not remain an <i>agunah</i> if the surgery rendered him incapacitated and incompetent. Rav Peretz Steinberg, a noted <i>dayan</i> from Queens, New York, conducted a conventional <i>get</i>. Similarly, Rav Yonah Reiss reports that when the Beth Din of America deals with husbands suffering from an early stage of Alzheimer’s disease, a conventional <i>get</i> is conducted.</i> Indeed, the Maharsha (in his concluding comment to <i>Masechet Gittin</i>) cites the aforementioned view of Rabbeinu Tam that wartime <i>gittin</i> were conducted unconditionally as proof that all <i>gittin</i> should be executed in this way.
The Rama (E.H. 155:9) rules in accordance with Rabbeinu Yechiel of Paris. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (E.H. <i>Seder Haget Hatemidi</i> 1; see also E.H. 147:11) likewise notes that we do not administer conditional <i>gittin</i> in our times and that a Rav should refuse to administer a <i>get</i> if the husband insists on attaching a condition.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In fact, while observing the Jerusalem Rabbinical Court in 1993, I watched a husband (who happened to be a rabbi) stubbornly insist that the <i>get</i> between him and his wife be conducted conditionally. He argued that the Talmud was replete with discussions of <i>gittin</i> conducted conditionally. One of the <i>dayanim </i>(rabinnic judges), Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg, told the man, “You are a <i>talmid chacham </i>(Torah scholar). You have heard of Rabbeinu Yechiel of Paris, yes?” The husband replied in the affirmative. Rav Goldberg continued, “Rabbeinu Yechiel of Paris decreed that we do not conduct conditional <i>gittin</i>,” which ended the discussion.</i>
There are some obvious disadvantages to conducting <i>gittin</i> unconditionally. First, couples may find it too discomforting. Moreover, there is no absolute guarantee of remarriage, since violation of the solemn promise to remarry does not invalidate the <i>get</i> according to most authorities (see <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> E.H. 145:30).<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See, though, <i>Teshuvot Mishkenot Yaakov</i> (E.H. 34), who rules that the <i>get </i>is void if the promise is not kept.</i> Furthermore, it might demoralize soldiers at a time when courage is needed most. Finally, and perhaps most important, this approach is unsuitable for a <i>kohen</i>, since a <i>kohen</i> is forbidden to marry a divorcee, even his former wife. Thus, despite the advantages of unconditional <i>gittin</i>, conditional <i>gittin</i> were often conducted in wartime.
<b>Conditional Gittin – <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> and Rav Chaim Ozer Grodzinsky</b>
Many soldiers insisted that a wartime <i>get</i> be given to their wives which would take effect only if they become classified as missing in action. The condition was often formulated as stating that the <i>get</i> takes effect retroactively if he does not return within two years of its execution. The disadvantage of conducting a <i>get</i> in this manner is that it involves the risk of improper execution. Moreover, the Shulchan Aruch (E.H. 147:1) rules in accordance with the view of the Rambam (<i>Hilchot Geirushin</i> 8:3) that conditions not be mentioned during the writing of the <i>get</i>. A condition should be mentioned only at the time of the <i>get</i>’s delivery.
In wartime situations, the scribes and witnesses constantly were busy with soldiers executing conditional <i>gittin</i> and were very much aware that a condition would be mentioned at the time of the delivery of the <i>get</i> from husband to wife. Many <i>poskim</i> considered this to constitute a very serious problem<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since the scribe and witnesses in reality were writing and signing the <i>get</i> conditionally, even though the condition was not formally stated beforehand, the <i>get</i>’s validity is questionable. </i> and therefore sought to convince husbands to execute unconditional <i>gittin</i>. The Maharsham (<i>Teshuvot Maharsham </i>3:7) and other <i>poskim</i> presented arguments that the <i>gittin</i> were valid even in such circumstances.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">For further discussion of this issue, see Rav Shlomo Yosef Zevin’s <i>Le’or Hahalchah</i> pp. 76-77.</i>
To compensate for this problem, most of the methodologies advocated by the <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> (4:156) for use by soldiers who fought during the Russo-Japanese war of 1905 involved the husband appointing a <i>sofer</i> to write a <i>get</i>, witnesses to sign it, and an agent to deliver it to the wife. The husband would authorize the agent to stipulate conditions at the time of delivery, giving the <i>sofer</i> no knowledge of any such conditions so as not to encounter the problem associated with such knowledge. The condition stipulated would be that the <i>get</i> takes retroactive effect if the husband does not return from war. Indeed, Rav Chaim Ozer Grodzinsky (in a letter printed in <i>Teshuvot Heichal Yitzchak</i> E.H. 2:36) notes that the standard procedure used by the Rabbis of Vilna for soldiers going to fight in the very early stages of World War II was the conditional <i>get</i>. Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 4:111) also advocates use of a conditional <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe, though, adds an additional step, as we shall discuss below.</i>
A problem that arose with conditional wartime <i>gittin</i> was the situations in which a <i>kohen</i> was not able to return before the stipulated time. In one such case that was presented to the Netziv (<i>Teshuvot Meishiv Davar</i> 3:51), a <i>kohen</i> stipulated that his <i>get</i> take effect if he did not return to a specific town within two years. Hostilities ceased by then, but the army did not permit him to travel to that town within the allotted two years. The husband desperately sought to return home so as to avoid the <i>get</i> taking effect and his wife being rendered forbidden to him forever. Fortunately, the Netziv ruled that the <i>get</i> was rendered invalid because the wife waived the stipulation that was made for her benefit.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">On the other hand, Rav Avraham Bornstein (<i>Teshuvot Avnei Neizer</i> E.H. 200) validated a <i>get</i> in a similar situation. The case involved a <i>kohen</i> who fought in the Russian army during the Russo-Japanese War and was unable to return within the stipulated two years because he was being held in Japan as a prisoner of war. The <i>Avnei Neizer</i> ruled that the <i>get</i> was valid despite the <i>kohen</i>’s written request to his wife to appear before the <i>beit din</i> and invalidate the conditional <i>get</i> he left for her. Sadly, the couple was forbidden to reunite after his return.</i> To avoid such scenarios, the <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> formulated the stipulation as stating that the <i>get</i> would take effect only after the wife presented the <i>dayan</i> at the <i>beit din</i> with a minimal amount of money. In this manner, the <i>get</i> would not take effect if the wife did not want it to.
<b>Writing the <i>Get</i> after the War – Rav David Singer of Pilzno, Galicia</b>
In the fall of 1914, as Jewish young men were being drafted into the armies of World War I, Rav David Singer of Pilzno, Galicia suggested an alternative to soldiers giving their wives conditional <i>gittin</i>. He wrote (as is recorded in <i>Teshuvot Minchat Elazar </i>3:68<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">A reproduced copy of the flier that Rav Singer produced was given to me by his son, Rav Yosef Singer of New York.</i>) that soldiers could appear before a competent <i>beit din</i> and appoint a scribe to write a <i>get</i>, witnesses to sign it, and an agent to deliver it. The <i>beit din</i> would record this event but would write and deliver the <i>get</i> only if the husband did not return from war and his death could not be ascertained.
The advantage of conducting a <i>get</i> in this manner, writes Rav Singer, is that it is simpler to execute than a conditional <i>get</i>. It is easier on the couple as well, as it avoids the discomfort of the husband handing a <i>get</i> to his beloved wife. This, in turn, motivates soldiers who might otherwise not do so to give a wartime <i>get</i> to avoid a potential <i>agunah</i> situation. Moreover, it facilitates conducting large numbers of wartime <i>gittin</i> for the thousands of soldiers who are being rushed to battle. Appointments of scribes, witnesses, and agents take a fraction of the time that it takes to execute a full <i>get</i>. Furthermore, it eases the toll on morale, as there is a more remote connection between the soldier and the <i>get</i>. If a full <i>get</i> would be performed, it would suggest the disturbing possibility that the husband might die or disappear. Finally, this method is in harmony with Rabbeinu Yechiel of Paris’s edict to avoid conditional <i>gittin</i> as much as possible.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The husband does not stipulate any conditions in the execution of the <i>get</i>, for it is understood that the <i>beit din</i> will not allow the scribe, witnesses, and agent to execute the <i>get</i> until it is necessary to do so. This is not considered a conditional <i>get </i>- see <i>Ba’eir Heiteiv</i> (E.H. 147:1).</i>
Rav Singer’s proposal was endorsed by two renowned <i>poskim</i> of the time, Rav Meir Arik and Rav Yosef Engel, as appears on the poster produced by Rav Singer. The <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> (4:156) also approves the use of Rav Singer’s method if the husband has children, although he does not prefer this method due to concern that one of the people appointed to execute the <i>get </i>will not be available to do so.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> advocates the use of Rav Singer’s method only if there is concern that the couple might not remarry if the <i>get </i>is delivered immediately. </i>
This proposal, however, is not without its disadvantages. It does not address the problem of <i>chalitzah</i>. Accordingly, if the husband is dead or missing, having authorized execution of a <i>get</i> would not obviate the wife’s obligation to undergo <i>chalitzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Shlomo Goren (<i>Teshuvot Meishiv Milchamah</i> 3:62) records that there were widows who experienced difficulties regarding <i>chalitzah</i> (due to brothers who lived outside the country or who were minors and thus ineligible to perform <i>chalitzah</i>) in the wake of both the Israeli War of Independence and the Yom Kippur War. </i> Unlike the first two options that we outlined, Rav Singer’s solution does not take effect immediately (or retroactively). Instead, it is executed only after the husband is assumed to be dead, at which point the <i>get</i> does not avert the need for <i>chalitzah</i> (Pitchei Teshuvah E.H. 141:70).<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>get</i> is executed and delivered only if there is insufficient evidence of the husband’s death to permit the wife to remarry. See, though, Pitchei Teshuvah (E.H. 141:72), who presents opinions that a <i>get</i> should not be delivered if the husband is assumed to be dead.</i>
In addition, since it does not operate retroactively, such a <i>get</i> is not effective in a case of the husband losing his mental faculties in war, since Halachah requires that the husband be of sound mind both at the time of appointing the scribe, witnesses, and agent as well as at the time of the delivery of the <i>get</i> (Shulchan Aruch E.H. 121).<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Teshuvot Minchat Elazar</i> (3:68) addresses the question of how we can write a <i>get</i> on behalf of the husband and not be concerned with the possibility that the missing husband became mentally incompetent and incapable of having a <i>get</i> executed on his behalf.</i>
Finally, Rav Singer does not address the possibility of the scribe, witnesses, or agent moving overseas or dying in the interim. Subsequent variations of Rav Singer’s proposal, such as that of Rav Herzog (<i>Teshuvot Heichal Yitzchak</i> E.H. 2:41), offer the option of appointing alternate scribes, witnesses, and agents in case any of the people appointed to execute the <i>get</i> are unavailable to do so.
<b>World War II</b>
<i>Poskim</i> were posed with two great challenges in preparing wartime <i>gittin</i> during World War Two.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The Jewish people were blessed with three great <i>poskim</i> who invested much time and energy in executing these <i>gittin</i>: Rav Yechezkel Abramsky in London, Rav Yosef Eliyahu Henkin in New York, and Rav Yitzchak Herzog in Jerusalem. In fact, the Israeli Chief Rabbinate’s archives contain more than two thousand documents, signed by married Jewish residents of Eretz Yisrael who joined the British army to fight the Nazis, authorizing a scribe, witnesses, and an agent to execute a <i>get</i> in case a soldier is declared missing(<i>Techumin</i> 27:420).</i> First, the great number of Jewish soldiers required the <i>poskim</i> to offer options of executing <i>gittin</i> in a most efficient manner. The <i>Encyclopedia Judaica</i> (11:1550) states that more than half a million Jews served in the United States army, over sixty thousand in the British army, and another thirty five thousand Jewish residents of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> volunteered in the British army. In addition, many of the soldiers had limited commitment to Torah observance, which added to the <i>poskim</i>’s burden to make the issuance of such <i>gittin</i> easy and expedient. Thus, the <i>poskim</i> used the option presented by <i>Teshuvot Divrei Malkiel</i> (4:156) of appointing a scribe and witnesses even not in the presence of the scribe and witnesses.
Appointing a scribe and witnesses in such a manner is highly controversial.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">For a review of this controversy, see my article regarding the propriety of executing a <i>get </i>by video teleconference (<i>Techumin</i> 14:272-276 and available at www.koltorah.org).</i> Nonetheless, there were circumstances in which soldiers were located very far from a <i>beit din</i>. In such cases, Rav Abramsky (<i>Hapardeis</i>, <i>Tishrei</i> 5701-1940), Rav Henkin (<i>Teshuvot Ivra</i> 80), and Rav Herzog (ad. loc.) all utilized this option and composed documents authorizing the writing and delivery of a <i>get</i>, which soldiers could effectuate by reading aloud and signing before a competent <i>beit din</i>.
The <i>Chazon Ish</i> (E.H. 85), however, vigorously protested the appointment of a scribe in such a manner. He writes as an alternative that a soldier stationed far from a <i>beit din</i> should be instructed to write a minimally valid <i>get</i> as a supplement to appointing a scribe to write a conventional <i>get</i> on his behalf. Most authorities, however, did not agree with the <i>Chazon Ish</i>’s approach, and it appears that his suggestion never was implemented.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chazon Ish</i>’s proposal is not currently used even in cases in which a husband is located far from a <i>beit din</i>. The reason appears to be that implementing this system would cause pandemonium in <i>get</i> administration, as the <i>Chazon Ish</i> himself indicates. A pillar of <i>get</i> administration is that a <i>get</i> may be executed only under the supervision of a highly trained and recognized rabbinical authority. Executing a <i>get</i> in the manner suggested by the <i>Chazon Ish</i> leaves uneducated Jews with the impression that a <i>get</i> may be conducted even in the absence of such supervision and that one may even deviate from the venerated text of the <i>get</i>. </i>
<b>Furloughs</b>
The second major hurdle faced by <i>poskim</i> during World War II was that since methods of transportation had improved, soldiers were given furloughs during which they would visit their families for a week or more.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (<i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer</i> E.H. 2:58) grapples with a situation in which a soldier who had divorced his wife with an unconditional get returned home on furlough. The husband returned to the army (without issuing a second get) and eventually became missing in action. The Chatam Sofer permitted the wife to remarry, asserting that the couple did not intend to remarry during the furlough, as they were under the mistaken impression that their original marriage was still in effect (when, in fact, it had been dissolved by the first get).</i> This posed an enormous problem, since if a husband and wife are secluded (<i>mityacheid</i>) after the scribe, witnesses, and agent are appointed, the appointments are rendered invalid (Shulchan Aruch E.H. 149:7; see the Beit Shmuel and Vilna Gaon thereupon). Rav Henkin (ad. loc.) writes that this problem was taken exceedingly seriously by the American <i>poskim</i>, to the extent that they considered the possibility of not encouraging wartime <i>gittin</i>. In fact, Rav Chaim Ozer Grodzinsky (in a letter printed in <i>Teshuvot Heichal Yitzchak</i> E.H. 2:36) writes that the practice in Vilna was to have Jewish soldiers who returned from furloughs issue appointments of the scribe, witnesses, and agent a second time before returning to the army.
The <i>poskim</i> sought to solve this problem in various manners. Rav Abramsky referred to the <i>Re’eim</i> (cited in the <i>Kenesset Hagedolah</i> 148:3 and <i>Ba’eir Heiteiv</i> E.H. 148:2), who essentially states that the concern of seclusion with one’s wife canceling authorizations to execute a <i>get </i>does not apply to a situation in which the appointments were issued with the intention of preventing the wife from becoming an <i>agunah</i>. Rav Herzog (<i>Teshuvot Heichal Yitzchak</i> E.H. 2:41) added a clause to the appointments in which the soldier declares that even if he returns home and is secluded with his wife, he does not cancel the appointments of the scribe, witnesses, and agent. Rav Henkin sought to send documents with soldiers so that they could reissue appointments of the scribe, witnesses, and agent before a <i>beit din</i> upon returning to the army after furlough.
<b>Israel’s War of Independence</b>
Dramatically lower numbers of married soldiers in the Israeli War of Independence signed authorizations to write <i>gittin</i> to avoid women becoming <i>agunot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Herzog (<i>Teshuvot Heichal Yitzchak </i>E.H. 1:2), in his introduction to his rulings regarding the <i>agunot </i>of the missing defenders of Kfar Etzion in the War of Independence, writes that the Chief Rabbinate made great efforts to ensure that every soldier signed <i>get</i>-authorization documents (as was done during World War II) but that in most cases such authorizations were not signed due to factors within the Israeli military. Rav Herzog therefore writes, “And we are vindicated from Hashem and from Israel.”</i> In that war, the Israel Defense Forces (IDF) issued orders for soldiers to sign <i>get</i> authorizations to avoid <i>agunot</i>. However, the Chief Rabbi of the Israel Defense Forces, Rav Shlomo Goren (<i>Teshuvot Meishiv Milchamah</i> 3 p. 60-61), records that commanders in the fledgling IDF vigorously opposed the signing of such documents, arguing that the toll on the morale of the soldiers was too great to justify their widespread usage. In addition, soldiers refused to issue such orders due to concern that the government would not support their widows in case of death, as it would claim that the couple divorced. Rav Goren notes that the <i>get </i>authorizations would have been signed by soldiers only if they were ordered to do so as a military command with serious punishments for those who did not comply. Rav Goren believes that such compulsion would have invalidated the <i>gittin</i>, since a <i>get</i> issued under coercion is invalid (Shulchan Aruch E.H. 134:7-8).<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">For a more thorough discussion of the parameters of this rule, see <i>Gray Matter 1</i> pp. 3-7.</i> Rav Goren records that he presented this concern to a special meeting of the Council of the Chief Rabbinate headed by Rav Herzog and his Sephardic counterpart, Rav Ben Zion Uzziel, and the council concurred with Rav Goren’s ruling. Rav Shlomo Yosef Zevin (<i>L’or Hahalachah</i> p. 77), however, notes that such <i>gittin</i> would appear to be valid, since Halachah mandates a husband to issue a <i>get</i> under such circumstances and may be coerced to do so (see <i>Teshuvot Harosh</i> 43:13 and Shulchan Aruch E.H. 154:9).<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The Shulchan Aruch mandates that a <i>beit din</i> coerce a husband to give a get in a case where he is compelled to leave his place of residence due to safety concerns.</i>. He cites the <i>Teshuvot Maharsham</i> (3:7) and the <i>Teshuvot Oneg Yom Tov </i>as support for his approach.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Zevin notes, though, that <i>Teshuvot Oneg Yom Tov</i> did not authorize coercion if the couple married after the husband already was drafted into the army, since the wife entered the marriage accepting that he would be going out to war (based on Rama E.H. 154:1 and Beit Shmuel 154:2). </i>
Nonetheless, the IDF did not compel soldiers to sign <i>get</i> authorizations, following the ruling of the Chief Rabbinate.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Goren describes the extreme lengths he went to in order to help the <i>agunot </i>of the Israeli War of Independence. He writes that he obtained special permits (facilitated by the ceasefire commission) from the enemy states to enter enemy territory to painstakingly inspect the battlefields (such as Latrun, Gush Etzion, and Jenin) and interview enemy soldiers and Arab shepherds and thereby piece together the evidence to permit the <i>agunot </i>to remarry. Rav Goren states that it took years for sufficient evidence to be amassed in order to resolve the plight of the <i>agunot</i> (as well as to bury the bodies of the soldiers left in the battlefield behind enemy lines). </i> Today, the IDF certainly does not compel soldiers to sign such documents, and Rav David Eisen, a chaplain in the Israel Defense Forces, informs me that this topic is not part of the training course of the IDF rabbinate. He also told me that before he went into combat in the Second Lebanon War of 2006, the topic of authorizing a <i>get</i> was not broached at all. A new reason not to sign such documents is the reduced risk of a woman being rendered an <i>agunah</i>, as technological breakthroughs such as DNA identification<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Gray Matter 2</i> pp. 122-123 and our later chapter about the admissibility of DNA evidence in <i>beit din</i> for an elucidation of the debate regarding how much credence is given to DNA evidence in the context of <i>agunot</i>.</i> and satellite surveillance greatly enhance the IDF’s ability to monitor the whereabouts and possible death of its soldiers. We should note that the risk unfortunately has not entirely been eliminated. A tragic example is captured Israeli aviator Ron Arad, missing (as of this writing) since 1986. His wife, unfortunately, remains an <i>agunah</i> at the present time.
Rav Eisen informs me that the official policy of the IDF rabbinate for many years has been not to encourage signing such authorizations due to concern for morale. Rav Eisen, though, informs me that quite a few rabbis in the IDF rabbinate seek to change this policy, as they believe that there are ways to conduct such authorizations without undermining morale. They suggest that such authorizations be done immediately before battle at the same time that soldiers sign for special life insurance coverage. Indeed, Rav J. David Bleich (<i>Contemporary Halakhic Problems I</i>:154) criticizes the IDF for not conducting such <i>get</i> authorizations due to concern for morale.
<b>The Six Day War – Rav Waldenberg and Rav Feinstein</b>
Although enthusiasm for the signing of authorizations to write a <i>get </i>diminished, individuals continued to authorize the writing and delivery of <i>gittin</i> in specific circumstances. For example, a soldier came to Rav Eliezer Waldenberg (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 11:90) at the Jerusalem <i>beit din</i> in the weeks before the Six Day War to execute a <i>get </i>before he left for a dangerous mission in enemy territory. He insisted that his wife not be aware of this authorization in order not to unduly alarm her. The appointments of scribe, witnesses, and agent could not occur immediately before he left on the mission, since he might be summoned to duty at a moment’s notice.
The only manner in which the news of the authorization could be withheld from the wife was for the husband to return home until he was summoned to service and continue living a normal married life. Rav Waldenberg was faced with the quandary of whether he could permit the authorization under such circumstances. In the end, he did permit the soldier to make the authorization, despite the fact that the <i>beit din</i> knew that he would return to his wife before leaving for his mission. Rav Waldenberg based his ruling on the aforementioned lenient approach of the <i>Re’eim</i> and Rav Abramsky.
Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 4:111), in a relatively brief responsum written in 1967, mere weeks before the beginning of the Six-Day War and during the Vietnam War, presented a new variation of how to conduct a wartime <i>get</i>. Offering a combination of the approaches previously outlined, his protocol involved the husband appearing before a competent <i>beit din</i> right before leaving for the army and handing a conditional <i>get </i>to his wife that would take effect in case he died or was considered missing. Rav Moshe adds, though, that the husband should also appoint anyone who sees his signature to serve as a scribe, witness, or agent to deliver an additional <i>get</i> to his wife in case the <i>get</i> he handed her became invalidated. This apparently authorizes the <i>beit din</i> to order the execution of another <i>get</i> in case the husband returns on furlough and thereby potentially invalidates the first <i>get</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that Rav Moshe’s logic is that in case the first <i>get</i> is possibly invalidated during a furlough, another <i>get</i> can be written, and in case the replacement <i>get</i> is not valid (as per the aforementioned objections of the <i>Chazon Ish</i>), the original <i>get</i> might remain valid despite the husband’s seclusion with his wife during the furlough.<br>It is interesting to note that Rav Moshe does not record the name of the person to whom he addressed this <i>teshuvah</i>. It is not clear whether Rav Moshe was directing Israeli <i>poskim</i> preparing for the Six Day War or the American <i>poskim</i> who were dealing with soldiers leaving for service in Vietnam.</i>
<b>Conclusion</b>
We have seen the great lengths to which <i>poskim</i> of old and of the twentieth century went to avoid women becoming <i>agunot</i> as a result of wars. Although such <i>gittin</i> are uncommon today, they are not unheard of<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yonah Reiss reports that the Beth Din of America occasionally has executed appointments of scribes, witnesses, and agents to perform<i> gittin </i>for husbands leaving for the war in Iraq.</i> and might be advisable in certain non-military circumstances, such as if a husband is undergoing risky surgery. In such a case, a competent <i>beit din</i> should be consulted to determine which of the options outlined should be used to administer the <i>get</i> and to oversee its execution.
Moreover, rabbis today should reinstitute the policy for all married soldiers to authorize a <i>get</i> before leaving for combat. After all, this involves simply reinstating a practice that was common even as late as the first half of the twentieth century. Otherwise, the suffering of women such as the wife of Ron Arad is compounded by their inability to renew their lives and remarry.

Blood Tests and DNA in Beit Din



Chapter 1

Beginning in the twentieth century, <i>poskim</i> have been faced with the issue of whether new forms of evidence, such as blood tests and DNA, are admissible in <i>beit din</i>. These tests have enormous potential to resolve heretofore irresolvable questions in a positive direction, but they also can reveal information that could have catastrophic ramifications. We shall begin with a discussion of blood tests.
<b>Blood Tests to Determine Paternal Identity – The Majority Opinion</b>
Since the early twentieth century, <i>poskim</i> have been faced with the question of whether blood tests may be accepted in <i>beit din</i> to help determine paternal identity. In many countries, blood tests were commonly used as evidence that a husband was not the father of his wife’s child. Most <i>poskim</i> ruled that such evidence is inadmissible in <i>beit din</i>. It seems that Rav Ben-Zion Uzziel (<i>Sha’arei Uzziel</i> 2:40:1:18) was the first to address this matter. He rules that blood tests are not admissible evidence based on the celebrated Gemara (Niddah 30a) that states that there are three partners in the creation of a person: Hashem, the mother, and the father. The Gemara goes on to explain what each partner contributes to the creation of the child. The mother, states the Gemara, contributes the red material from which blood is created. This passage is even applied by some authorities in a halachic context (see Rama Y.D. 263:2). Rav Uzziel concludes from this that <i>Chazal</i> believe that blood type is determined exclusively by the mother. Thus, argues Rav Uzziel, the modern scientific belief that blood type is influenced by both the mother and the father contradicts <i>Chazal</i>. Rav Uzziel claims that Halachah is determined by <i>Chazal</i>’s belief and not by scientific assertion, and he accordingly rules that blood tests are not admissible evidence regarding paternal identity.
Most <i>poskim</i> agree with Rav Uzziel’s ruling. These authorities include many of the great <i>dayanim</i> of the twentieth century, such as Dayan Ehrenberg (<i>Teshuvot Devar Yehoshua</i> 3 E.H. 5), Rav Eliezer Waldenberg (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 13:104), and Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 10 E.H. 12 and 13).<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Shalom Messas (<i>Teshuvot Shemesh Umagein</i> 3 E.H. 17-18) also utilizes this argument in combination with other factors.</i>
Rav Waldenberg adds that scientific approaches are very much subject to change, and that which is accepted today is often rejected soon after. This skeptical attitude towards scientific conclusions is commonplace in rabbinic thought throughout the ages, as Rav Waldenberg notes.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For a summary of the halachic literature regarding the admissibility of scientific evidence for halachic matters, especially the laws of <i>niddah</i>, see <i>Nishmat Avraham</i> (2:82-85). See our later chapter regarding the interface of archaeology and Halachah for more discussion of this question.</i> Therefore, Rav Waldenberg echoes the ruling of many <i>dayanim</i> that we accept the divinely inspired assertions of <i>Chazal</i>, of which we are certain, and not scientific assertions, which are subject to revision.
<b>Blood Tests to Determine Paternal Identity – The Minority Opinion</b>
Not all <i>poskim</i> agree with this approach. Rav Yitzchak Herzog argues (in a letter published in <i>Assia</i> 35) that <i>poskim</i> who adopt this approach are “sticking their heads in the sand” by ignoring scientific facts. Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach (cited in <i>Nishmat Avraham</i> 3:30-31) notes that the Gemara need not necessarily be interpreted as teaching that blood type is determined exclusively by the mother. It could be understood as teaching that the mother merely provides the catalyst for the production of blood, leaving open the possibility of paternal influence on blood type even according to <i>Chazal</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Dr. Dov Frimer (<i>Shenaton Hamishpat Ha’ivri</i> 5:230 note ב54) records that Rav Shlomo Zalman told him that the Gemara may even be describing metaphysical connections between parents and children, not natural, biological connections.</i>
Rav Shlomo Dichovsky (in a <i>teshuvah</i> printed in <i>Teshuvot Bikkurei Asher </i>6) points out that the Rambam (<i>Moreh Nevuchim</i> 3:14) and the <i>Tashbeitz </i>(1:165) write that the many medical assertions that appear in the Gemara are not derived from divine sources but rather from the medical knowledge of the time. According to this approach, the accepted scientific evidence in each generation should constitute admissible evidence in a <i>beit din</i> hearing. Rav Dichovsky notes, though, that the Rivash (<i>Teshuvot Harivash</i> 447) disagrees with the Rambam and <i>Tashbeitz</i> and argues that the medical assertions that appear in the Gemara are in fact divinely inspired just like the rest of the Talmud.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For a summary of the debate regarding the nature of <i>Chazal</i>’s medical claims, see Rav Yaakov Neuberger’s article in the <i>Torah U-Madda Journal</i> (3:82-89).</i>
<b>Rav Sa’adiah Gaon’s Blood Test</b>
It seems that another major consideration lurks beneath the ruling of the majority opinion that rejects blood tests for use in <i>beit din</i>. A fascinating case involving Rav Sa’adiah Gaon is recorded in the <i>Sefer Chassidim</i> (232). A wealthy man once traveled a long distance with his pregnant wife and his slave. The man died, whereupon the slave presented himself as the wealthy man’s son and was awarded the deceased’s fortune by the local authorities. The wife then gave birth, and when the child came of age, he went to Rav Sa’adiah Gaon to complain about what the slave had done. Rav Sa’adiah Gaon exhumed the father’s body and removed one of the bones. He took blood samples from both the son and the slave and placed the exhumed bone into each sample. The bone absorbed the son’s blood but not the slave’s blood, thereby proving, in Rav Saadiah’s opinion, the identity of the true son.
The <i>Eliyah Rabbah</i> (568) cites this story but poses a question based on an incident recorded in the Gemara (Bava Batra 58a). A couple had ten children, but on his deathbed, the husband said that only one of the sons was truly his child. In order to determine who should inherit the estate, Rav Bena’ah devised an interesting plan. He instructed the boys to hit their father’s grave. All of the boys hit the grave except for one, to whom Rav Bena’ah then awarded the estate. The Rashbam (<i>ibid</i>. s.v. <i>Amar Lehu</i>) explains that this decision was an example of “<i>shuda d’dayni</i>” (loosely translated as a Solomonic decision),<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For further explanation of this concept, see <i>Tosafot Kiddushin</i> 74a s.v. <i>Shuda D’dayni</i>.</i> as the true son would not have the audacity to hit his father’s grave.
The <i>Eliyah Rabbah</i> wonders why Rav Bena’ah had to employ <i>shuda d’dayni</i> if he could have used Rav Sa’adiah Gaon’s blood test to determine the true son. The Rashash (commenting on the Rashbam <i>ibid</i>.) asserts that Rav Bena’ah did not want to administer this test since it would reveal that nine of the boys were <i>mamzeirim</i> (illegitimate). Rav Bena’ah’s test, on the other hand, revealed only that one of the boys was a more refined person than his siblings but did not necessarily prove the other siblings’ illegitimacy. The Rashash bases his explanation on the Rambam and <i>Bartenura</i>’s assertion (<i>Eiduyot</i> 8:7 s.v <i>V’kirvah</i>) that <i>mamzeirut</i> should not be publicized.
An explanation of the Rambam and <i>Bartenura</i>’s approach is that a problem of <i>mamzeirut</i> exists only when one has knowledge of it. At the same time, though, one has no obligation to reveal such knowledge. It seems that the Ran (Kiddushin 30a-b in the pages of the Rif s.v. <i>Tannu Rabbanan</i>, based on Kiddushin 71a) and the Rama (E.H. 2:5; see the comments to this Rama summarized in the <i>Otzar Haposkim ibid</i>.) subscribe to this explanation. Rav Elchanan Wasserman (in an essay printed in the Torah journal <i>Degel HaTorah</i> 5681-5682 pp. 63-64) supports and rules in accordance with this approach, and Rav Hershel Schachter<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Schachter cites Rav Elchanan’s ruling in <i>B’ikvei Hatzon</i> p. 58. </i> once issued a ruling based on it. Rav Aharon Lichtenstein told me that the practice of many community rabbis in pre-war Europe was to leave a locale when they heard that someone they knew to be a <i>mamzeir</i> was about to marry. Indeed, Rav Shmuel Tokayer told me that Rav Moshe Feinstein advised him to conduct himself accordingly in a similar situation that he encountered when he served as a Rav in the 1960s. The Gemara (ad. loc.) refers to this lenient approach to <i>mamzeirut</i> as a <i>tzedakah</i> (charity) that Hashem does for <i>Am Yisrael</i>.
It appears that a motivation behind most <i>dayanim</i>’s refusal to admit blood tests as evidence of fatherhood in <i>beit din</i> is that they choose, as Rav Bena’ah did, not to administer a test that could potentially reveal that people are <i>mamzeirim</i>.

Chapter 2

We shall now turn our attention to the issue of whether DNA evidence is admissible in <i>beit din</i>. We shall first present the rulings issued before the World Trade Center terrorist attacks, and then proceed to discuss the propriety of admitting DNA evidence in order to identify the remains of a husband missing from those attacks.
<b>Rav Shlomo Dichovsky and the Ashdod Beit Din</b>
In 1982, Rav Shlomo Dichovsky sat as a member of the Ashdod district Israeli Rabbinate <i>beit din</i>. After the <i>beit din</i> had presided over a couple’s divorce agreement and <i>get</i>, the husband opened a file challenging his heretofore presumed fatherhood of his wife’s two children. After ordering the couple to perform a blood test (in accordance with Rav Dichovsky’s opinion that such tests are admissible evidence), the <i>beit din</i> was informed that DNA tests (accurate up to 99.6% at that time) were now being administered to determine paternal identity. The DNA test revealed that the husband was the father of only one of his wife’s two children. When the results were read in <i>beit din</i> in the presence of both parties, the wife still insisted that her husband was the father of both of their children, denying ever having had an affair during the marriage. The question was whether the <i>beit din</i> should regard the results of the DNA test as evidence that the husband was not the father or whether it still should assume that the husband was the father of both children.
Rav Dichovsky writes (in a <i>teshuvah</i> printed in <i>Teshuvot Bikkurei Asher </i>6) that this question presents a conflict between two mechanisms of resolving doubt regarding paternal identity when a wife is suspected of adultery. On one hand, the Gemara (Sotah 27a) states that in such a case we assume that the husband is the father since “<i>Rov be’ilot achar haba’al</i>,” (a wife will have most of her relations with her husband). On the other hand, the DNA tests are almost always accurate. Rav Dichovsky ruled that since we are faced in this case with a conflict of two presumptions, both of which are true in a majority of situations, the doubt remains unresolved. Therefore, Rav Dichovsky opined that the <i>beit din</i> cannot obligate the husband to pay child support for the child in question since a <i>beit din</i> cannot coerce someone to pay in a case of doubt (<i>Bava Kama</i> 46a).
On the other hand, Rav Dichovsky stated that the child is not a <i>mamzeir</i> because the level of certainty necessary to presume that the child is a <i>mamzeir</i> against the assumption of <i>rov be’ilot achar haba’al</i> is extremely high. In fact, the Gemara (Yevamot 80b, codified by the Shulchan Aruch E.H. 4:14) states that if a husband travels overseas and his wife gives birth to a child twelve months after his departure, we assume that the husband is the father and that the wife was pregnant for twelve months! Similarly, reasons Rav Dichovsky, since (in 1982) DNA tests were only 99.6% accurate, there is insufficient evidence to pronounce the child a <i>mamzeir</i>.
The other two <i>dayanim</i> on the court disagreed with Rav Dichovsky and ruled that the DNA evidence was not admissible in this case. They ruled that the husband must pay child support despite the DNA evidence, arguing that the DNA test cannot prove an assertion that contradicts the principle of <i>rov be’ilot achar haba’al </i>and the <i>chazakah </i>(status quo assumption) in existence from the child’s birth until after the administration of the <i>get</i> that the husband was the father of the child. The husband appealed the ruling to the Rabbinic Court of Appeals, which at the time included Rav Avraham Shapira, which sustained the ruling of the Ashdod <i>beit din</i>.
<b>Rav Ovadia Yosef and the Israeli Rabbinic Court of Appeals</b>
In 1986, the Israeli Rabbinate’s Rabbinic Court of Appeals, consisting of Rav Ovadia Yosef, Rav Yosef Kafich, and Rav Yitzchak Kulitz, was presented with an appeal of a ruling by a district Israeli Rabbinate <i>beit din</i> regarding a fascinating case. A young man and woman lived together without the benefit of marriage. Some time after the couple separated, the woman was discovered to be pregnant, and she subsequently gave birth to a child. The young man claimed to be the father, while the young woman vigorously denied this claim. The young man demanded that a DNA test be administered to prove the validity of his claim, to which the wife refused to consent. The district <i>beit din</i> ruled that if the woman persisted in her refusal, it would interpret this as an admission that the young man was indeed the father. This ruling implied that DNA evidence is acceptable in <i>beit din</i>, since the <i>beit din</i> essentially accepted the man’s argument that a DNA test should be administered in order to determine paternity.
The Rabbinic Court of Appeals rejected the ruling of the district <i>beit din. </i>Rav Ovadia (<i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 10 E.H. 13) explains that just as the consensus view among <i>dayanim</i> is to consider blood tests inadmissible evidence in <i>beit din</i>, so too the consensus should reject DNA tests as proof in <i>beit din</i>. He bases his ruling on a number of passages in the Gemara (e.g. Yevamot 99a) that speak of a child whose father is known to be one of two possible men. The Gemara treats this doubt as irresolvable. Rav Ovadia argues that the fact that the Gemara does not mention either blood tests or DNA tests as a possible means to resolve the doubt indicates that these two methods are not acceptable means of determining paternal identity.
Although this argument from omission seems a bit far-fetched, the Gemara sometimes presents theoretical possibilities, such as transportation on a “flying camel” (Makkot 5a), in order to bring out certain principles. In our case, the Gemara does not present a theoretical means of resolving the doubt, leaving no precedent for resolving questions of paternal identity by means of DNA testing.
<b>Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv</b>
Rav Eliashiv (<i>Kovetz Teshuvot</i> 1:135) was asked to adjudicate a case related to DNA testing. A wife had claimed when she was pregnant that her husband was not the father of the unborn child. Years later, the father wished to know whether he should conduct genetic tests to determine if the wife was correct. Rav Eliashiv rules unequivocally, “Since many years have passed, and a <i>chazakah</i> has been established that he is the father of the child, one has no right to cast aspersions on the legitimacy of the child by engaging in testing.”
As a precedent, Rav Eliashiv cites the Rashash (cited in the previous chapter), who explains that the <i>Amora’im</i> (scholars of the Gemara) did not engage in testing to determine paternal identity at the risk of revealing that someone who was heretofore presumed to be legitimate (<i>b’chezkat kashrut</i>) was in fact a <i>mamzeir</i>. Rav Mendel Senderovic (<i>Teshuvot Atzei Besamim</i> 16) observes that Rav Eliashiv does not rule that DNA evidence is inadmissible evidence in <i>beit din</i>. In fact, it seems he does deem it admissible evidence, since he wanted to avoid having such evidence presented in this particular case.
<b>Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach</b>
Prior to the World Trade Center attacks, the strongest ruling in support of the admissibility of DNA evidence was issued by Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach. His ruling (cited in <i>Nishmat Avraham</i> 3:37) concerned an event that occurred in an Israeli hospital in 1977. Two babies were confused after birth, and all investigations could not conclusively determine the identity of the respective parents of each baby. The hospital administration suggested undergoing DNA testing to reach a final conclusion. Dr. Abraham S. Abraham consulted Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach and Rav Eliezer Waldenberg, both of whom permitted utilizing the results as a consideration in the final determination of the identity of the parents of each child.
This is quite a significant ruling, since Rav Waldenberg (as we noted in our previous chapter) is a staunch opponent of admitting blood tests as evidence in a <i>beit din</i> hearing.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Ariel Herzog notes that there is a fundamental distinction between the case that Rav Dichovsky adjudicated, in which, according to the majority opinion, the DNA test contradicted the Talmudic principles of <i>rov be’ilot achar haba’al</i> and <i>chazakah</i>, and the case Rav Waldenberg dealt with regarding the hospital, in which there was no such conflict. Moreover, <i>Chazal</i> seem to have relaxed the rules of admissibility of evidence in the context of identifying a child, as the testimony of the midwife is accepted (Kiddushin 74a; see the Ran, Kiddushin 31a in the pages of the Rif s.v. <i>Ne’emenet</i>). Thus, it is not clear how Rav Waldenberg and the majority opinion in Rav Dichovsky’s case would rule in the other’s case. For a discussion of trusting contemporary hospital identification of babies, see <i>Teshuvot Moznei Tzedek</i> (E.H. 2:2).</i> In addition, Dr. Abraham writes that Rav Shlomo Zalman later wrote to him, “If this [DNA] test is well-known and accepted throughout the world as reliable as a result of numerous and unambiguous tests, it is reasonable to say that the results of this test constitute admissible evidence by halachic standards.”
<b>The World Trade Center</b>
Having seen the different opinions regarding DNA evidence, we will now discuss how <i>poskim </i>applied their rulings in the aftermath of the World Trade Center terrorist attacks. In some cases, DNA evidence was the only possible way to identify the remains of a missing husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The application of DNA evidence to the World Trade center <i>agunot</i> is discussed briefly in <i>Gray Matter 2 </i>pp. 121-123.</i> We will focus on Rav Mendel Senderovic’s discussion (<i>Teshuvot Atzei Besamim</i> 16) of this issue, which presents excellent arguments to support the unequivocal ruling of Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg that DNA is admissible evidence to identify the remains of a missing husband.
<b>DNA Evidence vs. Blood Tests</b>
Rav Senderovic notes that only blood tests seem to have a passage in the Gemara that precludes their use in determining paternal identity. The same Gemara notes that the father contributes the material that produces the bones and fingernails of the child. Thus, argues Rav Senderovic, a DNA sample may be taken from bones and fingernails in order to determine paternal identity. The Haifa <i>beit din </i>(cited in <i>Assia</i> 35:47) also drew such a distinction. Although there is no precedent in the Gemara that is analogous to DNA identification, there are no clear counter-indications that preclude its use.
In addition, the skepticism that Rav Waldenberg expressed in regards to scientific evidence seems to be irrelevant to DNA identification of a missing husband’s remains. DNA identification is based on the assertion that no one (save for identical twins) has the same DNA. Rav Senderovic notes that this has been empirically proven regarding more than one million individuals. Accordingly, skepticism about the accuracy of DNA testing appears misplaced in light of the overwhelming evidence supporting its accuracy.
<b>Can We Devise <i>Simanim</i>?</b>
Rav Senderovic notes that some have argued that the absence of a Talmudic precedent for DNA testing automatically precludes its use as a new means of identifying individuals. A proof for this might be derived from the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (<i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer</i> O.C. 207, cited by the Mishnah Berurah 648:65),<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chatam Sofer</i>, though, appears to have changed his mind about this issue; see <i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer</i> O.C. 183, also cited by the Mishnah Berurah 648:65.</i> who rules that we may not rely on the <i>simanim</i> (means of identification) devised by many <i>Acharonim</i> (cited in the <i>Mishnah Berurah ibid</i>.) to determine whether or not an <i>etrog</i> is a hybrid (<i>murkav</i>). The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> explains that the problem with these <i>simanim </i>(such as bumpy skin and an indented stem) is that they do not appear in the Gemara. One could claim that DNA evidence may not be relied upon since it also lacks a Talmudic source.
One could respond to this argument in a number of ways. Rav Senderovic notes that the Gemara (Chullin 64a) discusses <i>simanim </i>(such as having the yolk on the inside and the albumin on the outside) to identify eggs as the product of a kosher bird. According to Rashi (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Hachi Garsinan</i>), the Gemara rejects the use of these <i>simanim</i> because we do not have a tradition from Sinai that such <i>simanim</i> constitute legitimate evidence of kashrut. Rabbeinu Tam (cited in <i>Tosafot</i> ad. loc. s.v. <i>Simanin</i>), on the other hand, explains that <i>simanim</i> do not constitute adequate evidence because of the counterevidence of the eggs of a raven (a non-kosher bird), which have the <i>simanim</i> of the eggs of a kosher bird. The Ramban (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Hah</i>) combines the reasons of Rashi and Rabbeinu Tam, explaining that we do not rely on <i>simanim</i> for eggs because of both the counterevidence of the raven’s eggs and the lack of a tradition from Sinai.
Rav Senderovic argues that since after years of testing, scientists have not found two individuals (other than identical twins) with the same DNA, we may rely on DNA as a <i>siman</i> to identify the remains of a missing husband. Since there is no counterevidence to question its accuracy, Rabbeinu Tam and the Ramban would approve of DNA evidence. Rav Senderovic notes that Rashi seems to imply that this is a specific exclusion that applies only to eggs and is not a principle that applies to all halachic realms. Accordingly, all <i>Rishonim</i> would admit DNA evidence as <i>simanim</i>.
Most important, Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg and Rav Senderovic note, is the precedent of <i>poskim</i> relying on fingerprints (see <i>Otzar Haposkim</i> 17, <i>Rishum Simanim</i> 62) to identify the remains of a missing husband. DNA evidence is entirely analogous to finger prints, argue Rav Zalman Nechemia and Rav Senderovic, so DNA evidence, like fingerprints, should be reliable evidence to identify the remains of a missing husband.
<b>Random Sampling</b>
Rav Senderovic also notes that some have questioned the efficacy of DNA on the grounds that not every human being alive has been tested to determine if everyone has different DNA. They argue that science has proven only that the over one million people tested do not have the same DNA. This, in their view, does not constitute adequate evidence. Rav Zalman Nechemia responds that when <i>Chazal</i> made determinations, such as that a majority of animals do not have a blemish that would render an animal a <i>treifah</i> (thus permitting us to drink the milk of animals that have not been examined for <i>treifot</i>), they reached their conclusions based on random sampling. Rav Zalman Nechemia finds it counterintuitive to maintain that <i>Chazal</i> traveled throughout the world and inspected every animal to reach this determination.
Rav Senderovic suggests that one could counter that perhaps the assertions made by <i>Chazal</i> are derived from divine sources and therefore constitute impeccable evidence despite the fact that they did not check every animal in the world. Rav Senderovic rejects this line of thought, noting the Gemara (Chullin 47a) that records an incident in which Rav Ashi thought to classify an animal as a <i>treifah</i> because of what he perceived as an abnormality. However, Rav Huna Mar bar Avya told him that all of these “<i>boryata</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Boryata</i>) explains this to mean either rural or healthy.</i> animals have this abnormality and that the butchers are very familiar with it and even have a name for it. The Gemara gives Rav Huna Mar bar Avya the last word, indicating that Rav Ashi accepted his critique. Rav Senderovic derives from this passage that <i>Chazal</i> made at least some determinations based on their experience with animals from the area and did not conduct an investigation of every animal in the world in order to reach their conclusions.
<b>Combination of Inadequate Simanim</b>
Rav Senderovic notes that some have argued that DNA testing constitutes inadmissible evidence because its conclusions are based on the combination of thousands of sub-factors that individually constitute inadequate evidence. They cite the ruling of the Rama (E.H. 17:24; see <i>Gray Matter 2</i> pp. 120-121) that many inadequate <i>simanim</i> cannot be combined to constitute one adequate <i>siman</i>. Rav Senderovic responds that one views the thousands of sub-factors as one whole unit, which undoubtedly constitutes adequate evidence.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>siman</i> that is unique to fewer than one out of a thousand people constitutes an adequate <i>siman</i> to identify the remains of a missing husband, as noted by the Beit Shmuel (17:74). The possibility of two people having the same DNA ranges from one in ten billion to one in one quintillion.</i>
As a precedent, Rav Senderovic cites a ruling from the <i>Terumat Hadeshen</i> (239, the source of the aforementioned ruling of the Rama) that one may combine the fact that a missing husband’s remains show that he was blind in an eye and that he had a scar extending from that same eye to his mouth to constitute an adequate <i>siman</i> (also see Pitchei Teshuvah E.H. 17:107). Similarly, the <i>Mishpatim Yesharim</i> (1:39) rules that we accept a tooth that is cracked, protrudes, and has a black spot at its edge as an adequate <i>siman</i>. Just as we view the eye and the tooth as constituting one unit that has more than one feature rather than examining each identifying mark individually, so too we view DNA conclusions as constituting one <i>siman</i> even though they are created through a combination of many sub-factors. Moreover, it is even more compelling to view a person’s DNA as constituting one individual unit of evidence given that the sub-sections of DNA are inherently connected to each other. As support for his contention, Rav Senderovic once again cites the precedent of <i>poskim</i> relying on fingerprints as an adequate <i>siman</i>, as fingerprints prove identity by combining the strands from the entire fingerprint.
In line with the rulings of Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach, Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg, and Rav Mendel Senderovic, the Beth Din of America utilized DNA evidence as an important (but not exclusive) consideration in identifying the remains of husbands who were missing as a result of the World Trade Center terrorist attacks.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Shmuel Wosner and Rav Nissim Karelitz (<i>Techumin</i> 21:123) both consider DNA to constitute a semi-adequate <i>siman</i>. They also distinguish between DNA evidence taken from the person in question (e.g. his comb or brush), to which they give slightly more credence, and DNA evidence based on comparison to a relative, despite the fact that the possibility of error in the latter form still is less than one in ten billion. Rav Senderovic is astonished that Rav Wosner and Rav Karelitz do not offer a reason for not accepting DNA evidence as a full-fledged <i>siman</i>, as it seems to satisfy the criteria for such categorization. Rav Senderovic also finds the distinction between DNA evidence taken from the victim and DNA evidence based on a comparison to a relative to be shocking, as there seems to be no basis for such a distinction, and Rav Wosner and Rav Karelitz offer no explanation.</i>
<i><b>Mamzeirut</b></i><b> and DNA Evidence<i></i></b>
A potential problem with allowing DNA evidence is that it opens a proverbial Pandora’s box, since once <i>batei din</i> admit DNA as evidence in some cases, they seemingly must accept DNA as evidence of parentage (against the 1986 ruling of the Rabbinic Court of Appeals cited earlier). This potentially opens a floodgate of <i>mamzeirut</i> cases in which DNA evidence indicates that a husband is not the father of his wife’s child.
We should note that Rav Shlomo Dichovsky’s claim (cited above) that DNA testing is insufficient to determine <i>mamzeirut</i> since it is only 99.6% accurate is no longer valid, since the chance of error in a properly administered DNA test ranges from less than one in ten billion to less than one in one quintillion.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See, though, the rulings of Rav Ovadia Yosef and Rav Shalom Messas issued in 1996 and printed in <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 10 E.H. 8.</i>
Rav Mordechai Willig (<i>Kol Zvi</i> 4:12) grapples with this problem and at first argues that we should permit an <i>agunah</i> to remarry based on DNA identification despite concern that it will cause others to be declared <i>mamzeirim</i>.
