He notes the celebrated Mishnah (<i>Ohalot</i> 7:6) that states, “<i>Ein dochin nefesh mipnei nefesh</i>” (we do not sacrifice one soul in order to save another).<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This is a poetic way of saying that we do not choose one person’s gain at the expense of another’s loss.</i>
Rav Willig presents another potential distinction that may solve this problem. In a wide variety of halachic areas, we do not attach any significance to that which is not visible to the naked eye. The <i>Chochmat Adam</i> (<i>Binat Adam</i> 38:49), Rav Shlomo Kluger (<i>Teshuvot Tuv Ta’am Vada’at</i> 2 <i>Kuntress Acharon</i> 53), the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 84:36), and Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> 6:47) rule that we need not be concerned about consuming bugs that can be seen only with the aid of a magnifying glass. Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 3:120:5) rules that we need not determine that <i>tefillin</i> are square using a microscope.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Gemara (Menachot 35a) states that we have a tradition from Sinai that <i>tefillin</i> must be square.</i> The <i>Tiferet Yisrael</i> (Avodah Zarah 2:3 in <i>Boaz</i>) rules that a fish whose scales are visible only when viewed with a magnifying glass is not kosher. He similarly rules that an animal with a hole in its lung that can be seen only with a magnifying glass is not a <i>treifah</i>. The <i>Teshuvot Even Yekarah</i> (2:33) rules that a <i>Tanach</i> whose tiny letters are visible only if viewed with a magnifying glass is not considered “written.” Rav Yosef Messas (<i>Teshuvot Mayim Chaim</i> 1:259) permits using an <i>etrog</i> whose blemishes can be detected only when examined with a magnifying glass. As such, we need not be concerned with the microscopic strands of DNA that suggest that a child is a <i>mamzeir</i>.
Accordingly, one may ask how we can rely on DNA evidence if DNA strands are not visible to the naked eye. Rav Willig suggests that a distinction can be drawn between evidence to prove the death of a missing husband (<i>eidut ishah</i>) and other areas of Halachah. Classical halachic sources relax the rules of evidence in many aspects regarding <i>agunot</i>, such as accepting hearsay evidence and the testimony of those who are normally considered invalid witnesses (such as women).<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See the sources and explanation presented in <i>Gray Matter 2</i> p. 118.</i> Contemporary authorities continue this tradition by accepting the testimony of non-observant Jews who were raised as such (see <i>Gray Matter 2</i> p. 119) in the context of <i>agunot</i>. Similarly, we can argue that even though DNA evidence does not constitute evidence in other areas of Halachah, it does carry weight in the context of permitting an <i>agunah</i> to remarry.
Rav Mordechai Willig also suggests that DNA evidence would merely prove another man to be the father, not necessarily that the child is a <i>mamzeir</i>, since it is possible that the child was conceived through artificial insemination. According to Rav Yaakov Breisch (<i>Teshuvot Chelkat Yaakov</i> 1:24), Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 1:10), and Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik (cited in <i>Nefesh Harav</i> p. 255), a child conceived via artificial insemination is not a <i>mamzeir</i> even if the sperm donor is not the husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Many <i>poskim</i>, however, disagree; see <i>Teshuvot Minchat Yitzchak</i> 4:5, <i>Teshuvot Minchat Shlomo</i> 3:98, and the ruling of the Satmar Rebbe that appears in <i>Hama’or</i> 5724.</i> As we noted in our previous essay, if a husband was overseas and his wife gave birth to a child up to twelve months after he left, we do not consider the child to be a <i>mamzeir</i>. Thus, we see that Halachah will rely even on remote possibilities (in combination with the Gemara’s assertion that <i>rov be’ilot achar haba’al</i>) in order to avoid problems of <i>mamzeirut</i>.
A problem with this latter approach is that it has never (to my knowledge) been suggested by any of the other great <i>poskim</i> of the modern period who have been forced to grapple with a large number of potential <i>mamzeirut</i> situations. It also was not mentioned as a viable option in the responsa regarding the admissibility of blood tests as evidence of parentage. We should clarify that it would seem that even according to Rav Willig’s suggested approach, one cannot solve every case of <i>mamzeirut</i> by assuming that the child was conceived by artificial insemination. It seems that this line of reasoning is appropriate only in combination with the assumption of <i>rov be’ilot achar haba’al</i>.
<b>Conclusion</b>
While blood tests can be ignored at no “cost,” since there is (for the most part) no halachic benefit to relying on them, DNA evidence can benefit many people. Not surprisingly, we find contemporary <i>poskim</i> relying either fully or partially on DNA evidence, especially to identify the remains of a missing husband. At the same time, most <i>poskim</i> manage to avoid allowing DNA evidence to prove <i>mamzeirut</i>.

Kashrut

Chalav Yisrael

The Mishnah (Avodah Zarah 2:6) records that <i>Chazal</i> forbade the consumption of milk from a kosher animal that was milked by a non-Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For a discussion of whether a non-observant Jew is included in this category, see <i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> (Y.D. 1:46), the views of Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv and Rav Shmuel Wosner cited in <i>Bin’tiv Hechalav</i> (p. 32 and p. 35), and <i>Encyclopedia Talmudit</i> (15:174).</i> without Jewish supervision (<i>chalav akum</i>, as opposed to <i>chalav yisrael</i>, milk that was supervised). The Gemara explains that this was enacted out of concern that the non-Jew may have mixed non-kosher milk with the kosher milk. In recent times, the limited likelihood of this risk has sparked much debate within the Orthodox community as to how applicable this restriction is today. We shall present both sides of the question, starting with the view of Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik.
It is vitally important to note that we will address the issue as it pertains to the current realities in the United States. In other countries, however, it may be forbidden to consume unsupervised milk according to all authorities.
<b>Rav Soloveitchik’s Three Considerations for Leniency</b>
It is well known among Rav Soloveitchik’s students that when he resided in the United States, he drank packaged milk that did not have any special rabbinic supervision. Rav Menachem Genack related (in a <i>shiur</i> at Yeshiva University) that Rav Soloveitchik delineated three considerations to be lenient. First, some authorities rule leniently if there are no non-kosher animals in the herd that is being milked (<i>ein b’edro tamei</i>). Second, we may rely on the government (USDA) supervision and inspections to ensure that the milk we consume is in fact from cows. Finally, the rabbinic edict forbidding the consumption of milk from an animal that was milked by a non-Jew technically does not apply today, since the cows are milked by machines.
<i><b>Ein B’edro Tamei</b></i>
The <i>Rishonim</i> and <i>Acharonim</i> debate whether the prohibition against <i>chalav akum</i> applies even if the non-Jew has no non-kosher animals in his herd (see <i>Mordechai</i>, Avodah Zarah 826, <i>Teshuvot Radbaz</i> 4:1147, and <i>Semak</i> 223). Some authorities dismiss this exception entirely, while others are lenient only if no non-kosher animals are milked in an entire locale. The later <i>Acharonim</i> are divided as to how to resolve this issue. The <i>Pri Chadash</i> (Y.D. 115:6) and the <i>Chazon Ish</i> (Y.D. 41:4) rule leniently, <sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See, though, our discussion of the <i>Chazon Ish</i>’s opinion that appears later in this section.</i> whereas the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 115:5),<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 115:6) relates: <br>To emphasize our point (of not following the <i>Pri Chadash</i>’s leniency), I will present what someone once shared with me with a broken heart when I served as a Rav in a certain locale. One of the prominent community members of that locale adopted the lenient approach when traveling to the country’s largest city. He always frequented [a particular inn], and he and other guests, when they would drink their hot drinks every morning, would purchase a “fatty milk” from a non-Jewish merchant whose store was located near the inn. Once, they began to wonder how this very small store got such a large supply of fatty milk, and they went to the merchant to inquire about this matter. The merchant responded that he would purchase large quantities of animal brains from the [non-kosher] butcher, grind [and mix] them together with large quantities of milk, and then cook them together. Upon hearing this, they all fell on their faces upon realizing their great sin of consuming non-kosher meat as well as forbidden mixtures of milk and meat. The community member shared this with me and cried aloud “How great are the words of our sages.”</i> the <i>Chochmat Adam</i> (67:1), and the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (Y.D. 107, cited in the Pitchei Teshuvah Y.D. 115:3) rule strictly. The latter three authorities note that the custom among Eastern and Central European Jews was to follow the stringent opinion. The <i>Darchei Teshuvah</i> (115:6) reports that this also was the custom in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. The <i>Pri Chadash</i>, though, records that Amsterdam’s custom was to be lenient, and the <i>Darchei Teshuvah</i> (<i>ibid</i>.) writes the same about a number of communities.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For further sources on this hotly debated issue, see <i>Sdei Chemed</i> 8:45.</i>
The <i>Beit Meir</i> (cited in the <i>Darchei Teshuvah</i>) argues that even according to the lenient opinion, there is virtually no locale that has no non-kosher animals, so this line of leniency is hardly ever relevant. On the other hand, the <i>Pri Chadash</i> and his supporters believe that <i>ein b’edro tamei</i> means that no non-kosher animals are <b>milked</b> in the area. An animal in the zoo or a pet does not appear to impinge on the applicability of this rule according to the <i>Pri Chadash</i>.
Even if the strict ruling is adopted, the lenient opinions might still be used as a legitimate <i>snif l’hakel</i> (adjunct consideration) to a lenient ruling. One example of this usage is the responsum of Rav David Tzvi Hoffman (<i>Teshuvot Melamed L’ho’il</i> 2:33) in which he relies on the lenient opinion as one consideration to permit a sick individual, for medicinal purposes, to drink buttermilk that was not supervised. As non-kosher milk is not commercially available in the United States,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Avrohom Gordimer of the Orthodox Union confirmed that non-kosher milk is not commercially available in the United States, at least not in normative shopping venues that service the masses.</i> Rav Soloveitchik applies these lenient opinions as one consideration to rule leniently.
We must clarify, though, that these lenient opinions do not apply in Israel. Rav Zev Weitman, the Rav of the Israeli dairy giant Tnuva, reports (<i>Techumin </i>22:459) that camel milk is (regrettably) commercially available in Israel and actually is used as an ingredient in ice cream for sale in southern portions of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. This is one of the reasons that the Israeli Chief Rabbinate is not lenient regarding <i>chalav akum</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Rav Eliyahu Bakshi-Doron’s essay in <i>Techumin</i> (23:463-472).</i>
Nonetheless, Rav Weitman (<i>Bin’tiv Hechalav</i> p. 40) contends that since the price of non-kosher milk is tens of times greater than that of kosher milk, we need not be concerned that the former was introduced into the latter. He argues for extending the <i>Pri Chadash</i>’s interpretation of <i>ein b’edro tamei</i> to allow <i>chalav akum</i> even where non-kosher animals are milked as long as a large price difference discourages mixing the milks. The basis for this approach is the Gemara’s assertion (Avodah Zarah 34b) that although the Mishnah (Avodah Zarah 2:4) prohibits non-Jews’ <i>muryas</i> (oil from pickled fish, which sometimes contained wine) due to concern that non-kosher wine was added, the prohibition does not apply when wine is far more expensive than pure <i>muryas</i>.
<b>Government Inspection</b>
Although the custom in most of Europe was to follow the strict view of the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>, as we cited above, the practice of most observant Jews in the United States during the early part of the twentieth century was to follow the lenient opinion. Those who followed the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> constituted the exception rather than the rule. Rav Melech Schachter related to me that knowledgable American Jews assumed that they were following the lenient approach of the <i>Pri Chadash</i>. Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 1:46-48), however, presents two different arguments to defend the lenient practice.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In fact, Rav Moshe writes (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 1:46) that the custom of Ashkenazic Jews is not to follow the <i>Pri Chadash</i>. Hence, he forbids purchasing milk directly from a non-Jewish farmer whose milking process is completely unsupervised.</i> One argument is that in America, where the government supervises the milking process to ensure that only cow’s milk is being sold, even the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> would permit consuming <i>chalav akum</i>. This is because Halachah, in the context of the laws of testimony, equates clear and certain knowledge of an event with seeing an event (see Shevuot 34a). Our knowledge that the government monitors the milk in this country is the equivalent, argues Rav Moshe, of watching the milking process ourselves.
Rav Moshe’s second argument is based on the Gemara (Avodah Zarah 39b) that states that the Jewish supervisor need not constantly watch the milking, for as long as he has easy access to view the milking, the milk is acceptable. This is because the non-Jew milking the cow is afraid (<i>mirtat</i>) to introduce non-kosher milk, lest the Jew see him. It seems clear that as long as the non-Jew is afraid to put non-kosher milk into the kosher milk, one is permitted to consume the non-Jew’s milk. Hence, Rav Moshe asserts (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 1:46), “In any case in which there is fear of government penalty, this rabbinic prohibition does not apply.”
Many of the great twentieth-century authorities consider reliable government supervision as a factor in their rulings. These authorities include the <i>Chazon Ish</i> (Y. D. 41:4, though see below), Rav Tzvi Pesach Frank (<i>Teshuvot Har Tzvi</i> Y.D. 103, discussing powdered milk),<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Frank wrote his responsum in the summer of 1944, a very trying period in Eretz Yisrael. He does not specify, though, whether his ruling applies only in such a case of great need or in all cases. Additionally, Rav Frank rules leniently because powdered milk, unlike regular milk, was never included in any prohibition. He does not address the permissibility of regular milk, as that issue was not presented to him.</i> Rav Yosef Eliyahu Henkin (<i>Teshuvot Ivra</i> 43), and Rav Yaakov Kaminetzsky (<i>Emet L’yaakov Al Shulchan Aruch </i>p. 308).<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Kaminetzky does not explain the basis for his ruling (which he limits to cases of great need); he merely makes reference to the lenient position of the <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i>
<b>A Critique of Rav Moshe’s Leniency</b>
Rav Moshe’s argument is somewhat debatable. The point of the <i>Chatam Sofer </i>(in rejecting the <i>Pri Chadash</i>) appears to be that even though we are certain that no non-kosher milk was introduced into the milk, a Jew still must supervise the milking process. Thus, the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> seems not to accept the argument that knowledge is equivalent to vision in the context of this Halachah. Even in the aforementioned Gemara, the Jew who is not constantly watching at least is minimally involved with the milking process, as he is seated outside the barn. In the case of government supervision, in contrast, no Jew is involved at all in supervising the milking.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Those who adopt the strict approach to the <i>chalav yisrael</i> issue discuss whether supervision by video cameras and computers suffices even according to the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>. Rav Zev Weitman (<i>Techumin</i> 22:466-468) and Rav Mordechai Gross (<i>Bin’tiv Hechalav</i> pp. 54-56) address this issue at length. In these situations, a Jew is involved in the supervision process, apparently making them more analogous to the situation described in the aforementioned Gemara than to non-Jewish government supervision. Rav Weitman reports that Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv and Rav Shmuel Wosner ruled (in a ruling issued to the rabbinic supervisors of the <i>mehadrin</i> division of Tnuva) that supervision of the milking process by video cameras suffices to satisfy the opinion of the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> and to categorize the milk as <i>chalav yisrael</i>.</i>
On the other hand, the <i>Pri Chadash</i> (and, correspondingly, the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>) addresses a situation in which there is no external supervision of the milking process, and hence no <i>mirtat</i> on the part of the non-Jews milking the cows. Rather, the <i>Pri Chadash</i> relies solely on the reasoning that the likelihood that the non-Jews would be practically able to introduce non-kosher milk into their product is minute. Thus, it is debatable whether government supervision is closer to the case in the aforementioned Gemara or to that of the <i>Pri Chadash</i>.
The <i>Chazon Ish</i> (Y.D. 41:4) associates the idea of relying on government supervision with the reasoning of the <i>Pri Chadash</i>. According to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>, then, the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> rejects relying on government supervision in this context. We must clarify, though, that the <i>Chazon Ish</i> himself is quite inclined toward the view of the <i>Pri Chadash</i>, although he does not rule explicitly in accordance with it. The <i>Chazon Ish</i>’s brother-in-law, Rav Yaakov Kanievsky, clarifies (<i>Krayna D’igrata</i> 2:123) that the <i>Chazon Ish</i> relied on the <i>Pri Chadash</i> to permit frail individuals to drink powdered milk in difficult wartime years when milk was not readily available in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Thus, the <i>Chazon Ish</i> essentially accepts the view of the <i>Pri Chadash</i>, although he does so only in a case of great need.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">For further discussion of the <i>Chazon Ish</i>’s view on this issue, see <i>Bin’tiv Hechalav</i> p. 31.</i>
Many other <i>Acharonim</i> also adopt the approach that government supervision is inadequate to satisfy the view of the <i>Chatam Sofer</i>. These authorities include Rav Yaakov Breisch (<i>Teshuvot Chelkat Yaakov</i> 3:37-38), Rav Yitzchak Yaakov Weisz (<i>Teshuvot Minchat Yitzchak</i> 9:81), and Rav Shmuel Wosner (<i>Teshuvot Sheivet Halevi</i> 4:87).<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Wosner records a remarkable conversation he had with the <i>Chazon Ish</i> about this matter.</i>
Moreover, Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> 4:42) rules in accordance with the strict view despite the fact that the two primary advocates of the lenient view, the Radbaz and the <i>Pri Chadash</i>, are among the most authoritative Sephardic halachic authorities. Rav Ovadia bases his ruling on the Chida (<i>Shiyurei Berachah</i> 115:1), who records that the custom in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and its environs was to follow the strict opinion (as we quoted above from the <i>Darchei Teshuvah</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shiyurei Berachah </i>adds that if a community does not have an established practice, it should adopt the stringent position.</i> Indeed, Rav Mordechai Eliyahu writes (<i>Kol Tzofayich</i> 145) that even those who observe only the standard level of kashrut (not <i>mehadrin</i>)<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The Israeli Chief Rabbinate maintains two levels of kashrut certification: basic kosher and <i>mehadrin</i>, the latter being for those who wish to abide by stricter halachic standards.</i> should scrupulously avoid relying on the lenient opinion in this matter. Even Rav Moshe Feinstein writes in his many responsa on this topic that <i>a ba’al nefesh</i> (someone who is very meticulous in his halachic observance) should follow the strict opinion on this issue, as we shall discuss.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe writes (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 1:47), though, that one who adopts the stricter approach because he believes that government-supervised milk is even possibly forbidden is mistaken, and does not require <i>hatarat nedarim</i> (annulment of vows) should he choose to discontinue his strict practice. Rav Hershel Schachter stated (at a convention of the Rabinical Council of America) that one who heeds Rav Moshe’s recommendation to be strict need not follow his stringency when visiting others who are not strict about <i>chalav yisrael </i>(and no <i>hatarat nedarim </i>is required). He based this argument on the <i>Dagul Meir’vavah </i>(Y.D. 114:1), who rules that one who adopts a <i>chumra </i>(stringency) does so with the implicit intention that he does not accept it in circumstances where it would be very difficult to observe. We should note, however, that this applies only if one adopts Rav Moshe’s approach that government supervised milk is essentially permitted, not if one follows those <i>poskim </i>who rule that such milk is essentially forbidden.</i>
<i><b>Davar Sheb’minyan</b></i>
Despite Rav Moshe Feinstein’s lenient ruling, it is well known that he encouraged people, both in writing (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 1:47) and orally, to drink only <i>chalav yisrael </i>milk.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe </i>(Y.D. 2:35), where Rav Moshe strongly urges Yeshivot to serve only <i>chalav yisrael</i> milk despite the expense and economic difficulty involved in doing so, since “This also is part of a Jewish education - to teach that it is proper for <i>bnei torah</i> (those exceptionally dedicated to Torah life) to be strict even when there is only a mere concern for violating a prohibition, since from this they will realize how one must fear violating Torah prohibitions.” </i> The primary reason for this is the Gemara’s principle (Beitzah 5a), “<i>Davar sheb’minyan tzarich minyan acheir l’hatiro</i>,” which means, effectively, that a rabbinic edict applies even if its reason no longer applies. This point is strongly emphasized by the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> in his aforementioned responsum concerning <i>chalav yisrael</i>. Although the <i>Pri Chadash</i> argues that the decree against <i>chalav akum</i> was not a <i>davar sheb’minyan</i> (i.e. there was no formal prohibition in situations in which there was no practical concern for a mixture of non-kosher milk), the custom in most pre-war communities in Europe was not to rely on the <i>Pri Chadash</i>.
However, Rav Soloveitchik’s third reason to rule leniently might overcome this obstacle. He argues that the edict applies only if a non-Jew milks the animal, but not if a machine milks it.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Actions performed by a machine are not directly attributed to the individual who activated the machine (except for the very first action the machine performs). See Chullin 16a, which discusses indirect <i>shechitah; Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> (1:14), which summarizes the rich debate regarding machine-baked <i>matzah</i>); and <i>Chazon Ish</i> (O.C. 6) and <i>Teshuvot Har Tzvi</i> (O.C. 1:10), who discuss machines spinning wool for <i>tzitzit</i>. </i> According to this approach, the rabbinic edict technically does not apply to the milk we currently drink even if one assumes that <i>chalav akum </i>was prohibited by <i>Chazal</i> as a full-fledged <i>davar sheb’minyan</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">One might then ask why wine produced by a non-Jew should be a problem today, when the wine is produced entirely by machinery without any direct contact between the producer and the product. The answer is that regarding wine, the Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 125:2) states explicitly that if it is produced by non-Jews, it is prohibited even if they produced it indirectly and never touched it. In the Halachot of milk production, by contrast, the Shulchan Aruch includes no such proviso, leaving open the possibility that indirectly produced milk is permissible. Moreover, Rav Soloveitchik’s approach stems from the Mishnah’s formulation of the prohibition of <i>chalav akum</i> as “milk that was milked by a non-Jew,” which might imply that the enactment technically does not apply to milk that was not directly milked by a non-Jew. The prohibition to consume wine produced by a non-Jew is not formulated in this way, but rather as simply “[non-Jewish] wine.”</i>
None of the other twentieth-century <i>poskim</i> propose Rav Soloveitchik’s argument. Perhaps they believe that since the enactment against <i>chalav akum</i> stems from concern that non-kosher milk was introduced into the product, a concern that is relevant even if the milk was produced by machine, the enactment still applies in such circumstances.
<b>Following Different Opinions</b>
Those who follow the strict opinion should certainly not regard those who rule leniently as being unobservant of kashrut laws, since they are following eminent halachic authorities such as Rav Feinstein and Rav Soloveitchik. Rav Yaakov Kaminetzky (<i>Emet L’yaakov Al Shulchan Aruch </i>p. 308) rules that those who follow the strict opinion regarding <i>chalav yisrael</i> are even permitted to eat using the dishes and utensils of those who follow the lenient view. As a precedent, he cites the ruling of the Rama (Y.D. 64:9) regarding a certain fat whose kashrut was subject to halachic dispute, with different communities following different opinions. The Rama rules that those who adopt the strict view are permitted to eat from the dishes and utensils of those who adopt the lenient view. He rules similarly (Y.D. 115:1) regarding the dispute about butter produced by a non-Jew (which we shall discuss below), regarding which there are different practices among different communities.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">For an explanation of these rulings of the Rama, see <i>Gray Matter 1 </i>p. 247 note 2.</i> Rav Kaminetzky also rules that one who adopts the strict view is permitted to give non-<i>chalav yisrael</i> products to those who are lenient about this issue, and does not thereby violate the prohibition against causing others to sin.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">For an explanation of this ruling, see <i>Gray Matter 1 </i>p. 171.</i>
It is also important for one who is lenient to take care to serve only <i>chalav yisrael</i> to those who adopt the strict opinion (see Rama Y.D. 119:7).
<b>Other Potentially Relevant Issues</b>
One concern of those who rule strictly is that if <i>chalav yisrael</i> is not observed, this law will be forgotten by <i>Am Yisrael</i> (see <i>Teshuvot Chelkat Yaakov</i> 3:37). We may come to forget this Halachah and not know to apply it even when its reason is applicable, such as in countries or circumstances in which the lenient considerations are not relevant. Accordingly, those who adopt the strict position remind those who are lenient that sometimes milk can be considered non-kosher.
In addition, Rav Zev Weitman observes (<i>Techumin</i> 22:460-463) that today, a significant number of cows throughout the world undergo a surgical procedure that renders them (and the milk they produce) <i>tereifot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The third chapter of <i>Masechet Chullin</i> outlines eighteen defects in an animal from which one could expect that the animal will die within a year, rendering it forbidden to eat due to the prohibition to eat a <i>tereifah </i>(Shemot 22:30). A perforation in an animal’s stomach is one of these eighteen defects, and a contemporary surgical procedure involves making a hole in the animal’s stomach, albeit for therapeutic purposes.</i> Rabbinic monitoring of the situation is necessary to ascertain that this procedure does not become common enough to render generic milk as non-kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Weitman believes that this would cause all milk to become problematic because the lenient approaches outlined above all rely on the assumption that the milk supply is uncontaminated with non-kosher milk. Since milk from <i>treifot</i> is not kosher (following the rule “<i>yotzei min hatamei tamei</i>,” “that which emerges from a non-kosher source is not kosher;” see Bechorot 5b), the original reasons that prompted <i>Chazal</i> to prohibit <i>chalav akum</i> would again apply. One could argue, though, that the enactment stems only due to concern for milk from a non-kosher animal, not milk from an animal defined as a <i>tereifah</i>.</i> The Orthodox Union (see <i>Mesorah</i> 10:62-68), though, follows the rulings of Rav Yisroel Belsky and Rav Moshe Heinemann that this currently is not a problem in the United States, but one may not assume that this is not a problem in other parts of the world without consulting a competent Rav.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">See Rav Michoel Zylberman’s essay in the <i>Journal of Halacha and Contemporary Society</i> (54:93-113) for a presentation of the lenient considerations and opinions regarding the concern that most dairy cows today are <i>treifot</i>. See also Rav J. David Bleich’s essay on this topic in <i>Tradition</i> (41:1:55-70).</i>
Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 1:47) writes: “Most observant Jews and also many <i>rabbanim</i> (rabbis) are lenient regarding this matter, and God forbid that one should declare that they are acting improperly.” Indeed, many of Rav Soloveitchik’s students follow their Rav’s example and adopt the lenient approach to this issue. We should note, though, that today, a much greater percentage of the observant community adopts the strict approach than when Rav Moshe wrote his <i>teshuvah</i> in 1954.
The lenient position appears to be especially cogent given that there is no concern for violating a Torah prohibition. The Shach (Y.D. 118:8) points out that we are not concerned that the non-Jew added a large amount of non-kosher milk, because then the adulteration would be obvious, since, as the Gemara (Avodah Zarah 35b) notes, non-kosher milk looks different from kosher milk. Since the concern is only that a small amount of non-kosher milk was added, there always is a significant majority of kosher milk, and <i>min b’mino</i> (two like items, in this case the kosher and non-kosher milk)<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The Pitchei Teshuvah (Y.D. 118:1) questions the reasoning of this Shach based on the difference in taste between the two types of milk, which should therefore be considered <i>min b’she’eino mino</i> (two different items). In such a case, the kosher item must be more than sixty times as plentiful as the non-kosher item in order to nullify it even on a biblical level. Nonetheless, the <i>Chazon Ish</i> (Y.D. 41:1) maintains that the prohibition against unsupervised milk cannot be on a biblical level in any case, since the chance of a mixture of non-kosher milk is so small.</i> is nullified on a biblical level as long as there is a majority of the kosher product (Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 98:2). Thus, there is no biblical-level concern, and it certainly seems reasonable to rely on the well-founded lenient opinions regarding such a rabbinic prohibition.
<b>The Policy of the Israeli Chief Rabbinate</b>
Rav Eliyahu Bakshi-Doron writes (<i>Techumin</i> 23:463) that the policy of the Israeli Chief Rabbinate is not to rely on Rav Moshe Feinstein’s leniency even for its regular (non-<i>mehadrin</i>) kashrut standards. He explains that even Rav Moshe permitted government-supervised milk only in a case of need, but that in Israel there is no need, as Israel is a land flowing with <i>chalav yisrael</i> milk. It has no pressing need to import government-supervised milk or dairy products produced by non-Jews.
Of course, the Chief Rabbinate’s policy supports the dairy industry of <i>Medinat Yisrael</i>, thereby facilitating the fulfillment of <i>yishuv Eretz Yisrael</i>. The availability of more jobs in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> allows more Jews to live there and brings more tax revenue to the Israeli government, which it can then use to fulfill its role of facilitating Jews living securely in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Those who advocate the purchase of <i>chalav yisrael</i> milk in the United States argue, similarly, that this practice economically supports our fellow Jews. The Torah indeed advocates purchasing a product from Jews if possible.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">See Rashi to Vayikra 25:14 s.v. <i>V’chi</i>, <i>Teshuvot Rama</i> (10), <i>Ahavat Chessed </i>(1:5:6-7). See Rav Doniel Neustadt’s <i>The Monthly Halachah Discussion</i> (pp. 50-51) for a summary of the parameters of this <i>mitzvah</i>.</i>
Rav Bakshi-Doron writes that a major motivation behind the stringent ruling of the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> was distancing us socially from <i>nochrim</i>. He argues that <i>Chazal</i> enacted the prohibition of <i>chalav akum</i> not only because of kashrut concerns, but also to protect us from assimilation. He cites the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i>’s comment (Y.D. 115:6) that <i>Chazal</i> have covert as well as overt reasons for their enactments. The covert reason for <i>chalav yisrael</i>, Rav Bakshi-Doron argues, is to prevent assimilation. Indeed, observing the strict position requires one to live in an area blessed with a heavy concentration of observant Jews where there is sufficient consumer demand for <i>chalav yisrael</i> products.
Another reason that Rav Bakshi-Doron advances to adopt the strict approach today is the extreme complexity of contemporary food production. He is concerned that the milk producers might add non-kosher ingredients that government supervisors do not find objectionable. According to this reasoning, unsupervised milk would be forbidden today even according to the most lenient approach of the <i>Pri Chadash</i>! However, American rabbinical <i>kashrut </i>professionals do not share this concern.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Avrohom Gordimer outlined the policy of the Orthodox Union in this regard:<br>Fresh milk (unflavored, normal refrigerated milk – as opposed to long shelf-life boxed milk) in the United States has no <i>kashrus </i>concerns. The only concern is the use of non-kosher vitamins, which are <i>batel</i> (nullified) anyway, as well as the slight possibility of pasteurization on non-kosher equipment. Although the vitamins are always <i>batel</i>, the Orthodox Union will not certify milk without supervising that kosher vitamins are used. Additionally, the use of shared equipment for milk and non-kosher beverages (e.g. non-kosher grape juice blends) is not common and equipment sanitization between products on an “<i>ikkar hadin”</i> (essential Halacha) level will prevent such equipment from imparting <i>ta’am issur</i> (non-kosher taste particles) to fresh milk. All OU certified milk is made on <i>mashgiach</i>-verified kosher-dedicated equipment. The OU does not certify milk (or any products) based on these types of rationales (such as nullification and equipment sanitization), even though they may be relied upon.</i>
<b>Traveling Between Countries</b>
Halachah (see Pesachim 50a and Shulchan Aruch O.C. 468:4) requires a traveler to follow the stringencies of both the place he left and his destination. Accordingly, it would seem that visitors from Israel to the United States should maintain the stringent standard regarding <i>chalav yisrael</i>, since their practice at home is to be strict about this matter. For some reason, this does not seem to be the common practice among many Israeli travelers to America.
Rav Yaakov Borow told me, however, that the regular kosher (non-<i>mehadrin</i>) dairy products made in Israel often include dried <i>chalav akum</i> additives.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Borow informs me that there are general kashrut concerns with dried milk. For example, animal fats are sometimes dried on equipment that is also used for drying milk.</i> As such, the practice in Israel is not to be strict as far as dried milk is concerned. In addition, Rav Moshe Bleich suggests that Rav Moshe Feinstein’s lenient ruling is intended for situations in which <i>chalav yisrael</i> is not readily available. Since Israel is flowing with <i>chalav yisrael</i> milk, there is no pressing need to rely on Rav Moshe’s leniency.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Borow informs me that much of the Israeli milk industry uses foreign workers to do the actual milking, and there also are some farms owned by non-Jews. Thus, the Israeli Chief Rabbinate does at times have to take measures (i.e. require supervision) to ensure that the Israeli milk supply is <i>chalav yisrael</i>. </i> Thus, it is possible that even Israelis who drink only <i>chalav yisrael</i> milk may drink non-<i>chalav yisrael</i> milk when traveling in America if <i>chalav yisrael</i> is not readily available. However, since this question remains unresolved, a resident of <i>Eretz Yisrael </i>should consult his Rav regarding whether he may drink non-<i>chalav yisrael</i> fresh milk (or products made from liquid milk) when he travels outside of Israel. A resident of <i>Eretz Yisrael </i>also should ask his Rav if he is permitted to eat products imported from outside of Israel that are made from liquid milk even if the item is ceritified kosher by a reliable North American or European <i>kashrut</i> agency.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Conversely, Rav Hershel Schachter told me it is forbidden for residents of the United States to consume products that contain gelatin made from non-kosher sources even if they bear a reliable Israeli rabbinical certification, since the practice of Jews in the United States is to follow the ruling of Rav Moshe Feinstein (Teshuvot Igrot Moshe Y.D. 1:37 and 2:27) and Rav Aharon Kotler (<i>Mishnat Rabi Aharon</i> 1:16-17) forbidding such gelatin. </i>
<i><b>Chem’at Akum</b></i>
There is much controversy about the applicability of the prohibition of <i>chalav akum</i> to processed dairy products, centering on the question of <i>chem’at akum</i> (unsupervised butter produced by non-Jews). Although the Gemara does not discuss this issue, the Rambam (<i>Hilchot Ma’achalot Asurot</i> 3:15) cites unresolved arguments among the <i>Geonim</i> about it.
The Rambam explains the reasoning behind these two opinions. The lenient view argues that <i>Chazal</i> never banned consuming the butter of non-Jews, as we can be certain that the butter comes from a kosher animal because the milk of a non-kosher animal cannot be made into butter. The stringent view is concerned that there may remain leftover pockets of non-kosher milk that were in the product from which the butter was made.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For an enlightening analysis of the two views, see the Bei’ur Hagra (Y.D. 115:17).</i>
This issue remains unresolved, as the Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 115:3) notes that different communities maintain different practices. Rav Zev Weitman records (<i>Bin’tiv Hechalav</i> p. 41) that the policy of the Israeli Chief Rabbinate is to be lenient regarding <i>chem’at akum</i> for standard kashrut certifications and to be strict for <i>mehadrin</i> certifications.
We should clarify that the lenient approach does not imply that one may eat butter that is not certified as kosher by a reliable kashrut agency. Rather, it implies that butter does not require the type of constant supervision that the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> demands for milk. The kashrut agency still must verify and monitor the product to ensure that all of its ingredients are kosher. What is not required, according to the lenient view, is supervision of the entire butter production process - occasional inspections suffice. The <i>Chochmat Adam</i> (67:9) notes that common practice is to be lenient about this issue, and Rav Mordechai Willig (1981 SOY Guide to Kashrut p. 75) similarly writes that “The custom today is to be lenient and to permit butter produced by a Gentile.” This is why butter produced by large non-Jewish companies commonly receives kashrut certification, unlike comparably produced wine and cheese.
<b>Powdered Milk – Rav Tzvi Pesach Frank vs. the <i>Chazon Ish</i></b>
As we mentioned previously, Rav Tzvi Pesach Frank rules that it is permissible to drink powdered milk that is imported to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> from the United States. He compares powdered milk to <i>chem’at akum</i>, applying the same logic that would permit butter to permit powdered milk. The lenient opinion regarding butter, he contends, believes that <i>Chazal</i> imposed their decree only on plain milk, not on all milk products. Once the form of the milk has changed, the decree no longer applies.
The <i>Chazon Ish</i> (Y.D. 41:4) vehemently disagrees with Rav Frank. He completely rejects the comparison of powdered milk to butter, since the basis for leniency regarding butter is the fact that butter cannot be produced from the milk of a non-kosher animal. Powdered milk, on the other hand, can be produced from non-kosher milk as well. Hence, the <i>Chazon Ish</i> concludes that there is no distinction between fresh milk and powdered milk regarding <i>chalav yisrael</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Zvi Sobolofsky observed in a <i>shiur</i> that this dispute is characteristic of many halachic disputes that have emerged in the modern age concerning the status of items that have been dried to a powder. Examples include the dispute about the use of reconstituted grape juice for <i>kiddush</i> (see <i>Teshuvot Minchat Shlomo</i> 1:4 and the essay about this topic available at www.koltorah.org) and the <i>berachah</i> on Pringles, which are made from dried potatoes. Part of the question is whether we view the dried food that was reconstituted as a new entity or as the same entity that it was previously. The <i>Chazon Ish</i> seems to assume that drying an item to a powder does not create a new entity.</i>
This dispute has never been resolved. Rav Bakshi-Doron (<i>Techumin</i> 23:464-465) records that the Israeli Chief Rabbinate historically relied upon Rav Frank’s leniency because of the shortage of <i>chalav yisrael</i> powdered milk in Israel until recent years. Currently, the Chief Rabbinate relies on Rav Frank’s leniency for its standard kashrut certifications but not for its <i>mehadrin</i> certifications. Its policy is to require any product that relies on Rav Frank’s leniency to include a disclaimer on the package stating that it contains powdered milk from <i>chalav akum</i>. Rav Bakshi-Doron explains that the Chief Rabbinate seeks thereby to discourage reliance on Rav Frank’s leniency and eventually to discontinue relying on it entirely, since powdered milk from <i>chalav yisrael</i> sources is readily available in Israel today.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Borow informs me that some dairy ingredients, such as certain types of milk protein, are not made in Israel, and many companies continue to use these <i>chalav akum</i> ingredients in powdered products that receive standard kosher (non-<i>mehadrin</i>) certifications.</i>
Rav Bakshi-Doron also notes that the kashrut status of <i>chalav akum</i> milk proteins that are transformed into powder, such as casein and whey, hinges on this dispute between the <i>Chazon Ish</i> and Rav Frank. Hence, for <i>mehadrin</i> certifications, the Chief Rabbinate insists that milk proteins be derived only from <i>chalav yisrael</i> milk.
<b>Compromise Opinions</b>
I have heard that some people in America adopt a compromise position regarding <i>chalav yisrael</i>. They insist on <i>chalav yisrael</i> for actual milk, but they rely on Rav Frank’s leniency regarding powdered milk. The appeal of this compromise is that business people can take non-<i>chalav yisrael</i> powdered milk with them on their travels to places where <i>chalav yisrael</i> is not available. In addition, milk chocolates and ice creams made from powdered milk still can be consumed.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Hershel Schachter told me that as a youngster, when he would study at the Mirrer Yeshiva in Brooklyn, New York during summer vacations, the Yeshiva served regular powdered milk when <i>chalav yisrael</i> milk was unavailable.</i>
Another compromise that some American kashrut organizations once embraced was to permit the use of <i>chalav akum</i> in the production of cheese, even though they would not certify a product containing non-<i>chalav yisrael</i> milk. Their position was based on the Rama’s ruling (Y.D. 115:2) that cheese that was made with <i>chalav akum</i> is acceptable <i>b’dieved </i>(post facto), since cheese cannot be produced from milk from a non-kosher animal. These kashrut agencies reasoned that since at worst a minimally acceptable product would be produced, they could rely on Rav Moshe’s leniency <i>l’chatchilah</i> (ideally), even though they normally would not rely on it, to permit the production of cheese from <i>chalav akum</i>. Indeed, Rav Moshe (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 3:16) rules that even <i>ba’alei nefesh</i> (those who are exceptionally scrupulous in their observance of Halachah) need not be strict about cheese made from milk that did not have supervision.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Rav David Tzvi Hoffman’s lenient approach (outlined in <i>Teshuvot Melamed L’ho’il</i> 2:36) also serves as a basis for this compromise.</i> Rav Borow told me, though, that changes in the North American cheese industry have made it more difficult to rely on giving a <i>chalav yisrael</i> certification to a product that started with non-<i>chalav yisrael</i> milk.
<b>Conclusion</b>
We have outlined the variety of stances that <i>poskim</i> adopt toward <i>chalav yisrael</i> in the modern context. Each opinion has a strong basis both in traditional sources and among contemporary authorities. Accordingly, it is entirely inappropriate to dismiss any of these legitimate approaches – “<i>Eilu v’eilu divrei elokim chaim</i>” (these and these are the words of the Living God; Eruvin 13b).

Gevinat Yisrael

Unlike the prohibition of <i>chalav akum</i> (milk produced by a non-Jew), the prohibition of <i>gevinat akum</i> (cheese produced by a non-Jew) is upheld by all observant Jews in essentially the same manner. In this chapter, we shall outline the development and the parameters of this prohibition as well as some of the issues that are debated by twentieth-century <i>poskim</i>. We will base our discussion on three essays on this topic that have been written by three Kashrut professionals: Rav Yaakov Borow (Tnuva’s <i>Bin’tiv Hechalav</i> pp. 43-47), Rav Zushe Blech (the Orthodox Union’s <i>Daf Kashrut</i>, Adar I 5757), and Rav Avraham Juravel (Mehadrin kashrut journal, Adar II 5755).
<b>Talmudic Background</b>
The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Ma’achalot Asurot</i> 3:12-13) notes that there should be more reason to allow cheese produced by a non-Jew than milk produced by a non-Jew, because milk from a non-kosher animal cannot be made into cheese. Nevertheless, <i>Chazal</i> prohibited consuming cheese produced by a non-Jew. Although the Mishnah (Avodah Zarah 2:5; see Avodah Zarah 35a) indicates that <i>Chazal</i> at first concealed the reason for this prohibition, the Gemara (Avodah Zarah 35a-b), searching for the correct explanation, cites a plethora of reasons. It suggests that the non-Jews curdled the milk with the stomach lining from <i>niveilot</i> (animals that were not properly slaughtered); that the non-Jews did not take adequate care to cover the milk that would be used to make cheese, leading <i>Chazal</i> to worry that snakes would release their venom into the uncovered liquids;<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This explanation assumes that the decree on cheese is basically an extension of the decree against <i>mayim megulim</i> (water that was left uncovered), which <i>Chazal</i> also forbade out of concern for snake venom.</i> that the non-Jews smoothed over the cheese with pig fat; that there might be leftover drops of milk in the cheese that did not curdle, and these drops might have been from a non-kosher animal; and that the non-Jews made the cheese using non-kosher vinegar.
<i><b>Rishonim</b></i>
The <i>Rishonim</i> debate which of these reasons is accepted. The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Ma’achalot Asurot</i> 3:13) codifies the reason that they use the stomach lining of <i>niveilot</i> to curdle the cheese. Rabbeinu Tam (cited in <i>Tosafot Avodah Zarah</i> 35a s.v. <i>Chada</i>), on the other hand, believes that the primary concern is that the milk was exposed to snake venom. The Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 115:2) endorses the approach of the Rambam.
The <i>Rishonim</i> also debate whether the enactment forbidding <i>gevinat akum</i> applies even when the concerns for the enactment are not relevant. Rabbeinu Tam (<i>ibid</i>.) asserts that the concerns are not relevant today, since snakes are not prevalent in our environs. He argues that <i>Chazal</i> did not issue this enactment in a situation where concern for snake venom is not relevant. Furthermore, he states:
In many places, Jews eat cheese produced by non-Jews, since the non-Jews use flowers to curdle the milk,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Interestingly, Rav Blech writes that there is a type of cheese made in Portugal today that uses an enzyme derived from the thistle flower to curdle the milk. In addition, Rav Juravel writes that during World War I, when there was a severe shortage of animal rennet, people in many countries used date tree sap to make cheese. This sap contains an enzyme known as ficin that serves as a curdling agent.</i> and the great rabbis of Narbonne (Southern France) permitted this practice. However, in our locales (Northern France and Germany) there is reason to be strict, since they use stomach linings to curdle milk.
The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Ma’achalot Asurot</i> 3:14), however, quotes “some <i>Geonim</i>” as ruling that the prohibition of <i>gevinat akum</i> applies even when the reason for the enactment does not apply. He writes:
[Regarding] cheese that non-Jews curdle with grass or with fruit juice, such as date tree sap, when it is evident in the cheese [that an animal product was not used to produce it]: some <i>Geonim</i> ruled that it is nevertheless forbidden, because the enactment applies to all cheese produced by non-Jews whether a kosher or non-kosher curdling agent was used.
The Rambam does not cite any authority who disputes the ruling of these <i>Geonim</i>, nor does he criticize this ruling. Rav Yosef Karo (in both the <i>Kesef Mishneh ibid</i>. and the <i>Beit Yosef </i>Y.D. 115 s.v. <i>gevinot</i>) assumes, therefore, that the Rambam concurs with the ruling of these <i>Geonim</i>. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> (<i>ibid</i>.) explains that the reason for this ruling is that <i>gevinat akum</i> is a <i>davar sheb’minyan</i> – a full-fledged decree of <i>Chazal</i> of the sort that remains in effect even when the reason for the prohibition is no longer relevant (see Beitzah 5a).
<i><b>Shulchan Aruch</b></i><b> and Codes<i></i></b>
The Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 115:2) rules unequivocally in accordance with the Rambam. The Rama adds that this is the accepted custom and warns against being “<i>poreitz geder</i>” (breaking the “fence” of common practice enacted by <i>Am Yisrael</i>). He adds, though, that the stringency does not apply where the Jewish community has a tradition to follow the lenient ruling of the great rabbis of Narbonne. The <i>Beit Yosef </i>(ad. loc.), on the other hand, is far less tolerant of those places that maintain their tradition to follow the lenient approach. He strongly urges those few communities who follow this view to adopt the stringent practice of the overwhelming majority of Jewish communities throughout the world.
The <i>Chochmat Adam</i> (53:38 and 67:7) and the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 115:16-17) rule in complete accordance with the strict view, giving harsh rebuke to those who follow the lenient opinion. These authorities, writing in the nineteenth century, make no mention of entire communities that are lenient, apparently an indication that by their time, there were no longer any prominent communities that followed the lenient tradition.
This is an especially relevant issue today, as cheese is generally made using either non-animals sources, such as microbial rennet, or animal sources that have been reduced to a powder, which seems to reduce the stomach lining to “mere wood” (Rama Y.D. 87:10) and remove its prohibited status. Accordingly, the reason for this enactment is virtually never relevant today – yet the prohibition still applies, and all observant Jews strictly adhere to it.
<b>Stomach Lining of a Kosher Animal</b>
The <i>Rishonim</i> question why the stomach lining of a <i>niveilah</i> renders cheese non-kosher. Indeed, only a small amount of the lining is used, certainly not amounting to one sixtieth of the amount of milk used, which would seem to make the stomach lining <i>bateil </i>(nullified) in the milk. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> (to <i>Hilchot Maachalot Asurot</i> 3:13), citing the Ramban and the Rashba, answers that the fact that the taste of the stomach lining is nullified is irrelevant, because <i>Chazal</i> enacted the prohibition of <i>gevinat akum</i> primarily to create a social barrier between us and <i>nochrim</i> – not because of a kashrut problem. Accordingly, they did not apply the standard rules of kashrut, and they prohibited the cheese even though under normal circumstances such a small quantity of <i>niveilah</i> is <i>bateil</i>. This approach is quite cogent in light of our practice to prohibit <i>gevinat akum</i> even though the stated reason for its enactment does not apply.
The Rambam (ad. loc.), on the other hand, answers that the stomach is a <i>davar hama’amid</i> (something that establishes the form of the item) – it contains the catalyst that turns the milk into cheese – and therefore is not <i>bateil</i> even if it is outweighed sixty to one. This approach, too, is eminently logical. Normally, placement in a mixture sixty times an item’s volume makes the item <i>bateil</i> because it has no significance in such a large quantity. The stomach lining, though, cannot be described as insignificant, since it is indispensable in creating the cheese.
The Rambam (<i>ibid</i>. 9:16) then observes that if the stomach lining is never <i>bateil</i> because it is a <i>davar hama’amid</i>, cheese made from the stomach lining of an animal that was slaughtered properly should also be forbidden as a mixture between milk and meat. Why, then, does the Gemara mention concern only for the stomach of a <i>niveilah</i>? He answers, following the reasoning of his father’s Rebbe (the Ri Migash), that the rule that a <i>davar hama’amid</i> is never <i>bateil</i> applies only if the <i>davar hama’amid</i> is prohibited already (such as the stomach lining of a non-kosher animal). An item that is inherently kosher, however, such as the stomach lining from a properly slaughtered animal, cannot create a prohibited mixture of milk and meat just because it is a <i>davar hama’amid</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tosafot </i>(Avodah Zarah 35a s.v. <i>Mipnei</i>) for a different resolution of this problem.</i>
One also might ask how it is permissible to make kosher cheese if we are forbidden to intentionally nullify prohibited items (<i>ein mivatlin issur l’chatchilah</i>; see Shach Y.D. 87:33). For example, we are not permitted to intentionally place a bit of meat into a glass of milk if we wish to drink the milk, even if there is sixty times more milk than meat. How, then, are we permitted to add a bit of stomach lining to milk in order to make cheese? Rav Akiva Eiger (<i>Teshuvot Rav Akiva Eiger</i> 207, cited in the Pitchei Teshuvah 87:19) explains that the prohibition of <i>ein mivatlin issur l’chatchilah</i> does not apply if two lenient factors are in effect. In the case of cheese, there is, in addition to the factor of the lining’s nullification in more than sixty times its volume of milk, the common practice from the nineteenth century to produce cheese with completely desiccated stomach linings mixed together with other curdling items (see <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> Y.D. 87:43). Since the curdling process is effected by other items besides the stomach linings, this becomes a situation of <i>zeh v’zeh goreim</i> (an item that was created by two factors, one permissible and one forbidden). In such a situation, we may disregard the prohibited item if it could not have accomplished the task without the aid of the permitted item (see Rama Y.D. 87:11 and Shach Y.D. 87:35).<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For further discussion of this issue, see Pitchei Teshuvah (Y.D. 87:19) and <i>Darchei Teshuvah</i> (87:138).</i> This leniency, coupled with the <i>bittul</i>, allows us to produce cheese. <sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yaakov Borow notes that to his knowledge, no kosher cheese today is made with stomach linings, even ones that have been desiccated.</i>
<b>Jewish Supervision or Participation – Rama vs. Shach</b>
There are two unresolved debates concerning the production of kosher cheese. The Rama (Y.D. 115:2) rules (and notes that this is the common custom) that it is sufficient for a Jew to monitor the cheese-making process to render the cheese kosher. According to the Rama, the prohibition of <i>gevinat akum</i> parallels the prohibition of <i>chalav akum</i>, for which supervision likewise suffices to permit the product. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 115:19) cites the Rambam in his commentary to the Mishnah (Avodah Zarah 2:5 s.v. <i>Amar Lo</i>), who writes explicitly in accordance with the view of the Rama. The Shach (Y.D. 115:20), on the other hand, requires either Jewish ownership of the cheese or active participation of a Jew in the cheese-making. According to the Shach, then, the rules of <i>gevinat akum</i> parallel those of <i>pat akum</i> (bread baked by a non-Jew) in that Jewish participation is required to render the product permissible.
The Shach offers a textual proof to his ruling from the language of the Mishnayot that present the prohibitions of <i>chalav akum</i> and <i>gevinat akum</i>. The Mishnah (Avodah Zarah 2:6) that presents the former prohibition states explicitly that the milk is prohibited only if a Jew does not watch the milking, whereas the Mishnah (Avodah Zarah 2:5) that presents the prohibition of <i>gevinat akum</i> states simply that the cheese is prohibited, making no distinction as to whether a Jew must watch the cheese-making process or not. The Shach, accordingly, concludes that Jewish ownership or active participation is indeed required to permit us to eat the cheese.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See, however, the comments of Rav Yonatan Eybeshitz (<i>Mateh Yonatan</i> Y.D. 115:2), who seeks to refute this proof. </i>
This dispute has never been fully resolved. Among eighteenth-century authorities, the <i>Noda Biy’huda</i> (2 O.C. 37) rules in accordance with the Rama and notes that this is the accepted practice, whereas the Vilna Gaon (Bei’ur Hagra Y.D. 115:15) rules in accordance with the Shach. Among the nineteenth-century authorities, the <i>Chochmat Adam</i> (67:7) embraces the Shach’s opinion, but the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 115:19) essentially follows the Rama, though he writes that it is proper to accommodate the Shach’s strict ruling. Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 3:16) adopts the same approach as the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i>. Rav Zushe Blech notes that it seems that the generally accepted practice is to follow the Shach.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Avraham Halbfinger told me that Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik instructed the Rabbinical Council of New England to follow the Shach. </i>
This dispute has a major impact on the level of supervision required for the cheese-making process. According to the Rama, occasional inspections suffice, because the Gemara (Chullin 4a) states that <i>yotzei v’nichnas k’omeid al gabav dami</i> (spot checks are equivalent to constant supervision). According to the Shach, however, a <i>mashgiach</i> (supervisor) must constantly be available on location to participate in the cheese-making process. This explains why it is impractical for a large company to have its cheeses certified kosher and why kosher cheeses are generally made by companies that produce cheese specifically for the observant Jewish community.
<b>Soft Cheeses</b>
Another major debate rages about whether soft cheeses, such as cottage cheese and cream cheese, are included in the prohibition of <i>gevinat akum</i>. Logically, it would seem that since the concern according to the Rambam and the Shulchan Aruch is that the non-Jews used the stomach lining of a <i>niveilah</i> to curdle the cheese, the prohibition should apply only to cheeses that are produced using the enzyme from the stomach lining. Soft cheeses, on the other hand, were not originally made using enzymes. Before the modern age, cottage cheese was made by passively allowing the milk to ferment and separate into curds and whey. Since soft cheese does not need rennet to curdle it, it would seem that it is totally removed from the enactment against <i>gevinat akum</i>, since the premise of the enactment is that non-kosher stomach linings might be used in the cheese production.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Even though, as mentioned above, the prohibition of <i>gevinat akum</i> applies even where the reason is not relevant, perhaps soft cheeses, an entire category to which the reason never applied, were not included in the original prohibition.</i>
In the modern age, when a small amount of rennet is added to hasten the process of making soft cheeses, it can be argued that since the rennet is unnecessary for the actual cheese-making, it does not qualify as a <i>davar hama’amid</i>. Even if the rennet is not kosher, it would seem reasonable to overlook this ingredient under the principle of <i>zeh v’zeh goreim</i>. This rule, as we explained above, dictates that if both a forbidden and a permitted substance contribute together to the creation of a food product, we may ignore the contribution of the non-kosher item if the kosher item could have accomplished the task even without its aid.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Blech notes that soft cheese uses no more than 2 milliliters of rennet per 1000 pounds of milk, whereas hard cheese generally requires between fifty and ninety milliliters of rennet.</i> Since the cheese could have formed even without the rennet, the rennet, even if it is not kosher, does not render the cheese forbidden.
Accordingly, Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 2:48) is inclined to rule that the prohibition of <i>gevinat akum</i> does not apply to soft cheeses. He comments that the principle of <i>zeh v’zeh goreim</i> appears to apply to the production of cottage cheese. In fact, Rav Blech cites Rav Tuvia Goldstein<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe writes that some great rabbis permit soft cheeses <i>l’chatchilah</i>. Rav Yisroel Belsky reports that this refers to Rav Yosef Eliyahu Henkin. </i> as ruling that one may even rely on this approach <i>l’chatchilah</i> (ideally), since soft cheeses are equivalent to butter, regarding which most observant Jews follow the lenient view among the <i>poskim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">We discuss this issue in our earlier chapter about <i>chalav yisrael</i>.</i>
Rav Juravel suggests a fascinating proof to Rav Moshe’s approach. As noted earlier, the Rambam rules that cheese that was curdled using date sap is prohibited (even though no rennet was used). Rav Juravel notes that the enzyme from date tree sap creates hard cheese. He proposes that the reason why the Rambam presents the example of date tree sap in his description of the <i>gevinat akum</i> prohibition is specifically to illustrate that only hard cheeses are included in the ban.
Nonetheless, both the <i>Chochmat Adam</i> (53:38) and the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 115:16) rule that the prohibition applies even to cheeses whose production involves no rennet. This approach fits well with the ruling of the Rambam and the Shulchan Aruch that the prohibition applies even when its reason does not. Indeed, even Rav Moshe does not rule unequivocally that soft cheeses are not included in the prohibition of <i>gevinat akum</i>. He even writes that it is inappropriate for a Rav to publicize his lenient considerations.
Rav Blech and Rav Borow demonstrate that this dispute has been debated by authorities of previous generations, as well: the Radbaz (<i>Teshuvot Radbaz</i> 6:2291) rules that yogurt (which is made without rennet) is included in the <i>gevinat akum</i> prohibition, but the <i>Pri Chadash</i> (Y.D. 115:21) rules leniently.
Rav Blech questions Rav Moshe’s particular approach based on the assertion in Frank V. Kosikowski’s <i>Cheese and Fermented Milk Foods</i> (p. 111) that the rennet enzymes added in the production of soft cheeses provide for a sweeter cheese. Since this taste could not be attained without the rennet, one cannot use <i>zeh v’zeh goreim</i> as a lenient consideration. Rav Borow, on the other hand, states that the food technologists at Tnuva with whom he consulted stated that the rennet added to soft cheeses does not play a primary role in creating the cheese. Rather, it allows industrial-scale production of the cheeses to proceed more efficiently.
This dispute has never been fully resolved. Rav Avrohom Gordimer reports that the Orthodox Union’s policy is to rely on the rationale presented by Rav Moshe, which, although not endorsed <i>l’chatchilah</i> by Rav Moshe, was accepted <i>l’chatchilah</i> by Rav Yosef Eliyahu Henkin. This position explains the certification by reliable kashrut agencies of cottage cheese and cream cheese from companies that do not produce specifically for the observant Jewish community. Other kashrut agencies, though, are stricter and require the active participation of a <i>mashgiach</i> even for the production of soft cheeses. Of course, as Rav Borow notes, all agree that even soft cheese requires a reliable kashrut certification to ensure that all ingredients are kosher.
<b>Whey</b>
In the cheese-making process, the milk separates into curds and whey. It is questionable whether the whey (which is essentially a byproduct of the process but is used as an important ingredient in many industries) is included in the prohibition of <i>gevinat akum</i>. While Rav Eliyahu Bakshi-Doron (<i>Techumin</i> 23:466) believes that it should be included, Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 3:17) rules that it is not. Rav Moshe argues that the whey should be viewed as distinct from cheese, just as butter is treated as distinct from milk according to the lenient opinions regarding <i>chem’at akum</i>. Rav Shmuel Wosner (Teshuvot <i>Sheivet Halevi</i> 4:87) adopts a middle approach, applying the prohibition to whey only when the whey is heated together with the curd at a temperature higher than <i>yad soledet bo</i> (hot to the touch), which is the halachic definition of heat in the context of kashrut and Shabbat.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Poskim</i> have varying opinions regarding the parallel in degrees Fahrenheit, ranging from 110-120 degrees (see <i>Teshuvot Minchat Shlomo</i> 1:91:8, Rav Shimon Eider’s <i>Halachos of Shabbos</i> p. 243, and Rav Mordechai Willig’s <i>Am Mordechai</i>, Shabbat pp. 62-64).</i> Since <i>bli’ah</i> (absorption from food) occurs at <i>yad soledet bo</i>, Rav Wosner reasons that the whey absorbs flavor from the curd and is rendered forbidden as <i>gevinat akum</i>.
Rav Avrohom Gordimer reports that the policy of the Orthodox Union is to follow the approach of Rav Wosner, using Rav Aharon Kotler’s standard (as reported by Rav Shimon Eider and Rav Moshe Heinemann, cited by Rav Blech) of 120 degrees Fahrenheit for <i>yad soledet bo</i>. Although many ordinarily would not rely on Rav Kotler’s ruling in a context where it serves as a leniency, in this context it is justified because the custom in the United States (as mentioned in Rav Moshe’s <i>teshuvah</i>) and Israel (see <i>Bin’tiv Hechalav</i> p. 42) has been to follow Rav Moshe’s entirely lenient ruling. Accordingly, the OU’s policy actually represents an increase in stringency from the previously accepted practice.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The OU also requires that the ingredients used to make the cheese be kosher, including the enzymes.</i>
<b>Conclusion</b>
Unlike the area of <i>chalav yisrael</i>, there is near uniformity regarding the prohibition of <i>gevinat akum</i>. However, a few pockets of debate still persist, and about these we say “<i>Eilu v’eilu divrei elokim chaim</i>” (these and these are the words of the Living God; Eruvin 13b).
Although we can expect further areas of disagreement as modern food technology changes at breakneck speed, the <i>poskim</i> surely will continue to successfully apply our ancient and venerated Halacha to new, contemporary challenges.

Israel

Halachic Perspectives on Civilian Casualties

A critical halachic/ethical issue facing the Jewish State and, indeed, the entire civilized world is the question of avoiding civilian casualties when battling terrorists. Terrorist groups such as Al Qaeda and Hezbollah take advantage of Western sensibilities by deliberately embedding themselves within civilian populations, cynically using them as human shields. The civilized world struggles to strike a balance between combating such evil groups on one hand and trying to limit civilian casualties on the other.
Israel in particular must confront this terrible challenge. In recent years, Israel has risked and lost many of its precious soldiers in order to reduce Arab civilian casualties. For example, after a series of horrific terrorist attacks in the first half of 2002, which included the bombing of the Park Hotel in Netanya where 29 people participating in a <i>Pesach Seder</i> were murdered by a homicide bomber, the Israel Defense Forces (IDF) launched Operation Defensive Shield to cripple terrorist groups. A hotbed of terrorists was the Jenin refugee camp in the Northern Shomron. The IDF could have bombed this refugee camp from the air but instead chose to send foot soldiers house to house to locate and eliminate the terrorists located in the camp, hoping thereby to reduce civilian losses. Civilian casualties were certainly kept to a minimum in this effort. However, thirteen Israeli soldiers were killed in the Jenin operation, men who likely would have been spared had Israel attacked only from the air.
Similarly, in the summer of 2006, Hezbollah mercilessly pounded Northern Israel with hundreds of rockets. Israel could have responded by “carpet bombing” Southern Lebanon but instead chose to attack with a combination of aerial attacks and ground forces, in part as an attempt to reduce civilian deaths. While Israel undoubtedly reduced non-combatant losses, more than one hundred Israeli soldiers were killed, and the stated goal of eliminating Hezbollah’s presence in Southern Lebanon was not achieved.
The question we must ask is whether the Israeli government made an appropriate moral decision regarding both Jenin and Lebanon. In other words, does Halachah require sacrificing our own soldiers in order to reduce enemy civilian losses?
Rav Yuval Sherlow visited the Torah Academy of Bergen County in March 2007 and told the students of a similar issue that he was asked to resolve. The Israeli Air Force had located a very dangerous terrorist leader and had the opportunity to eliminate him. The terrorist noticed the plane and slipped into a taxi cab that had a civilian passenger in it. The question was whether to bomb the cab despite the presence of non-combatants in the car.
In this chapter, we shall outline the halachic and <i>hashkafic</i> issues involved in resolving such critical issues. We will not address the political and military questions involved in making these decisions, leaving those considerations for experts in these areas. There has been extensive halachic discussion of this issue, including a <i>teshuvah</i> written by Rav Shaul Yisraeli (<i>Teshuvot Ammud Hayemini</i> 16 and <i>B’tzomet Hatorah V’hamedinah</i> 3:253-289) and a lengthy essay written by Rav Dr. Neriah Gutel that appears in <i>Techumin</i> (23:18-42). We will seek to discover a consensus approach that has emerged from the prominent <i>poskim</i>, including Rav Yisraeli, Rav Yaakov Ariel, Rav J. David Bleich, Rav Aharon Lichtenstein, Rav Hershel Schachter, and Rav Asher Weiss, all of whom have addressed this issue.
<b>Shimon and Levi at Shechem</b>
The point of departure for the discussion of this issue is Shimon and Levi’s action at Shechem, recorded in the thirty fourth chapter of <i>Sefer Bereishit</i>. Subsequent to the kidnap and rape of Dinah, Shimon and Levi attacked Shechem and killed not only the rapist (Shechem) and the town leader (Chamor), but also all of the males of Shechem who had a <i>brit milah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For a discussion of whether they actually killed all the males, as well as a full analysis of this event, see Binyamin Malleck’s essay that appears in <i>Megadim</i> (23:9-30).</i> We shall survey the three primary views: the respective approaches of the Rambam, the Ramban and the Maharal.
The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 9:14) believes that Shimon and Levi’s action at Shechem was appropriate. He notes that one of the seven Noahide laws that Halachah demands all of humanity abide by is “<i>dinim</i>” – to eliminate evil from its midst. The failure of the males in Shechem to protest and/or prevent the rape and continued abduction of Dinah constituted a violation of the obligation of <i>dinim</i>, a crime punishable by death (see Sanhedrin 57a).
The Ramban (commentary to Bereishit 34:13 and 49:5-6) strongly disagrees with the Rambam’s opinion. While he believes that Shimon and Levi were justified in killing Shechem and Chamor, he argues that the killing of the other males of Shechem was entirely unjustified. The Ramban presents two basic arguments for his position. Firstly, the residents of Shechem did nothing wrong to Yaakov’s family. The Ramban asserts that the residents of an area do not deserve death for failure to control the evil actions of their leader. He adds that even if the people did in fact deserve to die due to other violations of the Noahide laws, Shimon and Levi were not authorized to execute such punishment.
<b>Proofs to the Rambam and Ramban</b>
The Ramban derives support for his opinion from the fact that Yaakov strongly criticized Shimon and Levi’s actions (Bereishit 34:30). The Rambam could counter that the Torah (<i>ibid.</i> 31) records the retort of Shimon and Levi to this criticism, to which Yaakov does not respond. On the other hand, the Ramban could reply that Yaakov further criticized Shimon and Levi on his deathbed (Bereishit 49:5-7). Thus, the Torah gives the last word to Yaakov.
The Rambam might respond by noting that Yaakov on his deathbed (Bereishit 49:7) criticized Shimon and Levi for their leading roles in the sale of Yosef, but not for killing the residents of Shechem.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramban would disagree since he believes that Yaakov never discovered that it was the brothers who sold Yosef; see his commentary to Bereishit 45:27.</i> Indeed, the words “<i>ish</i>” and “<i>shor</i>” used in Yaakov’s rebuke fit Yosef, as he is referred to as a <i>shor</i> in Moshe Rabbeinu’s final blessing (Devarim 34:17) and is called an <i>ish</i> no fewer than fourteen times in <i>Sefer Bereishit</i>.
<b>The Maharal and Twentieth-Century Applications</b>
The Maharal (<i>Gur Aryeh</i> to Bereishit 34:13) adopts a compromise approach between the Rambam and the Ramban. On the one hand, he agrees with the Ramban that the people of Shechem cannot be held accountable for the actions of their leader, arguing that their failure to execute <i>dinim</i> was due to coercion by their leaders.
On the other hand, the Maharal justifies the actions of Shimon and Levi, asserting that the Torah sanctions waging war when a nation attacks us. In such circumstances, we are permitted to respond to the other nation’s provocation. In responding, we attack the other nation and do not distinguish between the guilty members and the innocent members of that nation. Thus, Shimon and Levi appropriately responded to Shechem’s aggression. Once they responded, they were permitted to attack the entire nation, because this is the manner in which war is waged.
Rav Asher Weiss (<i>Minchat Asher</i>, Devarim p. 222) notes that the Maharal does not sanction frivolous attacks on civilian members of an enemy nation. Rather, he permits them only when the proper execution of battle plans necessitates killing non-combatants and there is no other way to accomplish the military goal. For example, it appears that the Maharal would approve the dropping of atomic bombs on Hiroshima and Nagasaki in 1945 despite the Japanese babies who were killed in this attack. He also would sanction the unrelenting Allied bombing against Germany toward the end of World War II despite the killing of German babies in towns such as Dresden.
I should stress that many people probably would not be alive today if not for these attacks. My father, for example, served as a combat soldier in the Pacific during World War II and might not have survived an American invasion of Japan. Many Holocaust survivors owe their survival to the Allied bombing of Germany, which brought that evil nation to its knees.
<b>Applying the Rambam, the Ramban, and the Maharal</b>
The Rambam and the Ramban argue as to whether Halachah considers an entire population responsible for the evil perpetrated by its leaders.
