As noted, it is difficult to discern whose opinion is endorsed by the <i>Tanach</i>. Indeed, Rav Shaul Yisraeli (ad. loc.) concludes his discussion of the Rambam-Ramban debate by noting that “In practice, there is insufficient basis to permit action against an entire community that has failed to execute its duty and remove murderers from its midst so long as it is reasonable to excuse them with the claim of fear, pressure, and the like.”
However, Rav Yaakov Ariel (<i>Arachim B’mivchan Hamilchamah</i> p. 83), Rav Dov Lior (<i>Techumin</i> 4:186), Rav Hershel Schachter (<i>B’ikvei Hatzon</i> p. 207), and Rav Asher Weiss (<i>Minchat Asher</i>, Devarim pp. 217-222) all rely upon the Maharal’s interpretation of the Shechem episode to allow harming anyone who belongs to an enemy nation during wartime. Rav Yitzchak Blau argues, though, that “Maharal is a decidedly minority viewpoint with regard to that story and thus is a shaky leg upon which to build a far reaching position” (<i>Tradition </i>39:4:11). Rav Neriah Gutel (<i>Techumin</i> 23:32) expresses similar reservations about applying the Maharal’s principle in practice. We will seek to demonstrate why the Maharal is a most solid source upon which to base a resolution to our question.
<b>Support for the Maharal</b>
The Maharal’s approach to the Shechem incident is endorsed by Rav Zalman Sorotzkin (<i>Oznayim Latorah</i>,<i> Bereishit</i> 34:25), and Rav Schachter (ad. loc.) argues that the Netziv advances a similar principle (<i>Meromei Sadeh</i>, Kiddushin 43a s.v. <i>Mah</i> and Eruvin 45a s.v. <i>Peirush Rashi</i>).
Even if the various commentators do not share the Maharal’s defense of Shimon and Levi, they do not necessarily imply a rejection of his principle. They could simply believe that killing Shechem and Chamor alone would have sufficed to rescue Dinah and that waging war against the entire town of Shechem was thus unjustified. In other words, the war against Shechem was uncalled for, but in a justified war one may attack without distinguishing between the innocent and guilty if it is impossible to effectively wage war in another manner.
Furthermore, Rav Asher Weiss points out that the Radak (<i>Divrei Hayamim</i> 1:22:8) also seems to subscribe to the Maharal’s principle. In his explanation of why David was disqualified from building the <i>Beit Hamikdash</i> due to the “blood that he had shed,” he writes that David had killed non-combatants in the course of battle but was not held accountable for their deaths “since his intention was to prevent evildoers from harming our nation.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">One might wonder, then, why David was disqualified from building the <i>Beit </i>Hamikdash. Rav Elchanan Samet’s <i>Iyunim B’farshiot Hashavua</i> (1:68-69) explains that even for an “<i>aveirah lishmah</i>” (a sin committed with a noble objective), there are consequences. Thus, although David acted properly, he still was disqualified from building the <i>Beit Hamikdash</i>. Rav Weiss cites this consequence as evidence that civilian casualties are tolerated only if there are no other alternatives.</i>
In addition, Rav Schachter argues that a principle presented by the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> (425:1) also accords with the Maharal’s approach. The <i>Minchat Chinuch</i> argues that the rules forbidding endangering oneself do not apply in a situation of war. If a war is mandated by the Torah, then by definition, explains the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i>, it demands that soldiers endanger their lives since, unfortunately, this is the normal course of war. Similarly, asserts Rav Schachter, the Torah expects that civilians will be killed during a war if this is necessary to achieve success. Rav Schachter notes that Rav Yitzchak Zev Soloveitchik (in his commentary to the <i>haftarah</i> of <i>parashat beshalach</i>) and the <i>Teshuvot Devar Yehoshua</i> (2:48)<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Devar Yehoshua</i> clarifies that this applies even to a discretionary war.</i> concur with the assertion of the <i>Minchat Chinuch</i>.
Rav Shaul Yisraeli (ad. loc.) notes that “We do not find the obligation in war to distinguish between blood and blood (combatants and non-combatants). In the course of war, when laying siege to a city and the like, there is no obligation to make such distinctions.” Rav J. David Bleich (<i>Contemporary Halakhic Problems III</i>:277) echoes this observation:
Not only does one search in vain for a ruling prohibiting military activity likely to result in the death of civilians, but to this writer’s knowledge, there exists no discussion in classical rabbinic sources that takes cognizance of the likelihood of causing civilian casualties in the course of hostilities legitimately undertaken as posing a halakhic or moral problem.
Indeed, the Gemara (<i>Bava Kama</i> 92a) seems to articulate this principle when it presents a basis in the <i>Tanach</i> for the folk saying, “The carob tree is struck together with its thorn.” Rashi (<i>ibid</i>. s.v. <i>b’hadei hotza</i>) explains that when one removes a thorn that grows by a carob tree, sometimes the carob tree is uprooted together with the thorn. The idea behind this folk saying, Rashi explains, is that “The neighbors of evildoers are punished along with the evildoers.”
Accordingly, we see that far from being a “decidedly minority viewpoint,” the Maharal’s principle constitutes a mainstream and normative concept that is appropriately applied by leading <i>poskim</i> such as Rav Ariel, Rav Schachter, and Rav Weiss. This is hardly surprising in light of King Shaul’s warning to the <i>Keini</i> to evacuate their homes lest they be harmed in the course of his war with <i>Amaleik</i>. We see that Shaul was prepared to endanger civilians in the course of war, and he is not censured for this willingness by either the <i>Tanach</i> or <i>Chazal</i>. Both Rav Ariel (<i>Techumin</i> 4:190) and Rav Bleich (ad. loc.) cite this as strong support for the principle articulated by the Maharal.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Weiss (ad. loc. p. 219) defends the use of the <i>Tanach</i> and its commentaries to decide this issue:<br>Since the time of the destruction of the <i>Beit Hamikdash </i>and the loss of Jewish sovereignty, these types of questions were not relevant in practice and little attention was devoted [in the Talmud and its commentaries] to the issue of wars and how to wage them. We have no choice other than to base our discussion on the wars waged by Jews as recorded in the <i>Tanach</i> and its commentaries throughout the generations when we come to render a decision in these matters.</i>
<b>The Maharal and the Geneva Convention</b>
Rav Yisraeli and Rav Gutel note that Halachah seems to require conforming to the Fourth Geneva Convention and the norms of civilized countries regarding the ethical manner in which to treat non-combatants during war.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Fourth Geneva Convention forbids harming non-combatants and engaging in collective punishment of non-combatants during war. It was ratified by the State of Israel in 1949.</i> This appears to apply even if the Convention contradicts Halachah, just as we were required to honor the treaty we signed with the <i>Givonim</i> (<i>Yehoshua</i> chapter nine) despite the fact that it violated Halachah (see Rambam, <i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 6:5). Rav Yisraeli notes, however, that this applies not to the theory or rhetoric, but rather to the manner in which the Geneva Convention is practiced by civilized countries.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This is similar to the idea I heard Rav Mordechai Willig cite in the name of Rav Aharon Kotler and Rav Moshe Feinstein that the rule of <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i> (the obligation to honor the laws of land in which we reside) applies to the law as it is applied, not as it is written. For example, Rav Kotler permitted driving sixty-two miles-per-hour in a fifty-five mile-per-hour zone, since police did not issue a ticket for traveling at less than sixty-three miles-per-hour.</i>
Regarding warfare, Harvard Law School Professor Alan Dershowitz writes (<i>The Case for Israel</i> p. 167):
Although collective punishment is prohibited by international law, it is widely practiced throughout the world, including the most democratic and liberty-minded countries. Indeed, no system of international deterrence can be effective without some reliance on collective punishment. Every time one nation retaliates against another, it collectively punishes citizens of that country. The American and British bombings of German cities punished the residents of those cities. The atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki killed thousands of innocent Japanese for the crimes of their leaders. The bombing of military targets inevitably kills civilians.
We may add the following examples to Professor Dershowitz’s list: the Allied blockade of the Central Powers during World War I to force them into submission via starvation; the doctrine of Mutually Assured Destruction, which prevented Soviet attack during the Cold War based on the threat of collective punishment on a massive scale; and the French fight against Algerian terrorists. The practice of Allied forces during the two World Wars established the norm for how civilized nations practice the Geneva Convention when fighting an evil and tenacious enemy that is bent on annihilating its opponents, a norm very much in harmony with the Maharal’s principle of conduct during warfare.
Rav Ariel, Rav Lior, Rav Schachter, and Rav Weiss are justified in following the principle articulated by the Maharal, which has a solid basis in the <i>Tanach</i>, <i>Chazal</i>, <i>Rishonim</i>, and <i>Acharonim</i>. Thus, Halachah permits waging war without excessive regard for civilian casualties if the war is justified and no viable alternative exists through which to wage a successful battle.
We should stress that the IDF does not deliberately target civilians in order to weaken the enemy, as the Allies did during World War II. Israel certainly is justified in attacking terrorists who use civilians as human shields, despite the risk of collateral damage.
Three critical issues must be addressed in order to apply the Maharal to <i>Medinat Yisrael</i>’s current struggle with Arab terrorism. Firstly, is the current struggle categorized as war? Second, is the struggle against a nation? Finally, must Israel risk the lives of its soldiers in an attempt to reduce civilian casualties?
<b>Is the Current Struggle Defined as War?</b>
It is important to stress that the Torah permits risking enemy civilians only during wartime. Rav Yisraeli notes that an individual may not save his life by killing another innocent human being. Thus, it is critical to determine if the current struggle against terrorism is defined as war. The intermittent battles against terrorists are fundamentally dissimilar to a “constant” war of the type Israel waged in the past, such as the Yom Kippur War.
Rav Yisraeli and Rav Hershel Schachter (<i>B’ikvei Hatzon</i> pp. 206-207) argue that the fight against terrorism is defined as a war. Rav Yisraeli addresses a specific situation that occurred in 1953 in which the Israeli forces raided an Arab village named Kibiyeh in response to a series of terrorist attacks that included the murder of a woman and her two small children in Yehud by Arab terrorists. Rav Yisraeli defends the legitimacy of such action as an act of war in which distinction is not drawn between guilty and innocent blood. We again stress that such permission applies only if the war is legitimate and the mission’s success hinges upon risking the lives of enemy civilians.
Rav Yaakov Kaminetzsky (cited by Rav Schachter) maintains that Israel has been in a constant state of war (from a halachic perspective) since the establishment of the state. Rav Yaakov accordingly ruled in 1970 that it was forbidden to ransom the great Rav Yitzchak Hutner, who at that time was being held captive in Jordan by Arab terrorists who had hijacked the flight on which he was a passenger. There was a suggestion to offer a huge sum to ransom Rav Hutner since <i>Tosafot</i> (Gittin 58a s.v. <i>Kol</i>) permit paying an exorbitant sum to save a great Rav. Rav Yaakov argued that <i>Tosafot</i>’s ruling applies only during peacetime. Since Israel’s ongoing struggle with terrorism constitutes a war, Rav Yaakov ruled, it was forbidden to ransom even one as great as Rav Hutner.
Indeed, Rav Yuval Sherlow notes the shifting paradigms of the concept of war. Terrorists wage war in a fundamentally different manner than armies during a conventional war. The military response necessarily must differ as well, and we cannot gauge the morality of such responses using the paradigms of “conventional wars.” The bottom line, Rav Sherlow maintains, is that this struggle is defined as war even if it differs from wars waged in prior generations.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Based on this, Rav Sherlow rules (in a <i>teshuvah </i>addressed to Rav David Eisen) that the IDF tactic of asking an Arab neighbor to knock on the door of a terrorist’s home, which risks the Arab’s life in an attempt to spare IDF soldiers, is completely in consonance with Halachah.</i>
<b>Are We Waging War Against a Nation?</b>
The question arises as to whether or not the State of Israel is considered to be waging a war against the Palestinian community, as it seems that the Maharal’s principle applies only when waging a war against a nation. Rav Yitzchak Blau argues that “Even after recognizing the evil done by terrorists, can it truly be said that modern Israel is in a state of war with the collective body of Palestinians when Israelis frequently hire Palestinian workers?” (<i>Tradition</i> 39:4:17). Nonetheless, Rav Yisraeli and Rav Schachter answer a resounding “Yes” to this question.
Rav Blau’s question emerges from his misapplication of the paradigm of the definition of war from a conventional war to the war against terrorism. The fact that, for example, Americans did not hire Japanese workers during World War II is entirely irrelevant to the current war on terrorism. Indeed, Israelis’ hire Arab workers with the intention, in part, of motivating them to prefer the stability of peace. Moreover, Rav Blau’s question seems to have become moot when the Palestinians elected Hamas to run the Palestinian Authority in 2006. How can one reasonably claim the innocence of the Palestinian people when they chose to elect a party that explicitly calls for Israel’s destruction? Furthermore, the Gaza Strip, which is governed entirely by Hamas, undoubtedly constitutes an enemy nation entirely analogous to the relationship between Japan and the United States during World War II.
Moreover, even if one asserts that Israel is engaged in a war against the army or community of terrorists and not the Palestinian people, the Maharal’s principle remains relevant. Shaul warned the <i>Keini</i> people to move away from <i>Amaleik</i> lest they be killed in the ensuing battle. We see that even though Shaul was waging war only against <i>Amaleik</i>, he was allowed to risk harming another people embedded within them.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The aforementioned Radak also does not limit the permission to risk non-combatants to members of the enemy nation; as long as the intention is to avoid harm to our nation, it is permissible.</i> Similarly, the Israeli army may risk the lives of Palestinian civilians who live among Palestinian terrorists. The same applies to Hezbollah terrorists embedded within the civilian population of Lebanon. As Rav Hershel Schachter commented to me, a war must be fought properly, not with one hand tied behind one’s back.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The idea of properly executing a battle plan even at the expense of civilian members of a nation distinct from the enemy need not necessarily be based on the Maharal. The precedent of Shaul and the <i>Keini</i> serves as a model for Rav Schachter’s assertion even if the Maharal’s principle is inapplicable to such a situation. See also <i>Minchat Asher</i>, Devarim p. 221.</i> Rav Yuval Sherlow similarly stated that there is an ethical obligation for a nation to win a justified war.
<b>Placing Soldiers at Risk to Reduce Civilian Casualties</b>
The Israeli army is clearly entitled to risk the lives of civilians in their efforts to eradicate terrorists. The crucial question, though, is whether civilized countries must risk their soldiers’ lives in order to <i>reduce</i> civilian casualties. This question is debated by leading <i>poskim</i> of our generation. Rav Aharon Lichtenstein believes Israel must “absolutely consider the extent of the justification of killing a large group [of civilians mixed with enemy soldiers] in order to save the life of an individual [Israeli soldier]” (<i>Techumin</i> 4:185). He regards the amount of civilian casualties as a factor to consider when conceiving battle plans. Rav Avraham Shapira (<i>Techumin</i> 4:182) and Rav Dov Lior (<i>Techumin</i> 4:186) strongly disagree. Rav Lior writes, “In times of war, there surely exists firm halachic basis for any action taken in order to ensure that not even one soldier is God-forbid harmed.” Rav Schachter and Rav Bleich told me that they agree with Rav Shapira and Rav Lior. In fact, Rav Schachter argues that Israel acted immorally by risking its soldiers in Jenin and Lebanon in order to reduce Arab civilian casualties. Rav Bleich concurred with Rav Schachter that it is forbidden to risk Israeli lives in order to save Arab civilians. Avi Levinson reports that Rav Mordechai Willig told him that he also agrees with the approach of Rav Shapira and Rav Lior.
We should note that neither side in this debate cites an explicit source regarding this matter.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Professor Eliav Shochetman, though, notes that this issue appears analogous to a questioned posed to Radbaz (<i>Teshuvot Radbaz </i>3:627). The Radbaz was asked whether it is permitted for a person to sacrifice one of his limbs in order to save his friend’s life. He responded that if there is a serious chance that the one sacrificing the limb will be endangered thereby, it is a “foolish piety” to do so, since “his chance [of death] is more significant than his friend’s definite [survival].” It thus appears that it is inappropriate for the IDF to risk its own soldiers in order to save enemies from certain death. The Radbaz’s position is accepted by most <i>poskim</i>. See, for example, <i>Teshuvot Chelkat Yaakov</i> 2:143 and <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 9:28:3 and 9:45. Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> 3:84) thus concludes, “The majority of <i>acharonim</i> concurs that one is forbidden to place himself in great danger in order to save his friend from certain danger.”</i> Rather, it appears to be a question of halachic-moral intuitions of great <i>poskim</i>. We should stress that we cannot say that one side is more stringent or maintains a higher moral standard, because each side believes the opposing position to be morally wrong. One could simply add that just as we cited from Rav Yisraeli and Rav Bleich that there is no halachic source “that takes cognizance of the likelihood of causing civilian casualties in the course of hostilities legitimately undertaken,” so too there exists no classic halachic source requiring or even permitting risking Israeli soldiers to save Arab civilian lives. In the absence of explicit sources in either direction, it is fair to say that the consensus opinion of major rabbinic authorities does not accord with the approach of Rav Lichtenstein on this matter.
Rav Bleich cautions, though, that in certain situations it seems that Israel might be justified in risking soldiers’ lives in order to spare Arab civilians if it feels that causing Arab civilian casualties will later endanger Israeli lives as a result of (unjustified) international condemnations. One might add that if Israel fears that Arabs will be incited by civilian casualties and therefore endanger Israeli lives, risking Israeli soldiers to save (other) Israeli lives might be permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is based on the Gemara (Shevuot 35b) that sanctions a king risking one sixth of the population during wartime. We see that leaders may risk a small segment of the population in the short term in order to preserve the population at large in the long term.</i> We should stress, though, that in these cases, risking Israeli soldiers may be justified solely due to the consideration that it will save Israeli lives in the long run.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Weiss concludes his discussion by noting that there may be a distinction between Jews and non-Jews in this regard. See also Rav Shlomo Yosef Zevin’s <i>L’or Hahalachah</i> pp. 16-18 regarding the issue of non-Jews waging war.</i>
<b>Conclusion</b>
The Torah wishes us to have a degree of compassion even for our enemies. For example, the Ramban (positive <i>mitzvah </i>5 of those the Rambam omitted) cites the <i>Sifri </i>that requires that when besieging an enemy position, we should not completely encircle them. We should leave one side open in order to give the enemy a chance to escape. The Ramban explains that one reason for this rule is that we should have mercy on the enemy soldiers. He adds that it is our interest to do so, since it will encourage enemy soldiers to flee, thereby weakening the morale of our opponents.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Professor Shochetman notes that there is a dispute regarding whether this <i>mitzvah</i> applies even to wars that we are required to wage (<i>milchemet mitzvah</i>), such as defending the Jewish people from attack. Rav Shaul Yisraeli (<i>Techumin</i> 4:35-36) believes it applies only to discretionary wars, whereas Rav Shlomo Goren (<i>Teshuvot Meishiv Milchamah</i> 3:5:245) feels that it applies even to an obligatory war.</i>
Nonetheless, as Rav Hershel Schachter and Rav Yuval Sherlow explain, we must win a war. The compassion we must have for our enemies cannot impede our ability to win a war. Indeed, Rav Sherlow suggests that the IDF’s code of ethics’ first clause should state that that it is a moral obligation for the Israeli army to win its justified battles. He believes that the failure to recognize victory as a fundamental moral principle significantly contributed to the lack of success in the Second Lebanon War of 2006.
The Jewish leadership in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> has made extraordinarily generous offers for peace towards its Arab neighbors throughout the past decades. It accepted the Peel Partition Plan of 1937, the United Nations Partition Plan of 1947, offered to exchange land for peace immediately after the Six Day War in 1967 and, in 2000, Prime Minister Ehud Barak offered stunning concessions to Yasser Arafat at Camp David. The Arabs have rejected every one of these concessions and have responded with wars intended to destroy the State of Israel and exterminate its citizens. Israel clearly has the right to defend itself and enjoys the ethical right, nay, obligation to wage war successfully. Misplaced compassion for enemy soldiers and civilians cannot hamstring our efforts to effectively wage war. The failures of 2006 clearly demonstrate this point.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">We must stress, however, that the Maharal authorizes only a government to harm civilians in war (if it determines that no viable alternative exists). Rav Yoezer Ariel (<i>Techumin</i> 5:356) cites Rav Avraham Shapira, who strictly forbids individuals to take the law into their own hands even if they feel the Israeli government does not adequately punish evildoers amongst our enemies.</i>
Our patriarch Avraham experienced moral anguish over the enemy soldiers that he killed in the war that he successfully waged against the four Mesopotamian kings (see <i>Bereishit Rabbah</i> 44:4 and Rashi to Bereishit 15:1). However, this emotion did not prevent him from carrying out his moral obligation to wage war vigorously and properly against the four Mesopotamian aggressors.
Avraham teaches timeless lessons about misplaced compassion toward our enemies. Similarly, the consensus rabbinic opinion regards the risking of Israeli soldiers and restraint from waging war properly in order to reduce Arab civilian casualties as misguided. May Hashem bless His nation with peace and render this discussion an entirely theoretical concern.

Israeli War Veterans and Nesi'at Kapayim

A question that <i>kohanim </i>who serve in the Israel Defense Forces (IDF) might face upon discharge is whether or not they may continue to perform the <i>mitzvah</i> of <i>nesi’at kapayim</i> (raising the hands to bless the people; Bemidbar 6:22-27). The basis of discussion of this issue is the Gemara (Berachot 32b) stating that a <i>kohen</i> who has killed is disqualified from performing <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>. As a source for this principle, the Gemara cites a <i>pasuk </i>from <i>Yeshayahu</i> (1:15), which teaches that because our hands are filled with blood, Hashem ignores us when we extend our hands (interpreted by the Gemara as a reference to <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>). The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Nesi’at Kapayim</i> 15:3) and the Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 128:35) codify this passage as normative Halachah. We will cite the rulings of Rav Moshe Feinstein, Rav Eliezer Waldenberg, and Rav Ovadia Yosef, who focus on the parameters and scope of this prohibition, discussing such issues as whether it applies to one who kills <i>b’oness</i> (under duress), one who kills a <i>nochri</i>, and one who kills in the course of performing a <i>mitzvah</i>.
<b>Rav Moshe Feinstein – <i>Oness</i></b>
In a brief responsum, Rav Moshe Feinstein (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> Y.D. 2:158) rules that a <i>kohen</i> who was drafted into an army during a time of war and killed someone in the course of battle “is obviously eligible to perform <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>.” Rav Moshe cites a ruling of the <i>Pri Chadash</i> (cited by the <i>Ba’eir Heiteiv</i> O.C. 128:59) as a precedent. The <i>Pri Chadash</i> argues that if a <i>kohen</i> killed after being ordered to kill an innocent individual on pain of death, he may continue to perform <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>. Although the <i>kohen</i> acted incorrectly, for in such a case Halachah (Pesachim 25b and Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 157:1) requires that one forfeit his life rather than kill, he is not disqualified, since he was coerced.
The Mishnah Berurah (128:128) cites the <i>Pri Chadash</i> as normative.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See, though, Sha’ar Hatziyun 128:99, where he expresses some reservation about this ruling.</i> Rav Feinstein writes: “If so (the <i>Pri Chadash </i>is correct), how much more so someone who was drafted into the army and is obligated to join due to the government regulations and did not act in contradiction to Halachah is permitted and even obligated to participate in <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>. This is a simple and clear matter.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">It appears that Rav Moshe’s ruling applies even to a <i>kohen</i> who was drafted to fight in an unjust war, since he was coerced to do so.</i>
<b>Rav Eliezer Waldenberg – <i>Nochri</i> Vicitms</b>
Rav Waldenberg (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 14:60) focuses on whether this prohibition applies to a <i>kohen</i> who has killed a <i>nochri</i>. Halachah clearly forbids murdering a <i>nochri</i>, and, because of the <i>chillul Hashem</i> involved, regards it as a more severe crime than killing a Jew.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Mechilta D’Rabi Yishmael</i>,<i> Mishpatim</i>,<i> Masechta D’nezikin</i>,<i> parashah</i> 4, Ra’avan to <i>Bava Kama</i> 113a, <i>Sefer Yerei’im</i> (245), <i>Meshech Chochmah</i> (Shemot 21:14), and Rav Yehudah Amital’s letter that appears in <i>Alon Shevut</i> 100:55-61 for more information about this subject.</i> Nonetheless, a major rule of Talmudic analysis is that the source of a Halachah shapes its character. Accordingly, the <i>Prishah</i> (O.C. 128:46, printed in the Machon Yerushalayim edition) rules that killing a <i>nochri </i>is not encompassed by this rule, since he understands the <i>pasuk</i> cited from <i>Sefer Yeshayahu</i> as referring only to <i>kohanim</i> who killed Jews.
On the other hand, the Pri Megadim (O.C. 128:51) cites the <i>Tzeidah Laderech</i> (to <i>Parashat Mishpatim</i>), who believes that this disqualification applies to one who murders a <i>nochri</i> as well, since one who kills a <i>nochri</i> is classified by Halachah as a <i>rotzei’ach</i> (murderer).<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For further analysis of this dispute between the <i>Prishah </i>and the <i>Tzeidah Laderech</i>, see Rav Moshe Shternbuch’s <i>Teshuvot V’hanhagot</i> (1:131).</i>
A number of twentieth-century authorities rule in accordance with the <i>Tzeidah Laderech</i>, including Rav Ben-Zion Uzziel (<i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Uzziel</i> 3:10), Rav Gedalia Felder (<i>Yesodei Yeshurun</i>,<i> Ma’arechet Nesi’at Kapayim</i> 31), and Rav She’ar Yashuv Cohen (<i>Techumin</i> 6:31-44).
Rav Waldenberg, however, rules in accordance with the <i>Prishah</i>. First, he notes that many prominent <i>Acharonim</i> endorse this view. These authorities include the <i>Ma’amar Mordechai</i> (O.C. 128:43) and the <i>Kaf Hachaim</i> (O.C. 128:208). Furthermore, Rav Waldenberg notes that the <i>Prishah</i>’s ruling was omitted from most editions of the <i>Tur</i> and was lost for many years due to censorship. Rav Waldenberg argues that as the <i>Tzeidah Laderech</i> was a student of the <i>Prishah</i> and most likely would not have disagreed with the ruling of his mentor had he been aware of it, the <i>Prishah</i>’s view should be considered authoritative. Rav Waldenberg applies the principle articulated by the Rama (C.M. 25:2) that a ruling of a later <i>poseik</i> need not be followed if an earlier source is discovered that contradicts the later <i>poseik</i>’s ruling, since perhaps the later authority was not aware of the earlier ruling and would have retracted his own ruling had he in fact known of it.
Rav Waldenberg thus concludes that a <i>kohen</i> who killed a <i>nochri</i> may perform <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>. He writes emphatically, “<i>Kal vachomer ben beno shel kal vachomer</i> (how much more so) a <i>kohen</i> who killed a <i>nochri</i> in case of war or other emergency should be entitled to participate in <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>.”
<b>Rav Ovadia Yosef – <i>Mitzvah</i></b>
Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> 2:14) refers to the <i>poskim</i> (<i>Teshuvot Tzvi Tiferet </i>37, <i>Teshuvot Levushei Mordechai</i> O.C. 2:17, and <i>Teshuvot Vayitzbor Yosef</i> 36) who grappled with the question of the eligibility for <i>nesi’at kapayim</i> of <i>kohanim</i> who fought in wars and killed soldiers from the opposing side. Rav Ovadia writes:
However, they addressed the issue based on their time and place, and the discussion focused primarily on the issue that they killed enemy soldiers among whom were Jews. [A lingering trauma in the collective Jewish conscience is the tragedy of Jews having fired on fellow Jews during World War I, since Jews were drafted into the armies of the opposing forces - C.J.] Here (in the case of Israeli soldiers who killed enemy soldiers), in a defensive war in which <i>kohanim</i> risked their lives to confront the enemy to save Jewish lives, it is proper to tell these soldiers ‘Yeyasher Kochacha and may your hands be strengthened.’ The matter is clear beyond any shadow of a doubt that they are eligible and worthy for <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>, and they should be blessed by the One Above.
We may add that Rav Ovadia’s ruling applies equally to non-Israeli citizens who volunteer to serve in the Israeli army, since they too are performing a <i>mitzvah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe’s reasoning, though, addresses only soldiers who were drafted into the army and presumably would not apply to volunteers.</i> The ruling also seems to apply to one who has legitimately killed enemy non-combatants in the course of a justifiable military operation, since this is a tragic yet legitimate aspect of the <i>mitzvah </i>of waging war and defending innocent lives.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See our earlier chapter on this subject.</i>
The Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 128:36)<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See the Mishnah Berurah 128:133, who applies this ruling expansively.</i> supports Rav Ovadia’s ruling. The Shulchan Aruch rules that a <i>kohen</i> remains eligible to perform <i>nesi’at kapayim</i> if an infant died as a result of the <i>brit milah</i> the <i>kohen </i>performed on him. The Mishnah Berurah (128:132, citing the Magen Avraham 128:52) presents three reasons for this ruling: the <i>kohen</i> intended to perform a <i>mitzvah</i>, perhaps the child was a <i>neifel</i> (non-viable),<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">A child less than thirty days old is considered to be a <i>safeik neifel</i>, possibly non-viable (Shabbat 135b). One is not culpable for killing a <i>neifel</i> (Rambam <i>Hilchot Rotzei’ach</i> 2:6).</i> and perhaps the child died of other, unrelated causes.
The first reason applies to a soldier in the IDF. We should note that this ruling of the Shulchan Aruch also seems to apply to a soldier who mistakenly killed a fellow Israeli soldier in a “friendly fire” incident. Since this is an almost unavoidable, albeit tragic aspect of warfare, it would seem that in such a case a <i>kohen</i> may continue to participate in <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>, since his intentions were noble. This ruling might not apply, though, if the soldier failed to exercise proper care and caution in the action that led to the death of his comrade.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>kohen</i> who, G-d forbid, killed someone due to reckless driving should consult a Rav to see if he may continue to perform <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>. See <i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> 6:16, <i>Teshuvot V’hanhagot </i>1:131, and Rav Ezra Basri’s essay in <i>Techumin</i> (3:250-254).</i>
<b>Conclusion</b>
Rav Waldenberg, Rav Feinstein, and Rav Yosef offer three distinct justifications for a <i>kohen</i> who killed enemy soldiers in the course of serving in the Israeli army to continue performing <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Yosef Dov Soloveitchik (cited in <i>Nefesh Harav</i> p. 132) disagrees with all of the aforementioned <i>poskim</i> and rules that <i>kohanim</i> who killed as part of his service in the IDF is disqualified from performing <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>. As a precedent, he cites that David was disqualified from building the <i>Beit Hamikdash</i> because he had shed much blood (<i>Divrei Hayamim </i>1:22:8). Rav Soloveitchik apparently accepts the ruling of the Pri Megadim (O.C. 128 <i>Eishel Avraham </i>66) that even a <i>kohen</i> who killed under duress is disqualified as well as the rulings of those authorities who maintain that killing a <i>nochri</i> is included in the disqualification. He also apparently feels that the Shulchan Aruch’s dispensation in the case of the <i>brit milah</i> applies only if all three of the reasons cited by the <i>Mishnah Beruruah</i> are relevant, which is almost never the case in battle. It appears that he did not issue this ruling very often, though, as Rav Aharon Lichtenstein (reported by Rav Ezra Frazer) stated that he never heard Rav Soloveitchik do so.<br>Rav Frazer (<i>Beit Yitzchak</i> 35:651-652) notes that the only compelling proof to Rav Soloveitchik’s approach is the <i>Zohar</i> (<i>Parashat Pinchas</i>), which, according to Rav Felder’s understanding (<i>Yesodei Yeshurun</i>,<i> Ma’arechet Nesi’at Kapayim</i> 31), teaches that Pinchas initially was disqualified from serving in the <i>mishkan</i> because he killed Zimri ben Salu (a leader of the tribe of Shimon), despite the fact that Pinchas’s intentions were for the sake of a <i>mitzvah</i> and ended up saving <i>Am Yisrael</i>.<br>Since the Rama (O.C. 128:35; see, though, <i>Shulchan Aruch ibid</i>. and <i>Bei’ur Halachah ibid.</i> s.v. <i>Afilu Asah</i>) rules that a <i>kohen</i> can regain his eligibility if he does <i>teshuvah</i>, it seems that a <i>kohen</i> is disqualified only if he is considered to have sinned. Thus, a soldier in the Israeli army, who certainly has not sinned by killing the enemies, should not be disqualified. Thus, Rav Soloveitchik’s ruling is quite difficult to understand. <br>Furthermore, I recall that the <i>kohanim</i> who fought in the 1982 Lebanon War continued to participate in <i>nesi’at kapayim</i> at Yeshivat Har Etzion despite the fact that Rav Soloveitchik’s son-in-law and leading student, Rav Aharon Lichtenstein, was one of the <i>roshei yeshivah</i> at that time. Following Rav Soloveitchik’s ruling would deal a significant blow to the morale of Israeli soldiers for whom it is essential to enter combat with a clear and unambiguous understanding that the battles that they wage are clearly justified both morally and halachically. (Rav Soloveitchik does not question the legitimacy of these wars, as he compares them to the wars of David.) Indeed, when <i>kohanim</i> who served during the 1982 Lebanon War asked if they may continue to perform <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>, Rav She’ar Yashuv Cohen (<i>Techumin</i> 6:31-44) responded with a resounding “Yes,” stressing the legitimacy of that war. Although Rav Soloveitchik’s ruling emerges from both his profound reverence for human life and his deep moral character, it does not seem to constitute normative halachic practice.</i> Rav Aharon Adler reports that this is the accepted practice in Israel. Indeed, Jesse Dunietz reports that Rav Aharon Lichtenstein told him that he permits IDF veterans to perform <i>nesi’at kapayim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe Shternbuch (<i>Teshuvot V’hanhagot</i> 1:131) records that the practice in England was to permit <i>kohanim</i> who fought in World War I to continue to participate in <i>nesi’at kapayim</i> even though they might have killed other Jews who were in the opposing armies. He does not record a contrary practice.</i>

Must Diaspora Jews Make Aliyah?

Reading <i>Chumash</i> should prompt Jews who voluntarily remain outside of Israel to think about this choice. We should be provoked to ponder whether we are obligated to follow the example of our ancestors and move to Israel.
We will review this highly sensitive and emotionally charged issue from two different perspectives. The first approach will be that of Rav Moshe Feinstein and Rav Moshe Shternbuch, and the second will be that of Rav Eliezer Waldenberg and Rav Hershel Schachter.
<b>Rav Moshe Feinstein</b>
Rav Moshe Feinstein was asked (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 1:102, written in 1952) whether one should move to Israel in accordance with the view of the Ramban (Bemidbar 33:53 and <i>mitzvah</i> 4 of the positive <i>mitzvot</i> omitted by the Rambam) that even in our day, every Jew is required to live in Israel, or if one should follow the opinion of Rabbeinu Chaim Cohen (cited in <i>Tosafot Ketubot</i> 110b s.v. <i>Hu Omeir</i> and the <i>Mordechai</i>, Ketubot 313) that the <i>mitzvah</i> to live in Israel does not apply today. The latter is of the opinion that since the journey to and subsequent life in Israel is fraught with danger and since it is difficult to fulfill the <i>mitzvot hatluyot ba’aretz</i> (land based <i>mitzvot</i>, which apply only in Israel), there exists no <i>mitzvah</i> to live in Israel “today” (the twelfth century).
Rav Feinstein argues that even though most authorities agree with the Ramban, they believe that if one moves to Israel he has fulfilled a <i>mitzvah</i> (<i>mitzvah kiyumit</i>) but that there exists no absolute obligation to do so (<i>mitzvah chiyuvit</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">An example of a <i>mitzvah kiyumit</i> is the opinion of the Vilna Gaon (<i>Ma’aseh Rav</i> 181, cited by the Mishnah Berurah 475:45) that although there is an obligation to eat <i>matzah</i> only on the <i>seder</i> night, one fulfills a <i>mitzvah</i> every time he eats <i>matzah</i> throughout <i>Pesach</i>.</i> Rav Feinstein believes that even the Ramban believes that living in Israel is only a <i>mitzvah kiyumit</i> and not a <i>mitzvah chiyuvit</i>.
Rav Feinstein seeks to prove that the Rambam does not believe that there is a <i>mitzvah chiyuvit</i> to move to Israel. The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 5:9) writes that it is prohibited to leave Israel but does not state that one is prohibited to reside outside Israel. If an absolute obligation to move to Israel exists, writes Rav Feinstein, the Rambam would have recorded a prohibition to live outside of Israel. Rav Feinstein concludes that since there is no absolute obligation to move to Israel, one must "certainly" consider Rabbeinu Chaim Cohen's concern that one might not fulfill the <i>mitzvot hateluyot ba’aretz</i> properly.
<b>Rav Moshe Shternbuch</b>
Rav Moshe Shternbuch (<i>Teshuvot V’hanhagot</i> 1:900) adopts a somewhat similar approach to this question. He writes:
We must weigh each case individually to see if it is appropriate for him to move to Israel, since the impact of the <i>yeitzer hara</i> (evil inclination) in the Holy Land, whose holiness is exceedingly great, is immense and very seductive. Therefore, if one has a great desire to move to Israel because his religious life outside of Israel is inadequate, it is advisable to first visit Israel and ascertain where he will live, where his sons and daughters will study, and how he will earn a livelihood, and only then he should make <i>aliyah</i>, and he will be successful in his service of Hashem in His holy palace.
<b>Rav Eliezer Waldenberg</b>
Interestingly, Rav Feinstein does not consider the impact of the establishment of the State of Israel in either a positive or negative direction. By contrast, Rav Waldenberg (<i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 7:48:12) very much takes <i>Medinat Yisrael</i> into consideration in an emotional and stirring responsum written shortly after the State was established.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Waldenberg also wrote a very lengthy work entitled <i>Hilchot Medinah</i>, which discusses how to administer the State of Israel in harmony with Halachah.</i> He writes:
With the establishment of <i>Medinat Yisrael</i>, the obligation to make <i>aliyah</i> has become magnified in two aspects. First, the barrier and obstacles of the danger to make <i>aliyah</i> have been removed, and [so has] the obstacle of the inability to earn a living in Israel to the extent of suffering to the point of starvation, G-d forbid. With the removal of these barriers, the halachic exemptions that <i>poskim</i> offer from the obligation to move to Israel are eliminated. Second, we can say the current state in which <i>Medinat Yisrael</i> finds itself – that it has now barely ‘gotten out of diapers’ and is surrounded by enemies sworn to its destruction, Heaven forfend – a special obligation devolves to “arm ourselves swiftly” (see Bemidbar 32:17) and quickly move to Israel and come to the aid of the Jewish people from the enemy that attacked them (see Rambam <i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 5:1)….Indeed, all groups that come to Israel, be they organized or not organized, obviously contribute in this effort, either directly or indirectly.
This nationalistic element does not merit any consideration in Rav Moshe’s <i>teshuvah</i>. Moreover, Rav Waldenberg speaks of moving to Israel as a “central and fundamental <i>mitzvah</i>” and compares it to “children returning to their mother’s embrace.”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Somewhat surprisingly, though, Rav Waldenberg approvingly cites Rav Moshe’s approach to <i>aliyah</i> in <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer </i>14:72, wherein he expresses reservations about encouraging youth to make <i>aliyah</i> against their parents’ wishes.</i> Rav Moshe seems to regard the <i>mitzvah</i> of <i>aliyah</i> as essentially no different than any other <i>mitzvah</i>.
<b>Rav Hershel Schachter</b>
Rav Hershel Schachter (<i>Journal of Halacha and Contemporary Society</i> 8:14-33) adopts the approach that is characteristic of Religious Zionist halachic authorities in Israel today. Rav Schachter cites a responsum of the <i>Avnei Neizer</i> (Y.D. 2:454; though see his addendum to this responsum<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In the addendum, he approvingly cites the Ritva (Yoma 38a s.v. <i>L’alexandria</i>), who asserts that “Nowadays, when it is decreed that we be scattered throughout the world, the only prohibition we have is not to move out of Israel.”</i>), who states that Rabbeinu Chaim Cohen's concerns no longer are relevant. Today, there exists a religious infrastructure that facilitates proper fulfillment of the <i>mitvot hatluyot ba’aretz</i>. No semblance of such a system existed in the days of Rabbeinu Chaim Cohen. Rav Schachter adds that "If conditions were not a hindrance when the <i>Avnei Neizer</i> penned his responsum some ninety years ago, surely now they are no problem."<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See, however, the <i>Pe’at Hashulchan</i> (1), who rules (in the early nineteenth century) that moving to Israel always constitutes an obligation. It is noteworthy that he ruled thus despite the fact that in his times it was very dangerous to do so - it was not uncommon for residents of Israel to die prematurely. The author of the <i>Pe’at Hashulchan</i> himself lost his first wife and almost all of his children due to the exceedingly difficult conditions in Israel at that time.</i>
Rav Schachter assumes that the Ramban believes that moving to Israel is an absolute obligation, not merely a <i>mitzvah kiyumit</i>. This assumption is supported by the Ramban’s words, "In my opinion, this is a positive command to live in Israel and to inherit it because it is given to us." The Pitchei Teshuvah (E.H. 75:6) and <i>Sdei Chemed</i> (<i>Ma’arechet Eretz Yisrael</i> 9), both state, “The Ramban counted it as one of the 613 <i>mitzvot</i>…and it applies in all times…and such is the opinion of all the earlier and later <i>poskim</i>.” This appears to corroborate Rav Schachter’s approach.
Rav Schachter summarizes the many opinions regarding the Rambam’s celebrated omission of the <i>mitzvah</i> of living in Israel from his list of the 613 <i>mitzvot</i>. He presents opinions (cited by <i>Sdei Chemed Ma’arechet Eretz Yisrael</i> 2) that the Rambam believes that the mitzvah to live in Israel is rabbinic and therefore does not list it as one of the 613 <i>mitzvot</i>. Rav Schachter then cites the <i>Avnei Neizer</i> (in his aforementioned responsum), who asserts that once the Rambam counted the <i>mitzvah</i> of conquering the seven nations who lived in Israel prior to the conquest of Yehoshua (Devarim 20:17), he did not find it necessary to count the actual conquest and settlement as a separate <i>mitzvah</i>.
Rav Schachter questions the approach of the <i>Megillat Ester</i>, who asserts that the Rambam does not list this <i>mitzvah</i> because it applies only in biblical and messianic times. At present, claims the <i>Megillat Ester</i>, we are bound by the oath imposed on the Jewish people not to take <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> by force (Ketubot 111a).<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The opinion of the <i>Megillat Ester</i> is the cornerstone of the Satmar Rebbe’s opposition to the establishment of the State of Israel prior to the arrival of <i>Mashiach</i>.</i> Rav Schachter cites the <i>Avnei Neizer</i>'s disproof of the <i>Megillat Ester</i> - that the Rambam includes separating <i>terumot</i> (the <i>Kohen</i>’s tithe) and <i>ma’aserot</i> (other tithes) in his count of the 613 <i>mitzvot</i> despite the fact that it does not apply today on a Torah level according to the Rambam himself (<i>Hilchot Terumot</i> 1:26).
Rav Schachter further notes that the <i>Megillat Ester</i>'s assertion is shocking, because it essentially states that in messianic times, a new <i>mitzvah</i> will be added to the Torah, namely, a <i>mitzvah</i> to live in Israel. The Rambam lists as one of the thirteen principles of faith that no new <i>mitzvot</i> can ever be added to the Torah. Rav Schachter concludes, “In view of the difficulties inherent in the approach of the <i>Megillat Esther</i>, most <i>Acharonim</i> conclude that <i>Yishuv Eretz Yisrael</i> constitutes a <i>mitzvah</i> according to both Ramban and Rambam.” We may add that the relevance of the <i>Megillat Esther</i>’s approach is diminished in light of Rav Meir Simchah of Dvinsk’s statement (cited in the <i>Encyclopedia Talmudit</i> 25:669 footnote 58) that the oath does not apply after the League of Nations endorsed the Balfour Declaration recognizing the right of the Jewish people to establish a homeland in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Aveni Neizer</i> similarly argues that if Jews have permission to move to Israel, the oath does not apply.</i> As such, establishing Jewish sovereignty there is no longer considered taking it by force.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Shlomo Aviner, in his <i>Kuntress Shelo Ya’alu Bachomah</i> (printed at the end of his <i>Hilchot Mashiach</i>) presents thirteen reasons why the oath does not pose an impediment to the creation of the State of Israel or moving to it. </i>
Rav Schachter therefore concludes that most <i>Acharonim</i> are of the opinion that living in Israel constitutes an obligatory <i>mitzvah</i>, even nowadays, according to both the Rambam and the Ramban. Rav Schachter concludes his essay, “Every period of history has its own <i>mitzvot </i>of the hour. Today, when every Jew settling in Israel contributes measurably to the security and economy of the State, and to the Jews in it, <i>Yishuv Eretz Yisrael</i> may indeed be called a <i>mitzvah</i> of the hour.”<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Schachter, though, does outline circumstances that would excuse a person from the obligation to move to Israel.</i>
<b>Reb Tzvi Glatt’s Mei’afar Kumi</b>
It should be noted that Reb Tzvi Glatt<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Reb Tzvi was a student at Yeshivat Mercaz Harav who moved to Israel from Brooklyn at age sixteen and was murdered by Arab terrorists in Chevron in 1980.</i> wrote a very special <i>sefer</i> entitled <i>Mei’afar Kumi</i> in response to Rav Feinstein's <i>teshuvah</i>. Reb Tzvi reviews the many <i>Rishonim</i> and <i>Acharonim</i> who comment on the <i>mitzvah</i> of living in Israel and concludes that the overwhelming majority rejects Rav Feinstein's approach to this question.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Incidentally, Rav Feinstein wrote a letter of approbation to this book, although he notes that he maintains his ruling and believes that Reb Tzvi “exaggerates.” </i> Reb Tzvi writes that he spoke to many of the great authorities in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, including Rav Shlomo Zalman Auerbach (as indicated in <i>Teshuvot Minchat Shlomo</i> 3:158:3), Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv (see, though, <i>Kovetz Teshuvot</i> 2:14), and Rav Yitzchak Yaakov Weisz, and they all agreed that there is an obligation upon everyone to move to Israel, even nowadays, if he can make a living. Indeed, Rav Ovadia Yosef, in his letter of approbation to <i>Mei’afar Kumi</i>, approves Reb Tzvi’s approach (consistent with his ruling in <i>Teshuvot Yechaveh Da’at</i> 4:49).
<b>Conclusion</b>
The question of whether one should make <i>aliyah</i> is complex and depends to some extent on one’s <i>hashkafah</i> regarding <i>Medinat Yisrael</i> specifically and Jewish nationalism in general. There are special circumstances to be considered,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The Pitchei Teshuvah (E.H. 75:6) cites the <i>Teshuvot Mabit</i>’s (2:216) ruling that an area is considered dangerous if merchants do not go there for business. Accordingly, if merchants go to Israel for business, it is not considered dangerous.</i> as we see that even the Pitchei Teshuvah and <i>Sdei Chemed</i> cite authorities who rule that one is not obligated to move to Israel if he is able to earn a living outside of Israel but is not able to do so in Israel. They cite the Gemara's (Shabbat 118a) rule, “<i>Aseih shabatecha chol v’al yitztareich laberiyot</i>” (it is better to eat weekday food on Shabbat than to be reliant on charity) as support for this assertion.
Nonetheless, we cannot be complacent regarding our decisions concerning the <i>mitzvah</i> of living in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Indeed, <i>Chazal</i> (Ketubot 110b-112b) greatly extol the <i>mitzvah</i> of living in Israel. Moreover, <i>Chazal</i> (Ketubot 110b, codified by the Shulchan Aruch E.H. 75:1) view one spouse’s refusal to fulfill the <i>mitzvah</i> to live in Israel as potential grounds for divorce, and they even permit asking a non-Jew to write on Shabbat to facilitate fulfillment of this <i>mitzvah</i> (Gittin 8b, cited by the Rambam, <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 6:11). Rav Aharon Lichtenstein (in a personal conversation) framed the issue succinctly: Just as a Jew would find it painful to live without <i>kedushat hazman</i> (holiness of time, such as Shabbat and <i>Yom Tov</i>), he should find it painful to live without the <i>kedushat makom</i> (holiness of space) of the land of Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">We must also consider Reish Lakish’s harsh criticism of the Jews of Babylon, who for the most part did not return to Israel when given the opportunity to do so (Yoma 9b). Rav Yehudah Halevi (<i>Sefer Hakuzari</i> 2:23-24) similarly criticized the Jews of his generation for failing to make <i>aliyah</i>. </i>

Halachic Process

Beit Din Explaining Decisions

In the Western world judges are expected to offer reasoned decisions for their rulings, which can be reviewed by an appellate court. They thereby publicly demonstrate that their decisions were not made arbitrarily, but rather were the result of a well-thought out and well-founded approach. This practice is a hallmark of a democratic government, a system in which leaders are subject to scrutiny, and transparency in leadership is very much expected.
In this chapter, we will discuss the halachic attitude toward a <i>beit din</i> revealing the logic behind its rulings. We shall discuss the classic sources in this regard as well as the practices of contemporary <i>batei din</i> in both Israel and the United States.
<i><b>Sanhedrin</b></i><b> 29a – No Explanation Required<i></i></b>
<i>Chazal</i> do not require a <i>dayan</i> (rabbinic judge) to explain the reasons for his decisions. The Mishnah (Sanhedrin 3:7) presents the procedure for a <i>beit din</i> issuing its decision: “The most prominent of the judges announces, ‘Mr. so-and-so, you have prevailed, and Mr. so-and–so, you are liable.’” No mention is made of a requirement for the <i>beit din</i> to offer explanations for its decision. In fact, the Shulchan Aruch (C.M. 19:2) states that when one of the litigants requests a written decision, the <i>beit din</i> writes, “So-and-so came with so-and-so his fellow litigant before <i>beit din</i>, and it emerged from their words that so-and-so was victorious and so-and-so was liable.” No mention is made of a requirement to explain the decision.
<i><b>Sanhedrin</b></i><b> 31b and Bava Metzia 69 – Two Possible Exceptions<i></i></b>
Despite the absence of any general requirement for a <i>beit din</i> to explain its decision, the Gemara addresses two exceptional situations in which it is expected that a <i>beit din</i> offer reasons for its decision. The Gemara (Sanhedrin 31b) speaks of two belligerent litigants who are fighting as to where their dispute should be adjudicated. One litigant insists that the local <i>beit din</i> decide the matter, while the other demands that the case be brought to the “<i>mekom hava’ad</i>” for adjudication. Rashi (<i>ibid</i>. s.v. <i>Hatokeif</i>) explains that the <i>mekom hava’ad</i> is an assemblage of many eminent Torah scholars.
The Gemara states that the local <i>beit din</i> may coerce the litigant to arbitrate the matter therein. The Gemara concludes that if the party that wished to go to the <i>mekom hava’ad</i> asks that the local <i>beit din</i> present the reasons for its decision, the <i>beit din</i> writes and delivers it to him. <i>Tosafot</i> (ad. loc. s.v. <i>V’im</i>) state that a litigant enjoys the right to demand an explanation of the decision only if he was coerced to litigate his case in the local <i>beit din</i>. The written decision enables him to bring the decision to the <i>mekom hava’ad</i> or a <i>beit din gadol</i> (rabbinic court of eminent stature) for review.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See our later chapter regarding the circumstances in which a decision may be appealed and/or reversed.</i> Otherwise, a <i>beit din</i> is not obligated to honor a request for an explanation of its decision.
Another case (Bava Metzia 69a-69b) is interpreted by the first opinion in <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>ibid</i>. 69b s.v. <i>Ki Hai Gavna</i>) as presenting another situation in which <i>beit din</i> should offer a reason for its decision. The Gemara describes a case in which one partner in a business venture divided the profits without the consent of the second partner. Rav Papa ruled that the first partner was entitled to do so. Subsequently, the two men partnered to sell wine, and the second partner divided the wine without the first partner’s consent. Rav Papa ruled that the second partner was not entitled to do this, since he might have not divided the wine fairly.
The second partner complained to Rav Papa that he seemed to always side with the first partner. Rav Papa responded, according to <i>Tosafot</i>’s first interpretation, that in such a situation one must present a reason for his decision, which Rav Papa proceeded to give. <i>Tosafot</i> explain that in such a situation, in which there is a basis for a litigant to suspect the <i>beit din</i> of bias, the <i>beit din</i> should reveal its logic in order to “be clean in the eyes of Hashem and Israel” (Bemidbar 32:22). The <i>Sema</i> (14:23) clarifies, though, that this applies only if there is a reasonable basis for the charge of bias, as there was in Rav Papa’s case.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A second opinion in <i>Tosafot</i>, however, does not interpret Rav Papa in a manner that requires a <i>beit din</i> to clarify its grounds in such a case. </i>
<i><b>Shulchan Aruch</b></i><b>, Rama and <i>Sema</i></b>
The <i>Rishonim</i> and <i>Acharonim</i> offer different approaches towards <i>beit din</i> revealing the rationale for its judgment, with some authorities expanding the obligation to present reasoning and others limiting it. The Shulchan Aruch (C.M. 14:1 and 4) codifies both the passage from Sanhedrin 31b (requiring explanation in case of coercion to litigate in the local <i>biet din</i>) and the first opinion in <i>Tosafot</i> (Bava Metzia 69b, requiring the <i>beit din</i> to reveal its logic in cases of suspected biases). The <i>Sema</i> (14:25) adds that even when the <i>beit din</i> is not obligated to disclose its logic, it will do so upon request. This does not seem to constitute an obligation upon <i>beit din</i>, but rather seems to be the appropriate and “righteous” step to take.
The Rama (ad. loc.), however, places three limitations on the obligation for <i>beit din</i> to explain its decision in case of suspicion. First, <i>beit din</i> is not obligated to disclose its logic by a specific time. Rather, whenever it finds the opportunity to do so, it presents its reasoning. Second, the <i>beit din </i>needs merely to write the respective claims of the litigants and the ruling of the <i>beit din</i>, not the reasons for that ruling. Finally, the Rama states that only a lower <i>beit din</i> must explain its reasoning. A <i>beit din gadol</i> need not state its reasoning “because we are not concerned for error, for if we were, there would be no end to the matter.”
The <i>Sema</i> (14:24), though, rules that the second limitation to the case applies only when a litigant seeks to appeal a case to a <i>beit din gadol</i>. An eminent <i>beit din</i> will be able to discern the basis for the ruling based on the facts of the case as presented by the lower <i>beit din</i> without any explanation. He argues that in a case of suspicion, <i>beit din</i> should reveal its logic as well.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">We should also note that the Pitchei Teshuvah (C.M. 14:10) cites the <i>Teshuvot Chavot Ya’ir</i> (in the addendum) as strongly questioning this limitation of the Rama.</i>
<i><b>Noda Biy’hudah </b></i><b>and <i>Chatam Sofer</i></b>
Two major late-seventeenth- and early-eighteenth-century authorities, the <i>Noda Biy’hudah</i> and <i>Chatam Sofer</i>, adopt different approaches as to whether <i>beit din</i> should disclose or withhold explanation. The <i>Noda Biy’hudah</i> (2 C.M. 1, cited by the Pitchei Teshuvah C.M. 14:11), in a characteristically brief but powerful responsum, enlarges the obligation by expanding the definition of coercion in this context. He states that as long as a litigant had to be summoned to <i>beit din</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Gray Matter 2</i> pp. 179-183 for a discussion of how and when these summonses are sent.</i> he is considered to be coerced, requiring <i>beit din</i> to state the claims and ruling in order to facilitate an appeal with a <i>beit din gadol</i>. He adds that this is necessary, “especially in our generation, when mistakes occur frequently.” He also limits a <i>beit din gadol</i>’s exemption from presenting reasons to a court in which each member is a Rav of eminent stature who is renowned for his Torah scholarship. He concludes the responsum with an exceptionally strong statement: “I do not suspect any Rav will refrain from doing so (revealing his reasoning) unless he knows that in truth he did not judge properly, whether deliberately or negligently, and is arrogant and ashamed to acknowledge the truth that he has erred.”
The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (<i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer</i> C.M. 12, cited by the Pitchei Teshuvah C.M. 14:8), however, seeks to limit the obligation on <i>beit din</i> to disclose its logic. He writes:
Granted, it is appropriate and proper for a <i>dayan</i> to explain his reasoning to remove any suspicion of impropriety. Nevertheless, a litigant is not authorized to make such a demand on a judge, and it is audacious of him to tell the <i>dayan</i> that he suspects him of wrongdoing. If he does make such a demand, the <i>dayan</i> should neither reveal his reasoning nor respond to the charge. Only if the litigant refrains from articulating his suspicion due to reverence and respect for the <i>dayan</i> is it proper for the <i>dayan</i> to take the initiative to explain his reasoning in order to extricate himself from suspicion.
These disparate approaches reflect the tension between two competing goals. On the one hand, <i>beit din</i> pursues truth and seeks to preserve its stellar reputation. On the other hand, we are obligated to revere and respect <i>dayanim</i>. Each approach seeks to achieve a balance in the effort to accomplish both goals.
<b>Contemporary <i>Beit Din</i> Practice</b>
It seems that the approach limiting the obligation to present reasons prevailed in the age prior to the twentieth century. Rav Gedalia Schwartz told me that it is evident from the responsa literature that in most cases, <i>beit din</i> did not articulate the basis for its ruling. He noted that one only need look at classic works of responsa and notice that the Choshen Mishpat<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Choshen Mishpat is the part of Shulchan Aruch that deals with monetary law.</i> sections in these works are much smaller than the other sections.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teshuvot Avnei Neizer</i> is one example of this phenomenon.</i>
Beginning in the twentieth century, however, things began to change. Sir Herbert Samuel,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Sir Samuel is cited in Professor Eliav Schochetman’s essay on this topic, which appears in <i>Shenaton Hamishpat Ha’ivri</i> (6-7:355).</i> the first high commissioner of the British Mandate over <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, pressured the Chief Rabbinate to create rabbinic courts of appeal as a prerequisite for the British authorities recognizing the rulings of any <i>beit din</i>. Samuel stressed the need to inspire confidence in <i>beit din</i> among the Jewish population. The Chief Rabbis at the time of the establishment of <i>Medinat Yisrael</i>, Rav Yitzchak Herzog and Rav Ben-Zion Uzziel, responded very positively to this request. Rav Uzziel writes (<i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Uzziel</i> C.M. 1):
There is a greater obligation in our times [for <i>beit din</i> to disclose its reasons], since civil courts explain their rulings with proofs to their decisions, and this enhances their reputations in the eyes of the people. Why should we not act similarly to inspire confidence in the eyes of the nation?…It is appropriate for all <i>beit din</i> decisions, except for conventional and simple cases, to present a summary of the respective arguments of the litigants and the reason for the decision in order to provide the opportunity for appellate court review and to teach Torah law to the nation.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">According to this approach, reasons should be given even for <i>pesharah </i>and<i> pesharah krovah l’din</i> (equity and compromise) decisions, since these rulings also should not be made arbitrarily, as explained in <i>Gray Matter 2</i> pp. 193-200.</i>
Rav Herzog (cited in Professor Eliav Shochetman’s <i>Seder Hadin</i> p. 370) writes that even the <i>Beit Din Hagadol</i> (the Israeli Supreme Rabbinical Court) should write the basis for its decision, “in order to set an example for others and, besides, this practice has manifold benefits.”<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, some Israeli rabbinic court decisions have been printed and published in a collection entitled <i>Piskei Din Rabbaniyim</i>. This series is greatly respected and is a major contribution to the responsa literature. </i>
In practice, however, many Israeli <i>dayanim</i> do not heed the call of Rav Herzog and Rav Uzziel, following instead the traditional system of refraining from offering an explanation for their rulings. Indeed, Professor Shochetman (ad. loc.) writes, “The facts show that in many cases, <i>dayanim</i> do not include reasons for the decisions they issue.”<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Professor Shochetman published this assessment in 1988.</i>
In 1999, responding to the situation described by Professor Shochetman, Rav Tzvi Yehudah Ben Yaakov, a member of the Haifa rabbinic court, renewed the call for <i>batei din</i> to elucidate the reasons for their decisions. He writes (<i>Techumin</i> 19:234): “In our times, one may assume that all <i>dayanim</i> are suspected by the religious public and certainly by the secular public….The broader community suspects that <i>dayanim</i> do not investigate matters thoroughly and rule simply based on impressions and arbitrary reasoning.”
<b>Conclusion – The Practice in America</b>
<b>Rav Ben Yaakov’s call has been heeded among some <i>dayanim</i> who recognize the need for <i>beit din</i> to inspire confidence in the community so that people will choose <i>beit din</i> as the venue to resolve disputes instead of using civil courts, which violates a severe prohibition.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Gray Matter 2</i> pp. 164-178 for a discussion of the parameters of this prohibition.</i> Indeed, a number of American <i>dayanim</i> routinely write explanations of their rulings. It should be noted, though, that sometimes it is in the best interest of both parties for the <i>dayanim</i> to refrain from explaining their decision. Thus, the Beth Din of America’s protocols do not include a requirement that <i>dayanim</i> present the reasons for their verdicts.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The American Arbitration Association (a leading organization of arbitrators in the United States) advises arbitrators in certain types of cases to refrain from explaining the basis for their decisions. They recommend this both to ensure expediency and to avoid frivolous attempts to have the ruling reversed by civil court (communication from the American Arbitration Association).</i> If the parties notify the <i>beit din</i> before the hearing that they desire an elucidation of the reasoning behind the decision, the <i>beit din</i> will likely agree to that request.</b>

Appealing a Halachic Decision

In this section, we will discuss the parameters of when Halachah permits a second Rav to appeal or reverse a halachic decision of another Rav.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For further discussion of the parameters of when a halachic decision may be reversed, see <i>Encyclopedia Talmudit</i> 8:507-510.</i> In this context, we will also discuss the institution of the Rabbinical Court of Appeals that functions in Israel under the auspices of the State of Israel’s Chief Rabbinate.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For further discussion of this fascinating institution, see Rav J. David Bleich’s <i>Contemporary Halakhic Problems IV</i>:17-45 and Professor Eliav Shochetman’s <i>Seder Hadin</i> pp. 443-470.</i>
<b>The Gemara’s Story of Yalta</b>
The Gemara (Niddah 20b) relates that Yalta, the wife of Rav Nachman and a remarkable Talmudic figure in her own right, once brought <i>dam </i>(blood) to Rabbah bar bar Chanah to determine whether it rendered her a <i>niddah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tosafot</i> ad. loc. s.v. <i>Kol</i>, Rosh to <i>Nega’im</i> 2:5, Shach Y.D. 187:5, and <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 6 Y.D. 18 for a discussion of why she did not consult her eminent husband for a halachic decision.</i> Rabbah bar bar Chanah ruled that it did render her a <i>niddah</i>. Yalta appealed the ruling to Rav Yitzchak the son of Rav Yehudah, who ruled that she was not a <i>niddah</i>.
The Gemara is troubled by Rav Yitzchak’s ruling in light of the principle that once a Rav has ruled, it is forbidden for another Rav to reverse the decision of his colleague. The Gemara therefore explains that Rav Yitzchak at first told Yalta that she should follow Rabbah bar bar Chanah’s ruling. Yalta, however, explained to Rav Yitzchak that Rabbah had routinely ruled that the shade of <i>dam</i> that she had shown him did not render her a <i>niddah</i>. It seemed, argued Yalta, that in this instance, Rabbah bar bar Chanah’s eyesight had been impaired, preventing him from rendering an accurate decision. Rav Yitzchak accepted this argument and only then ruled that Yalta was not a <i>niddah</i>. Thus, Yalta’s situation differed from a typical situation of one Rav attempting to reverse another’s decision.
<b>Limitations on the Second Rav</b>
The <i>Rishonim</i> offer various theories for why Halachah generally imposes limits on the second Rav. Rashi (ad. loc. s.v. <i>Mei’ikara</i>) indicates that the rule stems from concern for the dignity of the first Rav who was consulted, while the Ran (Avodah Zarah 1b-2a in the pages of the Rif, s.v. <i>Hanish’al</i>) explains that reversal makes it “appear as if there are two Torahs.” The Ran also quotes from the Ra’avad that when one presents an issue to a Rav, he binds himself to the jurisdiction of that Rav, creating an obligation equivalent to that of a <i>neder </i>(vow). This Talmudic concept is referred to as “<i>Shavya anafsheih chaticha d’issura</i>,”- “He has established the item as prohibited for himself.”
<i>Tosafot</i> (Niddah 20b s.v. <i>Agamrei</i>) are troubled by Yalta’s apparent violation of the Gemara’s (Avodah Zarah 7a) rule that if one posed a question to a Rav and received a strict ruling, he may not appeal the decision to another Rav. Even if Rav Yitzchak was allowed to reverse Rabbah bar bar Chanah’s decision, how was Yalta permitted to ask him to do so? <i>Tosafot</i> explain:
The prohibition devolves on the Rav, not on the individual posing the question. The questioner may ask as much as he wishes – he will merely cause the second Rav to investigate the matter more thoroughly, and sometimes, as a result, it will be discovered that the first Rav erred.
In other words, there is no prohibition to appeal a decision, but it has little purpose in most cases, because a Rav cannot reverse a decision under normal circumstances.
<i>Tosafot</i> in Avodah Zarah (7a s.v. <i>Hanish’al</i>), though, take a more restrictive approach to this issue. They resolve the inconsistency by explaining that the prohibition applies only when the questioner does not disclose to the second Rav that he previously received a strict ruling from another Rav. Since Yalta told Rav Yitzchak that she already received a ruling from Rabbah bar bar Chanah, she was permitted to ask him to reevaluate the situation. <i>Tosafot</i> also state that the second Rav is forbidden to reverse the first Rav’s decision unless he succeeds in convincing the first Rav that he erred. <i>Tosafot</i> in Niddah, by contrast, appear to permit the second Rav to reverse the first Rav’s decision even in the absence of the latter’s consent.
<b>Rama and <i>Acharonim</i></b>
The Rama (Y.D. 242:31), following the Ran,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Also see <i>Tosafot</i> to Chullin 44b s.v. <i>Heichi</i>, who present a similar approach.</i> offers a compromise between the two approaches of <i>Tosafot</i>. He rules that the second Rav may overrule the first Rav if the first made a blatant mistake – an error in “<i>devar mishnah</i>,” accepted halachic practice. Additionally, if the second Rav believes the first to have made an error in judgment – what the Gemara calls “<i>ta’ut beshikul hada’at</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Sanhedrin 33a, Rosh to Sanhedrin 4:6, and Shulchan Aruch C.M. 25:2 for the parameters of what is considered <i>shikul hada’at</i> in contradistinction to <i>devar mishnah</i>.</i> – the former should attempt to convince the latter to retract his ruling.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chochmat Adam</i> (104:6) clarifies that if the second Rav “cannot support his argument with proofs, the [original] strict ruling remains in effect even if the [first] Rav retracts his ruling, since the item already has been established as prohibited.”</i> If the first Rav refuses to retract, though, the second Rav may not reverse the decision of the first.
The Shach (Y.D. 242:53) cites differing opinions among the <i>Rishonim</i> as to whether the second Rav is authorized to reverse the decision of the first if the former is of greater stature. The lenient view maintains that the greater Rav is indeed authorized to do so, even in a matter of <i>shikul hada’at</i>. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 242:62) is inclined to follow this approach, arguing that the reasoning of a more eminent Rav should be considered more compelling.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chochmat Adam</i> (104:6) also presents the lenient position as the primary one.</i> Indeed, superior reasoning ability is often the basis for regarding a particular Rav as greater than his colleagues. On the other hand, Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Taharat Habayit </i>1:323) rules in accordance with the <i>Rishonim</i> who forbid even a great Rav to reverse the halachic decisions of a Rav of a lesser stature unless the first Rav erred in a <i>devar mishnah</i>. Needless to say, it is often exceedingly difficult to decide which Rav is of greater stature.
No restrictions apply, however, if the first individual who rendered a decision was a Torah scholar but not qualified to render halachic decisions (<i>lo higia l’hora’ah</i>). As the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> notes, the halachic decisions of such an individual are considered null and void. Of course, it is difficult at times to determine if someone is regarded as <i>lo higiah l’hora’ah</i>. Obviously, each Torah scholar and Rav must be honest with himself and refrain from issuing rulings when it is not appropriate for him to do so.
The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (Y.D. 242:62) rules that if the Rav is the <i>mara d’atra</i> (Rav of the area or synagogue),<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The concept of <i>mara d’atra</i> appears in Shabbat 19b, Eruvin 94a, Pesachim 30a, and Chullin 53b.</i> he may reverse a decision issued in violation of his jurisdiction.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Conversely, it appears that the decisions of the <i>mara d’atra</i> cannot be reversed even by a Rav of greater stature.</i> It is essential to show proper respect for the authority of a community’s <i>mara d’atra</i>. Rav Hershel Schachter extends this so far as to state that one should not observe a <i>chumrah </i>(stringency) that the Rav of his shul does not observe. Rav Schachter gives the example of standing for <i>keriat hatorah</i>, which essentially is not required by Halachah (see Shulchan Aruch O.C. 146:4).
If a Rav rendered a halachic decision for a Sephardic Jew in accordance with Ashkenazic authorities but in conflict with Rav Yosef Karo, Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Taharat Habayit</i> 1:331) writes that the decision may be overturned, because Rav Yosef Karo is viewed as the <i>mara d’atra</i> of all Sephardic Jews.
<b>The Israeli Rabbinical Court of Appeals</b>
The concept of a court of appeals is widely accepted in the Western world as a basic element of a fair judicial system. Accordingly, beginning in 1921, originally upon the insistence of the British Mandatory Authority, the Israeli Chief Rabbinate’s system of rabbinic courts has included a Supreme Rabbinical Court of Appeals. Rav Avraham Yitzchak Hakohen Kook and Rav Ben-Zion Uzziel (<i>Teshuvot Mishpetei Uzziel </i>C.M. 1), the Chief Rabbis of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, endorsed the establishment of this institution. Rav Uzziel sees this as an example of incorporating positive ideas from <i>nochrim</i> (non-Jews) into the Torah system.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The Gemara (Megillah 9b) refers to this as “<i>Yafyuto shel Yefet yishkon b’aholei Sheim</i>.”</i> Many in the <i>Chareidi</i> community, however, object to this institution, viewing it as an inappropriate emulation of foreign systems of law. Rav Moshe Shternbuch (<i>Teshuvot V’hanhagot</i> 1:796), for instance, writes, “The great rabbis of Israel vigorously protested the establishment of the Rabbinical Court of Appeals as nothing but an imitation of foreign legal systems.”
