Many eminent <i>poskim</i> have sat on this special <i>beit din</i>, including Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg, Rav Yitzchak Herzog, Rav Eliezer Waldenberg, and Rav Ovadia Yosef.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Hundreds of this beit din’s rulings are cited throughout Professor Eliav Shochetman’s Seder Hadin. Many of these decisions involved the reversal or modification of the rulings of lower batei din.</i>
<b>The Halachic Basis for the Court of Appeals</b>
Scouring all of the Talmud, <i>Rishonim</i>, and <i>Acharonim</i> yields no explicit mention of a <i>beit din</i> of appeals in a halachic context.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Although the <i>Seforno</i> understands Shemot 18:21 as presenting a system of appeals, the <i>Seforno</i> is generally seen as a commentary meant to explain verses in the Torah, not as a halachic source.</i> Indeed, the Gemara (Bava Batra 138b) states, “<i>Beit dina batar beit dina lo dayki</i>,” “One <i>beit din</i> does not challenge the ruling of another,” which serves as the basis for both the <i>Sema</i> (C.M. 19:2) and the Shach (C.M. 19:3) to forbid a <i>beit din</i> to rehear a case that another <i>beit din</i> already has judged. Nonetheless, rabbinic courts of appeals functioned in a number of Jewish communities before the twentieth century (as noted by Rav Bleich, <i>Contemporary Halakhic Problems IV</i>:21-24). Indeed, the Gemara itself (Sanhedrin 33a) provides criteria for when a judicial decision may be reversed, and it records cases (ibid. and Ketubot 50b) in which this actually was done. Thus, <i>beit din</i> decisions may be reversed, but there appears to be no traditional formal system for doing so.
Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer </i>2 C.M. 2) justifies the institution of the Supreme Rabbinical Court of Appeals with several arguments. First, the Gemara states only that a rabbinic court “does not” review the decisions of another rabbinic court. Rav Ovadia understands this to mean that their practice was not to do so, but not that it is forbidden to do so.
Second, the Shach and the <i>Sema</i> prohibit only rehearing a case from the beginning. However, Rav Ovadia writes, it is entirely permissible for a second <i>beit din</i> to review the reasons the original <i>beit din</i> gave for its decision and to see if it erred in its decision.
Finally, since it has become accepted practice to maintain a supreme rabbinical court of appeals, it is understood that the <i>dayanim </i>in the lower <i>batei din </i>issue their decisions only on condition that their rulings are not reversed by the Appeals <i>beit din</i>. Indeed, we saw earlier that the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> is inclined to rule that a Rav of greater stature (which the Appeals <i>beit din</i> should be) may always overturn the rulings of a Rav of lesser stature. In addition, since the institution of the Appeals <i>beit din</i> appears in the <i>takanot hadiyun </i>(Rules of Conduct) for the State of Israel Rabbinic Courts, all litigants accept in advance that the Appeals <i>beit din</i> may legitimately reverse the decision of the lower <i>beit din</i> (see <i>Piskei Dinim Rabbaniyim</i> 10:180). The <i>takkanot hadiyun</i> of 5753 (section 135) allow for the Appeals <i>beit din</i> to reverse the decision of the lower <i>beit din</i> if there is either a halachic error, an obvious error in judgment or establishment of the facts, or procedural mistakes that affect the results of the litigation.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Rav J. David Bleich (<i>Contemporary Halakhic Problems IV</i>:44) observes that these categories can be understood as conforming with the Gemara’s (Sanhedrin 33a) standards for when a <i>beit din</i>’s ruling may be reversed. The establishment of the Appeals <i>beit din</i>, accordingly, merely formalizes a mechanism presented in the Gemara. However, some <i>Rishonim </i>permit reversal only if the original <i>beit din</i> concedes that it erred. These <i>Rishonim</i> might agree, though, that a <i>beit din </i>of greater stature may reverse the decision even without the consent of the original <i>beit din</i>.</i>
Rav Ovadia Yosef observes that the Supreme Rabbinical Court of Appeals has worked quite well, and “many times it is found that the lower <i>beit din</i> has erred.” He adds that the Appeals <i>beit din</i> “performs a great <i>mitzvah</i> by insuring proper justice.” Indeed, Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik has stated that history can sometimes resolve a <i>hashkafic</i> or halachic dilemma (<i>Nefesh Harav</i> p. 88).<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See the chapter dealing with incorporating Torah verses into songs and the chapter dealing with the role of archaeology in Halachah for further applications of this assertion.</i> Accordingly, the question of the propriety of the Appeals <i>beit din</i> might be resolved by the fact that it has worked well for many decades.
<b>Conclusion</b>
In certain cases, Halachah provides for the reversal of halachic authorities’ decisions in both ritual and monetary matters. However, the authorities of the past did not institute a formal system for making such appeals. It is possible that Halachah has allowed each community to establish its own mechanisms for appealing halachic rulings. In Israel, Rav Ovadia Yosef reports that the institution of a Supreme Rabbinical Court of Appeals has worked well for many decades, and this model could be emulated by Jewish communities elsewhere as well. Indeed, the rules and procedures of the Beth Din of America (available at www.bethdin.org) provide for appealing rulings to the <i>Av Beit Din</i> (chief justice).<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Ronald Warburg informs me that the <i>Av Beit Din</i> actually has reversed some decisions.</i>

The Role of Archaeology in Halachic Decision Making

A very exciting and relatively new area of halachic concern is the potential effect of archaeological discoveries upon halachic decision-making. The areas of possible impact include proper positioning of <i>mezuzot</i>, <i>mikveh</i> construction, identification of <i>techeilet</i>, the proper time for <i>megillah</i> reading, and the weight of coins used for <i>pidyon haben</i> (redemption of the firstborn).<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah (Bemidbar 18:15-16) requires that a firstborn male child be “redeemed” by giving five <i>shekalim</i> to a <i>kohen</i>.</i> We will discuss the extent to which Halachah accords credibility to archaeological discoveries and conclusions.
<b>Four Classic Discussions</b>
Four classic cases in the Gemara and <i>Rishonim</i> raise the question of the halachic utility of archaeological discoveries. First, the Gemara (Bava Batra 73b-74a) relates that Rabbah bar bar Channah was once traveling in the desert with an Arab guide, who directed him to the graves of the <i>dor hamidbar</i> (those who died during <i>Am Yisrael</i>’s forty-year sojourn in the desert). Rabbah bar bar Channah sought to remove the <i>tzitzit</i> from one of the bodies, hoping to bring it to the <i>beit midrash</i> for scrutiny by the sages, but his efforts failed. When he returned, his colleagues chided him, saying that if he simply wanted to determine whether Halachah follows Beit Shammai or Beit Hillel regarding the number of strings on the <i>tzitzit</i>, he merely had to examine the <i>tzitzit</i> and report his findings, not try to remove a sample.
Rav Hershel Schachter (<i>Nefesh Harav</i> p. 53 footnote 26) notes that <i>Chazal</i> were willing to consider the <i>tzitzit</i> of the <i>dor hamidbar</i> in deciding between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel. Apparently, they believed that we may consider archaeological evidence in rendering halachic decisions. On the other hand, Rav Chaim Kanievsky (<i>Ta’ama D’kra</i>, <i>Parshat Shelach</i>) and Rav Shlomo Aviner (<i>Iturei Kohanim</i> 174:34) argue that Rabbah bar bar Channah’s failure to derive halachic conclusions from his discovery indicates that Hashem does not want us to draw such conclusions from artifacts of the past.
The latter approach seems to contradict the celebrated principle of “<i>lo bashamayim hi</i>” (literally “it is not in heaven”), which states that Heavenly decrees after the time that the Torah was given play no role in halachic decision-making (see Bava Metzia 59b). We may respond that the Gemara merely teaches that halachic authorities should discount divine declarations of agreement with specific rabbinic opinions. However, suggests Rav Yehudah Shaviv (editor’s note to <i>Techumin</i> 24:496), if the divine guidance of history indicates general principles, Halachah might endorse them. Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik adopts a similar approach (see <i>Nefesh Harav</i> p. 88 footnote 29; also see <i>Nefesh Harav</i> p. 53 footnote 26), arguing that Hashem’s will is discernible from the direction of history.
The second classic source on this issue is the <i>Semag</i> (<i>Aseih</i> 22), who endorses the common practice to wear <i>tefillin</i> whose <i>parshiyot</i> are arranged in accordance with Rashi’s view.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi and Rabbeinu Tam dispute the order in which the last two <i>parshiyot</i> should be placed in <i>tefillin</i>. Rashi (Menachot 34b s.v. <i>V’hakorei</i>) believes that “<i>Shema</i>” should come before “<i>V’haya im shamo’a</i>,” while Rabbeinu Tam (cited in <i>Tosafot ibid</i>. s.v. <i>V’hakorei</i>) believes that “<i>V’haya im shamo’a</i>” should precede “<i>Shema</i>.”</i> He supports this position from an ancient set of <i>tefillin</i> that were found buried in the area of the prophet Yechezkel’s grave. The <i>Drishah </i>(O.C. 34) objects that this find does not necessarily disprove the dissenting opinion of Rabbeinu Tam, as these <i>tefillin</i> may have been buried precisely because they were invalid! The <i>Bach</i> (ad. loc.) responds, though, that improper ordering of the <i>tefillin</i> would not have warranted burial, as the <i>parshiyot</i> simply could have been rearranged in the proper order.
A more significant problem with the <i>Semag</i>’s argument is the unreliability of conclusions drawn from one artifact; it is entirely possible that in other digs, <i>tefillin</i> whose <i>parshiyot</i> are ordered according to Rabbeinu Tam’s opinion will be found. In fact, in twentieth-century archaeological excavations of the Dead Sea area, both types of <i>tefillin</i> have been discovered.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Encyclopedia Judaica </i>(15:904). See also <i>Piskei Tosafot </i>(Menachot 92), which records that “In Nahardea and Jerusalem, two pairs [of <i>tefillin</i>] were found, one in accordance with Rashi and the other in accordance with Rabbeinu Tam.” The discovery that the Rashi-Rabbeinu Tam debate already raged in earlier generations should not surprise us. The Gemara frequently records disputes between the <i>Amoraim</i> about issues that the <i>Tannaim</i> had debated in earlier generations. I encountered this phenomenon in the late 1980s, when I became involved in <i>eruv</i> design and construction. I learned that <i>poskim</i> in America and Israel debated whether the positioning of a <i>lechi</i> beneath a wire should be determined by plumb line or by eyesight alone (see <i>Gray Matter 1</i> pp. 182-184). I thought that I could resolve this debate simply by asking older authorities what the practice was in pre-war Europe. To my surprise, I discovered that the same difference of opinion had existed then and merely had reemerged in the 1980s when Jews began building community <i>eruvin</i> in America.</i>
The third classic case of discovering ancient artifacts is recorded in some editions of the Ramban’s commentary to Shemot 30:13, where the Ramban discusses the debate between Rashi and the Rif regarding the weight of a <i>shekel</i>. This impacts a number of areas of Halachah, such as determining the minimum weight of the coins used for <i>pidyon haben</i>. According to Rashi’s opinion, the <i>shekel</i> is one-sixth lighter than the Rif believes it to be. Though the Ramban originally supported the opinion of the Rif, he writes that when he made <i>aliyah</i>, the local Samaritans showed him an ancient coin with “<i>shekel hashekalim</i>” written on one side and “<i>Yerushalayim hakedoshah</i>” written on the other. Upon weighing this ancient <i>shekel</i>, he discovered that it accorded with Rashi’s view, whereupon he concluded, “And behold the words of Rabbeinu Shlomo (Rashi) are supported<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Note that the Ramban states that Rashi’s opinion is “supported,” not “proven,” by the discovered artifact. </i> by a great support.”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yonatan Adler (<i>Techumin</i> 24:497) reports that many such coins have since been discovered throughout <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and have been dated by academicians to the period of the last years of the Second Temple.</i>
Interestingly, the Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 305:1) does not rule in accordance with Rashi despite the Ramban’s discovery. This might be based on two criticisms of the Ramban’s evaluation of his discovery. The Abarbanel (Shemot 30:13-14) notes that it is possible that the <i>shekel</i> lost some of its weight over time.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I have been informed, though, that whether or not the <i>shekel</i> lost weight can be tested scientifically.</i> In addition, the <i>Tashbeitz</i> (3:226) is disturbed that the Ramban relied on Samaritans to decipher the writing on the coin. Since we have profound ideological differences with the Samaritans, their testimony holds no halachic credibility. These two criticisms of the Ramban’s approach foreshadow the two main concerns that some <i>poskim</i> express today regarding the reliability of ancient finds – the integrity of the discoveries and the credibility of archaeologists who are not observant Jews.
The fourth classic case is the dispute (Shabbat 63b) regarding whether the words “<i>kodesh lahashem</i>” (holy to Hashem) on the <i>tzitz</i> (headplate of the <i>kohen gadol</i>) were engraved on one line or two lines. Although the <i>Tanna Kama</i> (the first, anonymous opinion quoted) believes it should be on two lines, Rabi Eliezer the son of Rabi Yose believes it should be on one line. Rabi Eliezer bases his opinion on the fact that he saw the <i>tzitz</i> in Rome, and on that <i>tzitz</i>, the words <i>kodesh lahashem</i> were written on one line.
The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Klei Hamikdash</i> 9:1) presents a nuanced ruling regarding this issue. On the one hand, he rules in accordance with the <i>Tanna Kama</i> that <i>kodesh lahashem</i> should appear on two lines. However, he also considers the opinion of Rabi Eliezer by stating that if it appears on one line, it is acceptable, and he adds that “Sometimes it was written on one line.” The <i>Kesef Mishneh</i> (ad. loc.) explains that the Rambam partially codifies Rabi Eliezer’s opinion because of his claim that he had seen an actual <i>tzitz</i>. Rav Hershel Schachter told me that this also indicates that Halachah considers archaeological evidence.
We may add though, that the Rambam does not extrapolate from the <i>tzitz</i> in Rome as much as Rabi Eliezer did. The latter drew the broad conclusion that if this one <i>tzitz</i> had <i>kodesh lahashem</i> on one line, every other <i>tzitz</i> throughout history did as well. The Rambam, on the other hand, concludes from the sighting only that “sometimes” <i>kodesh lahashem</i> is on one line. After all, only one <i>tzitz</i> was sighted. It is possible that the Rambam might be open to changing his ruling if more artifacts were discovered.
<b>Modern Archaeology and its Limitations</b>
The four cases we have discussed deal with fairly concrete artifacts. The issues raised by modern archaeology, however, are often much more nuanced and abstract. For example, archaeologists looking to date human bones would likely compare them to pottery found in the same stratum. If the pottery has been determined by archaeologists to be from the Canaanite period (before Avraham Avinu’s arrival), archaeologists might conclude that the bones, too, are from that period and hence must be of non-Jewish origin. Does Halachah permit relying on such assertions of professional archaeologists? To answer this question, we will briefly explore the advances and limitations of modern archaeology.
The study of archaeology has advanced very significantly in the past hundred years. Archaeology is constantly evolving, and each succeeding generation has introduced new methods for more accurate exploration and assessment of the past. Today, computers and other technological equipment are standard tools in archaeologists’ ever-expanding arsenal of exploratory techniques. Naturally, this means that each generation of archaeologists can produce more reliable conclusions than its predecessors.
Such rapid evolution, however, is a double-edged sword – archaeology is often questioning and challenging its own findings. Since the early 1900s, each succeeding generation has identified flaws in some of the methods and techniques previously employed. Even some current techniques will possibly be viewed as somewhat antiquated in as little as twenty years as technology makes available new tools for the processing and analysis of artifacts. Accordingly, while we may admire the achievements of archaeologists, we must at the same time recognize the limitations inherent in some of their conclusions.
On the other hand, certain archaeological findings are unlikely to be challenged, such as the readings of the inscriptions on the hundreds of <i>shekalim</i> that were common after the Second Temple era. Similarly, after finding over one hundred stepped pools in Israel that can be identified as <i>mikva’ot</i>, it is unlikely that significant new classes of <i>mikva’ot</i> will be unearthed.
There are other significant limitations that we must also bear in mind when assessing the value of archaeological findings. First, the survival of most artifacts is inherently limited, as use and exposure to the environment cause them to deteriorate over time. Organic items such as food, papyrus, and animal skins do not last unprotected for long. Even metal and stone objects often do not survive in their original form, as we noted earlier. Most items were meant to be used – they were not created with the intention that they endure forever – so only a small percentage of the entire corpus of material actually survives. Second, only tiny percentages of areas of interest have been excavated. The reasons for this include excavation costs, the fact that some areas are inhabited and thus unavailable for archaeological digs, and the wish to allow future archaeologists to test their theories and methods on existing sites. Hence, it is difficult to draw broad conclusions based on documents or artifacts that have <i>not yet</i> been found in archaeological excavations.
Additionally, recorded ancient histories that have been unearthed often include bald lies and exaggerations. Ancient kings frequently employed individuals to record “history” as flatteringly as possible for their regimes rather than as objectively as possible. Even for accurately recorded documents, an integral component of many archaeological studies is the interpretation of the materials that have been unearthed. Interpretation is by definition subjective, and the archaeologist’s political or religious beliefs might color his analyses.
An example is the conclusion of some archaeologists that the battle of Ai described in <i>Sefer Yehoshua </i>did not occur, a conclusion based on excavations at Ai showing that the city was not inhabited at the time of Yehoshua’s entry into <i>Eretz Yisrael </i>(<i>Encyclopedia Judaica</i> 2:471-472). However, Rav Yoel Bin Nun asserts (<i>Mechkerei Yehudah V’Shomron-Ariel</i>, second conference, 5752, pp. 278-289) that he demonstrated that they had excavated the wrong area. He claims to have found the correct location of Ai, which, when subsequently excavated, yielded evidence that it was in fact inhabited during the time of Yehoshua’s conquest.
<b>Twentieth-Century Evaluations – <i>Chazon Ish</i> vs. Rav Kook</b>
Two of the greatest authorities of the first half of the twentieth century expressed their evaluations of archaeological enterprise. The <i>Chazon Ish</i> deals with the question of whether the laws of <i>shemittah</i> apply to produce grown in the city of Beit She’an. The Gemara (Chullin 6b) records that produce grown in Beit She’an is exempt from <i>shemittah</i> restrictions, leading the inhabitants of modern-day Beit She’an to ask whether they could assume their city to be the one the Gemara calls by that name. The <i>Chazon Ish</i> (<i>Shevi’it</i> 3:18-19) rules unequivocally that we may not assume that it is the same Beit She’an. He believes that the practice of identifying places in Israel with their biblical and Talmudic namesakes is built on mere “<i>umdenot</i>” (conjectures), which are insufficient evidence for halachic purposes.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since the time of the <i>Chazon Ish</i>’s death in 1953, the site of Beit She’an has been excavated extensively. Archaeologists today have a much better idea of the borders of the city in ancient times.</i>
In his letters (<i>Collected Letters of the Chazon Ish</i> 3:19), the <i>Chazon Ish</i> reveals his underlying attitude towards archaeology. He writes, “I am not acquainted with the endeavor of excavations and studies of antiquities, and I oppose this enterprise because of the many uncertainties involved.” The <i>Chazon Ish</i> seems to reject wholesale the value of investigating the past by searching for artifacts, believing that anything we need to know about our past has been preserved throughout the generations; we may assume that anything that was not preserved was not worth preserving.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to be identical to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>’s approach to the discovery of previously unknown manuscripts of early halachic authorities. He is famous for rejecting the attachment of any halachic significance to these newly discovered manuscripts. He reasons that Hashem allowed only those manuscripts that were worth preserving to be transmitted from generation to generation; if the transmission of a manuscript was interrupted, it means that Hashem did not want this manuscript to be part of the <i>mesorah</i> (tradition) and the halachic process. It should be noted, though, that not all authorities subscribe to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>’s view on this matter. For example, Rav Ovadia Yosef often relies upon recently discovered manuscripts in issuing halachic rulings. For further discussion of this issue, see Rav Moshe Bleich’s essay in <i>Tradition</i> (27:2:22-25).</i>
Rav Avraham Yitzchak Hakohen Kook (<i>Igrot Hare’iyah</i> 423) adopts an ostensibly similar yet fundamentally different approach to this issue in a brief but illuminating responsum to Rav Yechiel Michel Tukachinsky. The specific issue he addresses is whether a particular location should read the <i>megillah</i> on the fifteenth of Adar based on contemporary scholarship’s conclusion that the area was surrounded by a wall in the time of Yehoshua bin Nun.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Cities surrounded by a wall at the times of Yehoshua read the <i>megillah</i> on the fifteenth of <i>Adar</i>, while those that were not surrounded by a wall at that time read on the fourteenth of <i>Adar</i> (Megillah 1:1).</i> Rav Kook writes (in 1912):
Regarding the issue of establishing the reading of the <i>megillah</i> in the [new] settlements [on the fifteenth of <i>Adar</i>], I do not find that the evidence you have sent me is sufficient to establish these places as having been surrounded by walls during the period of Yehoshua. The evidence does not even rise to the level of doubt, since it must overcome the Rambam’s observation that the <i>rov </i>(majority) of cities of the world was not surrounded by walls during the time of Yehoshua. This entire enterprise of “<i>Eretz Yisrael</i> scholarship” is filled with guesswork. Although this endeavor is worthy of respect and warm admiration for the scholars involved in this study, due to our love of holy Torah matters, one nonetheless cannot make halachic decisions based on the Arab names of a specific area. Nevertheless, if you have any fundamental doubts or insights regarding any of the new settlements, kindly inform me of them and <i>b’li neder</i> I will express my views on this matter.
Although Rav Kook shares much of the <i>Chazon Ish</i>’s skepticism regarding the field of academic <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> studies, he nevertheless seems to evaluate the entire enterprise with a crucially different attitude. First, he articulates positive sentiments about archaeological endeavors in general. Second, Rav Kook keeps an open mind about this matter, expressing willingness to consider more conclusive evidence. The only specific tool he rejects is the use of Arab names for an area.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">It is entirely possible that the Arabs named the town after a different town whose ruins are located in the general vicinity. We rely upon Jewish traditions, such as the identification of an <i>etrog</i> as the “<i>pri eitz hadar</i>” mentioned in the Torah (Vayikra 23:40), because each generation is meticulous about passing on the correct identification to the next generation. We should note that there are other types of evidence that have been used in many cases to identify ancient sites since the time Rav Kook wrote this letter in 1912, as we will see in our later discussion of when to observe <i>Purim</i> in Lod.</i> He expresses a similarly skeptical yet positive and open attitude in <i>Igrot Hare’iyah</i> 574, in which he discusses basing halachic rulings on ancient coins, and in <i>Igrot Hare’iyah </i>91, wherein he discusses the implications of finds from the ancient past regarding the age of the universe.
<b>The <i>Techeilet</i> Controversy</b>
In the early 1990s, Rav Eliyahu Tavger<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Tavger was assisted by Dr. Ari Greenspan, Dr. Baruch Sterman, and Mr. Joel Guberman.</i> (see <i>Techumin</i> 9:425-428) sought to demonstrate that the <i>murex trunculus</i> (a type of snail) is the <i>chilazon</i>, the crustacean whose blood must be used to create the <i>techeilet</i> dye for <i>tzitzit</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Some of the <i>tzitzit</i> strings are supposed to be dyed with <i>techeilet</i>, but the Mishnah (Menachot 4:1) states that we fulfill some aspects of the <i>mitzvah</i> even if we do not have this dye, which has been the case for at least one thousand years. </i> Among Rav Tavger’s proofs are archaeological finds on the northern coast of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, where huge mounds of <i>murex trunculus</i> shells were discovered alongside dyeing vats (<i>Encyclopedia Judaica</i> 15:914). His claim sparked a great controversy, as some believed it likely that a <i>mitzvah</i> that had been lost from <i>Am Yisrael</i> for over one thousand years<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">For a discussion of when precisely the use of <i>techeilet</i> ceased, see Rav Tavger’s essay on this topic published in <i>Kuntreis - Topics in Tekhelet, 5767</i> (available at www.tekhelet.com). He reviews the debate between the Radzyner Rebbe and Rav Yitzchak Herzog regarding whether <i>techeilet</i> was used during the times of the <i>Geonim</i>.</i> finally had been restored, while others doubted this claim. This controversy still has not been resolved, leading some Jews to wear <i>tzitzit</i> dyed with this “new” <i>techeilet</i> and others to refrain.
Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv argues against wearing this <i>techeilet</i> (<i>Kovetz Teshuvot</i> 1:2). One of his arguments is that the Radzyner Rebbe, writing in the late nineteenth century, also claimed that he had discovered the lost <i>techeilet</i>, identifying it as the <i>sepia officinalis</i> (cuttlefish). Subsequently, in the early twentieth century, Rav Yitzchak Herzog demonstrated that the Radzyner’s identification of the <i>techeilet</i> was incorrect, asserting that the <i>techeilet</i> was actually from a snail known as the Janthina. Now, less than a century later, others have refuted Rav Herzog’s claim, too, and have identified a different snail as the authentic source of <i>techeilet</i>. “And,” Rav Eliashiv argues, “we do not know if, in the coming years, others will come and disprove their claim as well.”<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Menachem Burstein, in his work <i>Hatecheilet</i>, fully explains the respective approaches of the Radzyner Rebbe and Rav Herzog. Abundant material regarding the current <i>techeilet</i> is available at www.tekhelet.com.</i>
Rav Eliashiv’s skepticism has ample precedent among the <i>Acharonim</i>. Rav Yonatan Eybeshitz (<i>Kreiti Upleiti</i> 40:4), for one, writes that scientific claims should be treated with great skepticism. He notes that although the works of Galen and Aristotle were accepted as truth for many centuries, they are nowadays dismissed as incorrect. Rav Kook (<i>Teshuvot Da’at Kohen</i> 140) likewise writes that Halachah treats scientific claims as only possibly correct. He even argues that we rely on a physician’s assessment that someone must eat on <i>Yom Kippur</i> merely because of the possibility that he is correct (<i>safeik nefashot l’hakeil</i>).
Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg and Rav Hershel Schachter, on the other hand, consider the current identification of <i>techeilet</i>. Rav Tavger’s identification is based on the work of Rav Herzog, which is based on the work of the Radzyner Rebbe. Each generation does not simply dismiss the previous generation’s work, but rather builds on it to advance the process of identifying <i>techeilet</i>. The contemporary <i>poskim</i> who advocate wearing the <i>techeilet</i> believe that at some point, the evidence becomes convincing enough to rise at least to the level of <i>safeik</i>. Moreover, there are times when <i>poskim</i> accept certain scientific claims as certainly correct, such as in the context of <i>hilchot niddah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See the discussion of the credibility of doctors in <i>Nishmat Avraham </i>2:82-85. </i>
The advocates of the “new” <i>techeilet</i> therefore believe that while it is wise to maintain a healthy skepticism about archaeological and other scientific claims, it is also wise to keep an open mind about them. These <i>poskim</i> consider the current identification of <i>techeilet</i> to be possibly correct (s<i>afeik techeilet</i>). As a precedent that one must wear <i>safeik techeilet</i> since there is a chance that he will be performing a <i>mitzvah </i>thereby, Rav Tavger (in a letter available at www.tekhelet.com) cites the Mishnah Berurah’s ruling (39:26) that one who has access only to <i>tefillin</i> that are merely possibly kosher must wear such <i>tefillin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Dr. Baruch Sterman and Rav Chaim Twerski have written articles that discuss this topic for the <i>Journal of Halacha and Contemporary Society </i>(43:112-124 and 34:77-102, respectively). Both are available at www.tekhelet.com.<br>Since the new techeilet is at best <i>safeik techeilet </i>even according to its supporters, we must also assess whether wearing it entails any halachic risk – whether wearing potentially false <i>techeilet</i> is worse than wearing none at all. Rav Eliashiv argues that there is such a risk, as the Shulchan Aruch (O.C. 9:5; see the Pri Megadim cited in the Mishnah Berurah 9:15) cites an opinion in the <i>Rishonim</i> that the color of the <i>tzitzit</i> strings not dyed with true <i>techeilet</i> should match the color of the <i>tallit</i> to which they are attached. Rav Hershel Schachter responds (<i>Ginat Egoz</i> 2:13) that the possibility of properly fulfilling the biblical <i>mitzvah</i> of <i>tzitzit</i> with <i>techeilet</i> enjoys priority over satisfying this minority opinion cited in the Shulchan Aruch. Of course, since we are not certain that we have succeeded in identifying the correct <i>techeilet</i>, one should not attach wool <i>tzitzit</i> even with the new <i>techeilet</i> to a four-cornered linen garment (see Shulchan Aruch O.C. 9:2). </i>
<b>Can Archaeological Discoveries Substitute for a <i>Mesorah</i>?</b>
Among the reasons marshaled against accepting the “new” <i>techeilet</i> is that a <i>mesorah</i> (tradition) from our ancestors is necessary to identify the authentic <i>chilazon</i>. Indeed, Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik (<i>Shiurim L’zeicher Abba Mari Z”l</i> 1:228) reports that his great grandfather, the <i>Beit Halevi</i>, rejected the Radzyner Rebbe’s identification of the <i>techeilet</i> precisely for this reason. Rav Soloveitchik argues that just as we know that the <i>etrog</i> is the “<i>pri eitz hadar</i>” mandated by the Torah (Vayikra 23:40) purely from a tradition handed down from generation to generation, so must the identity of any animal or plant involved in the fulfillment of <i>mitzvot</i> come from such a tradition. This approach inherently rejects the possibility of reviving a lost tradition before the arrival of <i>Mashiach</i>. The <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (O.C. 9:12) seems to adopt Rav Soloveitchik’s approach as well, as he writes that the <i>mitzvah</i> of <i>techeilet</i> will not be restored until the time of <i>Mashiach</i>.
Rav Shabtai Rappaport suggests a creative response to this objection. One may argue that <i>Mashiach</i> himself will be identified by <i>simanim</i> (signs), namely, the descriptions of <i>Mashiach</i> that are outlined by the <i>Tanach</i>, <i>Chazal</i>, and the Rambam. We can identify the <i>techeilet</i> in a similar manner, since the Gemara in various places (such as Menachot 42b-43a) describes many aspects of the <i>techeilet</i>-making process. On the other hand, we may argue that this is precisely why it is necessary for Eliyahu Hanavi to precede <i>Mashiach</i>’s arrival (Eruvin 43b and Rashi <i>ibid</i>. s.v. <i>Lifnei Bo</i>) – to identify the authentic <i>Mashiach</i> without resorting to <i>simanim</i>. <i>Techeilet</i>, too, might require more than just <i>simanim </i>to be trustworthy.
Rav Hershel Schachter (<i>Nefesh Harav</i> p. 53 footnote 26) notes that the <i>Beit Halevi</i>, in a letter he wrote to the Radzyner Rebbe, adopts a significantly different stance from that presented by his great-grandson.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The letter was first published by the Radzyner in <i>Ein Hatecheilet</i> p. 13, and it also appears in Rav Menachem Burstein’s <i>Hatecheilet </i>p. 190. </i> The <i>Beit Halevi</i> emphasizes that the fish (and the method of extracting its dye) that the Radzyner identified as the <i>chilazon</i> was known among Torah scholars for many generations, yet they never identified it as the <i>chilazon</i>. Thus, we have a “de facto <i>mesorah</i>” that this fish is <b>not</b> the authentic <i>chilazon</i>. In contrast, Rav Schachter comments, Torah scholars in earlier generations seem not to have known about the <i>murex trunculus</i>. Moreover, the method of obtaining a sky-blue dye from this snail was unknown until it was discovered serendipitously in an Israeli laboratory in the 1980s.
The letter published in the Radzyner’s work does not prove Rav Soloveitchik’s presentation of his ancestor’s idea to be incorrect; it simply shows that the <i>Beit Halevi</i> articulated different approaches to this issue. However, the idea expressed in the letter does present an alternative outlook to that presented by Rav Soloveitchik, one that opens the possibility of restoring a lost tradition through the use of archaeology. In fact, the <i>Beit Halevi</i> wrote in his letter that “If this fish [or the method of procuring its dye] was lost and newly rediscovered, we would be obligated to listen to [the Radzyner] and wear [his <i>techeilet</i>].”
Nonetheless, Rav Eliashiv raises another problem with reviving the <i>mitzvah</i> of <i>techeilet</i> today: we lack a <i>mesorah</i> for how to resolve disputes among the <i>Rishonim</i> about the production of the <i>techeilet</i>. Rav Elazar Meyer Teitz (in a personal communication) similarly pointed out the lack of a <i>mesorah</i> for how to tie the knots of the <i>tzitzit</i> and how many strings to dye with the <i>techeilet</i>, both of which the <i>Rishonim</i> debate.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">For a discussion of the many opinions regarding these issues, see Rav Yehudah Rock’s essay in <i>Techumin</i> (16:412-432). </i> Other <i>poskim</i>, such as Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg (in an essay available at www.tekhelet.com) and Rav Hershel Schachter (<i>Ginat Egoz</i> 2:16-18), argue that sufficient analytical bases exist in the Shulchan Aruch and the <i>Acharonim</i> (for example, Mishnah Berurah 9:7) to resolve these disputes. We also have the precedent of <i>shemittah</i> and other <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>-dependent <i>mitzvot</i>, regarding which modern-day <i>poskim</i> have resolved halachic issues despite the absence of clear halachic precedents.
<i><b>Mezuzah</b></i>
Archaeological evidence also appears to be relevant in interpreting the Gemara’s statement (Menachot 33a) that mezuzot may not be placed “in the manner of carpenters.” Rashi (ad. loc. s.v. Avida) explains that this forbids placing mezuzot horizontally, while Rabbeinu Tam (cited in Tosafot ad. loc. s.v. Ha) believes that it forbids placing them vertically. Whereas the Sephardic tradition employs Rashi’s vertical orientation (Shulchan Aruch Y.D. 289:6 and Yalkut Yosef, Y.D. 285:5), Ashkenazic Jews customarily attempt to compromise between the two opinions, placing their mezuzot on a slant (see Rama ad. loc.).<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Essentially, though, even Ashkenazic Jews accept Rashi’s position, as noted by the Vilna Gaon (Bei’ur Hagra Y.D. 289:14).</i> An archaeological perspective on this issue comes from the notches that our ancestors used to etch in their doorways into which they would insert their mezuzot. All the notches that have been found have been vertical or slanted,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">It is interesting to note that the notches have the top portion facing inward, matching the Ashkenazic custom recorded by the Rama.</i> apparently supporting Rashi’s opinion.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Slanted <i>mezuzot</i> are acceptable according to both Rashi and Rabbeinu Tam, as noted by <i>Tosafot</i> (Menachot 33a s.v. <i>Ha</i>).</i> None has yet been found with horizontal notches, which would accord with Rabbeinu Tam’s view.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tarbut Chomrit B’Eretz Yisrael Biymei Hatalmud</i> pp. 60-69, where Professor Daniel Sperber marshals other archaeological evidence based on the construction of doors unearthed at Beit She’arim in Northern Israel which support Rashi’s view.</i>
Unlike the evidence for <i>techeilet</i>, this evidence should not move Ashkenazic Jews to change their tradition. Firstly, the archaeological data might be inadequate. It is possible that homes where the <i>mezuzot </i>were affixed in accordance with Rabbeinu Tam will be found, just as <i>tefillin</i> have been found that match each of Rashi and Rabbeinu Tam’s opinions about the order of the <i>parshiyot</i>.
A second reason for Ashkenazic Jews to maintain their custom is that we should not abandon our tradition even in light of archaeological evidence. As we have seen with respect to the <i>techeilet</i>, archaeology possibly can play a role when there is no <i>mesorah</i>, but it cannot uproot one.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The archaeological findings, though, could reinforce Sephardic Jews’ resolve to abide by the ruling of the Shulchan Aruch (Y.D. 289:6) to follow Rashi’s view. Rav Moshe Shternbuch similarly uses archaeological findings to support the non-Chassidic style of writing the letter <i>tzadi</i>, as we shall see later.</i>
Another reason is that perhaps the debate between Rashi and Rabbeinu Tam already raged in ancient times, and the Jews who kept their <i>mezuzot</i> on a slant did so as a compromise between the two views, much as Ashkenazic Jews do today. Thus, the fact that some <i>mezuzot</i> were slanted does not indicate that the position of Rabbeinu Tam is unfounded.
<i><b>Mikveh</b></i>
There is no exclusive tradition regarding how to construct a mikveh. In fact, at least five different styles of mikveh construction are employed throughout the world today.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Gray Matter 2</i> pp. 255-298 for a fuller discussion of the various methods of constructing and maintaining <i>mikva’ot</i>.</i> The many mikva’ot unearthed in Jerusalem (Techumin 19:448-455) and Massada (Techumin 17:389-398) were rendered kosher using either zeri’ah<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the method of pouring a large amount of non-kosher water into a preexisting kosher pool, which renders the previously unacceptable water kosher.</i> or hashakah.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This is the process whereby a pool of non-kosher water is placed next to a kosher pool and allowed to mix freely with it, which makes the non-kosher water acceptable.</i> There were no split-level rainwater collection pools, unlike many of the mikva’ot constructed today (such as those following the model of Rav Yaakov Breisch, Teshuvot Chelkat Yaakov 3:53-54). Should contemporary mikva’ot be adjusted to match the design of those unearthed in Israel, particularly those discovered outside the southern wall of the Temple Mount?
Contemporary <i>mikveh</i> design does not take these findings into account. Technological advances (and greater availability of water) allow us to construct <i>mikva’ot</i> that meet “higher” standards than were possible in ancient times. In addition , we believe in the concept of <i>yeridat hadorot</i>, meaning that earlier generations, who were closer to the revelation at Sinai, were on a higher spiritual level than we are today (Shabbat 112b). Hence, we require more safeguards in our <i>mikveh</i> design than did our ancestors in Israel. Indeed, contemporary <i>mikveh</i> design is motivated to a great extent by the desire to avoid potential errors.
<i><b>Purim</b></i>
Since cities that were surrounded by walls during the time of Yehoshua bin Nun celebrate <i>Purim</i> on the fifteenth of <i>Adar</i>, various places in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> have encountered the question of whether to consider archaeological evidence in determining what their status was at that time. This question has arisen in two variations. The first concerns areas that have not maintained a tradition that they might have been surrounded by walls during the time of Yehoshua, but which twentieth-century archaeological evidence indicates were surrounded by walls at that time. A variant question arises when the archaeological evidence simply confirms an existing tradition that a city might have been surrounded by walls in Yehoshua bin Nun’s time.
<i><b>Purim</b></i><b> in Beit El<i></i></b>
The first variation arises in a number of places, such as the town currently called Beit El. The <i>Tanach</i> (<i>Shoftim</i> 1:22-25) clearly indicates that the town known in biblical times as Beit El was surrounded by a wall during the time of Yehoshua bin Nun. Yoel Elitzur (<i>Techumin</i> 1:109-118) argues that archaeological (and other) evidence proves that the contemporary town of Beit El (established in the 1970s) is located in the same place as the biblical Beit El. He therefore suggests that <i>poskim</i> should consider ruling that <i>Purim</i> be observed exclusively on the fifteenth of <i>Adar</i> in contemporary Beit El.
The reactions of <i>poskim</i> (recorded in <i>Techumin</i> 1:120-127) were mixed. Rav Shaul Yisraeli felt that <i>Purim</i> should be observed only on the fourteenth of <i>Adar</i>, contending (among other objections) that the archaeological evidence was inadequate to determine that the contemporary Beit El was surrounded by walls during the time of Yehoshua. Rav Ovadia Yosef, though, felt that the evidence was sufficient to rise to the level of <i>safeik</i>, compelling him to rule that “it is worthwhile and proper” to read the <i>megillah</i> in Beit El on the fifteenth of <i>Adar</i> without a <i>berachah</i> in addition to the regular reading on the fourteenth with a <i>berachah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The Gemara (Megillah 5b) records that Chizkiyah maintained such a practice in Tiberias due to unresolved questions as to its status as a walled city. </i> Rav Mordechai Eliyahu was even more inclined to rule that <i>Purim</i> should be observed in Beit El on the fifteenth of <i>Adar</i>, though he stressed the importance of reaching a rabbinic consensus on this matter so as to avoid halachic pandemonium.
Longtime Beit El residents report that they have never heard of anyone in the city reading the <i>megillah</i> on the fifteenth. They follow the ruling of the longtime Rosh Yeshiva of Yeshivat Beit El, Rav Zalman Melamed, who authored a responsum (<i>Techumin</i> 1:130-134) arguing that it is sufficient to read it on the fourteenth. Rav Melamed emphasizes that he believes the archaeological evidence to be so “far from certain” that “In [my] opinion, even a halachic <i>safeik</i> has not been created.” In a conversation with Rav Melamed in 2004, he confirmed that no one actually reads the <i>megillah</i> on the fifteenth in Beit El. He cited the practical difficulties associated with observing <i>Purim</i> on two days and the majority opinion amongst the <i>poskim</i> (based on the Yerushalmi, Megillah 1:1) that if a resident of a walled city (mistakenly) observes <i>Purim</i> on the fourteenth, he nevertheless fulfills his <i>Purim</i> obligations. Rav Ovadia Yosef also notes this last point in his responsum.
<i><b>Purim</b></i><b> in Lod<i></i></b>
A variation of the Beit El debate has emerged regarding the city of Lod. The Gemara (Megillah 4a) states unequivocally that Lod was surrounded by walls during the time of Yehoshua bin Nun. However, Rav Yechiel Michel Tukachinsky (in his famed <i>Luach Eretz Yisrael</i>) rules that Lod should observe <i>Purim</i> on both the fourteenth and fifteenth days of <i>Adar</i>, since we are uncertain whether the city we refer to today as Lod is located precisely where the ancient city of Lod stood. The suggestion emerged in the 1980s, though, that residents of Lod should observe <i>Purim</i> exclusively on the fifteenth, as archaeological evidence seemed to prove incontrovertibly that the current city of Lod lies on the ruins of its ancient namesake.
Yoel Elitzur (<i>Techumi</i>n 9:367-380) suggests to <i>poskim</i> that <i>Purim</i> should now be observed only on the fifteenth of <i>Adar</i> in Lod. He presents what he deems to be overwhelming evidence that the city of Lod is built on the ancient city. He notes that in practically every change to the infrastructure of Lod, ancient relics have been discovered.
Once again, the reactions of <i>poskim </i>(recorded in <i>Techumin</i> 9:365-366) have been mixed. None of the <i>poskim</i> ruled that <i>Purim</i> should now be observed exclusively on the fifteenth of <i>Adar</i> in Lod, but subtle differences are discernible in their respective approaches. Rav Yosef Shalom Eliashiv (<i>Kovetz Teshuvot</i> 1:68:2) seems not to be moved by the archaeological discoveries, writing that the practice recorded by Rav Tukachinsky should be maintained. Dayan Weisz (<i>Teshuvot Minchat Yitzchak</i> 8:61), on the other hand, writes that although <i>Purim</i> should still primarily be observed on the fourteenth of <i>Adar</i> in Lod, as has been the custom, Lod residents should nevertheless be especially careful to hear the <i>megillah </i>without a <i>berachah</i> on the fifteenth of <i>Adar</i>.
Rav Natan Ortner, the Rav of Lod (whose comprehensive essay on this topic appears in <i>Techumin </i>9:341-366), told me in 2004 that when asked, he advises that the <i>megillah</i> be read again on the fifteenth without a <i>berachah</i>, in accordance with the rulings of Rav Eliashiv and Dayan Weisz. He added that although <i>Purim</i> generally is observed on the fourteenth of <i>Adar </i>in Lod, some shuls do indeed conduct <i>megillah</i> readings both in the evening and in the morning of the fifteenth.
<b>Identification of Bones</b>
Probably the most delicate issue that emerges in the context of the debate over the acceptability of archaeological evidence in Halachah is whether or not <i>poskim</i> may accept an archaeologist’s claim, based on currently accepted archaeological techniques, that a set of human remains is not from a Jewish person.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Proof for such an assertion might come from dating the remains to the Canaanite period.</i>
We should note first that Halachah favors claims presented by Torah-observant archaeologists. Although it also accords credibility to non-Torah-observant professionals because we assume that they do not wish to jeopardize their professional standing (see, for example, Shulchan Aruch O.C. 20:1), Halachah nevertheless prefers the advice of a Torah-observant professional. For example, it is preferable to seek the judgment of an observant doctor to determine if a sick individual must fast on <i>Yom Kippur</i> (see Bei’ur Halachah 618:1 s.v. <i>Choleh</i>).
The reason for this is straightforward: as the Mishnah (Bechorot 4:10) records, “One who does not observe a particular <i>mitzvah</i> cannot serve as a judge or witness regarding that <i>mitzvah</i>.” For example, one cannot trust the kashrut of someone who does not abide by the laws of kashrut. One who does not observe a Torah law sometimes cannot psychologically grasp the importance of observing that law meticulously. An archaeologist who does not observe Torah law might not be sensitive to the importance of the great dignity with which Halachah requires us to treat the dead.
However, it is not obvious that Halachah accepts the claims of an observant archaeologist either. <i>Poskim</i> regard many scientific claims with a healthy dose of skepticism, because a later generation of scientists might disprove and reject the claims being made today. Thus, even if a Torah-observant archaeologist claims that scientific methods preclude any chance that bones in an excavation are of Jewish origin, <i>poskim</i> might regard this claim only as possibly correct. <i>Poskim</i> consider the possibility that later generations will reject the validity of the scientific methods of the current generation of archaeologists.
Nonetheless, Rav Kook does write (<i>Teshuvot Da’at Kohen</i> 79 and 191 and <i>Teshuvot Ezrat Kohen</i> 41) that Halachah can accept scientific claims as being either certain or very likely to be certain when ample empirical evidence exists to support the claims. He cites numerous examples in which <i>Chazal</i> accepted specific scientific claims as the basis for their halachic rulings (see <i>Tosefta Ohalot</i> 4:2, <i>Bava Kama</i> 91a, and Sanhedrin 78a).
One example of this is the procedure delineated by the Mishnah (Makkot 3:11) for administering <i>malkot</i> (lashes), which relies upon a doctor’s evaluation of how many lashes the offender can sustain. It seems from this Mishnah that we will rely upon a doctor’s counsel if <i>poskim</i> determine that it is based on a solid foundation of evidence. Rav Yonatan Adler thus concludes (<i>Techumin</i> 24:504) that each specific claim of archaeologists should be evaluated by <i>poskim</i> to determine whether it should be dismissed as conjecture, regarded as possibly correct, or accepted as certain or almost certain truth.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">See our earlier discussion regarding the admissibility of blood tests and DNA evidence in <i>beit din</i> for other examples of how <i>poskim</i> display varying levels of acceptance of scientific claims.</i> Cooperation between <i>poskim</i> and observant archaeologists would be most helpful in reaching appropriate conclusions about such matters.
<b>Writing the Letter <i>Tzadi</i></b>
A final illuminating example of a halachic evaluation of archaeological evidence is a <i>teshuvah</i> of Rav Moshe Shternbuch (<i>Mo’adim Uzmanim</i> 2:166 footnote 2) in which he discusses the celebrated dispute surrounding how to write the Hebrew letter <i>tzadi</i> in Torah scrolls. He reports that ancient <i>tefillin </i>that have been dated to the time of the Bar Kochba revolt support the non-Chassidic, Ashkenazic tradition regarding the shape of this letter. Although Rav Shternbuch expresses very serious reservations about relying on archaeological findings regarding halachic matters, he writes that the <i>tefillin</i> nonetheless demonstrate that many Jews used the non-Chassidic, Ashkenazic style in ancient times. Thus, the Vilna Gaon and the <i>Chazon Ish</i> (O.C. 9:6) are supported in their insistence that non-Chassidic Ashkenazim not deviate from their tradition of how to write the <i>tzadi</i>. Rav Shternbuch even urges them to be sure to hear <i>parashat zachor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This is the section from Devarim 25:17-19 that is read the Shabbat before <i>Purim</i>. The consensus amongst <i>poskim</i> is that this reading is biblically mandated (see Shulchan Aruch O.C. 685:7). As such, we are more stringent regarding the requirements for this reading (see, for example, <i>Mishnah Berurah ibid</i>. 18).</i> read from a Torah scroll whose letters are written in accordance with this tradition. He does not suggest, though, that Sephardic or Chassidic Jews alter their practice based on the archaeological evidence; he merely uses the artifacts as support for those who follow the Ashkenazic practice in this matter. Indeed, it is entirely possible that in the future, <i>tefillin</i> will be found supporting the Sephardic and Chassidic <i>tzadi</i>. Moreover, the mid-twentieth-century work <i>Tzidkat Hatzaddik</i>, written expressly to defend the Chassidic and Sephardic form of the <i>tzadi</i>, includes (p. 40) a picture of a Torah scroll written by the Ran in which the <i>tzadi</i> is indeed written in the style of the Sephardim and Chassidim (also see <i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer </i>2 Y.D.20).<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Another example of a question that could be resolved by archaeology is the size of eggs and olives. The <i>Teshuvot Noda Biy’huda</i> (1 O.C. 38; see also Mishnah Berurah 486:1 and Bei’ur Halachah 271:13 s.v. <i>Shel Revi’it</i>) asserts that eggs today are half as large as those in the times of <i>Chazal</i>, thereby requiring us to use the equivalent of two modern day eggs in order to determine the <i>shiurim</i> of an egg (required for Halachot such as sitting in the <i>sukkah</i>; Shulchan Aruch O.C. 639:2) and an olive (one half of an egg, the <i>shiur</i> required for many <i>mitzvot</i>, including <i>matzah</i>; Shulchan Aruch O.C. 486:1). Other <i>poskim</i>, such as the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (O.C. 168:13 and 472:12), question the <i>Noda Biy’huda</i>’s claim. Professor <br>Avraham Yehudah Greenfield (<i>Techumin</i> 16:433-442) marshals archaeological (and other) evidence that the size of eggs has not changed dramatically since the times of <i>Chazal</i>. Similarly, researchers at Bar-Ilan University announced in 2008 (reported by the Jerusalem Post, April 11, 2008) that they had discovered that the olives in the times of <i>Chazal</i> were in fact smaller than those available today, which would make the <i>shiur</i> of an olive smaller than is commonly assumed. However, the <i>Chazon Ish</i> (O.C. 39:6) rules that the <i>shiurim</i> given by <i>Chazal</i> are by definition imprecise and can be calculated based only on the olives and eggs of each time and place, a position that eliminates the halachic impact of these archaeological findings.</i>
<img><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">These pictures, showing the different styles of writing the <i>tzadi</i>, appear in <i>Sefer Torah Shenimtzah Bah Ta’ut</i> p.49.</i>
<b>Conclusion</b>
The <i>Chazon Ish</i> and Rav Kook might be interpreted as disagreeing as to whether <i>poskim</i> should consider the findings of archaeology. We have seen this debate play out amongst the late-twentieth-century <i>poskim</i>, some of whom are open to the findings of archaeology, and others of whom seem to disregard them. Indeed, we have seen that Rav Mordechai Eliyahu, Rav Moshe Shternbuch, Dayan Weisz, and Rav Ovadia Yosef consider archaeological evidence in their rulings. However, even those who consider the findings of archaeology to be of halachic significance view the findings critically, declining to rule based on archaeologists’ findings when they contradict a <i>mesorah</i> of <i>Am Yisrael</i>.

Estate Planning

Introduction to the Laws of Yerushah and the Ethics of Jewish Estate Planning

<i>Yerushah</i> (inheritance) is one of the most difficult areas of Halachah to apply in modern-day contexts. This section concerns the basic Torah guidelines and attitudes regarding <i>yerushah</i> and grapples with their application in a world whose standards of inheritance generally do not match those of the Torah. We will begin with a discussion of the background of the Torah’s requirements for inheritance and some basic Torah values that should serve as guidelines when drafting a will that conforms to both Halachah and the spirit of the Torah.
<b>The Torah Order of Succession</b>
The Torah (Bemidbar 27:8-11) outlines the halachic order of succession:
If a man dies and has no son, then you shall pass his inheritance on to his daughter. And if he has no daughter, then you shall give his inheritance to his brothers. And if he has no brothers, then you shall give his inheritance to his father’s brothers. And if his father has no brothers, then you shall give his inheritance to the relative who is closest to him of his family, and he shall inherit it. This shall be for the children of Israel as a decree of justice (<i>chukat mishpat</i>), as Hashem commanded Moshe.
A number of points must be clarified regarding the Torah’s order of <i>yerushah</i>. The Mishnah (Bava Batra 8:2) explains that the lineal descendants of any person with priority to succession take precedence over the next in line after that person. For example, the grandchild (son of a son) of the deceased has priority over the daughter of the deceased. If a man dies with no living son, the inheritance passes to any deceased son’s lineal descendants (the grandchildren or great-grandchildren of the deceased), and only if there are no such descendants does it pass to a daughter.
Sons, as stated clearly in the Torah, have priority to inherit. If the deceased was not survived by any sons, his daughters inherit everything. The Mishnah explains that the decedent’s father is third in the line of succession, after sons and daughters, even though the father is not explicitly mentioned in the Torah’s list. Thus, if the decedent was not survived by children, his father inherits all. If the father is not alive, the decedent’s brothers inherit, if there are any; after that, the closest relative inherits.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The entire sequence of looking for heirs is recursively repeated on each of the ancestors and siblings of the deceased until an heir is found. See Shulchan Aruch C.M. 276 and <i>Pitchei Choshen</i> 8:1 for a thorough description of the Torah’s line of succession.</i>
<b>The Special Status of the Firstborn (<i>Bechor</i>)</b>
The Torah (Devarim 21:16-17) commands us to give a firstborn male a double portion of his father’s estate.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A full presentation of the details concerning the special entitlement of the <i>bechor</i> appears in Shulchan Aruch C.M. 277 and <i>Pitchei Choshen</i> 8:2.</i> Thus, if the deceased was survived by five sons, the <i>bechor</i> receives two-sixths of the inheritance, and the other sons each receive one-sixth.
There are several important exceptions to this seemingly simple Halachah. First, a son born by Caesarean section does not qualify for the double portion (Bechorot 8:9). In addition, the Mishnah (<i>ibid.</i>) teaches that the <i>bechor</i> is entitled to receive a double share only from assets held by the decedent at the time of his death (<i>muchzak</i>). The <i>bechor</i> does not receive a double portion from the contingent assets (<i>ra’ui</i>) – the assets to which the decedent had a right at the time of death but which he did not actually have in hand (e.g. unpaid debts). There is considerable debate concerning the implementation of this rule. For example, Rav Ovadia Yosef (<i>Teshuvot Yabia Omer</i> 8 C.M. 8) and Rav Yaakov Blau (<i>Pitchei Choshen</i> 8:2:26) rule that money deposited in a bank is considered <i>ra’ui</i>. On the other hand, Rav Hershel Schachter records (<i>Sha’arei Tzedek</i> 7:110) that Rav Moshe Feinstein believed that money deposited in a bank, including the interest accumulated before death, is considered <i>muchzak</i>. A similar dispute exists between Rav Yechezkel Landau (<i>Teshuvot Noda Biy’huda</i> C.M. 1:34) and the <i>Aruch Hashulchan</i> (C.M. 278:13) as to whether government bonds are considered <i>ra’ui</i> or <i>muchzak</i>.
<b>Spouses</b>
The husband is heir to his wife’s estate and takes precedence over all other heirs. There is considerable debate as to whether this Halachah is a Torah law or a rabbinic enactment.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Bava Batra 109b; also see Rambam, <i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 1:8 and the comments of the Ra’avad, <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, and <i>Kesef Mishneh</i> there.</i> The wife, in contrast, does not inherit her husband’s estate (Bava Batra 111b). She is entitled to be supported out of the husband’s estate only until she remarries or demands payment of her <i>ketubah</i> (Ketubot 4:12).
<b>Daughters’ Inheritance</b>
Although a daughter does not inherit if there are sons, each unmarried daughter is entitled to ten percent<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Attorney Martin Shenkman suggests that this distribution is determined net of any estate tax because of <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i> (the obligation to follow the laws of the country in which we reside), as the tax money effectively belongs to the government from the start.</i> of the estate to be used for her dowry (Ketubot 6:6, <i>ibid</i>. 68a, and Shulchan Aruch E.H. 113:1). In addition, a daughter has the right to be supported by the estate until she is betrothed or reaches the age of <i>bat mitzvah</i> (Ketubot 4:11).
<b>Ethical Wills</b>
Rav Yechiel Michel Tukachinsky, in his classic work <i>Gesher Hachaim </i>(1:41), writes that everyone should write an ethical will in addition to the standard will concerning his assets. As a source for this practice, he cites the celebrated <i>pasuk</i> (Bereishit 18:19) that states that Hashem considered Avraham special “because he commands his children and his household after him that they keep the way of Hashem, doing charity and justice.” Moreover, the Ralbag (I <i>Melachim</i> 2:46) writes that everyone should learn from the example set by Moshe Rabbeinu, Yehoshua, and David Hamelech (we may add Yosef Hatzadik to the list), all of whom presented ethical wills before they died. There is a long tradition among great rabbis, including the Vilna Gaon, Rav Aryeh Levine, and Rav Shlomo Wolbe, to follow the example set by these biblical figures.
A will, or, preferably, a separate personal letter stating one’s Torah vision for his family is a vehicle that can encourage the family and children to follow the Torah lifestyle he wishes for them. Such a personal communication can be extraordinarily beneficial for his children and other heirs. The last communication from parent to child should not be about money alone.
<i><b>Shelom Bayit</b></i>
The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 6:13), in the midst of presenting detailed Halachot concerning inheritance, interjects, “Our rabbis have commanded that one should not treat one child differently from another child even regarding small matters, lest the children come to competition and jealousy, such as what occurred with Yosef and his brothers.” This is surprising, as the Rambam’s <i>Mishneh Torah</i> is a halachic code in which ethical statements are almost always confined to dedicated sections, such as <i>Hilchot Dei’ot</i> and concluding paragraphs of major sections. It appears, then, that the Rambam is teaching that maintaining <i>shelom bayit</i> (familial harmony) should be a prime objective of estate planning.
Obviously, one maintains familial harmony by fairly distributing his assets in the will. One should take special care to avoid using the will as a tool for revenge. In fact, a <i>beit din</i> might invalidate a will if it is evident that the testator sought to take revenge on a family member (<i>Pachad Yitzchak</i> s.v. <i>Ma’avir Nachalah</i> and <i>Techumin</i> 17:301-311).
The clearer the parent’s desires concerning the distribution of assets, the smaller the chance of fighting among family members. The <i>Pitchei Choshen</i> (8:168-169) urges one to plan his will with extreme care in order to avoid family disputes. He writes that one should spell out the terms of his will as clearly as possible, determinedly avoiding any ambiguity. An improperly drafted will can lead to horrific consequences, such as those presented in <i>Techumin</i> (20:94-99).
I believe that one should also avoid discussing the distribution of one’s assets with his heirs; the will should serve as the exclusive (and absolutely clear) determinant of the distribution. Oral instructions create the potential for disharmony, as heirs may note discrepancies between the will and the oral instructions.
The executor of the will should be chosen on the basis of not only of financial acumen and honesty, but also on ability to resolve conflicts and promote Torah values. He should be familiar with the unique familial dynamics so he can manage challenges prudently and sensitively.
Furthermore, providing a mechanism for deciding delicate issues, such as designating a specific Rav or <i>beit din</i> to settle any disputes that may arise,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Teshuvot Tzitz Eliezer</i> 5:29 for an example of a rabbinic resolution of a dispute surrounding the interpretation of a will.</i> greatly increases the chances of preserving familial harmony. Potentially contentious issues include transplants, autopsies, end of life health care, telling the truth to patients, arrangements for nursing home care, and burial arrangements.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Among the burial matters that one should explicitly clarify is his desired burial location (e.g. Israel, family plot, etc.).</i> The need to stipulate a specific mechanism for resolving such issues is even greater in families in which second marriages have occurred. Special care must be taken in these situations to maintain <i>shelom bayit</i>.
Attorney Martin Shenkman suggests that in certain cases one should consider including a letter explaining why he chose to distribute the assets in the manner he spelled out in the will – another worthwhile safeguard against familial discord.
<i><b>Tzedakah</b></i><b> and Truthfulness<i></i></b>
In addition to maintaining familial harmony, every will should be in keeping with Torah values of just, righteous behavior. For instance, the <i>Chafetz Chaim</i> specifically encourages making large charitable gifts in wills (<i>Ahavat Chessed</i> 3:4), a point we will discuss in a later chapter. Truthfulness in carrying out a will also carries great significance.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, the Gemara (Shabbat 55a) states that truth is the “seal” of Hashem – the emblem by which He is known. An example of heroic honesty in connection with estates is the story Rav Hanoch Teller (in his work <i>Builders</i> pp. 90-91) relates about Rav Shneur Kotler.</i> This value mandates, among other things, that one present an accurate picture of an estate on any tax filing.
<b>The Need for a Monetary Will</b>
According to all authorities, one causes a violation of Halachah if he does not leave a halachically acceptable will to ensure that his estate is distributed properly, because the laws of intestacy (dying without a will) almost always contradict the Torah’s directives for the distribution of an inheritance. Thus, it is proper for every Jew to draft a secular will, and, according to most halachic authorities, to take additional steps, as we shall see. Indeed, Rav Feivel Cohen wrote to me that one is fully obligated to draft these documents. He explains that one who fails to do so violates the prohibition of <i>lifnei iveir lo titein michshol</i> (the prohibition for one to facilitate a violation of Halachah) even though the facilitated prohibition will occur only after his death. Similarly, Rav Yitzchak Elchanan Spektor (in a responsum printed in <i>Teshuvot Mateh Levi</i> 13) rules that one is required to take action to ensure that his heirs abide by the Halachot governing inheritance. He compares this to <i>Tosafot</i>’s ruling (Bava Metzia 30b s.v. <i>Afkerah</i>) that one must take proactive steps to ensure that others do not violate the prohibition of theft.
Moreover, Rav Feivel Cohen wrote to me that even though the <i>Chaim Uvrachah L’mishmeret Shalom</i> (in his discussion of wills) writes that one should write a will at age fifty, one should write a will considerably earlier than that age. Rav Cohen bases his ruling on the Gemara (Shabbat 153a) that states that one should consider and plan for the possibility that one might die the next day. He notes (<i>Kuntress Midor L’dor</i> p. 6) the vital importance for parents of young children to write a will to name appropriate guardians for their children in case of death or incapacitation.
Interestingly, the <i>Chaim Uvrachah L’mishmeret Shalom</i> records that Rav Shlomo Kluger wrote a will at age fifty and lived to the age of eighty-six (which was relatively rare in the nineteenth century). Indeed, Rav Ezra Basri writes (<i>Sefer Hatzava’ot</i> p. 5) that one need not be concerned for <i>ayin hara</i> or bad “<i>mazal</i>” as a result of writing a will. He writes that on the contrary, if one’s intentions are to “increase peace in the world,” it “brings one <i>mazal</i>.”

The Halachic Dilemma and Potential Solutions

Although Torah law dictates that wives do not inherit their husbands’ estates and that daughters inherit nothing from a testator who has sons, the most commonly desired distribution today is for sons and daughters to share equally in the estate and for one’s wife (if she is the mother of his children) to inherit the entire estate if he predeceases her. How can one achieve this personal objective without violating the halachic requirements of <i>yerushah</i>? One cannot simply stipulate that he wants his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Mordechai Willig notes (in an essay entitled “Inheritance Without a Fight,” available at www.torahweb.org) that in the common case of joint ownership of a home or other assets, the surviving spouse probably is the owner according to the Halachah as well (as we discussed in an earlier chapter).</i> and/or daughters to inherit, as Halachah regards this as an invalid stipulation (Bava Batra 8:5). Even though we accept the opinion of Rabi Yehudah that “<i>B’davar sheb’mamon tena’o kayam</i>” (monetary stipulations are valid even if they contradict Torah law; Bava Metzia 94a and Shulchan Aruch E.H. 38:5), stipulations made in contradiction to the Torah rules of <i>yerushah</i> are invalid. The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 6:1) explains that the Torah (Bemidbar 27:11) describes the rules of inheritance as “<i>chukat mishpat</i>” (a decree of judgment), meaning that it applies in all circumstances and cannot be overridden by stipulations.
Thus, we are left in a quandary: how can a person distribute his estate to non-halachic heirs, such as daughters or a wife, in a halachically acceptable manner? We shall discuss this predicament from several angles, starting with the question of whether the principle of <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i>, the halachic obligation to follow the law of the land in which we reside (as codified in the Shulchan Aruch C.M. 369), can be invoked to solve the problem.
<i><b>Dina D’malchuta Dina: </b></i><b>A Ruling of the Rashba<i></i></b>
In a variety of contexts, the Gemara mentions the rule of <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i>, which obligates us to follow civil laws such as taxes and traffic regulations.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See our earlier chapter regarding contemporary financial arrangements between a husband and wife for a more thorough discussion of the applicability of this rule when it contradicts the monetary laws of the Torah.</i> However, the rule of <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i> does not apply to <i>yerushah</i>. This point is best illustrated by a responsum of the Rashba (6:254) concerning the following case:
A woman, whom we shall call “Leah,” daughter of “Reuven,” married “Shimon” and gave birth to a daughter. Shortly afterward, Leah and her daughter both died. Reuven subsequently claimed, based on the law of the land, that he had the right to reclaim the large dowry he had given Leah. Shimon, on the other hand, claimed that he had the right to the dowry as per the Halachah that the husband is the primary heir of his wife. The Rashba responded sharply that the halachic standard prevails over <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i> in such a situation. He writes that <i>dina d’malchuta dina</i> applies only to external matters such as taxes and the functioning of a country, but not to internal matters between Jews. If Jews would embrace the civil laws to govern even internal matters, it would lead to complete abandonment of Talmudic civil law. “In that case,” argues the Rashba, “what would become of the holy books of the Mishnah and the Talmud? God forbid, such a thing must never happen in Israel, lest the Torah wrap itself in the sackcloth of mourning.”
This responsum of the Rashba is accepted as normative Halachah, cited by both the <i>Beit Yosef</i> (C.M. 26 s.v. <i>Katav Harashba</i>) and the Rama (C.M. 369:11). <i>Dina d’malchuta dina</i>, then, does not override the Halachot governing <i>yerushah</i>. Accordingly, if one does not take active measures to ensure that his estate is distributed in conformity with the Torah’s order of inheritance, his heirs, as defined by civil law, will violate Halachah. If there is no will, the civil authorities will distribute his estate in accordance with the state laws of intestacy, which almost invariably differ from the laws of the Torah (as noted by Rav Feivel Cohen in <i>Kuntress Midor L’dor</i> p. 6). By not taking the necessary measures, one will cause money to be taken from his halachic heirs and to be given to those who are not halachically entitled to the estate. Halachah views this as unmitigated theft on the part of the non-halachic heirs (see <i>Chiddushei Rav Akiva Eiger</i> C.M. 26:1).<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Beneficiaries who are not halachically entitled to receive their portion of an estate should consult a Rav for guidance on how to rectify this problem.</i>
<b>Rav Moshe Feinstein’s Ruling</b>
It is clear from the Rashba that if one dies intestate, Halachah, not civil law, must control the distribution of his assets. Rav Moshe Feinstein, however, argues (<i>Teshuvot Igrot Moshe</i> E.H. 1:104) that a will drafted in compliance with civil law is halachically valid and that the heirs as set forth in the will are not guilty of theft even if Halachah normally excludes them from inheriting. Rav Moshe acknowledges that since a will attempts to bestow a gift (bequest) to be made after the death of the testator, the will normally is not valid in the eyes of Jewish law. This is because halachic heirs inherit immediately upon the death of the testator. The testator then ceases to own the object in question and therefore is unable to enact a proper <i>kinyan</i> (transfer of title) to the beneficiaries (Gittin 13a). Nevertheless, according to the law of the land, one may transfer property after death even though it no longer belongs to the testator. Rav Moshe writes:
It appears, in my humble opinion, that a [secular] will of this kind, which will definitely be put into effect by the civil authorities of the country in which he resides, does not need a <i>kinyan</i>, for there is no greater <i>kinyan</i> than this [transfer effectuated by the civil law]. Therefore, since a <i>kinyan</i> is not necessary, [the secular will] is halachically effective, even to the extent of receiving money in place of halachic heirs.…And this is a significant basis for the practice [of observant Jews] in this country [the United States] to rely on these types of [secular] wills.
<b>Critique of Rav Moshe’s Opinion</b>
Rav Moshe’s ruling aroused great opposition. Dayan Aryeh Leib Grossnass, for instance, wrote a thorough critique of Rav Feinstein’s ruling (<i>Teshuvot Lev Aryeh</i> 2:57). The authorities who concur with Dayan Grossnass include Rav Ezra Basri (<i>Dinei Mammanot </i>3:208-213), Rav Feivel Cohen (<i>Kuntress Midor L’dor</i> pp. 1-4), Rav Zalman Nechemia Goldberg (<i>Techumin</i> 4:342-344), Rav Hershel Schachter (in a lecture to rabbinical students at Yeshiva University), and Rav Mordechai Willig (in an essay entitled “Inheritance Without a Fight,” available at www.torahweb.org). Several classical <i>poskim</i> contradict Rav Moshe’s position, including the <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (<i>Teshuvot Chatam Sofer</i> C.M. 142), Rav Yaakov Ettlinger (<i>Teshuvot Binyan Tzion Hachadashot</i> 24), and Rav Chaim Ozer Grodzinsky (<i>Teshuvot Achiezer</i> 3:34). All of these authorities reject Rav Moshe’s conclusion and would not sanction the use of a secular will without supplementary halachic mechanisms.
Dayan Grossnass cites a passage from the Gemara (<i>Temura</i> 25a) that seems to clearly disprove Rav Moshe’s contention. The Gemara teaches that when there is a conflict between an individual’s directive and Hashem’s directive, the latter prevails. For example, if one declares that when a firstborn animal will be born it will be a <i>korban olah </i>(burnt offering), Hashem’s designation of the animal from birth as a <i>bechor</i> (a firstborn, which must be brought as a sacrifice; Shemot 13:2) prevails over the individual’s desire. Similarly, Dayan Grossnass argues, if one stipulates that at death his assets should belong to a halachically unrecognized heir, Hashem’s directive that the assets belong to the halachic heir prevails.
